稍后，当它对问题的另一部分有价值时，再恢复一个放弃的想法。实际上，没有坏主意，只有错误地点或错误时间的想法。轮船接手后，帆船消失了，但随着能源成本的上升，风帆正在卷土重来。旧观念不会过时；善于解决问题的领导者记忆力极佳，而且对时机的把握更为敏锐。

Revive a dropped idea later, when it has value for another part of the problem. Actually, there are no bad ideas, only ideas in the wrong place or at the wrong time Sailing vessels disappeared when steamships took over, but as energy costs rise, sails are making a comeback. Old ideas don’t wear out; a problem-solving leader has a terrific memory and an even better sense of timing.

控制质量

CONTROLLING THE QUALITY

柴郡猫告诉爱丽丝，如果她走得足够远，她一定会到达某个地方，就像一个没有目标的黑客，因此无法衡量质量。但是“某处”对于解决问题的领导者来说还不够好。一旦确定并接受了目标，解决问题的领导者就永远不会愿意接受有缺陷的解决方案。领导者通过以下行动控制质量环境。

Cheshire-Cat told Alice that if she walked long enough, she was sure to get somewhere, like a hacker who has no goal, and thus no way to measure quality. But “somewhere” isn’t good enough for a problem- solving leader. Once a goal has been defined and accepted, the problem-solving leader is never willing to accept a defective solution. The leader controls the environment for quality with actions such as the following.

随着项目的进行衡量质量。重新检查规范的能力并不意味着能够在质量上妥协。仅当您实际创建指定的内容时，了解问题才会有所帮助。所有伟大的厨师都会在准备过程中品尝食物，有效解决问题的领导者从不在质量上妥协。他们意识到，如果您不必解决给定的问题，那么任何问题都是微不足道的。

Measure quality as the project proceeds. The ability to reexamine the specifications does not mean the ability to compromise on quality. Understanding the problem helps only if you’re actually creating what is specified. All great chefs taste the food during preparation, and effective problem-solving leaders never compromise on quality. They realize that any problem is trivial if you don’t have to solve what you were given.

设计工具和流程以在构建解决方案时衡量质量。制造商不会偶然地满足时间表和规范，也不会告诉人们更加努力地工作。高科技行业的实施过程本身就是一种高科技产品，需要最好的解决问题的领导能力。在项目过程中，衡量真正的进度和质量有时似乎是一种负担，但好的工具创造了一种环境，使质量控制看起来很自然。

Design tools and processes to measure quality as you build a solution. Manufacturers don’t meet schedules and specifications by accident, or by telling people to work harder. The implementation process in high-tech industries is itself a high-tech product, requiring the best in problem-solving leadership. Measuring true progress and quality some- times seems a burden during a project, but good tools create an environment that makes quality control seem natural.

测量实施速度，将其与进度表进行比较，并准备好更改解决方案程序。完成任务的时间始终是规范的一部分，并且必须始终与原始规范的真实进度进行比较。上市仅晚了几个月，就已经让许多高科技公司倒闭了。

Measure the speed of implementation, compare it to the schedule, and be prepared to change the solution procedure. Time to accomplish the task is always part of the specification, and must always be compared with true progress against the original specification. Coming to market only a few months late has literally put many a high-tech company out of business.

退出该项目以更新您的观点并评估其可行性。有时最好的衡量工具是对您正在做的事情的全新视角。在软件业务中，超过一半的开始实施的项目从未交付。一个注定要失败的项目越早放弃，节省的钱就越多。解决问题的领导者不仅能够看到一个项目何时注定失败，而且能够说服其他人接受厄运，然后再将更多的精力投入到毫无希望的事业中。

Step back from the project to refresh your perspective and to assess its viability. Sometimes the best measurement tool is a fresh perspective on what you’re doing. In the software business, more than half of all projects that begin implementation are never delivered. The earlier a doomed project is abandoned, the more money is saved. Problem- solving leaders are able not only to see when a project is doomed, but also to persuade others to accept doom before pouring more effort into a hopeless cause.

在实施之前与客户核实想法。在流行的形象中，解决问题的领导者是孤独的天才，但真正的领导者更喜欢取得成功。虽然客户可能并不总是对的，但客户是付钱的人，领导者知道如果客户不付钱就不算成功。如果他们的领导建立某种形式的与客户的持续检查，那么不得不放弃的项目就会更少。

Check ideas with the customer before implementing them. In the popular image, a problem-solving leader is a solitary genius, but the true leader prefers to produce a success. Although the customer may not always be right, the customer is the one who pays, and a leader knows it’s not a success if the customer won’t pay for it. Fewer projects would have to be abandoned if their leaders built in some form of continuous checking with the customer.

当一个想法崩溃时恢复士气。解决问题的领导者不愿接受失败，并且知道如何在挫折面前继续前进，尤其是当敬业的员工将挫折视为个人悲剧时。然而，在一个有效的领导者手中，失败实际上是从束缚到无结果的想法的释放，一种更新想法循环并使过程比以往任何时候都更有成效的释放。

Restore morale when an idea collapses. Problem-solving leaders are unwilling to accept failure and know how to keep things moving in the face of setbacks, especially when dedicated workers take setbacks as a personal tragedy. In the hands of an effective leader, though, failure is actually a release from bondage to a fruitless idea, a release that renews the idea cycle and makes the process more productive than ever.

问题

QUESTIONS

  1. 观察你认为是领导者的人。列出此人与他人合作时的活动，看看其中有多少符合理解问题、管理思想流和控制质量的类别。此列表中是否有您从未做过的活动？为什么不？
  2. Observe someone you consider a leader. Make a list of this person’s activities when working with others, and see how many of them fit into the categories of understanding the problem, managing the flow of ideas, and controlling the quality. Are there activities on this list that you never do? Why not?
  3. 观察一个你不认为是领导者的人。列出此人错过的一些行使领导力的简单机会。你错过了这些同样的机会吗？为什么？
  4. Observe someone you don’t consider much of a leader. Make a list of some simple opportunities for exercising leadership that this person misses. Do you miss these same opportunities? Why?
  5. 您是否遇到过让人们关注您的想法的困难？你对他们的想法有何反应？
  6. Do you ever have trouble getting people to pay attention to your ideas? How do you react to their ideas?
  7. 当您在团队中工作时，您使用什么技巧来了解您正在做的事情？你什么时候一个人工作？你会如何提高你看待自己行为的能力？
  8. What techniques do you use for gaining perspective on what you are doing when you are working in a group? when you are working alone? How might you improve your ability to see your own actions?
  9. 下次你与团队一起工作时，列出你为锻炼领导力所做的所有事情。如果您没有至少 10 个项目，请再次执行作业，并继续执行，直到您从一项活动中获得包含 10 个项目的列表。有了清单后，将项目归入理解问题、管理思想流和控制质量的类别。您的风格是否倾向于偏爱一个类别而不是其他类别？您的哪些行为不属于这些类别中的任何一个？
  10. Next time you work with a group, list all the things you do to exercise leadership. If you don’t have at least ten items, do the assignment again, and keep doing it until you get a list of ten things out of one activity. When you have your list, put the items into the categories of understanding the problem, managing the flow of ideas, and controlling the quality. Does your style tend to favor one category over the others? Which of your actions don’t fit into any of these categories?
  11. 总体而言，您需要采取哪些新行动来加强您作为解决问题领导者的风格？
  12. Overall, what new actions would you need to practice to strengthen your style as a problem-solving leader?

第 4 章领导者如何发展

Chapter 4. How Leaders Develop

年复一年见证默默耕耘

Year after year beheld the silent toil

展开他光亮的线圈；

That spread his lustrous coil;

尽管如此，随着螺旋上升，

Still, as the spiral grew,

他离开了去年的住所，来到了新的，

He left the past year’s dwelling for the new,

轻轻地踏过它闪亮的拱门，

Stole with soft step its shining archway through,

建立它的空门，

Built up its idle door,

在他最后找到的家中伸展，并且

Stretched in his last-found home, and

不再认识旧的了。

knew the old no more.

为你建造更庄严的豪宅，0我的灵魂，

Build thee more stately mansions, 0 my soul,

随着快速的季节滚动！

As the swift seasons roll!

离开你低矮的过去！

Leave thy low-vaulted past!

让每一座新的神殿，比上一座更尊贵，

Let each new temple, nobler than the last,

用更大的穹顶将你与天堂隔绝，

Shut thee from heaven with a dome more vast,

直到你最终获得自由，

Till thou at length art free,

把你那长不完的壳留在生命的波涛汹涌的大海中！

Leaving thine outgrown shell by life’s unresting sea!

——奥利弗·温德尔·福尔摩斯，《密室鹦鹉螺》

—Oliver Wendell Holmes, “The Chambered Nautilus”

领导者是变革的领导者——他人的变革、工作组的变革以及组织的变革。最重要的是，领导者本身就是变革的领导者。要成为领导者，您必须了解变化是如何发生的；但是很难看到自己的变化。现在我们有了一个简单的模型来帮助我们理解领导者做什么，让我们开发另一个简单的模型来解释领导者是如何发展的。

Leaders are leaders of change–change in other people, change in working groups, and change in organizations. Above all, leaders are leaders of change in themselves. To become a leader, you have to understand how change happens; yet it’s difficult to see change in yourself. Now that we have a simple model to help us understand what leaders do, let’s develop another simple model to explain how leaders develop.

该模型有两个主要的交替阶段，缓慢增长和快速增长，我们分别称之为高原和峡谷。它们形成一个循环，所以我们不能说哪个先到。我们可以从任意一点开始描述循环。效仿福尔摩斯，我可以用鹦鹉螺来说明生物学的过程。或者我可以使用 Nautilus 设备培训，以生理学来说明它。但由于我是弹球手而不是健美运动员，所以我想坚持这个例子。那么，要问的问题是，一个人如何提高弹球的水平？

The model has two major alternating stages, slow growth and fast growth, which we call plateaus and ravines, respectively. They form a cycle, so we cannot say which comes first. We can start describing the cycle at any arbitrary point. Following Holmes, I could use the nautilus to illustrate the process in biology. Or I could use training on Nautilus equipment, to illustrate it in physiology. But since I’m a better pinballer than a body builder, I’d like to stick with that example. The question to ask, then, is, How does one improve at pinball?

熟能生巧

PRACTICE MAKES PERFECT

我父亲在台球上总是能打败我，但在弹球上，我有优势。我想知道他怎么会成为这么好的台球手，他想知道为什么他打弹球打不过我。我仍然会玩相当公平的弹球游戏，比我的任何孩子都好。但在电子游戏中，它们让我望尘莫及。他们不明白像我这样的老笨蛋怎么能成为银球巫师，当我无法在 Gorf 或 Snerg 获胜时，我感到很困惑。

My father could always beat me at pool, but at pinball, I had the advantage. I wondered how he had gotten to be such a good pool player, and he wondered why he couldn’t beat me at pinball. I still play a pretty fair game of pinball, better than any of my kids. But at video games, they leave me in the dust. They can’t figure out how an old coot like me can be a silver ball wizard, and I’m baffled when I can’t win at Gorf or Snerg.

如果你在过去四十年的选定时间点测试我的弹球技能，它可能看起来像图 4.1。连接这些点的线似乎是相当稳定的进展；回想起来，我不记得我开始打得更好的任何特定时刻。所以从这个角度来看，你如何提高弹球的答案是：通过每天练习四十年，你的弹球技术稳步提高。

If you tested my skill at pinball at selected points over the past forty years, it might look like Figure 4.1. A line connecting the points would appear to be pretty steady progress; and looking back, I can’t recall any particular moment when I began to play significantly better. So from this point of view, the answer to the question of how you improve at pinball would be this: By practicing every day for forty years, you steadily improve your pinball skills.

这个答案对我们这些弹球老玩家有很大的吸引力。它说因为我们浪费了我们的青春，所以年轻的鞭子永远不会赶上来。但偶尔，他们中的一个确实会赶上来，就像几年前从我手中夺走城市冠军的那个长着痘痘的 12 岁小男孩。也许简单的练习并不是全部。

This answer has a lot of appeal for us old pinball players. It says that because we misspent our youth, the young whippersnappers are never going to catch up. But once in a while, one of them does catch up, like that little twelve-year-old with zits who took the city championship from me a couple of years ago. Maybe simple practice isn’t the whole story.

图 4.1。GMW 四十多年的弹球技艺。

Figure 4.1. GMW’s skill at pinball over forty years.

练习是缓慢成长阶段的一部分，毫无疑问，练习会有所帮助，但故事还有更多内容，即使我们不记得了。（我总是有点怀疑自己的记忆力，尤其是在四十年的时间里。）也许更短的时间跨度和更准确的记录会揭示其他一些因素。

Practice is part of the slow growth stage, and there’s no doubt that practice helps, but there’s more to the story, even if we don’t remember it. (I’m always a bit suspicious of my memory, especially over a forty-year period.) Perhaps a shorter time span, with a more accurate record, would reveal some other factor.

大跃进

THE GREAT LEAP FORWARD

每当一台新机器进入社区时，我都觉得有必要掌握它。图 4.1 也可以是我在几周内在一台新机器上的得分图。再一次，看起来稳定的练习等于稳定的进步，但是当我绘制更精确的数据时，我得到了一个截然不同的画面。图 4.2 显示了我在黑骑士上的平均每日分数，从我是新手到我以 3,568,200 的分数刷新本地记录。

Whenever a new machine is brought into the neighborhood, I feel compelled to master it. Figure 4.1 could also be a plot of my scores on a single new machine over a period of a few weeks. Again, it looks like steady practice equals steady progress, but when I plot more precise data, I get a rather different picture. Figure 4.2 shows my average daily scores on the Black Knight from when I was a novice until I captured the local record with a score of 3,568,200.

大多数增长似乎来自从一个高原到另一个高原的突然跳跃。尽管在两次飞跃之间有缓慢而稳定的增长，但它只占总增长模式的一小部分。

Most of the growth seems to come in sudden leaps from one plateau to another. Although there is slow, steady growth between the leaps, it accounts for only a minor part of the total growth pattern.

真正的大进步来自于一些突破。就黑骑士而言，这些并不是更好的脚蹼工作或碰撞。他们对如何更好地玩游戏有不同的想法。例如，如果你把事情安排得恰到好处，黑骑士会同时把三个球放在比赛场地上；在三球比赛中，您的任何得分都算三倍！我的一大飞跃是意识到在三分球的情况下比分可以上升多快。

The really big progress comes from just a few breakthroughs. In the case of the Black Knight, these weren’t better flipper work or box bumping. They were different ideas about how the game could be played better. For instance, if you get things set up just right, the Black Knight will put three balls on the playing field at the same time; and while the three balls are in play, any score you make counts triple! One of my great leaps forward was realizing how fast the score could mount in the three-ball situation.

图 4.2。GMW 在黑骑士上的平均得分。

Figure 4.2. GMW’s average scores on the Black Knight.

但是，如果没有一定程度的练习，我永远不会体验到三分球的情况。在我掌握了黑骑士的一些小技巧之前，获得正确设置的可能性很小，因此在高原上的稳定改进最终对于离开高原并进入下一个高原至关重要。

Without a certain amount of practice, though, I never would have experienced the three-ball situation. Until I had mastered some of the Black Knight’s little tricks, there was little probability of getting the right setup, so the steady improvement on the plateau was ultimately essential to getting off the plateau and onto the next one.

然后，图 4.2 提出了一个更复杂的成长模型：熟能生巧，但是当你开始觉得自己真的变好了时，就开始寻找一些概念上的突破。换句话说，花一些时间掌握战术，但不要忘记寻找更好的策略。

Figure 4.2, then, suggests a more sophisticated model of growth: Practice makes perfect, but when you begin to feel you’re really getting good, start looking for some conceptual breakthrough. In other words, spend some time mastering tactics, but don’t forget to look for a better strategy.

坠入深谷

FALLING INTO THE RAVINE

“寻找更好的策略”说起来容易，但做起来并不容易。您可能认为玩三个球会更好，但如果您尝试这种设置，您可能会落入机器的陷阱之一。要找到更好的弹球策略，你必须在实践中检验你的想法，但要做到这一点，你必须偏离你已经知道如何做得很好的策略。

It’s easy to say, “Look for a better strategy,” but it’s not so easy to do. You may have an idea that playing three balls would be better, but perhaps if you try for that setup, you’ll fall into one of the machine’s traps. To find a better pinball strategy, you have to test your idea in practice, but to do that, you have to deviate from the strategy you already know how to do so well.

图 4.2 显示了我在 Black Knight 上的平均进度，但图 4.3 显示了相同的进度，但没有对一些细节进行平均。每座高原前都有一条峡谷。每当我试图改进时，我都没有经历过一次大的飞跃，直到我经历了一次小小的倒退。

Figure 4.2 shows my average progress on the Black Knight, but Figure 4.3 shows the same progress without averaging out some of the details. In front of each plateau is a ravine. Whenever I tried to improve, I didn’t experience a great leap forward until I had experienced a small stumble backward.

比如我掌握了三球策略后，我的成绩就跃上了一个新的台阶，感觉还不错。我现在可以打败 Red Baron 的所有常客，尽管我仍然没有赚到足够的免费游戏来免费玩一整天。当我在高原上慢慢改善时，我开始怀疑我错过了什么。虽然打三个球的时候我的得分很快，但偶尔，当我试图让三个球都打下去时，我一下子就失去了三个球。它只发生了四分之一，但这意味着我失去了三个宝贵的转弯之一。

For example, once I had mastered the three-ball strategy, my scores leapt to a new plateau, which made me feel pretty good. I could now beat all the regulars at the Red Baron, though I still wasn’t earning enough free games to play all day for free. As I slowly improved on my plateau, I began to suspect that I was missing something. Although my scores mounted quickly when three balls were in play, once in a while, when trying to keep all three balls going, I lost all three at once. It happened only about one time out of four, but it meant I had lost one of three precious turns.

我想知道如果我不再试图让所有三个球继续运转，而是专注于确保三个球中的一个留在比赛场地上，会发生什么。当我尝试这个新策略时，我的分数立即跌入谷底。事实上，当时我正在和一个相当公平的孩子比赛，他开始打我。无法面对失败，我又回到了原来的策略，让他回到原位。

I wondered what would happen if I stopped trying to keep all three balls going and concentrated instead on being sure that one of the three was retained on the playing field. When I tried this new strategy, my scores immediately fell, into the ravine. In fact, I was playing against a pretty fair kid at the time, and he started beating me. Unable to face defeat, I went back to my old strategy and put him in his place.

不过后来，当我没有观众时，我又尝试了新策略。我的分数再次下降，但我注意到我并没有那么频繁地输掉一个回合，当我同时打三个球时，也许只有五次输一次。稳步地，通过练习，我提高了忽略其他两个球并将至少一个球留在比赛场地上的能力。每次打三球我的得分都不是那么高，但我的总得分越来越高。我还必须为自己的季度打更长的时间，这是比赛的主要目标之一。事实上，我开始赢得足够的免费游戏来玩一个季度的几个小时，这是游戏的真正目标。

Later, though, when I didn’t have an audience, I tried the new strategy again. Once again my scores dropped, but I noticed I wasn’t losing a turn so frequently, perhaps only once in five times when I played three balls at once. Steadily, with practice, I improved my ability to ignore the other two balls and keep at least one ball on the playing field. I didn’t score so heavily each time there were three balls in play, but my overall scores got larger. I also got to play longer for my quarter, which is one of the major objectives of the game. In fact, I started winning enough free games to play for several hours on one quarter, which is the real objective of the game.

图 4.3。GMW 的弹球技巧如同沟壑和高原。

Figure 4.3. GMW’s pinball skill as ravines and plateaus.

因此，图 4.3 代表了一个更加完善的增长理论。有高原，但你不是真的跳跃，而是攀登。为了攀登，你必须离开稳固的立足点，放弃你已经做得很好的事情，并可能滑下峡谷。如果你从不放弃你已经做得很好的事情，你可能会继续稳步前进，但你永远不会走出高原。

Figure 4.3, then, represents an even more refined theory of growth. There are plateaus, but you don’t really leap, you climb. In order to climb, you must leave the sure footing, letting go of what you already do well and possibly slipping downward into a ravine. If you never let go of what you already do well, you may continue to make steady progress, but you’ll never get off the plateau.

在现实世界中成长

GROWTH IN THE REAL WORLD

峡谷-高原增长模型有多普遍？它适用于弹球以外的任何事物吗？图 4.3 的平滑曲线表明该模型有点过于简化。实际增长通常看起来更像图 4.4，它代表了我的黑骑士分数在没有平均的情况下的样子。弹球游戏，就像生活一样，有很大一部分随机性，任何理论都无法解释。

How general is the ravine-plateau model of growth? Does it apply to anything besides pinball? The smooth curve of Figure 4.3 suggests the model is a bit oversimplified. Real growth generally looks more like Figure 4.4, which represents what my Black Knight scores looked like without averaging. Pinball, like life, has a good share of randomness for which no theory can account.

图 4.4。GMW 的黑骑士得分没有平均。

Figure 4.4. GMW’s Black Knight scores without averaging.

我们最直接体验到的成长部分是随机性，它掩盖了其他模式。当一次幸运的击球让我的分数上升了350,000分时，我顿时信心倍增。当无法解释的倾斜使我跌破一百万时，我开始相信我正在变老，并且正在下滑。当你经历天气时，很难看到气候。

The part of growth we experience most directly is the randomness, which obscures the other patterns. When a lucky shot raises my score by 350,000 points, I suddenly feel a great surge of confidence. When an unexplainable tilt drops me below a million, I start to believe I’m getting old, and slipping. It’s hard to see the climate when you’re experiencing the weather.

但被许多真实生长过程的噪音所掩盖，存在着峡谷-高原模式。整个国家都遵循这种模式，各种规模的行业、组织和团队也是如此。个人也是如此。

But masked by the noise of many real growth processes, there is a ravine-plateau pattern. Entire nations follow the pattern, as do industries, organizations, and teams of all sizes. So do individuals.

我重视我的独特性，我讨厌那些告诉我我只是一个巨大的无形模式的一部分的人。我永远不会说任何人都“只是”更大模式的一部分；这意味着缺乏自由选择。此外，人不能成为模式的“一部分”，因为模式不是真实的。它们是帮助我在几章中传达一生经验的工具。了解他人的经历可以扩大我们的选择范围。通过了解这些模式，解决问题的领导者可以做出更好的职业选择——与成为“模式的一部分”完全相反。

I value my uniqueness, and I resent people who tell me I am merely part of a large invisible pattern. I would never say that anyone is “merely” part of a larger pattern; it implies lack of free choice. Besides, people cannot be “part” of a pattern because the patterns are not real. They are devices to help me communicate a lifetime of experience in a few chapters. Understanding the experiences of others expands our choices. By understanding these patterns, problem-solving leaders can make better career choices—quite the opposite of being “part of a pattern.”

在接下来的章节中，我们将更详细地了解人们在成为解决问题的领导者时会发生什么。为此，无论是模型还是描述都不足以告诉您您需要了解的关于您自己的开发的信息。模型和其他描述可以说出外面发生的事情，但不知道发生时的感受——比如站在高原上的感觉是多么的令人兴奋，抬头看下一个高原是多么的可怕，跌入高原是多么的痛苦峡谷。

In the following chapters, we’ll look in more detail at what happens to people as they become problem-solving leaders. To do this, neither models nor descriptions will be sufficient to tell you what you need to know about your own development. Models and other descriptions can tell what happens on the outside, but not how it feels when it happens—like how exhilarating it feels to be on top of a plateau, how frightening to look up at the next plateau, and how painful to tumble into the ravine.

无论你变得多么高高在上，你都永远不会忘记那些峡谷的真正痛苦。然而，如果没有更好的希望，痛苦会在你开始之前让你退缩。因此，在您开始下一次攀登之前，您需要了解到达山顶时的感觉。

No matter how high and mighty you get, you never forget the very real pain of those ravines. Without the hope of something better, however, the pain would turn you back before you got started. So before you embark on your next climb, you’ll want to have an idea of what it will feel like when you reach the crest.

成长的感觉

HOW GROWTH FEELS

了解领导者成长感受的唯一方法是通过自己的经历和其他人的自传。感觉比事实细节可靠得多。虽然我不记得任何技术细节，但我仍然记得 25 多年前成为旧金山最好的 IBM 650 程序员的感觉是多么美妙。这是高原中期的典型感受——得意洋洋。你在艰难的攀登中幸存下来，但你仍在学习，所以没有人能告诉你你停滞不前。

The only way you can know how it feels to grow as a leader is through your own experience and through other people’s autobiographical accounts. Feelings are much more reliable than factual details. Though I can’t recall a single technical detail, I can still remember how splendid it felt to be the best IBM 650 programmer in San Francisco, more than a quarter-century ago. This is the typical feeling in the middle of the plateau stage—smug satisfaction. You’ve survived the difficult ascent, but you’re still learning, so nobody can tell you that you’re stagnant.

但事件可以告诉你。虽然我是 IBM 650 的主人，但地平线上出现了一台更大、更强大的机器：IBM 704。我搬到洛杉矶与 704 程序员一起工作，他们根本不关心我在最佳位置放置指令的技能在 650 的主内存鼓上。这就是高原阶段开始崩溃的方式——随着一些外来元素的引入。我的第一反应很典型：我试图拒绝外来因素。我认为鼓式内存优于核心内存，十进制编码本质上优于二进制。我仍然记得他们笑时我的感受。

But events can tell you. Although I was master of the IBM 650, there was a bigger, more powerful machine on the horizon: the IBM 704. I moved to Los Angeles to work with 704 programmers who couldn’t care less about my skill at placing instructions in optimal locations on the 650’s main memory drum. That’s how the plateau stage begins to crumble—with the introduction of some foreign element. My first reaction was typical: I tried to reject the foreign element. I argued that drum memory was superior to core memory, that decimal coding was intrinsically superior to binary. I still remember how I felt when they laughed.

为了保护自己的感情，我试图暗中了解704，但很快就掉进了深谷。我花了几天时间写了一个简单的 704 程序，我本可以在 650 上用十分钟构建。我无法预测编写需要多长时间，运行需要多长时间，甚至无法预测它是否可以正确运行. 我感到颤抖，一有任何借口就跳起来回到 650。在这混乱之中，有一些新意识的微光。我发现二进制 704 上可用的巧妙技术在十进制 650 上不可用。我开始欣赏丰富的存储空间（用现代术语来说，大约 36,000 字节对大约 10,000 字节）和高速（12 微秒内存访问对 120 毫秒） .

To protect my feelings, I tried learning about the 704 in secret, and promptly fell into the ravine. It took me days to write a simple 704 program that I could have constructed in ten minutes on the 650. I was unable to predict how long it would take to write, how long it would take to run, or even whether it would run correctly. I felt shaky and jumped at any slight excuse to return to the 650. In the midst of this chaos, there were a few glimmers of a new awareness. I discovered neat techniques available on the binary 704 that were not available on the decimal 650. I began to appreciate the abundant storage (in modern terms, about 36,000 bytes versus about 10,000 bytes) and high speed (12 microsecond memory access versus 120 milliseconds).

我对所有这些新知识保密，但它开始影响我对世界的看法。然后有一天，我遇到了一个客户，他的计算问题根本无法在 650 上完成。我记得那一刻，我清楚地看到 704 确实是为此类问题而生的。从那一刻起，我成为了一个皈依者，相当难为情，但却是一个真正的信徒，笨拙地走在一个新高原的小坡上。

I kept all this new knowledge a secret, but it began to color my perceptions of the world. Then one day, I encountered a client with a computing problem that simply could not be done on the 650. I remember the moment when I saw clearly that the 704 was quite literally made for problems like this. From that moment on, I was a convert, rather self-conscious, but a true believer awkwardly walking the slight upward grade of a new plateau.

元周期

THE METACYCLE

我跋涉到 704 高原的尴尬步骤之一是掌握八进制表示法。后来，当 IBM 360 出现时，我不得不放弃八进制并拥抱十六进制，以及全新的机器语言词汇——另一个峡谷，但不像第一个那样痛苦。我感到同样的颤抖，同样的退缩冲动，但不知何故，我对自己能够活下来更有信心。我什至感到了一丝激动。

One of the awkward steps in my trek to the 704 plateau was mastering octal notation. Later, when the IBM 360 came along, I had to let go of octal and embrace hexadecimal, along with an entirely new machine language vocabulary—another ravine, but not quite as traumatic as the first one. I felt the same shakiness, the same urge to pull back, but somehow I felt more confident that I could survive. I even felt a trace of excitement.

几年后，我被迫从汇编语言跳到高级语言；这是一个巨大的牺牲，因为那时我已经写了一本非常流行的关于汇编语言的书。我本可以再享受汇编语言高原几年，甚至更长的时间。但到了这个时候，我有信心自己可以应对这种变化，而且我更多地将其视为一种创造性的挑战，而不是一种需要忍受的考验。

A few years later, I was forced to jump from assembly languages to higher-level languages; this was a great sacrifice, because by that time I’d written an extremely popular book on assembly language. I could have savored the assembly language plateau for a few more years, perhaps even longer. But by this time, I was confident that I could handle the change, and I looked upon it more as a creative challenge ‘than a trial to be endured.

高原-峡谷模型描述了一个循环，但它也描述了一个元循环——一个循环的循环，一个螺旋，就像有腔的鹦鹉螺。每次我掌握了另一个峡谷，我不仅跳到了一个新的高原，而且我沿着一个高原又上升了几步，掌握了生长过程本身。我正在学习新的计算机语言，但更重要的是，我正在学习如何学习新的计算机语言。我的元学习在我对新语言的情绪反应中表现得最清楚。我没有感到焦虑、防御和一文不值，而是感到兴奋、富有创造力并且能够应对几乎任何事情。

The plateau-ravine model describes a cycle, but it also describes a metacycle—a cycle of cycles, a spiral, like the chambered nautilus. Each time I mastered another ravine, I not only jumped to a new plateau, but I ascended another few steps along a metaplateau, mastering the growth process itself. I was learning new computer languages, but even more, I was learning how to learn new computer languages. My metalearning showed most clearly in my emotional reaction to new languages. Instead of feeling anxious, defensive, and worthless, I felt excited, creative, and capable of coping with almost anything.

要实现这种元学习，你必须熬过第一个峡谷。不是每个人都有足够的动力，或者可以承受情绪冲击。我的一些老朋友还在第一高原上，不断地完善自己的汇编语言编码，为自己做的很好。但他们中的大多数人都像我一样攀登到其他技术高原，越来越感到轻松和舒适。

To achieve this kind of metalearning, you have to survive the first ravine. Not everyone has sufficient motivation, or can take the emotional shock. Some of my old friends are still back there on the first plateau, constantly refining their assembly language coding, and doing quite well for themselves. But most of them have climbed like me to other technical plateaus, with a growing sense of ease and comfort.

即便如此，当我们试图成为领导者时，我们的技术元学习帮助有限。有些人试图通过被任命为老大来避开峡谷，但大多数人在跌回峡谷时摔断了腰。我尝试通过教学和领导小组来逐步攀升到领导地位；即便如此，我还是经常被痛苦折磨。然后，元学习逐渐接管了一切。现在，当我接近一个新的小组时，我会享受我的焦虑，并很容易将其转化为兴奋。就像有腔的鹦鹉螺一样，我的成长似乎是一个螺旋，每个腔室都比上一个更高贵。

Even so, our technical metalearning was of limited help when we tried to become leaders. Some tried to avoid the ravine by being appointed boss, but most of them broke their backs when they fell back into the ravine. I tried a more gradual climb to leadership through teaching and leading small groups; even so, I was frequently racked with anguish. Then, little by little, metalearning took over. Now, when I approach a new small group, I enjoy my anxiety, and easily convert it to excitement. Like the chambered nautilus, my growth seems to be a spiral in which each chamber grows nobler than the last.

几年前，我尝试管理自己的公司。公司很成功，但那座豪宅对当时的我来说有点太庄严了。我成功地让每个人都痛苦不堪，包括我自己，直到我离开那个长得太大的壳。没有人说每条沟壑都通向高原。有些只是峡谷。

A few years ago, I tried my hand at managing my own company. The company was successful, but that mansion was a bit too stately—for me at that moment in time. I succeeded in making everyone miserable, including myself, until I left that outgrown shell. Nobody said that every ravine leads to a plateau. Some are just ravines.

不过，至少我学到了，而且我在另外两个 metacycles 中幸存下来的知识给了我创办另一家公司的勇气。这并不容易，但到目前为止，我似乎已经从元元学习中受益。

At least I learned, though, and the knowledge that I had survived two other metacycles gave me the courage to start yet another company. It’s not been easy, but so far I seem to have benefited from meta- metalearning.

问题

QUESTIONS

  1. 您是否有一些技能，如弹球、举重或制作模型牛车，是您经过长时间的锻炼而提高的？你能画出你的进步吗，你能把你的学习方法应用到学习方法中，成为一个更好的解决问题的领导者吗？
  2. Do you have some skill, like playing pinball, lifting weights, or building model oxcarts, that you have improved over a long period of time? Can you plot your progress, and can you apply your methods of learning to that of learning to be a better problem-solving leader?
  3. 你能描述一下你现在占据的高原吗？是否有迹象表明您可能正在接近峡谷？你是想远离峡谷，还是想从翻滚中学到什么？
  4. Can you describe a plateau you are now occupying? Are there signs that you may be approaching the ravine? Are you trying to stay out of the ravine, or to learn what you can from tumbling in?
  5. 自从您上次攀登到一个新的高原以来已经有多久了？你还在享受平房的感觉吗？你在做什么为下一个做准备？
  6. How long has it been since the last time you climbed to a new plateau? Are you still enjoying the feeling of being on the flat? What are you doing to get ready for the next one?
  7. 在你的人生历程中，你对学习有哪些体会？
  8. In the course of your life, what have you learned about learning?
  9. 为自己设定一些小的个人成就，您可以每天练习 15 分钟，持续一周。记录你的进步。下周，选择另一个成就。
  10. Set yourself some small personal achievement that you can practice for fifteen minutes every day for a week. Keep a record of your progress. Next week, pick another achievement.

第 5 章 但我不能因为

Chapter 5. But I Can’t Because

……想法是真实力量之间关系的象征

… ideas are the symbols of relationships among real forces that

让人们吃早饭迟到，带走他们的早餐，那

make people late for breakfast, that take away their breakfast, that

让他们在早餐桌上互相殴打

make them beat each other across the breakfast table

——丽贝卡·韦斯特，《黑羊与灰隼》

—Rebecca West, Black Lamb and Grey Falcon

这是一本关于成长的书，但在我们审视成长的细节之前，我们还必须迈出一步。对我来说，成长带来了一些痛苦的时刻，每当我面临新的痛苦的可能性时，我都会寻找一些借口来避免它。如果你像我一样，你可能会想到一些这样的借口。为了让你充分参与本书的其余部分，我需要处理一些最常见的借口来避免成为技术领导者的痛苦。

This is a book about growth, but before we examine the details of growing, there is one more step we must take. For me, growth has brought some painful moments, and whenever I’m faced with new possibilities of pain, I look for some excuse to avoid it. If you’re like me, you may have some such excuse in mind. To get your full participation in the rest of the book, I’ll need to dispose of some of the most common excuses for avoiding the pain of becoming a technical leader.

我不是经理

I’M NOT A MANAGER

关于领导力最广为流传和最有害的神话可能是只有领导者才能领导，其中大写字母 L 表示某人已被任命为领导者。刚才，我正在看 Michael Maccoby 的书The Leader的封面。有了这样的标题，人们会认为它是关于各种领导者的，但实际上它只是关于管理者——任命的领导者。这本书的副标题是美国管理的新面孔。

Probably the most widespread and pernicious myth about leader- ship is that only Leaders can lead, where the capital L indicates that someone has been appointed to the position of Leader. Just now, I’m looking at the cover of Michael Maccoby’s book The Leader. With a title like that, one would think it is about all kinds of leaders, but in fact it’s only about managers—appointed leaders. The subtitle of the book is A New Face for American Management.

只有在威胁/奖励模型中，领导和管理才是同义词。事实上，潜在的领导者比领导者多得多。解决问题的领导力特别关注领导者，以及领导者；所以如果你专注于指定的类型，你就不会明白如何成为解决问题的类型。您可能拥有小组组长之类的头衔，但不是领导；或者你可能根本没有头衔，但却是让你的团队开始以新的、更有效的方式运作的人。

It’s only in threat/reward models that leadership and managership are synonymous. There are, in fact, many more potential leaders than Leaders. Problem-solving leadership especially concerns leaders, as well as Leaders; so if you concentrate on the appointed kind, you’re not going to understand how to become the problem-solving kind. You may have a title like Group Leader, yet not be leading; or you may have no title at all, but be the one who makes your group start to function in new and more effective ways.

我想起了古董表不走的村里白痴的故事。他撬开它，发现里面是一只死蟑螂。“难怪它不起作用，”他说，“经理已经死了。”

I’m reminded of the story of the village idiot whose antique watch stopped running. He pried it open and found a dead cockroach inside. “No wonder it doesn’t work,” he said, “the manager is dead.”

有机模型说，任何工作组都是一个系统，通常不能通过拆解它并给每个部分一个标题来理解。举一个更技术性的例子，假设一个程序员刚刚构建了一个操作系统，但在高于平均负载的情况下表现不佳。假设程序员的经理说，“只需更换包含低效的模块。然后它会跑得更快。” 因为它是一个操作系统，负载下的低效率很可能源于模块之间的交互，而不是单个模块的低效率行为。

Organic models say that any working group is a system and can’t generally be understood by disassembling it and giving each piece a title. To take a more technical example, suppose a programmer has just built an operating system that wasn’t performing too well under higher-than-average load. And suppose the programmer’s manager says, “Just replace the module that contains the inefficiency. Then it will run faster.” Because it’s an operating system, inefficiency under load is likely to stem from interactions between modules, not from individual modules behaving inefficiently.

很少有软件经理对操作系统的性质有这样的误解，但在涉及到人的系统时，很多人都是这样。如果团队效率不够高，经理会决定，“就换掉不能发挥领导作用的人。然后团队会工作得更快。” 当然会。如果我们只放入一只活蟑螂，手表就会重新开始运行。

Few software managers are that misguided about the nature of operating systems, but many are when it comes to systems involving people. If the team isn’t productive enough, the manager decides, “Just replace the person who’s not providing the leadership. Then the team will work faster.” Sure it will. And if we just put in a live cockroach, the watch will start running again.

由于有时系统是线性的，所以这种错误的想法一直存在。对于手表这样的机械系统，我们经常可以找到一个坏掉的零件。操作系统比手表更复杂，但有时我们仍然可以识别出一个模块，在重写后可以显着提高效率。一名新手程序员可能会成功进行这样的替换两到三次，并得出结论，总有一个模块“包含低效率”。但随着经验的积累，这种错误的观念很快就会消失。

Such fallacious ideas persist because sometimes systems are linear. With a mechanical system such as a watch, we can often find one broken part. Operating systems are more complex than watches, but sometimes we can still identify one module that when rewritten, produces a striking increase in efficiency. A novice programmer might succeed at such a replacement two or three times and conclude that there is always one module that “contains the inefficiency.” But with experience, this fallacious notion will soon disappear.

工作组的情况并非如此。有时，更换一名成员会在生产力方面产生显着的收益或损失，但并非总是如此。事实上，通常不会，但管理人员往往会忽略他们不起作用的更改。一段时间后，他们的选择性记忆中包含了许多引人注目的个人“领导力”的故事。

Not so in the case of working groups. Sometimes, replacing one member produces a striking gain or loss in productivity, but not always. Not usually, in fact, but managers tend to ignore their changes that don’t work. After a period of time, their selective memory contains many tales of striking individual “leadership.”

我们不应该对经理如此苛刻。当事实不符合我们最喜欢的模型时，我们所有人都会进行这种选择性遗忘。经理最喜欢的第一模特是什么？任何经理？好吧，假设你问主发条是手表的一个重要部件是什么？

We shouldn’t be so hard on managers. All of us do this sort of selective forgetting when the facts don’t fit our favorite model. What is the number one all-time favorite model of a manager? of any manager? Well, suppose you asked the mainspring what was the one essential part of a watch?

如果你问任命的领导者组织的一个重要组成部分是什么，如果你听到，你不会太惊讶，

If you ask appointed leaders what is the one essential part of an organization, you won’t be too surprised if you hear,

“任命的领导者是团队的主要推动力。”

“The appointed leader is the mainspring of the group.”

当然，这是任命的领导人的神话。

This, of course, is the myth of the appointed leader.

对于这个神话的持续存在还有另一种解释。如果你问机械师为什么你的跑车引擎听起来很糟糕，回答很可能是，“你检查过火花塞了吗？” 经验表明，如果出现任何故障，很可能是插头。很容易从这一观察中得出火花塞是发动机关键部件的谬论。

There is another explanation for the persistence of this myth. If you ask a mechanic why your sports car’s engine sounds so awful, the response is likely to be, “Have you checked the spark plugs?” Experience says that if anything is failing, it’s most likely the plugs. It’s easy to slip from this observation into the fallacy that the spark plug is the critical component in an engine.

同样，在工作组中，如果任何成员以明显的方式崩溃，则指定的领导者很可能就是那个人。与主发条和火花塞一样，很容易从“最薄弱”的环节滑到“最关键”的环节，并开始相信指定领导者的神话。

Similarly, in working groups, if any one member breaks down in an obvious way, the appointed leader is likely to be the one. As with mainsprings and spark plugs, it’s easy to slip from “weakest” link to “most critical” link and start believing the myth of the appointed leader.

为什么任命的领导人最有可能崩溃？矛盾的是，这是因为有那么多人相信任命领导人的神话。老板相信；工人们相信；甚至被任命的领导人也相信。因此，当事情变得有点失控时，每个人都会求助于指定的领导者来让事情恢复正常。被任命的领导者的负担增加使事情变得更糟。

Why is the appointed leader the one most likely to break down? Paradoxically, it’s because so many people believe in the myth of the appointed leader. The boss believes; the workers believe; even the appointed leader believes. Thus, when matters get a little out of hand, everyone turns to the appointed leader to put them back in order. The increased load on the appointed leader makes matters even worse.

如果压力导致崩溃，那么崩溃的就是指定的领导者。即使领导者避免了崩溃，但每个人都可以看到，任命的领导者是危机期间最积极的人。因此，被任命的领导人的神话变成了一个自我实现的预言。

If the pressure leads to a breakdown, it’s the appointed leader who breaks. Even if the leader averts a breakdown, everyone can see that the appointed leader was the most active person during the crisis. Thus, the myth of the appointed leader becomes a self-fulfilling prophecy.

在设计良好的引擎中，没有最薄弱的环节。如果火花塞确实是最薄弱的环节，那么卓越的发动机设计可以完全消除火花塞，从而为我们提供柴油发动机。或者，如果您想不出如何淘汰火花塞，至少您可以让它们易于互换。

In a well-designed engine, there is no weakest link. If spark plugs are truly the weakest link, then a superior engine design eliminates the spark plugs altogether, giving us the diesel engine. Or, if you can’t think how to eliminate spark plugs, at least you make them easily interchangeable.

同样，设计最好的工作组是每个人都发挥领导作用的工作组，而不仅仅是指定的领导者。因此，您不必等待，您也不应该等待——等待预约。

In the same way, the best-designed working groups are those in which leadership comes from everybody, not merely the appointed leaders. Therefore, you need not wait you should not wait—for an appointment.

我不是领导者类型

I’M NOT THE LEADER TYPE

也许您根本无法将自己视为领导者类型。我当然可以理解你的困难。每当我听到领袖这个词时，我的第一印象就是泰迪·罗斯福率领圣胡安山冲锋陷阵。当然，泰迪比我早得多，但因为他和我生日相同，所以我一直对他的事迹特别感兴趣。作为一个小男孩，我试图通过模仿泰迪的积极进取方式来成为领导者——通过激励来领导。不幸的是，每当我试图带领同学们冲锋时，我发现自己独自冲过操场。

Perhaps you simply cannot think of yourself as the type of person who is a leader. I can certainly appreciate your difficulty. Whenever I hear the word leader, my first image is of Teddy Roosevelt leading the charge up San Juan Hill. Of course, Teddy was way before my time, but because he and I have the same birthday, I’ve always had a special interest in his exploits. As a little boy, I tried to be a leader by imitating Teddy’s gung ho approach—leadership through motivation. Unfortunately, whenever I tried to lead my schoolmates in a charge, I found myself charging alone across the playground.

随着年龄的增长，我研究了泰迪后来担任总裁的职业生涯。我了解到，随着领导者年龄的增长，他们通常会在自己的技能库中加入第二种类型的领导力。他们不是率领部队冲锋陷阵，而是组织部队，到战时自己冲锋陷阵。我试图采用这种领导风格，但了解到它需要更多的激励型领导来完成这种组织变革。

As I grew older, I studied Teddy’s later career, as President. I learned that as leaders grow older, they often add a second type of leadership to their repertoire. Instead of simply charging out at the head of the troops, they organize the troops so that when the time comes for battle, they’ll charge off by themselves. I tried to adopt this leadership style, but learned that it required even more of the motivational kind of leadership necessary to accomplish such organizational changes.

我无法成为另一个泰迪·罗斯福，这可能让我对成为领导者的想法感到失望，并让我怀疑所有我认为是领导者的人。我没有注意到我自己已经成为了一名领导者，因为我成为的领导者类型与我对泰迪·罗斯福的形象不符。我周围都是追随我的人，因为他们看到我可以解决其他人无法解决的技术问题。如果有人问谁在领导，我会回答说：“没有人在领导。考虑到我们要解决的问题，我们只是在尽最大努力。我们不在乎谁提供了创意，只要它们最有效即可。”

My inability to become another Teddy Roosevelt probably soured me on the idea of being a leader, and made me suspicious of all those I identified as leaders. I didn’t notice that I myself had become a leader, because the type of leader I had become didn’t match my Teddy Roosevelt image. I was surrounded by people who followed my lead because they saw that I could solve technical problems that nobody else could solve. If someone had asked who was leading, I would have replied, “Nobody’s leading. We are simply doing the best possible job, given the problem we are trying to solve. We don’t care who supplies the ideas, as long as they work best.”

这既不是动机领导，也不是组织领导。它是创新领导，通过将新技术添加到团队完成工作的方式中。它解释了当我连女鞋都卖不出去时，我是如何卖掉价值数百万美元的电脑的。它解释了当我什至不能成为我的沙地棒球队的队长时，我如何组织设计和构建空间跟踪计算机网络的团队。这也解释了为什么我花了三十年才认识到自己的领导能力。

This is neither leadership by motivation, nor by organization. It is leadership by innovation, by adding new techniques to the group’s ways of getting things done. It explains how I could sell millions of dollars worth of computers, when I couldn’t even sell women’s shoes. It explains how I could organize the team that designed and built the space tracking computer network, when I couldn’t even be captain of my sandlot baseball team. It also explains why it took me thirty years to recognize my own leadership ability.

像我这样的人——也许像你一样——缺乏世界泰迪熊特有的魅力和组织能力的人可以通过创新来发挥领导作用。它甚至可以由非常令人讨厌的人来练习，只要他们的技术技能足以克服他们令人讨厌的行为。一些人甚至通过冒犯的程度来衡量他们的技术能力，或者至少是他们在不失去支持的情况下可以表现出的特殊行为。当我从事太空追踪工作时，我开始留胡子，直到今天。当时在 IBM 内部，留胡子而不被解雇是技术天才无可争辩的标志。

Leadership by innovation can be quietly exercised by people like me—and perhaps like you—who lack the charisma and organizational skills that characterize the world’s Teddies. It can even be exercised by people who are positively obnoxious, as long as their technical skills are sufficiently great to overcome their disagreeable behavior. A few people even measure their technical competence by the degree of offensive, or at least idiosyncratic, behavior that they can exhibit without losing their support. When I worked in space tracking, I started the beard that I have to this day. Within IBM at that time, growing a beard without getting fired was an indisputable mark of technical genius.

在 IBM 的背景下，胡子可能帮助我弥补了我缺乏激励技能，为我赢得了不止一位高管的全神贯注。由于他们无法通过技术技能可靠地识别解决问题的领导者，因此高管们经常使用无关的外在标志，如胡须或赤脚。如果你不能留胡子，或者如果你胆怯，你不必对成为解决问题的领导者感到绝望。如果我拥有其他领导风格的一小部分，我可能就不需要胡子了。

In the IBM context, the beard probably helped me compensate for my lack of motivational skills, earning me the undivided attention of more than one executive. Because they can’t reliably identify problem- solving leaders by their technical skills, executives often employ extraneous outward signs, like beards or bare feet. If you can’t grow a beard, or if you get cold feet, you need not despair of becoming a problem- solving leader. Had I possessed even a small measure of the other styles of leadership, I probably wouldn’t have needed the beard.

成为一名成功的解决问题的领导者需要的不仅仅是创新思维和长毛的能力。无论您是否留胡子，您都至少需要一些激励和组织技能才能被确定为真正的解决问题的领导者。相反，可以通过创新的小规模领导来增强由动机或组织领导的力量。这是任何人都可以通过研究如何成为解决问题的领导者来学习的一课，无论您是否认为自己是那种类型。

Becoming a successful problem-solving leader does require more than the ability to think innovatively and grow hair on your face. Whether you’re bearded or not, you’ll need at least some motivational and organizational skills to be identified as a true problem-solving leader. Conversely, the power to lead by motivation or organization can be enhanced by a small measure of leadership through innovation. This is a lesson that anyone can learn by studying how to become a problem-solving leader, whether or not you think you’re the type.

我会失去我的技术技能

I’LL LOSE MY TECHNICAL SKILLS

对于成为领导者的技术明星来说，最艰难的选择之一就是与最新技术失去联系。在我自己的职业生涯中，我肯定不得不牺牲一些技术货币来提高我的人际交往能力。我不知道我现在是否可以改变那个决定，即使我想。像许多从百分百创新立场发展而来的解决问题的领导者一样，我愿意相信我可以随时回到技术主流，但事实可能并非如此。

One of the hardest choices for technical stars who become leaders is losing touch with the latest in technology. In my own career, I’ve definitely had to sacrifice some of my technical currency to improve my interpersonal skills. I don’t know whether I could reverse that decision now, even if I wanted to. Like many problem-solving leaders who have evolved from the one hundred percent innovation position, I’d like to believe I could get back into the technical mainstream at any time, but that may not be true.

攀登总是吃力的，但放手是残酷的。大多数解决问题的领导者都是在一个领域取得第一次成功，通常是高度专业化的子领域。他们可能在相关领域有其他解决问题的成功经验，但他们始终至少对自己的出身保持一种情感上的依恋。最近一周，我拜访了两位高管——一位是一家大型软件服务公司的负责人，另一位是一家计算机制造商的研究总监。每个人都有一个很大的角落办公室，里面有一张沙发和一张咖啡桌，上面整齐地摆放着一排报纸和杂志。每个人都有财富和华尔街日报; 但是一个有两个数值数学期刊，而另一个有三个高能物理期刊。我怀疑这些高管中的任何一个都没有阅读他们的技术期刊，但询问是不礼貌的。

Climbing is always strenuous, but letting go is brutal. Most problem-solving leaders create their first success in a single field, often a highly specialized subfield. They may have other problem-solving successes in related fields, but they always retain at least a sentimental attachment to their origins. In one recent week, I visited two executives—one the head of a large software services company, the other the director of research for a computer manufacturer. Each had a large corner office with a couch and a coffee table covered with a neat array of papers and magazines. Each had Fortune and The Wall Street Journal; but one had two journals in numerical mathematics, while the other had three journals in high energy physics. I doubt that either of these executives reads their technical journals, but it would have been impolite to inquire.

此外，这并不重要。他们是否曾经是他们原来领域的超级巨星甚至都不重要。这两个人都超越了他们的专业领域，现在都以一种普遍的方式掌握了技术。他们可以与数十个领域的技术专家对话，轻松分清麦子。这就是他们工作所需的技术技能。用 MOT 术语来说，这意味着朝着三个因素之间的平衡方向发展，即朝着激励和组织能力的提高方向发展，而代价是创新技能的下降。这是一个不可避免的权衡，也许是他们不得不做出的最艰难的权衡。

Besides, it’s not important. It’s not even important if they were ever superstars in their original fields. Both of these men have transcended their specialized fields and both now master technology in a generalized way. They can talk with technical specialists in dozens of fields, and easily separate the wheat from the chaff. That’s the kind of technical skill their jobs require. In MOT terms, this means an evolution toward a balance among the three factors, toward an increase in motivational and organizational abilities, paid for by a decrease in innovative skills. It’s an inevitable tradeoff, and perhaps the hardest one they’ve had to make.

我正处于成长的严重危险中

I’M IN GRAVE DANGER OF GROWING

尽管这种权衡是一个完全合理的职业决定，但我还是不愿使用这两位高管的名字。我认为他们可能会因为在报纸上看到他们不是技术巨星而感到受伤——也许从来都不是。对于解决问题的领导者而言，无论处于什么变态阶段，纯粹创新的领导者仍然是文化英雄。

Even though this tradeoff is a perfectly justified career decision, I hesitate to use the names of these two executives. I think they might be hurt to see in print that they are not technical superstars—and perhaps never had been. To the problem-solving leader, at whatever stage of metamorphosis, the purely innovative leader remains the culture hero.

我知道承认我的技术能力已经下降对我来说是非常痛苦的。我可能又富又胖又出名，但我经常梦想再次年轻，当我在 Project Mercury 监控系统中寻找最新的鲜为人知的事物时，接受我的团队的赞美之词。有时，当攀登特别艰难时，我发誓如果不是迫于环境，我永远不会再改变

I know it’s extremely painful for me to admit that my technical skills have waned. I may be rich and fat and famous, but I often dream of being young again, accepting the admiring remarks of my team as I track down the latest obscure but in the Project Mercury Monitor System. Sometimes, when the climb is especially devastating, I swear that I will never change again if I’m not forced to by circumstances

不要误会我的意思。推迟改变直到环境迫使你改变是不错的。没有一种风格比另一种更好，除非你有一些你正在努力实现的目标。如果你没有特定的目标，或者如果你正在完成你想完成的事情，不要仅仅因为别人希望你改变而改变你的领导风格。如果你这样做，你会特别不开心，特别是如果它是为了获得某种类型的老板职位的晋升。再次重申，你不必成为老板才能成为领导者。

Don’t get me wrong. It’s not bad to postpone change until circumstances force it upon you. No style is better than another, except insofar as you have some goal you’re trying to accomplish. If you have no particular goal, or if you’re accomplishing what you want to accomplish, don’t try changing your leadership style just because somebody else wants you to. You’ll be especially unhappy if you do, particularly if it’s to obtain a promotion to some type of boss position. Once again, you don’t have to be a boss to be a leader.

与其他人一样，您确实拥有一种领导风格，即使目前在所有三个类别中都处于较低水平。在你职业生涯的任何时刻，你都可以选择坚持你所拥有的。这避免了痛苦，就像我父亲选择坚持打台球或我选择坚持打弹球一样。我的模型非常清楚地告诉我要成为一名视频大师我必须放弃什么，但我现在不想做那种特别的努力。我太擅长弹球，不需要学习电子游戏，就像我太擅长程序员一样，不需要学习成为一名有效的领导者。毫无疑问，在最后一台弹球机被锁在博物馆之前，我会鄙视电子游戏。那么我将面临再次成长的严重危险。

Like everyone else, you do possess a leadership style, even if it’s currently low in all three categories. At any moment in your career, you can choose to stick with what you’ve got. This avoids the pain, like my father’s choice to stick with pool or mine to stick with pinball. My models tell me quite clearly what I have to relinquish to become a video master, but I don’t want to make that particular effort just now. I’m too good at pinball to need to learn video games, just as I was too good a programmer to need to learn to be an effective leader. No doubt, I’ll despise video games until the last pinball machine is locked away in a museum. Then I’ll be in grave danger of growing again.

我不想要那么大的力量

I DON’T WANT THAT MUCH POWER

我们现在知道，解决问题并不局限于高科技环境。诚然，这种领导风格首先在高科技领域崭露头角，但这只是因为技术理念具有巨大的经济实力。高科技不是唯一有力量的领域，经济力量也不是唯一值得考虑的领域。今天我们意识到技术领导者只是一种解决问题的领导者。

We now know that problem solving is not restricted to a high-tech environment. It’s true that this style of leadership first came into prominence in high-tech situations, but that’s only because of the enormous economic power of technical ideas. High-technology isn’t the only area where ideas have power, and economic power isn’t the only kind worth reckoning. Today we realize that technical leader is but one type of problem-solving leader.

在我们这个高科技、金钱驱动的社会中，很容易忘记基本原理并且看不到显而易见的东西。当我们刚开始教授解决问题的领导风格时，它只是高技术研讨会的一小部分。在数十名学生告诉我们他们可以将技术领导风格转移到他们生活的非技术领域之后，我们开始意识到我们正在做一些更基本的事情。我们甚至注意到，车间教学正在影响我们自己的领导风格。

In our high-tech, money-driven society, it’s easy to forget the fundamentals and miss seeing the obvious. When we first started teaching the problem-solving leadership style, it was but a small part of a highly technical workshop. After dozens of students told us that they could transfer the technical leadership style to nontechnical areas of their life, we began to realize that we were on to something more fundamental. We even noticed that teaching in the workshop was affecting our own leadership styles.

奇怪的是，当我开始意识到解决问题的领导力有多么广泛时，我开始担心我们可能会赋予一小群人太多的权力。更奇怪的是，我们的一些学生对自己也表达了同样的怀疑。“我们不想变得那么强大，”他们有时会说。

Curiously, when I began to realize how wide-ranging problem-solving leadership was, I began to worry that we might be giving too much power to a small group of people. Even more curiously, some of our students expressed the same doubts about themselves. “We don’t want to be that powerful,” they sometimes said.

不久的将来，世界会由不同风格的领导者塑造吗？我对此表示怀疑。技术领导者在我们的社会中拥有巨大的权力，但他们既非恶魔也非神灵。他们是普通人，恰好拥有解决问题的有效方法，一种基于创新领导力的方法。即使没有任何技术背景，其他普通人也可以通过掌握这种方法的某些要素来增强自己和他人的能力。在可预见的未来，我们普通人仍然要与魅力四射的宗教人物、铁腕将军、圆滑狡猾的政客抗衡。也许解决问题的领导风格的大部分影响将一如既往地受到技术的巨大影响。或许它会触动我们每个人的创造力种子，

Will the world of the near future be shaped by a different style of leader? I doubt it. Technical leaders possess great power in our society, but they are neither demons nor gods. They are ordinary people who happen to possess an effective approach to problem solving, an approach based on leadership through innovation. Even without any technical background, other ordinary people can empower themselves and others by mastering some elements of this approach. For the foreseeable future, we ordinary people will still have to contend with charismatic religious figures, iron-jawed generals, smooth but tricky politicians. Perhaps most of the impact of the problem-solving leadership style will be as it has always been through the considerable impact of technology. Or perhaps it will touch that creative seed in each of us, that seed that tells us there must be a better way.

如果是这样，那么我们将享受许多新的领导选择。威胁/奖励模型可能会说改变来自高层，但我的经验告诉我，改变始于我们选择早餐吃什么。此外，生活不仅仅是大型组织。您可能会发现，将这种安静的风格应用到生活、自由和追求幸福等日常问题上会很有力量。

If it does, then we will enjoy many new leadership choices. The threat/reward model may say that change comes from the top, but my experience tells me that change starts with what we choose to have for breakfast. Besides, there’s more to life than large organizations. You may find it empowering to apply this quieter style to such everyday problems as life, liberty, and the pursuit of happiness.

问题

QUESTIONS

  1. 你认识一个你特别欣赏其领导风格的人吗？尝试创建此人的简短职业传记，以 MOI 术语为特征。如果可能，请采访此人以检查您的看法。
  2. Do you know someone whose leadership style you particularly admire? Try to create a short career biography of this person, characterized in MOI terms. If possible, interview the person to check your perceptions.
  3. 您上一次在职业生涯中做出重大改变是什么时候？你记得最清楚的是事实细节还是感受？
  4. When was the last time you made a major change in your career? Which do you remember best, the factual details or the feelings?
  5. 上一次您熟悉的人做出重大职业转变时，您有何反应？这还告诉你关于你自己的什么？
  6. How did you react the last time someone you know well made a major career change? What else does that tell you about yourself?
  7. 您在职业生涯中做出了哪些您仍然不完全接受的改变？不完全承认别人？给自己？现在可以放下了吗？为什么？
  8. What change in your career have you made that you still don’t fully accept? don’t fully admit to others? to yourself? Can you now let go of it? Why?
  9. 您还记得您职业生涯中的第一个重大决策点吗？你还能回忆起你的感受吗？你害怕迈出这一步吗？你的恐惧有道理吗？无论如何值得吗？
  10. Can you remember the first major decision point in your career? Can you still recall your feelings? Were you afraid to take the step? Was your fear justified? Was it worth it anyway?
  11. 您对下一个重大决策点的选择是什么？当你考虑每一种可能的选择时，你的身体会发生什么变化？你的脑海里突然浮现出什么信息？
  12. What are your choices for the next major decision point? What happens to your body as you contemplate each possible alternative? What messages pop into your mind?
  13. 你曾经被任命为任何事物的领导者吗？人们是否开始假设您作为领导者现在可以处理他们自己可以完全处理的情况？你是如何处理这些情况的？您是否对自己的新身份如此着迷，以至于您试图自己处理它们，而不是将它们委托给它们所属的地方？你的行为如何影响团队后来对你作为领导者的反应？
  14. Have you ever been appointed Leader of anything? Did people begin to assume that you, as Leader, would now handle situations that they could perfectly well handle themselves? How did you deal with these situations? Were you so taken with your new status that you tried to handle them yourself, rather than delegating them where they belonged? How did your actions affect the group’s later reactions to you as Leader?
  15. 下次你被任命为某个团队的领导者时，请列出人们认为你作为领导者会提出关键想法的情况，但你通过改变环境来发挥领导作用，以鼓励他们这样做。继续下去，直到你有至少十件物品。
  16. Next time you are appointed Leader of some group, keep a list of situations in which people assume that you as Leader will come up with the crucial ideas, but in which you exercise leadership by changing the environment so as to encourage them to do it. Keep going until you have at least ten items.
  17. 当你被任命为 Leader 时，你有没有觉得你对团队来说是必不可少的？当你不在的时候，小组做了什么？当你最终离开时，他们做了什么？会不会有某种自我实现的预言在起作用？
  18. Have you ever felt when appointed Leader that you were essential to the group? What did the group do when you were not around? What did they do when you eventually left? Could there have been some sort of self-fulfilling prophecy in operation?
  19. 对于与您一起工作的某些团队，请考虑您在该团队中的影响力有多少来自您的指定职位。有多少来自你的创意力量？有多少来自于提升他人的创意力量？你对这个组合满意吗？
  20. For some group you work with, consider how much of your influence in that group derives from your appointed position. How much comes from your idea power? How much from enhancing the idea power of others? Are you satisfied with this mix?
  21. 明天早上以某种方式改变您的传统早餐。注意你的一天是否有变化。
  22. Tomorrow morning change your traditional breakfast in some way. Notice if your day changes.
  23. 在接下来的两周内，根据每天的一些新想法（例如更少或更多卡路里、更快或更慢准备、或多或少有益健康、多与他人互动、少与他人互动、更吸引人）来改变你的早餐看看，三十分钟后，三十分钟前，在不同的地方，使用不同的器具，热的而不是冷的，冷的而不是热的，更多的液体，更少的液体）。记下发生在你身上的事情（如果有的话），并在两周结束时总结影响。
  24. For the next two weeks, change your breakfast each day according to some new idea each day (such as fewer or more calories, faster or slower preparation, more or less wholesome, more interaction with other people, less interaction with others, more appealing to look at, thirty minutes later, thirty minutes earlier, in a different place, using different utensils, hot instead of cold, cold instead of hot, more liquid, less liquid). Note what, if anything, is happening to you, and summarize the effect at the end of the two weeks.
  25. 明天改变你与一些你经常看到的人互动的方式。尝试以改善您的互动的方式进行。注意结果。
  26. Tomorrow change the way you interact with some person you see frequently. Try to do it in such a way as to improve your interaction. Note the results.

第二部分：创新

PART TWO: INNOVATION

技术明星是创新者——这就是他们成为明星的方式——但他们作为个人创新者的技能常常阻碍他们攀登领导高原。当他们不了解自己的创新过程时，他们可能会在承担领导责任时失去权力。如果他们不了解创新的源泉，他们自己的创新尝试可能会破坏他人的创新环境

Technical stars are innovatorsâ€”that’s how they got to be starsâ€” but their skills as individual innovators often obstruct their climb to a leadership plateau. When they’re not aware of their own innovative processes, they may lose their powers when they assume leadership responsibilities. If they don’t understand the sources of innovation, their own attempts to innovate may destroy the creative environment for others

在接下来的章节中，我们将探讨创新的主要障碍，以及如何克服这些障碍，既有你自己的，也有其他人的。

In the following chapters, we’ll explore the major obstacles to innovation and how they can be overcome, both in yourself and in others.

第 6 章创新的三大障碍

Chapter 6. The Three Great Obstacles to Innovation

甚至在她的童年时代，她就从生活中汲取了通常只有创造性思维才能获得的双重乐趣。“现在我正在做这个。现在我正在这样做，”她边做边告诉自己。她参与时在一旁观看。——埃德娜·费伯，《这么大》

Even in her childhood she extracted from life double enjoyment that comes usually only to the creative mind. “Now I am doing this. Now I am doing that,” she told herself while she was doing it. Looking on while she participated. —Edna Ferber, So Big

足够的理论。你实际上在做什么，要么让你有创造力，要么阻碍你的大脑？一种新的领导模式真的对你有用吗，或者它只是和最新的饮食时尚一样有用吗？节食的理论很简单：你所要做的就是在输入和输出之间取得平衡。根据平衡理论，我不应该在中间有这个凸起，但看看我。

Enough of theory. What are you actually doing that either makes you creative or blocks your brain? Can a new model of leadership really work for you, or is it only as useful as the latest diet fad? The theory of dieting is simple enough: All you have to do is achieve a balance between input and output. According to the balance theory, I shouldn’t have this bulge around the middle, but look at me.

理论就是这样。我可以给你各种各样的技巧来培养你解决问题的能力或领导能力，但我发现你没有取得任何进展。在本章中，我想探讨在解决问题型领导力方面最常见的障碍，首先是为什么我有时会不知不觉地长胖。

Theories are like that. I can give you all sorts of techniques for developing your problem-solving or leadership abilities, yet find that you’re making no progress. In this chapter, I’d like to explore the most common obstacles to progress toward problem-solving leadership, starting with the question of why I sometimes put on pounds without knowing why.

你知道你吃了什么甜点吗？

ARE YOU AWARE OF WHAT YOU HAD FOR DESSERT?

保持整洁对我的业务很重要，因为人们更关注你所做的而不是你所说的。作为一个应该帮助精简组织的顾问，如果我自己不是一个精简的人，我会做出一个糟糕的模型。在一个这样的组织中，丹妮和我正在寻找他们生产力下降的根源。在我们观察和采访了一天之后，系统分析部门的经理雪莉邀请我们回家吃饭。

Keeping trim is important to my business, because people pay more attention to what you do than what you say. As a consultant who’s supposed to help streamline organizations, I make a poor model if I’m a not exactly streamlined myself. At one such organization, Dani and I were looking for the source of their diminished productivity. After we spent the day observing and interviewing, Shirley, the manager of the systems analysis sections, invited us home for dinner.

雪莉和她的丈夫哈里森带着他们的三个儿子住在一个繁华但很有吸引力的街区，这似乎很符合雪莉的气质。当我搜查雪莉的冰箱寻找餐前零食时，我惊讶地发现她正在努力控制体重的证据。里面放着低热量的食物，门上贴着卡路里表，上面放着一盒食欲抑制剂。当她准备晚饭时，我们讨论了我们的共同问题。

Shirley and her husband, Harrison, lived with their three sons in a busy but attractive neighborhood, which seemed well matched to Shirley’s temperament. When I raided Shirley’s refrigerator for a pre-dinner snack, I was surprised to find evidence that she was struggling to control her weight. There were low-cal foods inside, calorie charts on the door, and a box of appetite suppressants on top. As she prepared supper, we discussed our common problem.

“我一定是新陈代谢出了问题，”我说。

“I must have a metabolic problem,” I said.

“我也是。以前吃零食有问题，控制住了，还是瘦不下来。”

“Me, too. I used to have trouble with snacks, but I’ve controlled that, yet I still can’t lose weight.”

“也许是出去吃饭了。你知道，丹妮和哈里森已经向孩子们保证，晚饭后我们会去斯文森家。我宁愿呆在家里，因为我无法抗拒他们的冰淇淋。”

“Maybe it’s eating out. You know, Dani and Harrison have already promised the kids we’ll go out to Swenson’s after dinner. I’d rather stay home because I can’t resist their ice cream.”

“好吧，我能抗拒。”雪莉吹嘘道。“我只要一杯咖啡。”

“Well, I can resist,” Shirley boasted. “I’ll just have a cup of coffee.”

“也许如果我吃了一顿丰盛的晚餐，”我说，“我就没有胃口吃甜点了。” 但我的声音里没有太多的说服力。

“Maybe if I eat a full supper,” I said, “I won’t have any appetite for dessert.” But there wasn’t much conviction in my voice.

我吃了一顿丰盛的晚餐，但在 Swenson's，我忍不住点了一盘覆盆子果子露。丹妮点了一个小棉花糖圣代，哈里森点了一个大香蕉片，两个大男孩点了特制的儿童圣代，最小的点了一个巧克力甜筒。只有雪莉拒绝了，义正言辞地要了一杯咖啡。不过，我注意到雪莉在她的咖啡里加入了奶油。还有糖。

I ate a full supper, but at Swenson’s I wasn’t able to resist ordering a dish of raspberry sherbet. Dani had a small marshmallow sundae, Harrison had a large banana split, the two oldest boys had special children’s sundaes, and the youngest had a chocolate chip cone. Only Shirley resisted, righteously and vocally ordering a cup of coffee. I noticed, though, that Shirley took cream in her coffee. And sugar.

然后，当 Buddy 的甜筒送来时，Shirley 似乎很担心它会掉下来或滴水。“我会把多余的剪掉，”她宣布，然后将 Buddy 的勺子缩小到原来的一半。不知何故，多余的东西进入了她的嘴里。

Then when Buddy’s cone arrived, Shirley seemed quite concerned that it was going to fall or drip. “I’ll just trim off the extra,” she announced, and proceeded to reduce Buddy’s scoop to half its original size. Somehow, the excess went into her mouth.

与此同时，哈里森让她“尝尝”他的香蕉片。“只是尝一尝，”她说，但他坚持让她尝尝三种美味中的每一种，加上三种糖浆中的每一种、一些生奶油、一点香蕉和两种樱桃。没过多久，菜就搁在两人中间，她用咖啡勺继续“品尝”。

In the meantime, Harrison offered her a “taste” of his banana split. “Just a taste,” she said, but he insisted that she taste each of the three delicious flavors, plus each of the three syrups, some of the whipped cream, a bit of the banana, and both cherries. Before long, the dish was resting halfway between them, and she was using her coffee spoon to continue her “tasting.”

她还品尝了另外两个孩子的圣代，高贵地吃掉了他们不喜欢的部分。为了洗刷这一切，她点了一杯咖啡，然后又点了一杯，都加了奶油和糖。不知怎么的，她的嘴里好像有一块食物磁铁，无法抗拒地吸引着桌上所有松散的食物。我发现自己不得不给她一些覆盆子果子露，但她正忙着吃完其中一块装饰饼干。

She also sampled the other two children’s sundaes, nobly eating the parts they didn’t like. To wash all this down, she ordered a refill on coffee, then another, both with cream and sugar. Somehow, there seemed to be a food magnet in her mouth, irresistibly attracting all loose food on the table. I found myself compelled to offer her some raspberry sherbet, but she was busy finishing one of the decorative cookies.

那天晚上晚些时候，孩子们躺在床上，我们四个成年人围坐在一起聊天。十一点左右，雪莉问有没有人饿了。我们都不是，但她还是溜进了厨房。当她端着一盘奶酪、水果和坚果回来时，她没有特别向任何人宣布：“好吧，你们都有冰淇淋，而我没有。”

Later that evening, with the kids tucked into bed, we four adults sat around having a nice chat. Around eleven, Shirley asked if anyone was hungry. None of us were, but she slipped into the kitchen anyway. When she returned with a plate of cheese, fruit, and nuts, she announced to nobody in particular, “Well, you guys all had ice cream, and I didn’t have any.”

“但你做到了，”我观察到，立即后悔了。

“But you did,” I observed, regretting it immediately.

雪莉疑惑地看着我。“不。你不记得了吗？我没有点任何冰淇淋。只是咖啡。” 没错，她没有点冰淇淋，但她不知道自己吃的量至少是桌上其他人的两倍吗？

Shirley regarded me with a puzzled look. “No. Don’t you remember? I didn’t order any ice cream. Just coffee.” True, she hadn’t ordered any ice cream, but didn’t she realize that she’d eaten at least twice as much as anyone else at the table?

自盲：头号障碍

SELF-BLINDNESS: THE NUMBER ONE OBSTACLE

对很多人来说，工作就像是雪莉的吃饭。他们把时间浪费在追寻死胡同、拖延打电话或卷入无用的争论中，却从来没有意识到为什么他们没有完成任何事情。例如，作家们会放弃许多错误的开始，或者盯着一页纸看几个小时而不打一个字。许多程序员陷入了一些错误的论点，他们寻找一个错误的时间远远超过了他们应该寻求帮助的时间点。

For many people, work is like Shirley’s eating. They waste time pursuing dead ends, dragging out phone calls, or getting involved in useless arguments, yet never realize why they don’t accomplish anything. Writers, for example, throw away many false starts, or sit staring at a page for hours without typing a single word. Many programmers, stuck in some fallacious argument, search for a single error long past the point where they should seek help.

我知道其他人也会做这些事（丹妮和我一直都在看），但我敢肯定我不会做——至少不会经常做。如果我这样做了，我会不记得吗？

I know that other people do those things (Dani and I watch them all the time), but I’m sure that I don’t do them—not very often, anyway. Wouldn’t I remember if I did?

事实上，我不记得了，就像雪莉不记得她吃了什么甜点一样。和其他人一样，我完全看不到自己在行动，尤其是当我表现出效率最低的行为时。

In fact, I wouldn’t remember, any more than Shirley could remember what she had for dessert. Like everyone else, I’m quite unable to see myself in action, particularly when I’m exhibiting my least productive behaviors.

每当我看到像雪莉这样的人不注意自己时，我的意识就会提高，至少几天。我吃得少了，体重也减轻了。但是很快我又不看了，我的备胎开始充气了。我需要的是一个顾问，一个看着我吃东西并向我报告我自己看不到的东西的人。我们所有人也是如此。我们看到自己的唯一方法是通过其他人。

Whenever I watch someone like Shirley not watching herself, my consciousness is raised, for a few days anyway. I snack less and lose weight. But soon I stop watching again, and my spare tire starts to inflate. What I need is a consultant, someone to watch me eat and report to me what I can’t see for myself. The same is true for all of us. The only way we can see ourselves is through other people.

这种无法像别人看待我们那样看待自己是自我完善的头号障碍。绝大多数想成为解决问题的领导者都被这一障碍所困。为了克服它，他们必须招募其他人来帮助他们。让别人看你的最好方法可能是通过约定看那个人作为回报。即使是相互观察的关系也是一种相当微妙的关系，因此可能需要一些时间才能发展出一种运作良好的关系。

This inability to see ourselves as others see us is the number one obstacle to self-improvement. The great majority of would-be problem-solving leaders are stuck on this one obstacle. To surmount it, they must recruit others to help them. Probably the best way to get someone to watch you is by making a pact to watch that person in return. Even a mutual observing relationship is a rather delicate one, so it may take some time to develop a relationship that works well.

无论你做什么，保持相互。永远不要像我对雪莉所做的那样自愿提供你对人的观察，无论你认为这对他们有多大帮助。雪莉很好，没有打我，但我很幸运。

Whatever you do, keep it mutual. Don’t ever volunteer your observations about people as I did with Shirley, no matter how helpful you think it would be for them. Shirley was nice enough not to punch me, but I was lucky.

即使人们询问您的意见，他们也不会总是喜欢您说的话。我曾经让丹妮注意我的饮食习惯。作为一名人类学家，她是一名专家级观察员——事实上，她太好了，以至于差点毁了我们的婚姻。一直被人监视可不是一件有趣的事，所以无论你做什么，都不要选择你的配偶作为你的监视伙伴。

Even when people ask for your observations, they won’t always like what you have to say. I once asked Dani to watch my eating patterns. As an anthropologist, she’s an expert observer—so good, in fact, that it almost broke up our marriage. It’s just not much fun to be watched all the time, so whatever you do, don’t pick your spouse as your watching partner.

无问题综合症：第二大障碍

NO-PROBLEM SYNDROME: THE NUMBER TWO OBSTACLE

在我们访问雪莉的同一次旅行中，我遇到了第二个障碍的例子，它干扰了成为更有效的问题解决者的一切努力。我称之为无问题综合症。我当时在萨克拉门托，在数据处理管理协会的地方分会发表演讲。我在演讲开始时谈到了之前作为 IBM 新员工的一次旅行，那次访问我永远不会忘记。

On the same trip that we visited Shirley, I encountered an example of the second obstacle that interferes with every effort to become a more effective problem solver. I call it the No-Problem Syndrome. I was in Sacramento, addressing the local chapter of the Data Processing Management Association. I began my address by talking about a previous trip as a new employee at IBM, a visit I’ll never forget.

州议会刚刚通过了一项法律，允许在车牌上使用字母和数字。法律的反对者认为，某些字母组合可能会令人反感。该法案的支持者承诺从车牌中剔除所有令人反感的字母组合，但他们没有具体的计划来解决这个问题。有人告诉他们电脑会有很大帮助，所以他们打电话给 IBM。那就是我进来的地方。

The State Legislature had just passed a law allowing letters as well as numbers on license plates. Opponents of the law had argued that certain combinations of letters might prove offensive. The bill’s backers promised to cull all offensive letter combinations from the plates, but they had no particular plan as to how to go about this. Somebody told them that a computer would be a big help, so they called IBM. That’s where I came in.

我是一个刚出道的年轻 IBM 员工，整装待发，准备用一个神来之笔的计算机程序清除世界上的脏话。不幸的是，机动车登记处的人至少提出了三个我无法满足的要求：

I was a fresh young IBMer, all suited up and ready to purge the world of dirty words with a whiz-bang computer program. Unfortunately, the people from Motor Vehicle Registration had at least three requirements that I couldn’t possibly satisfy:

• 一些“令人反感”的词不是英语单词，只是看起来像英语单词。要理解这个问题，请从您最喜欢的四个字母的咒骂中删除一个字母。有时新词是无害的，但有时它和原来的词一样令人反感。

• Some “offensive” words weren’t words in English but only looked like words in English. To understand this problem, drop one letter from your favorite four-letter expletive. Sometimes the new word is innocuous, but other times it’s as offensive as the original.

• 加利福尼亚有许多讲多种不同语言的族群。该程序应该消除任何可能冒犯任何说西班牙语、中文、希伯来语、意第绪语、希腊语、法语、亚美尼亚语和其他一些我不记得的人的东西。

• California had many ethnic groups speaking many different languages. The program was supposed to get rid of anything that might be offensive to anybody speaking Spanish, Chinese, Hebrew, Yiddish, Greek, French, Armenian, and a few others I can’t remember.

• 我们还必须删除有朝一日可能会冒犯任何说这些语言的人或有朝一日可能访问加利福尼亚的任何人的任何语言的词。

• We also had to remove words that might someday be offensive to anybody speaking any of these languages or any languages of anyone who might someday visit California.

我讲了车牌的故事，开始讨论是什么让问题难以解决。然后发了一套题给大家试试。当我在房间里走来走去看看每个人都在做什么时，我注意到一个男人坐在显眼的地方，双臂紧紧地交叉在胸前。

I told the license plate story to kick off a discussion of what makes problems difficult to solve. Then I distributed a set of problems for everyone to try. As I circulated through the room to see how everyone was doing, I noticed one man sitting conspicuously with his arms folded tightly across his chest.

“你已经做完了吗？” 我问他（过去式。

“Have you finished already?” I asked him.

“不，”他说，“我不做它们。为什么要浪费我的时间？你为什么不快点告诉我们你到底想告诉我们什么？”

“No,” he said, “I’m not doing them. Why waste my time? Why don’t you just hurry up and tell us what it is you’re trying to tell us?”

“我不能告诉你，”我说，“因为我想让你感受到试图解决某些困难问题时的挫败感。只是告诉你是不一样的。”

“I can’t tell you,” I said, “because I want you to feel the frustration of trying to do certain kinds of difficult problems. Telling you just isn’t the same.”

“好吧，你不妨告诉我，”他反驳道，“因为我可以解决你给我的任何问题。事实上，你‘无法解决’的许可问题实际上是微不足道的。”

“Well, you might as well tell me,” he countered, “because I can solve any problem you can give me. In fact, your ‘unsolvable’ license problem is actually trivial.”

“琐碎的？” 我问。

“Trivial?” I asked.

“绝对地。借助现代技术，您所需要的只是一本大词典。您将字母组合传递给字典并删除不需要的字母。只是没问题。”

“Absolutely. With modern technology, all you need is a big dictionary. You pass the combinations of letters against the dictionary and eliminate the ones you don’t want. It’s just no problem.”

我的第一直觉是争论。我可能会问他他将如何获得一本尚未创造的单词词典。或者，一旦你构建了一个冒犯性词汇的字典，为什么还要费心使用计算机。然后我意识到这个可怜的人患有严重的“无问题综合症”，简称 NPS。我自己也患过这种病，所以我对这个可怜的人只有同情。我可不喜欢打残障人士，笑笑就走。

My first instinct was to argue. I might have asked him how he was going to get a dictionary of words that hadn’t been coined yet. Or why you would bother with a computer, once you had constructed a dictionary of offensive words. Then I realized that the poor man was suffering from a severe case of “No-Problem Syndrome,” or NPS for short. I had suffered from this disease myself, so I had nothing but sympathy for the poor man. I don’t get any kicks out of attacking the handicapped, so I simply smiled and walked away.

也许您从未听说过 NPS？我没有与任何神经生理学家核实过这一点，但它似乎是耳朵没有正确连接到大脑的情况。声音输入正常，但它们触发了一种与它们的含义无关的刻板反应。一个人描述了一个非常令人烦恼的问题，但另一个人只是冷酷地回应，“没问题。”

Perhaps you’ve never heard of NPS? I haven’t checked this with any neurophysiologist, but it seems to be a condition in which the ears are not properly connected to the brain. The sounds enter all right, but they trigger a stereotyped response that has nothing to do with their meaning. One person describes a terribly vexing problem, but the other merely responds with a callous, “No problem.”

无问题综合症与耳聋不同。事实上，聋人不可能有 NPS，因为反应必须由到达耳朵的关键词“问题”触发。一旦这个词出现，耳朵就会选择性失聪，这是综合症的第一阶段。第二阶段似乎是在脑海中回忆一些最喜欢的解决方法，即使有必要打断问题描述，也会立即呈现给说话者。我的叔叔马克斯有 NPS，他最喜欢的解决方案是恢复公立学校打孩子的做法，就像他在家里打孩子一样。如果经济不景气，那是因为他们不再在学校打孩子了。如果犯罪率上升，或者天气不好，也是出于同样的原因。

No-Problem Syndrome isn’t the same as deafness. In fact, deaf people couldn’t have NPS because the response has to be triggered by the key word, “problem,” reaching the ear. Once that word registers, the ears become selectively deaf, the first stage of the syndrome. The second stage seems to be the mental retrieval of some favorite solution method, which is immediately presented to the talker, even if it’s necessary to interrupt the problem description. My Uncle Max had NPS, and his favorite solution had to do with restoring the practice of beating children in the public schools, much as he beat his children at home. If the economy was down, it was because they don’t beat kids in school any more. If crime was up, or the weather was bad, it was for the same reason.

像大多数孩子一样，我不理解 NPS 的心碎。以前还笑话我舅舅，做梦也没想到NPS会遗传。因为缺乏自知之明，直到第一次去萨克拉门托后，我才知道了一个悲惨的消息：我自己得了NPS！

Like most kids, I didn’t understand the heartbreak of NPS. I used to laugh at my uncle, never dreaming that NPS might be hereditary. Because I lacked self-awareness, it wasn’t until after my first visit to Sacramento that I found out the tragic news: I had NPS myself!

是车牌问题泄露了我的可怕秘密。汽车公司的人告诉我他们的要求，但显然他们的话从未在我的脑海中出现过。在他们说完之前，我向他们保证没有问题。在我理解他们的问题是什么之前，我设法编写了一个程序来解决它。

It was the license plate problem that revealed my terrible secret. The Motor Vehicle people told me about their requirements, but evidently their words never registered in my brain. Before they had finished talking, I had assured them that there was no problem. Before I understood what their problem was, I had managed to write a program to solve it.

你可以想象，当一群公务员拒绝他所有出色的工作时，这对一个年轻的 IBM 人来说是多么大的打击。另一方面，您可以想象，当一个年轻的 IBM 人甚至没有听取他们的要求，告诉他们他们并没有真正的“问题”时，这对他们来说是多么大的打击。拥有 NPS 可能很糟糕，但成为其受害者之一更糟糕。

You can imagine what a blow it was to a young IBMer when a bunch of civil servants rejected all his brilliant work. On the other hand, you can imagine what a blow it was to them when a young IBMer, not even listening to their requirements, told them they didn’t really have a ‘problem. It may be terrible to have NPS, but it’s even worse to be one of its victims.

我曾经认为计算机会发出一些破坏神经的高频声音，因为 NPS 似乎影响了很大一部分计算机专业人员。每当他们在同一句话中听到“问题”和“计算机”这两个词时，他们就会发起谩骂，这会让老麦克斯叔叔感到羞耻。它总是以“没问题”这句话开头。

I used to think that computers emit some nerve-damaging high frequency sounds, because NPS seems to affect a large percentage of computer professionals. Whenever they hear the words “problem” and “computer” in the same sentence, they launch into a diatribe that would put old Uncle Max to shame. And it always starts with the words, “No problem.”

然而，随着年龄的增长，我意识到计算机本身实际上并不会导致 NPS。也许这是行业的快速发展，这降低了问题解决者花时间了解他们在做什么的可能性。此外，我注意到 NPS 折磨着所有高科技行业的人，所以它不仅仅是计算机。它也影响了很多低技术行业的人，或者根本不在行业的人。

As I grew older, however, I realized that computers themselves do not actually cause NPS. Perhaps it’s the fast pace of the industry, which reduces the likelihood that problem solvers will take time to be aware of what they’re doing. Besides, I’ve noticed that NPS afflicts people in all high-tech industries, so it can’t just be computers. It also affects quite a few people in low-tech industries, or people not in industries at all.

随着年龄的增长，我自己的症状有所减轻，所以也许这个问题只是计算机行业年轻化的表现。不过，除了衰老，我不知道有什么方法可以治愈 NPS。我希望我能帮助这些可怜的残障人士，但痛苦的经历告诉我，去斯里兰卡治愈麻风病人会取得更大的成功。

My own symptoms have abated a bit with age, so perhaps the problem is simply a manifestation of the youthfulness of the computer industry. Other than senility, though, I don’t know of any cure for NPS. I wish I could help these poor handicapped souls, but bitter experience has taught me that I’d have more success going to Sri Lanka to cure lepers.

有问题需要他人解决的人应该更好地了解 NPS 的危险。因为它无法治愈，所以他们最好通过我的早期 NPS 检测四步计划来学习保护自己：

People who have problems for others to solve should be better informed about the perils of NPS. Because it can’t be cured, they had better learn to protect themselves through my four-step plan for early NPS detection:

  1. 你描述了你非常困难的问题。
  2. You describe your very difficult problem.
  3. 对方说：“没问题！”
  4. The respondent says, “No problem!”
  5. 你说，“哦，那太棒了！你能描述一下你要解决的问题吗？
  6. You say, “Oh, that’s terrific! Could you please describe my problem that you’re going to solve?”

4a. 如果受访者随后描述了您的问题，即使是错误的，也不是 NPS 的情况，而只是热情的情况。

4a. If the respondent then describes your problem, even erroneously, it’s not a case of NPS but only a case of Enthusiasm.

4b. 如果受访者描述了针对您的问题的建议解决方案而不是问题本身，那么遗憾的是它是 NPS。您能为所有相关人员做的最友善的事情就是微笑并轻快地走到最近的出口。

4b. If the respondent describes a proposed solution to your problem rather than the problem itself, then sadly it’s NPS. The kindest thing you can do for all concerned is smile and walk briskly to the nearest exit.

有时四步检测方案可以用于自我诊断，但如果NPS太超前就不行了。要检测您自己的 NPS，您必须能够听到自己说：“没问题！” 或者至少在你确认你理解对方的问题之前先听听你自己给出解决方案。但是，唉，终末期 NPS 患者无法很好地听到其他人的声音；他们根本听不到自己的声音。他们不仅自盲，而且自聋。

Sometimes the four-step detection plan can be used for self- diagnosis, but if the NPS is too far advanced, it won’t work. To detect your own NPS, you have to be able to hear yourself say, “No problem!” or at least hear yourself giving solutions before you’ve confirmed that you understand the other person’s problem. But, alas, terminal NPS patients can’t hear other people very well; and they can’t hear themselves at all. They’re not only self-blind, they’re self-deaf.

单一解决方案信念：第三大障碍

SINGLE-SOLUTION BELIEF: THE NUMBER THREE OBSTACLE

我们可以将 NPS 描述为对您自己的主智能的不可动摇的信念。这个定义清楚地说明了为什么 NPS 是成为创新领导者的第二大障碍。中国人说，知识的第一步是承认无知。如果你已经知道一切，你将如何学习任何东西？

We could characterize NPS as the unshakable belief in your own master intelligence. This definition makes it clear why NPS is the number two obstacle to becoming an innovative leader. The Chinese say that the first step to knowledge is a confession of ignorance. If you already know everything, how will you ever learn anything?

尽管您不应该相信自己无所不知，但了解一些事情确实有帮助。没有人否认优秀的问题解决者必须聪明，但缺乏智慧并不是三大障碍之一。我们都知道很多高智商的人并不擅长解决现实生活中的问题。或许我们应该更加怀疑心理学家识别“智力”的程序。

Even though you shouldn’t believe you know everything, it does help to know something. Nobody denies that good problem solvers have to be intelligent, but lack of intelligence isn’t one of the three big obstacles. We all know many high-IQ people who aren’t good at solving real-life problems. Perhaps we ought to be more skeptical about the procedure by which psychologists identify “intelligence.”

最近，一家科学杂志刊登了一个由 Mensa 准备的“Mind Benders”专栏，Mensa 是一个由在标准化智商测试中得分前 2% 的人组成的组织。在 Mind Benders 中有以下两个问题，这使我的思想转向了 Mensa 可能无意的方向。

Recently, a science magazine ran a “Mind Benders” column prepared by Mensa, an organization of people who score in the top two percent on standardized IQ tests. Among the Mind Benders were the following two questions, which bent my mind in a direction perhaps not intended by Mensa.

  1. 我办公室的秘书都不到21岁。我办公室的小姐姐们都很漂亮。我的秘书有一头金色的长发和蓝色的眼睛。
  2. All the secretaries in my office are under 21. All the young ladies in my office are very beautiful. My secretary has long blond hair and blue eyes.

以下哪项陈述可以根据所提供的信息加以证明？

Which statement(s) below can be justified by the information given?

a) 我的秘书未满 21 岁。

a) My secretary is under 21.

b) 我的秘书是一位漂亮的年轻女士。

b) My secretary is a beautiful young lady.

c) 以上都不是。

c) Neither of the above.

d) 前两个。

d) Both of the first two.

  1. 在某一领域，既有马又有人。场上有26个头和82个脚（或蹄）。有多少男人？几匹马？
  2. In a certain field there are both horses and men. There are 26 heads and 82 feet (or hooves) in the field. How many men are there? How many horses?

以下是他们的回答：

Here are their answers:

  1. 给出的“正确”答案是 (a) 我的秘书未满 21 岁。应该让您感到困惑的是所有秘书都是女性的假设，但并未说明这一点。但是假设我的秘书在我的办公室呢？它没有这么说，但也没有说我的秘书是女性。根据您选择的假设集，所有四个答案都是可能的。虽然我个人从未有过男秘书，但我曾遇到过我的秘书在另一个办公室，甚至在另一个城市的情况。这是否让我不如提出问题的心理学家聪明？
  2. The “correct” answer is given as (a) my secretary is under 21. What’s supposed to trip you up is the assumption that all secretaries are female, which isn’t stated. But what about the assumption that my secretary is in my office? It doesn’t say that, but then it doesn’t say my secretary is female. Depending on which set of assumptions you choose, all four answers are possible. Although I’ve never personally had a male secretary, I have worked in situations where my secretary was in another office, even in another city. Does this make me less intelligent than the psychologist who posed the question?
  3. 通过简单的代数，如果你假设每匹马有 4 英尺，每个人有 2 英尺，你会得到官方答案（15 匹马和 11 个人）。我对马不太了解，但我在观看退伍军人节后阅读了这个测试游行。对我来说很明显，一种可能的解决方案是 16 匹马和 10 个人，其中一个是没有腿的老兵。当然，如果您不是心理学家，沿着这条线还有很多其他解决方案。
  4. By simple algebra, you get the official answer (15 horses and 11 men) if you assume that each horse has 4 feet and each man has 2. I don’t know much about horses, but I read this test after watching a Veterans Day parade. It was obvious to me that one possible solution is 16 horses and 10 men, one of whom is a veteran with no legs. Of course, there are lots of other solutions along this line, if you’re not a psychologist.

任何接触过这种测试的聪明人都经历过同样的挫败感：你可以想出几个可能的答案，但你知道心理学家只想要一个；而且你不被允许提问。当问题出现在杂志上只是为了好玩，甚至出现在 Mensa 入学考试中时，情况并没有那么糟糕。

Any intelligent being who has been exposed to this sort of testing has experienced the same frustration: You can think of several possible answers, but you know the psychologists want only one; and you’re not allowed to ask questions. It’s not so bad when the questions are in a magazine just for fun, or even in the Mensa admission test.

但是如果你想上大学怎么办？还是找工作？还是在二年级进入喜欢的轨道？心理学家拥有阻止你得到你想要的东西的力量，他们的力量是毋庸置疑的。

But what if you want to get into college? or get a job? or get into a favored track in the second grade? The psychologists hold the power to keep you from getting what you want, and their power is unquestionable.

学术心理学很可能是有史以来最傲慢的职业。在第 1 章中，我们已经遇到了学术心理学的中心法则：每个问题都只有一个正确的解决方案——心理学家知道这一点。这个教条同样适用于对人的测试或对老鼠的迷宫测试。任何对心理学家的设置表现出一点怀疑的老鼠走迷宫的速度都会慢一点，并被贴上“不太聪明”的标签。对我来说，任何不怀疑心理学家实验的人都应该被贴上“不太聪明”的标签。

Quite possibly, academic psychology is the most arrogant profession of all time. In Chapter 1, we already encountered the central dogma of academic psychology: There is one and only one correct solution to every problem—and the psychologist knows it. This dogma applies equally to tests for people or mazes for rats.
