Any rat who displays a modicum of suspicion for the psychologist’s setup runs the maze a little slower and is labeled “less intelligent.” To me, anyone who fails to be suspicious of psychologists’ experiments should be labeled “less intelligent.”

中心法则对被心理学家困住的个人或老鼠的伤害已经够大了，但它对社会的长期影响可能更糟。学校和雇主奖励那些想法恰好与心理学家的想法相符的人，所以人们要么学会那样思考，要么发现自己被冷落。过了一段时间，我们发现在解决问题的情况下，人们确实相信每个问题都有一个且只有一个解决方案，一个不可避免的解决方案，以至于他们在找到它时会认出它。

The central dogma is damaging enough to the individual person or rat trapped by the psychologist, but its long-range effects on society may be even worse. Schools and employers reward people whose thinking happens to match the thinking of psychologists, so people either learn to think that way or find themselves out in the cold. After a while, we find people in problem-solving situations who literally believe that every problem has one and only one solution, a solution so inevitable that they will recognize it when they find it.

对于一个未来的问题解决型领导者来说，对中心法则的信仰是“使人衰弱的疾病”，也是成为明星问题解决型领导者的第三大障碍。受感染的设计师很少考虑足够数量的备选设计，并且除了凭自己的直觉之外，从不考虑测试设计。受感染的程序员面对以任何方式偏离明显答案的错误都无能为力。

For a would-be problem-solving leader, belief in the central dogma is ‘debilitating disease and the third great obstacle to becoming a star problem-solving leader. Infected designers rarely consider an adequate number of alternative designs, and never consider testing the design other than by their own intuition. Infected programmers are powerless in the face of a bug that deviates in any way from the obvious answer.

被中心法则感染的管理者表现得像心理学家；他们将工作分配给下属，并希望以一种正确的方式完成工作——他们的方式。不久，他们按照自己的形象创造了下一代。

Managers infected by the central dogma act like psychologists; they assign work to their subordinates and expect to have it done in one right way—their way. Before long, they create another generation in their own image.

概括

SUMMARY

创新的三大障碍值得总结：

They’re worth summarizing, these three great obstacles to innovation:

  1. 自我盲目，隐藏自己的行为，让你没有机会改变
  2. self-blindness, concealing your own behavior, so you have no chance of changing
  3. 无问题综合症，让你相信你已经知道所有问题的答案
  4. No-Problem Syndrome, convincing you that you already know the answer to all problems
  5. 相信学术心理学的中心教条，使您对替代解决方案视而不见，即使是您可以在没有其他人帮助的情况下产生的解决方案
  6. belief in the central dogma of academic psychology, blinding you to alternative solutions, even ones you could generate without help from anyone else

这些根深蒂固的障碍形成了一个封闭的系统，阻碍了它们自己的清除。自盲的人可能会阅读此列表并点头表示同意，但对其他人来说。患有 NPS 的人甚至不会首先阅读该列表。相信中心法则的人已经踏上了他们认为唯一且唯一的成功之路。

These deeply imbedded obstacles form a closed system, standing in the way of their own removal. People who are self-blind might read this list and nod their heads in agreement, but about other people. People who suffer from NPS will not even read the list in the first place. People who believe the central dogma will already be on what they consider to be the one and only path to success.

因此，让我们告别那些人，专注于那些仍有希望的人。让我们坚持你们这些有创造力的人，他们知道在你做的时候看你在做什么和在你看的时候嘲笑你自己的双重乐趣。对于您，我提供了以下章节，介绍如何克服您通过创新成为领导者的道路上的一些较小障碍。

So let’s bid those people goodbye and concentrate on those for whom there is still some hope. Let’s stick with you creative people who know the double enjoyment of watching what you’re doing while you’re doing it and laughing at yourself while you watch. To you, I offer the following chapters on how to overcome some lesser obstacles in your path to becoming a leader through innovation.

问题

QUESTIONS

  1. 知道你吃了什么甜点是对健康问题的一般自我意识的应用。您对自己的健康状况了解多少，以及它如何影响您的领导风格？你现在感觉如何？盘点您的身体状况，并描述它目前如何影响您作为领导者的表现。
  2. Knowing what you had for dessert is an application of general self-awareness to the question of health. How aware are you of your own health and how it affects your leadership style? How do you feel right now? Take an inventory of your physical condition, and describe how it currently affects your performance as a leader.
  3. 健康不佳是创新和其他一切的障碍。您的长期健康状况如何影响您的职业？您未来的健康状况如何？如果你很难回答这个问题，是什么让你认为你的健康不受你自己的控制？你在做什么来让它在你自己的控制之下？对你以后的职业生涯有什么影响？
  4. Poor health is an obstacle to innovation and just about everything else. How has your long-term health affected your career? What is your health going to be like in the future? If you have a hard time answering that question, what makes you think your health is not under your own control? What are you doing to keep it under your own control? How will it affect your career in the future?
  5. 在回答前两个问题时，您是否回答过您的健康“没有问题”？这告诉你关于你自己的什么？
  6. In answering the previous two questions, did you respond that your health was “no problem”? What does that tell you about yourself?
  7. 你知道你的智商吗？你让别人知道吗？了解您的智商会影响您的领导能力吗？如何？
  8. Do you know your IQ? Do you let other people know? Does knowing your IQ affect your ability to lead? How?
  9. 你喜欢参加考试吗？如果你知道你肯定会做得很好，你愿意参加考试吗？如果你确信你做得不好怎么办？如果您必须参加考试，但永远不知道自己的成绩怎么办？这些问题与领导风格有什么关系？
  10. Do you like to take tests? If you knew you were assured of doing well, would you like to take a test? What if you were assured of doing poorly? What if you had to take the test, but were never to find out how well you did? What do these questions have to do with leadership style?
  11. 找到一些选择题测验，并按照以下方式完成：不要选择一个答案，而是依次选择每个答案，并给出一个很好的理由说明为什么这个答案可能是答案。然后给出一些不在选项中的答案的充分理由。
  12. Find some multiple choice quiz and go through it in the following way: Instead of picking one answer, take each answer in turn and give a good reason why that could be the answer. Then give a good reason for some answer that isn’t among the choices.
  13. 下次你在会议上提出几个想法时，应用上一个问题的技巧。也就是说，确保你给与会者一个充分的理由来解释为什么每个想法都可能是你正在寻找的解决方案。然后再提供至少一个。
  14. Next time you’re in a meeting and several ideas are brought up, apply the technique of the previous question. That is, make sure you give a good reason to the meeting’s participants to explain why each idea could be the solution you’re seeking. Then offer at least one more.

第 7 章培养自我意识的工具

Chapter 7. A Tool for Developing Self-Awareness

问：换一个灯泡需要多少精神科医生？

Q. How many psychiatrists does it take to change a light bulb?

A. 只有一个，如果灯泡真的想换的话。

A. Only one, if the bulb really wants to change.

创新的三大障碍真的如此令人虚弱以至于没有改变的希望吗？对一些人来说，是的。对于其他人来说，如果有改变的愿望，就有希望。我知道，因为我目睹了一些显着的转变。

Are the three great obstacles to innovation really so debilitating that there is no hope for change? For some, yes. For others, there is hope if there is a desire for change. I know because I have witnessed some remarkable transformations.

在本章中，我想推荐一个你可以自己使用的最佳工具。它可以帮助你克服阻碍克服其他障碍的自我盲目。作为说服您尝试的一种方式，我想与您分享一些使用此工具的人的话，以帮助他们更清楚地看到自己。

In this chapter, I want to suggest the single best tool that you can use by yourself. It can help you overcome self-blindness, which stands in the way of overcoming the other obstacles. As a way of convincing you to try it, I’d like to share with you the words of some people who used this tool to help them see themselves more clearly.

测试你的动机

A TEST OF YOUR MOTIVATION

如果这个工具这么棒，你为什么需要说服力？根据 MOI 理论，要想成功地将自己转变为更有效的解决问题的领导者，您需要三样东西：动机、组织和想法。作为作者，我可以为你提供想法和组织建议，但关于你的动机，如果你像灯泡一样不想改变，我无能为力。

If this tool is so wonderful, why would you need convincing? According to the MOI theory, you’ll need three things to succeed at transforming yourself into a more effective problem-solving leader: motivation, organization, and ideas. As an author, I can supply you with ideas and with suggestions for organization, but concerning your motivation, I am powerless if, like the light bulb, you don’t really want to change.

然而，我可以给你一个测试，这样你就可以自己发现你是否有足够的动力来成功地实现自己的转变。测试很简单，它不仅衡量您是否准备好改变，还会为您提供改变的想法以及构建这些想法的一些组织。这是测试：

I can give you a test, however, so you can discover for yourself if you have sufficient motivation to succeed in your own transformation. The test is simple, and it not only measures whether you are ready to change, but also gives you ideas for change and some organization for structuring those ideas. Here’s the test:

从现在开始，持续三个月，每天花五分钟写个人日记。

Starting now, and continuing for three months, spend five minutes each day writing in a personal journal.

你的最初反应

YOUR INITIAL REACTION

现在暂停一下，拿一张纸，写下你最初的反应。该反应是测试的重要部分。不仅如此，你在那张纸上写的是你的第一个日记条目。如果你没有写它，你已经有麻烦了，但你有很好的陪伴。我们的许多客户都有这种反应，例如 Birgitt：

Pause now, get a piece of paper, and write down your initial reaction. This reaction is an important part of the test. Not only that, but what you wrote on that piece of paper is your first journal entry. If you didn’t write it, you’re already in trouble, but you’re in good company. Many of our clients have had that reaction, like Birgitt:

“当 Jerry 建议进行这项测试时，我首先想到的是写任何东西都是令人反感的，但写自己却令人反感。然而，考试是每天写五分钟，而我花了那么多时间刷牙。所以我想，如果我不能在三个月内做到这一点，也许我根本就不想成为一个解决问题的领导者。”

“When Jerry suggested this test, the first thing I thought was that writing about anything was distasteful, but writing about myself was revolting. Yet, the test is to write for five minutes a day, and I spend that much time brushing my teeth. So I thought that if I can’t do that for three months, perhaps I don’t want to be a problem-solving leader after all.”

彼得最初拒绝了这个想法：

Peter initially rejected the idea:

“我只是看不出在我的日程安排上增加另一个负担有什么意义，所以我从来没有真正考虑过这样做。四个月后的元旦那天，当我在看碗赛时，这个想法再次出现在我的脑海中。在一次淡啤酒广告中，我计算出测试要求我三个月的时间比我花在 Fiesta Bowl、Rose Bowl 和 Orange Bowl 上一天的时间还少。我决定试一试——一个月。”

“I just couldn’t see the point of having another burden to add to my schedule, so I never really considered doing it. Four months later, on New Year’s Day, I was watching the bowl games when the idea popped into my head again. During a light beer commercial, I calculated that the test asked me for less time over three months than I was spending on the Fiesta Bowl, Rose Bowl, and Orange Bowl in one day. I decided to give it a try—for one month.”

这是他们的第一个条目，您稍后会读到 Birgitt 和 Peter 的遭遇，但现在您可以看到他们是如何通过测试的。他们对自己的领导力目标非常认真，以至于冒着每天浪费五分钟的风险。如果他们没有那么认真，那么我向他们提出的任何其他建议都会浪费我的时间。

These were their first entries and you’ll read later what happened to Birgitt and Peter, but for now you can see how they passed the test. They were serious enough about their leadership goals to risk wasting five minutes a day. If they hadn’t been that serious, then anything else I suggested to them would have been a waste of my time.

您的个人日记

YOUR PERSONAL JOURNAL

如果你决定继续写个人日记，你很快就会有很多松散的纸片，所以我建议你买一本精装的书和一个舒适的书写工具，或者一个文字处理器。这些看似不重要，其实不然。你需要一切帮助和激励才能忠于录音。

If you decide to go ahead with a personal journal, you’ll soon have a great many loose pieces of paper, so I suggest getting a nice, bound book and a comfortable writing instrument, or a word processor. These may seem unimportant, but they aren’t. You’ll need every aid and incentive to be faithful about recording.

但是，如果您决定使用计算机，请不要返回并修改以前的条目。以后，当你想回顾你写的东西时，你会希望你有最初的印象。相反，获取打印副本并将其粘贴在笔记本上，最好是精装本，并注明页面日期。

If you do decide to use a computer, though, don’t go back and revise previous entries. Later, when you want to review what you’ve written, you’ll wish you had your original impressions. Instead, get a printed copy and paste it in a notebook, preferably hardbound, with dated pages.

如果您对写日记感到压抑，也许是因为童年时与兄弟姐妹或父母一起阅读日记的不愉快经历，请务必让您的日记远离好奇的眼睛。这并不意味着您永远不能与他人共享日记的某些部分，只是您必须是控制访问的人。这一点尤其重要，这样您才能完全诚实地表达自己的感受，并呈现出公正的自我形象。

If you feel inhibited about keeping a diary, perhaps because of an unhappy childhood experience with a sibling or parent reading it, be sure to keep your journal secure from curious eyes. This doesn’t mean that you can never share parts of your journal with others, but only that you must be the one to control access. This is especially important so that you are completely honest about your feelings, and that you present an unbiased picture of yourself.

选择一天中的什么时间写作并不重要，甚至每天在同一时间写作 Sr 也不重要。重要的是你找到了一些方法来确保你每天写下你的五分钟。Nadia 在找到有效的方法之前必须尝试几种方法：

It’s not important what time of day you choose to write, or even Sr that you write at the same time each day. What is important is that you find some method that ensures you write your five minutes each day. Nadia had to try several things before she found one that worked:

“因为我使用了文字处理器，所以我不得不在工作中写日记。但在工作中，我的时间经常被其他人控制——这是我记日记的第一个好教训。所以我在门口做了一个标志，上面写着女性写作 - 请勿进入。这很好用，但没有解决我周末写作的问题，所以我买了一个老式笔记本。

“Because I used the word processor, I had to do my journal at work. But at work my time was often controlled by other people—which was my first good lesson from keeping the journal. So I made a sign for my door that said WOMAN WRITING-DO NOT ENTER. That worked pretty well, but didn’t solve my problem of writing on weekends, so I bought an old-fashioned notebook.

“在家里，我仍然很难找到一个可以指望每天在同一时间不受打扰的五分钟的时间。我试着在睡觉前写作，但有时我太困了。此外，有时我丈夫不喜欢它。最终我发现我可以在喂完狗后和早餐前写作。对我来说，这是一项很好的活动，可以让人们以正确的心态轻松度过一天——尽管我想每个人都必须找到适合自己的活动。”

“At home, I still had trouble finding a time I could count on to have five uninterrupted minutes at the same time every day. I tried writing just before going to bed, but sometimes I was too sleepy. Besides, sometimes my husband didn’t like it. Eventually I found I could write after feeding the dogs, and before breakfast. For me, it’s a good activity for easing into the day in the right frame of mind—though I suppose each person will have to find something that works right for them.”

对彼得来说，写作就像锻炼：

For Peter, writing was like a workout:

“我错误地认为，如果五分钟好，十分钟更好。我每天都试图延长我的时间，最终我开始害怕我的写作时期。现在我将计时器设置为五分钟。铃声响起时，我可以继续，但我知道停下来也没关系。时间是我的保证，我不会对自己要求太多。

“I made the mistake of thinking that if five minutes was good, ten minutes was better. I tried to stretch my time every day, and eventually I was getting to dread my writing period. Now I set a timer for five minutes. When the bell rings, I can continue if I want, but I know it’s all right to stop. The time is my assurance that I’m not asking too much of myself.

“如果我想在其他时间写日记，那就太好了。每当精神感动我时，我都会写下我想要的一切。但无论那天你写了多少其他内容，你都必须遵守常规的五分钟时间。否则，就像破坏训练一样。”

“If I want to write in my journal at other times, that’s terrific. I write all I want whenever the spirit moves me. But no matter how much else you have written that day, you must still honor your regular five-minute period. Otherwise, it’s like breaking training.”

换句话说，精神动了才写重要，精神不动了才写更重要。如果您找不到定期花时间进行自我观察的模式，那么您的领导力发展计划就会遇到严重的麻烦。

In other words, it’s important to write when the spirit moves you, but it’s even more important to write when the spirit doesn’t move you. If you can’t find a regular pattern of some time for self-observation, your leadership development program is in serious trouble.

写什么

What to write about

Nadia 和 Peter 的证词都表明，即使你从写日记中一无所获，你也会了解当你试图改变自己的行为时你会如何反应。如果你想成为解决问题的领导者，你需要了解自己。至少，当您要求其他人进行简单的更改时，您会开始体会他们的感受。

The testimonies of Nadia and Peter both illustrate that even if you get nothing else out of keeping the journal, you’ll learn about how you react when you try to change your behavior. If you want to be a problem-solving leader, you’ll need to know this about yourself. At the least, you’ll begin to appreciate how other people will feel when you ask them to make a simple change.

人们写什么？我喜欢纳迪亚所说的方式：

What do people write about? I like the way Nadia puts it:

“我读过很多关于写什么的规则，但只有一条对我来说至关重要：写你自己。我日记的主题是我——我喜欢什么，我每天做什么，我对此有何感受，我如何看待别人对我的反应。”

“I’ve read many rules about what to write about, but only one is crucial for me: Write about yourself. The subject of my journal is me—what I’m like, what I do each day, how I feel about it, how I see others reacting to me.”

Mavis 以不同的方式表达她在日记中所写的内容：

Mavis has a different way of expressing what she writes in her journal:

“我坚持三件事：首先，我把发生的事情写下来，尽量描述清楚，不加标签或评判。然后，我描述了我对它的反应——我的想法，如果它让我生气，如果我梦到它。最后，我记下了我从中学到的东西（如果有的话）。大多数时候，我什么都学不到，直到很久以后我再次​​阅读条目时。”

“I stick to three things: First, I write down what happened, as descriptive as I can make it without labeling or judging. Then, I describe how I reacted to it—what I thought about, if it made me angry, if I dreamt about it. Finally, I put down what, if anything, I learned from it. Most of the time, I don’t learn anything, until much later when I read the entry again.”

我们的许多客户都遵循这个“事实、感受、发现”的公式，但 Juan 有不同的看法：

Many of our clients follow this “facts, feelings, findings” formula, but Juan had a different view:

“我对所有那些垃圾的人际关系——感情、梦想和幻想——印象不深。给我印象最深的是杰里说，从事实验室工作的专业工程师和科学家都记日记。所以我写的是我的想法——新的技巧和设计，以及当我在系统上尝试时发生的事情。我会写下我遇到的错误，以及我是如何尝试修复它们的。”

“I wasn’t impressed with all that human relations garbage—feelings and dreams and fantasies. What impressed me most was when Jerry said that professional engineers and scientists doing laboratory work all kept journals. So what I wrote about was my ideas—new tricks and designs and what happened when I tried things out on the system. I would write about the bugs I had, and how I tried to fix them.”

也许彼得代表多数人的立场，他说，

Perhaps Peter represents the majority position when he says,

“我写日记来了解自己。如果我和其他人一样，我就不需要记日记了。我是独一无二的，所以其他人怎么能告诉我在日记中保留什么。那么我在日记中写什么呢？一只九百磅重的大猩猩会写什么？我想写什么就写什么！”

“I keep a journal to learn about myself. If I was the same as everybody else, I wouldn’t need to keep a journal. I’m unique, so how can anyone else tell me what to keep in my journal. So what do I write in my journal? What does a nine-hundred pound gorilla write? I write whatever I want!”

期刊的作用

What the journal does

我推荐该期刊作为成为技术领导者的第一步，因为这是一个很小的承诺，没有理由不这样做。你也可以在领导力研讨会上了解自己，但一个好的研讨会通常需要你至少一周的时间，再加上旅行，可能要花费一千美元或更多。我总是发现一个好的工作室值得每一分钟和每一分钱，但如果你从未有过这种经验，你可能会犹豫是否要冒这样的风险。再说了，也不是每个车间都是好车间，就算拿回了钱，时间也白白浪费了。

I recommend the journal as a first step to becoming a technical leader because it is such a little commitment that there really is no valid excuse for not doing it. You can also learn about yourself in leadership workshops, but a good workshop will generally require at least a week of your time, plus travel, and might cost a thousand dollars or more. I’ve always found a good workshop to be worth every minute and every cent, but if you’ve never had the experience, you might be hesitant to take such a risk. Besides, not every workshop is a good workshop, and even if you get your money back, your time is lost forever.

仅仅在一本小书上涂鸦，你能学到什么？日记法的一大优点是它不像一本书或一场讲座，里面的一切都与你相关。因为每个人的学习都是个人的，我不能告诉你你会学到什么，但我可以保证你会学到一些东西。您可以将以下客户评价作为典型示例：

What can you learn from just doodling in a little book? One great advantage of the journal method is that unlike a book or a lecture, everything in it is relevant to you. Because each person’s learning is personal, I can’t tell you what you’ll learn, but I can guarantee you’ll learn something. You can take the following testimonials as typical examples:

Birgitt：“即使我没有学到任何其他东西，我发现我对写作的禁忌让我赢得了不记录我的程序和不回复用户来信的名声。我断定我不是不会写作的智障，而是因为四年级的一些不愉快经历，我真的很幼稚。某个领导！我要求我的公司送我参加技术写作课程，他们很高兴。现在，我又消除了成为解决问题领导者的障碍——这是我自己竖起的障碍。”

Birgitt: “Even if I had learned nothing else, I found out that my inhibitions abut writing had earned me a reputation for not documenting my programs and not answering letters from my users. I decided that I wasn’t a mental defective who couldn’t write, and that I was being really childish because of some unpleasant experiences in the fourth grade. Some leader! I asked my company to send me through a technical writing class and they were delighted. Now I’ve removed one more barrier to becoming a problem-solving leader—a barrier I’d erected myself.”

彼得：“那个月底回顾我的日记，我意识到我有一个一贯的模式，即以繁忙的日程为借口，不退后一步看看我在做什么。与我共事的一些人对我这个他们所谓的领导者不屑一顾。这本杂志让我有机会偶尔跳出最重要工作的陷阱，从不同的角度看待生活。我仍然看很多足球比赛，而且我已经将我的日记写作延长到整整三个月——到目前为止有两次即时重播（重读）。”

Peter: “Reviewing my journal at the end of that month, I realized that I had a consistent pattern of using my busy schedule as an excuse for not stepping back and looking at what I was doing. Some of the people I work with were getting short shrift from me, their supposed leader. The journal gave me a chance to get outside the trap of all-important work once in a while and see life in a different perspective. I still watch a lot of football, and I’ve extended my journal writing to the full three months—with two instant replays (rereadings) so far.”

Juan：“我保留的错误报告最终向我展示了我自己的行为模式，这种模式确实适得其反。在我最终放弃并向某人寻求帮助之前，我会浪费很多时间来寻找错误——此时我们通常会在几分钟内找到问题。我的猪脑袋妨碍了我做好工作。那个洞察力真的让我得到了坚持写日记的回报。”

Juan: “The bug reports I kept eventually showed me a pattern in my own behavior that was really counterproductive. I would waste many hours searching for a bug before I finally gave up and asked someone for help—at which point we usually found the problem in a few minutes. My pig-head was getting in the way of my doing a good job. That one insight really paid me back for keeping the journal.”

纳迪亚：“我以为我在写我自己，但过了一会儿，我发现我的大部分条目都是关于其他人的：查理让我在会议上看起来很糟糕；Greg 导致我的项目出现严重错误；玛丽搞砸了我的日历。我总是花费大量的精力来解决问题或避免下一个问题。该期刊帮助我减少了责备，人们说我更容易共事。”

Nadia: “I thought I was writing about myself, but after a while, I ‘taw that most of my entries were about other people: Charlie made me look bad in a meeting; Greg caused my project to go out with a serious error; Mary screwed up my calendar. I was always spending a lot of energy I could have spent solving the problem or avoiding the next one. The journal has helped make me less of a blamer, and people have remarked that I’m easier to work with.”

梅维斯：“这本杂志所做的最重要的一件事就是让我意识到在事情发生的时候我是多么认真对待每件事。一周后回顾我日记中的一个事件，我无法想象为什么我会为此如此激动。大多数时候，它实际上看起来很有趣。我想现在我更随和了。我知道我笑得更多，胃痛也更少。”

Mavis: “The biggest single thing the journal has done is make me realize how seriously I take everything, at the time it happens. Reviewing an event in my journal after a week, I can’t imagine why I got so worked up about it. Most of the time, it actually seems funny. I think that now I’m more easy-going. I know I laugh more and have fewer stomach pains.”

换句话说，这些记者中的每一个都了解了他们最需要了解自己的东西。我不知道你需要了解自己什么，而且很可能你也不知道。这不是不记日记的借口。事实上，这是保留一个的最佳理由。

In other words, each of these journalists learned what they most needed to learn about themselves. I don’t know what you need to learn about yourself, and chances are you don’t either. That’s no excuse for not keeping a journal. In fact, it’s the best reason to keep one.

问题

QUESTIONS

  1. 您已经想到了多少不开始学习日记的原因？
  2. How many reasons have you thought of already for not starting a learning journal?
  3. 如果你有一本日记或你过去写过的任何其他作品，把它拿出来复习一下。你首先注意到什么？它测量了哪些个人变化？你是因为改变了这么多而沮丧还是高兴，还是因为没有太大改变而沮丧或高兴？如果你对自己的过去没有任何记录，你不后悔你无法衡量你的进步吗？
  4. If you have a journal or any other writing you did in the past, take it out and review it. What do you notice first? What personal changes does it measure? Are you depressed or elated that you’ve changed so much, or are you depressed or elated that you haven’t changed very much? If you don’t have any records of your own past, aren’t you sorry you can’t measure your progress?
  5. 下周每天一次，按照以下方式完成一些熟悉的任务：当你完成任务的每一步时，对自己说（如果可能的话，大声说），“现在我正在做某某某事。” 例如，你可能会说，“现在我要打开抽屉拿一双袜子。现在我正在选择一种颜色。现在，我正在将一只蓝色袜子与其搭档配对。现在我要关上抽屉。现在我正靠近我的鞋子坐下来穿袜子。现在我要穿上我右边的袜子。” 您可以随时停下来问自己一些问题，例如“为什么我要先穿上右脚的袜子？”
  6. Once a day for the next week, do some familiar task in the following way: As you do each step of the task, say to yourself (out loud, if possible), “Now I’m doing such-and-such.” For instance, you might say, “Now I’m opening the drawer to get a pair of socks. Now I’m choosing a color. Now I’m matching the one blue sock with its partner. Now I’m closing the drawer. Now I’m sitting down close to my shoes to put on my socks. Now I’m putting on my right sock.” You may pause at any time to ask yourself questions such as, “Why am I putting on my right sock first?”
  7. 你早上先穿哪只鞋——右鞋还是左鞋？答应自己一份五美元的礼物，如果你明天能把另一件放在第一位的话。在早餐桌上放一张纸条，这样您穿上鞋子后，系统就会提醒您检查您的赌注。继续这样做，直到你成功。
  8. Which shoe—right or left—do you put on first in the morning? Promise yourself a five-dollar present if you can put the other one on first tomorrow. Put a note on the breakfast table so you’ll be reminded to check your bet after your shoes are on. Keep doing this until you succeed.
  9. 你的腿和脚现在在做什么？
  10. What are your legs and feet doing right now?
  11. 为来年设定一些个人发展目标。在你的日记中记下你在设定这个目标时的反应，并在你的日记中记下你朝着这个目标取得的进展。
  12. Set some personal development goal for yourself for the coming year. Note in your journal your reactions as you set this goal, and note your progress toward that goal in your journal.
  13. 至少读一本你崇拜的人的自传。在你的日记中记下那些让你特别惊讶的部分，以及那些最让你感动的部分。
  14. Read at least one autobiography of someone you admire. Note in your journal those parts that are particularly surprising to you, and those parts that move you most.
  15. 在阅读本书时，将这些问题的答案写在日记中。
  16. As you read this book, write the answers to these questions in your journal.

第 8 章 发展思想力量

Chapter 8. Developing Idea Power

当老师、父母、治疗师或其他促进者允许个人完全自由地表达符号时，创造力就会得到培养。这种宽容给予个人完全的自由去思考、成为他内心最内在的东西。它培养了开放性，以及感知、概念和意义的有趣和自发的杂耍，这是创造力的一部分。——卡尔·罗杰斯谈成为一个人

When a teacher, parent, therapist, or other facilitating person permits the individual a complete freedom of symbolic expression, creativity is fostered. This permissiveness gives the individual complete freedom to think, to be, whatever is most inward within himself. It fosters the openness, and the playful and spontaneous juggling of percepts, concepts, and meanings, which is part of creativity. —Carl Rogers On Becoming a Person

自我意识显然对各种领导风格都是必不可少的，但解决问题的领导是通过想法的领导，所以你还需要策略来帮助发展你的想法力量。

Self-awareness is obviously essential to all kinds of leadership styles, but problem-solving leadership is leadership through ideas, so you also need strategies to help develop your idea power.

一种策略是寻找机会练习你想学的东西——解决问题。试试这个，例如：

One strategy is to look for opportunities to practice what you’re trying to learn—to solve problems. Try this one, for instance:

一个人雇用一名工人做 7 天的工作，条件是每天结束时该工人会得到报酬。这个人有一根七英寸长的金条，工人每天必须得到恰好一英寸长的金条。在付钱给工人时，这个人只在酒吧里做了两次直切。他是怎么做到的？

A man hires a worker to do seven days of work on the condition that the worker will be paid at the end of each day. The man has a seven-inch bar of gold, and the worker must be paid exactly one inch of the gold bar each day. In paying the worker, the man makes only two straight cuts in the bar. How did he do it?

这个问题的“正确”答案应该是这个人把钢筋切成一英寸、两英寸和四英寸的长度。通过做出改变，他可以每天付给他一英寸的钱——一个非常聪明的解决方案。作为一个老二进制机器人，我马上就找到了这个解决方案。当我看到其他人为此苦苦挣扎时，我感到自己非常聪明。

The “right” answer to this problem is supposed to be that the man cuts the bar into lengths of one, two, and four inches. By making change, he can pay the man exactly one inch per day—a very clever solution. Being an old binary machine person, I found this solution right away. I felt awfully intelligent as I watched other people struggle with it.

解决问题领导者的中心信条

THE PROBLEM-SOLVING LEADER’S CENTRAL DOGMA

当几个人想出另一个解决方案时，我不再觉得自己那么聪明了。这个问题没有提到弯曲杆，所以他们让这个人把杆做成一个 S 字形。然后用两个切口，你正好得到七块，并做一个美元符号，启动。

I no longer felt so intelligent when several people came up with another solution. The problem says nothing about bending the bar, so they had the man form the bar into a figure S. Then with two cuts, you get exactly seven pieces, and make a dollar sign, to boot.

这个案例很典型。解决问题的领导者喜欢违反学术心理学的中心法则。无论我向他们提出什么问题，也无论我认为什么是“正确”的答案，他们总是想出更好的答案。可能是我太笨了，但我愿意相信有不同的解释。

This case is typical. Problem-solving leaders love to violate the central dogma of academic psychology. No matter which problems I offer them, and no matter what I believe is the “right” answer, they invariably come up with something better. It could be that I’m just stupid, but I’d like to believe there’s a different explanation.

我在解决问题的领导者身上的经验告诉我，他们中最优秀的人都遵循与学术心理学不同的中心教条，即

My experience with problem-solving leaders tells me that the best of them operate on a central dogma different from that of academic psychology, namely

任何真正的问题都有一个解决方案，但还没有人找到。

Any real problem has one more solution, which nobody has found—yet.

他们可能找不到，或者现在找不到。在目前的情况下可能不值得寻找。但它就在那里。

They may not be able to find it, or find it right now. It may not be worthwhile finding under the present circumstances. But it’s there.

为什么解决问题的领导者相信总有另一种解决方案？我认为这个问题可能是落后的。相信学术心理学中心教条的人从不尝试寻找额外的解决方案，因此他们自然很少找到它们。他们永远不会成为有效的问题解决者，因此他们当然也永远不会成为解决问题的领导者。创新者从未了解到只有一种解决方案，因此他们无论在哪里都能看到新的解决方案想法。

Why do problem-solving leaders have faith that there is always another solution? I think the question is probably backward. People who believe the central dogma of academic psychology never attempt to find additional solutions, so naturally they seldom find them. They never become effective problem solvers, so they certainly never become problem-solving leaders. The innovators are the ones who never learned that there was only one solution, and consequently they see new solution ideas wherever they look.

创意错误

CREATIVE ERRORS

对于没有被威胁/奖励模型蒙蔽的人来说，这个世界充满了创意。事实上，每一个错误都是一个新的想法，如果被一个准备使用它的头脑看到的话。贝克勒尔发现 X 射线是因为他不小心弄坏了一些胶片，但其他人在贝克勒尔之前就弄坏了胶片，却什么也没发现。弗洛伊德通过注意口误引发了一场心理学革命，但数以百万计的其他人都听说过口误，但对它们一无所知。从某种意义上说，唯一真正原创的想法来自错误。我从印刷错误中得到了很多想法。一旦我为“改变”输入“机会”，就触发了《通用系统思维导论》中的一整章. 还有一次，我遇到了一个为“交钥匙系统”编写“火鸡系统”的客户。这个错误给了我一个关于现成系统的危险的讲座的机会。

To anyone who isn’t blinded by the threat/reward model, the world is overflowing with ideas. In fact, every mistake is a new idea, if seen by a mind prepared to use it. Becquerel discovered x-rays because he accidentally spoiled some film, but others had spoiled film before Becquerel without discovering anything. Freud started a revolution in psychology by noting slips of the tongue, but millions of other people had heard slips of the tongue and made nothing whatsoever of them. In some sense, the only truly original ideas come from mistakes. I get many ideas from typographical errors. Once I typed “chance” for “change,” and triggered a whole chapter in An Introduction to General Systems Thinking. Another time, I caught a client writing “turkey system” for “turnkey system.” This mistake gave me the opening I needed for a lecture on the perils of off-the-shelf systems.

偷来的点子

STOLEN IDEAS

即便如此，创造性错误是相对罕见的创意来源。或者，也许我们很少利用我们的错误，因为一生的学校教育已经教会我们不惜一切代价避免错误。或者，也许我们懒得去查看错误，因为窃取新想法要容易得多。通过“窃取”，我既包括从一个人那里获取想法（称为“剽窃”），也包括从许多人那里获取想法（称为“研究”）。我特别喜欢用创造性的方法来做我的研究，这样其他人就可以完成所有的工作。

Even so, creative errors are relatively rare sources of ideas. Or perhaps we rarely take advantage of our errors because a lifetime of schooling has taught us to avoid error at all costs. Or perhaps we don’t bother looking at errors because it’s so much easier to steal new ideas. By “stealing,” I include both taking ideas from one person (which is called “plagiarizing”) or from many (which is called “research”). I especially like to use creative methods of doing my research, so that other people can do all the work.

例如，作为一名专栏作家，我有成千上万的读者都在为我研究好点子。偶尔，他们中的一个人读了一篇专栏文章后说，“嗯，这还不错，但我自己对这个主题的看法要好得多。我想我会写信给温伯格，告诉他他真正应该怎么想。” 当我第一次读到这样一封信时，它触动了我的 NPS 神经：“这个人怎么可能知道我这个伟大的‘作者’还不知道的事情？” 并非所有这些信件都像作者认为的那样出色，但事实证明，每封信都比我最初认为的更出色。如果我不知道我的 NPS，我就无法窃取这些想法，所以我必须提供我自己的所有材料，这将是很多额外的工作。

As a columnist, for example, I have thousands of readers all working on good ideas for me. Once in a while, one of them reads a column and says, “Well, that’s not bad, but my own thoughts on the subject are much better. I think I’ll write to Weinberg and tell him what he really should be thinking.” When I first read such a letter, it touches my NPS nerve: “How can this person possibly know something that I, the great ‘author, don’t already know?” Not all of these letters are as brilliant as their authors think they are, but every one of them has turned out to be more brilliant than I first thought they were. If I wasn’t aware of my NPS, I’d be unable to steal these ideas, so I’d have to supply all my own material, which would be a lot of extra work.

作为一名顾问，我得到了许多相同的好处。我拜访的每个客户似乎都急于告诉我他们正在付诸实践的所有新想法。在我只见过几个客户之后，我得到的轰动性想法比我在一年拜访其他客户时所能使用的还要多。此外，我不会为“窃取”他们的想法而感到内疚，因为我总是给他们许多其他的想法作为回报。如果我相信学术心理学的中心教条，我就不会进行这些交流。我会忙于证明我的想法是正确的，我什至从来没有听过他们的想法。

As a consultant, I get many of the same benefits. Each client I visit seems eager to tell me all the new ideas they’re putting into practice. After I’ve seen just a few clients, I’ve got more sensational ideas than I could use in a year of visiting other clients. Moreover, I don’t feel guilty about “stealing” their ideas, because I always give them many other ideas in return. If I believed the central dogma of academic psychology, I wouldn’t be making these exchanges. I’d be so busy battling to show that my idea was right, I’d never even hear their ideas.

为了不失去任何客户，我应该明确表示我不是在谈论“窃取”我客户的任何专有创意。我从不透露任何对我客户的意愿有丝毫怀疑的事情，但他们担心的想法通常不值得窃取。窃取的好点子通常是客户认为理所当然的“微不足道”的点子，或者甚至不归类为“点子”。就像植物一样，在一个环境中微不足道的想法在移植到更肥沃的土壤中时可能会绽放出重大突破。

Just so I won’t lose any clients, I should make it clear that I’m not talking about “stealing” any of my clients’ proprietary ideas. I never reveal anything about which there is the slightest doubt concerning my client’s wishes, but the ideas they’re worried about are usually not worth stealing. The good ideas to steal are usually “trivial” ideas that the client takes for granted, or doesn’t even classify as “ideas.” Like plants, ideas that are trivial in one environment may bloom into major breakthroughs when transplanted to more fertile soil.

损坏的偷来的想法

CORRUPTED STOLEN IDEAS

另一个对我有利的因素是我的误解能力。当我窃取一个想法时，我经常会引入一个错误，有时这个错误会变成最有创意和最有价值的部分。有时，我什至将改造后的偷来创意反馈给它的原主人，他现在发现它值了一大笔钱。

Another factor in my favor is my ability to misunderstand. When I steal an idea, I often introduce an error, and sometimes the error turns out to be the most creative and valuable part. Sometimes I even feed the transformed stolen idea back to its original owner, who now finds it worth a fortune.

例如，一组经理告诉我，他们计划使用他们的大型计算机为一些新的个人计算机编写程序。老实说，我以为他们说他们要用 PC 为大机器编译程序，至少要输入程序并执行一些操作员协助和错误检查。当我向同一家公司的另一个小组提到这种方法时，他们热情洋溢地决定他们也应该为每个程序员个人使用一台 PC。由于他们是非常糟糕的打字员，这种方法使他们摆脱了大型机器强加的瓶颈。

For example, one group of managers told me they were planning to use their large computer to compile programs for some new personal computers. I honestly thought they said they were going to use the PCs to compile programs for the big machine, at least to enter the programs and perform some operator assistance and error checking. When I mentioned that approach to another group in the same company, they went wild with enthusiasm and decided that they too should have a PC for each programmer’s personal use. Inasmuch as they were exceptionally poor typists, this approach freed them from the bottleneck imposed by the large machine.

最终，他们做得更好。我了解到该组织正在使用个人计算机在模拟终端上培训数据录入员。我天真地问他们为什么不使用相同的软件来训练他们的一些程序员成为更好的打字员。最终，我说服了他们使用这个想法，主要是通过指出这不是我的，而是他们的。一年后，我回来发现原来的团队偷回了他们腐败的想法，现在给每个程序员一台个人电脑，用于数据输入和培训。

Eventually, they did even better. I learned that the organization was using personal computers to train data-entry clerks on simulated terminals. I naively asked why they didn’t use the same software to train some of their programmers to be better typists. Eventually, I convinced them to use this idea, mostly by pointing out that it wasn’t mine, but theirs. A year later, I returned to discover that the original group had stolen back their corrupted idea and had now given a personal computer to each programmer, for data entry and for training.

交配

COPULATION

这个最新的例子也说明了交配的价值：将两个想法放在一起形成一个比其父母中的任何一个都更好的新想法。事实上，大多数好主意，就像大多数好人一样，都源于交配。你可能喜欢鸡蛋，你可能喜欢糖，但你只会喜欢美味的蛋白酥皮。事实上，我喜欢蛋白酥皮，尽管我讨厌鸡蛋。

This latest example also illustrates the value of copulation: putting together two ideas to form a new one that’s better than either of its parents. Actually, most good ideas, like most good people, originate from copulation. You may like eggs, and you may like sugar, but you’ll just love a good meringue. In fact, I love a meringue even though I detest eggs.

那些理解交配价值的领导者能够很好地解决因相信学术心理学的中心教条而产生的冲突。当一个团队的两个成员争论谁的想法更好时，机敏的领导者会试图找到第三种方法，一种是将另外两种方法结合起来的结果。

Those leaders who understand the value of copulation are well equipped to resolve conflicts that arise out of a belief in the central dogma of academic psychology. When two members of a group are arguing over whose idea is better, the alert leader tries to find a third way, one resulting from a coupling of the other two.

我的一个客户试图提高他们软件的质量，但在一次会议上，两个程序员为他们应该首先安装哪个系统而争论不休：技术审查系统还是正式的测试计划系统。他们的经理通过询问是否不可能对测试计划进行正式审查来平息整个争论。两位程序员都支持这个想法，他们都相信这是他们自己的想法。

One of my clients was trying to improve the quality of their software, but in a meeting, two of the programmers got into a fight over which they should install first: a system of technical reviews or a formal test planning system. Their manager defused the whole argument by asking if it wouldn’t be possible to conduct formal reviews of test plans. Both programmers got behind this idea, which they each believed was their own.

为什么想法看起来很邪恶

WHY IDEAS SEEM WICKED

错误、盗窃和交配是发展思想的三大策略，这并非偶然。它们的基本性质体现在它们在生命系统遗传学中所扮演的角色上，但我不需要详细说明你的父母是如何碰巧结合他们的遗传思想来产生你这个了不起的人的。可以说，这种结合过程被许多人视为整个基因周期中最令人愉快的部分。但遗憾的是，有些人实际上对这种想法的象征性表达感到不安。

It’s not an accident that error, theft, and copulation are the three great strategies for developing ideas. Their fundamental nature is shown by the role they play in the genetics of living systems, but I need not go into detail about how your mother and father happened to combine their genetic ideas to produce the wonderful person that is you. Suffice it to say that this combining process is regarded by many as the most pleasant part of the whole genetic cycle; but alas, some people are actually disturbed by the symbolic expression of this idea.

也许这种厌恶可以解释为什么我有时很难想出好点子。当我在学校的时候，威胁/奖励的人教会了我一连串我不能做的坏事，甚至不能谈论。排在榜首的是错误、盗窃和交配——这些正是产生创意的东西。

Perhaps this aversion explains why I sometimes have trouble coming up with good ideas. When I was in school, the threat/reward people taught me an entire litany of wicked things that I mustn’t do, or even speak about. High on this list were error, theft, and copulation—the very things that generate ideas.

当我上交作业进行评分时，我了解到错误会受到低分的惩罚。当我试图通过从教科书或其他学生那里复制 I 来避免错误时，我发现我只是提高了惩罚的规模。对于拼写或标点符号错误，我可能会失去百分之十的成绩。最坏的情况是，放学后我可能不得不留下来在黑板上写上一千遍正确答案。但是为了抄袭，我成了“骗子”，被派到校长那里。

When I turned in papers for grading, I learned that mistakes are punished with bad grades. When I tried to avoid mistakes by copying I from the textbook or from another student, I found that I had merely escalated the scale of penalties. For a spelling or punctuation error, I might lose ten percent of my grade. At worst, I might have to stay after school and write the correct answer on the blackboard a thousand times. But for copying, I became a “cheater” and was sent down to the principal.

如果我在交配时被抓到，或者甚至在读一本关于交配的书时，这种惩罚会让去校长办公室的旅程看起来像是去斯德哥尔摩的诺贝尔奖之旅。对于威胁/奖励人群来说，产生好想法的方法越有效，惩罚就越严厉。

If I’d been caught copulating, or even reading a book about copulating, the punishment would have made the trip to the principal’s office seem like a trip to Stockholm for the Nobel Prize. To the threat/reward crowd, the more effective the method for generating good ideas, the more severe the punishment.

不要误会我的意思：我不是那些不相信惩罚效果的胆小的自由主义者。惩罚是我们最有效的教学方法之一。它教导我们避免惩罚。因错误、盗窃和性交而反复受到惩罚的人不太可能产生伟大的想法。他们甚至不会认为自己有能力产生想法。

Don’t get me wrong: I’m not one of those lily-livered liberals who don’t believe in the efficacy of punishment. Punishment is one of our most effective teaching methods. It teaches us to avoid punishment. People who are repeatedly punished for error, theft, and copulation are unlikely to generate great ideas. They won’t even think they’re capable of generating ideas.

到这个时候，您可能已经注意到三大创意产生者与创新的三大障碍之间的关系。缺乏自我意识意味着我们永远不会注意到我们的错误，所以我们无法捕捉它们并将它们转化为美妙的东西。相信自己的大师级智慧意味着我们绝不会想到抄袭别人的作品，因此我们无法从创造性盗窃中获益。并且知道每个问题都有一个而且只有一个正确的解决方案使得组合想法看起来很愚蠢，因此排除了交配。

By this time, you may have noticed the relationship between the three great idea generators and the three great obstacles to innovation. Lack of self-awareness means that we never notice our errors, so we cannot capture them and convert them to something wonderful. Belief in our own master intelligence means that we would never think of copying the work of someone else, so we cannot benefit from creative theft. And knowing that there is one and only one right solution to every problem makes combining ideas seem foolish, so copulation is ruled out.

三种伟大的策略可以幽默地表述为错误、偷窃和交配，但学校教育告诉我们这些都是可怕的策略并没有什么好笑的。错误的解释是，学校教育可以教给我们一些教训，使我们无法成为有效的问题解决者。强调不犯错误可能会导致自我盲目，成为一种自我保护的策略。强调竞争，合作被贴上“作弊”的标签，可以创造出相信成为最聪明的人是可取的人，而强调测试可以灌输这样一种观念，即任何事情都有一个而且只有一个正确答案。

The three great strategies can be expressed humorously as error, theft, and copulation, but there’s nothing funny about schooling that teaches us that these are terrible strategies. Wrongly interpreted, schooling can teach us lessons that prevent us from being effective problem solvers. The emphasis on not making errors can create self- blindness as a tactic for self-protection. The emphasis on competition, where cooperation is labeled “cheating,” can create individuals who believe that it’s desirable to be the smartest, and the emphasis on tests can inculcate the idea that there is one and only one right answer to everything.

我知道我在学校犯了很多错误。我变成了一个盲目的小完美主义者，宁愿不承认自己的错误。我也时不时地作弊，直到今天这让我对建立在其他人的工作上过于敏感。不过，幸运的是，我非常害怕女孩，以至于我从来没有让老师有机会抓住我交配的机会。因此，我从不害怕将两个好的想法放在一起成为一个伟大的想法。这种策略使我摆脱了无聊的贫困生活。

I know that I made many errors in school. I developed into a blind little perfectionist who would rather not acknowledge his mistakes. I also cheated a bit now and then, which has to this day left me overly squeamish about building on the work of other people. To my good fortune, though, I was so afraid of girls that I never gave my teachers a chance to catch me copulating. Consequently, I never learned to fear putting two good ideas together to make one great one. That tactic has saved me from a life of boring poverty.

因此，我对任何想成为解决问题领导者的人的建议是：过一个干净健康的青春——或者至少不要被抓住和受到惩罚！如果你的青春已经虚度光阴，也许还有希望，正如下一章试图证明的那样。

So here’s my advice to anyone who would like to become a problem-solving leader: Live a clean and wholesome youth—or at least don’t get caught and punished! If your youth has already been ill-spent, there still may be hope, as the next chapter tries to prove.

问题

QUESTIONS

  1. 你犯过最大的错误是什么？你从中学到了多少？这种学习的成本与您教育其他部分的成本（例如参加课程或阅读书籍）相比如何？
  2. What’s the biggest mistake you ever made? How much did you learn from it? How does the cost of this learning compare with the cost of other parts of your education, such as attending courses or reading books?
  3. 领导或缺乏领导在你最大的错误中扮演了什么角色？什么样的培训或以前的经验会帮助你提高你在那个场合行使的领导素质？它会有什么不同？
  4. What part did leadership or absence of leadership play in your biggest mistake? What kind of training or previous experience would have helped you to increase the quality of leadership you exercised on that occasion? What difference would it have made?
  5. 您能否列出您在过去一个月中使用过的至少十种不同的新想法来源？你能再列出十个你现在没有利用的吗？
  6. Can you list at least ten different sources of new ideas that you have used in the past month? Can you list ten more that you’re not now taking advantage of?
  7. 你的大部分想法会怎样？大部分都实现了吗？有多少人死于缺乏动力？有多少人死于缺乏支持环境？
  8. What happens to most of your ideas? Are most of them implemented? How many die from lack of motivation? How many die from lack of a supporting environment?
  9. 你如何看待符号表达？例如，您对“荒谬”的建议有何反应，例如头脑风暴会议中有人提出您烧毁建筑物的想法？
  10. How do you feel about symbolic expression? What is your reaction, for example, to “ridiculous” suggestions, such as someone in a brainstorming session offering the idea that you burn down the building?
  11. 头脑风暴是一种结构化的方法，一群人可以通过这种方法提高他们的创意能力。如果你从来没有读过关于头脑风暴的书，那就买一本关于它的书来研究它。头脑风暴的实践如何遵循本章中表达的关于创意力量从何而来的想法？
  12. Brainstorming is a structured method by which a group of people increase their idea power. If you’ve never read about brainstorming, get a book about it and study it. How does the practice of brainstorming follow the ideas expressed in this chapter about where idea power comes from?
  13. 下次你开会的时候，把两个连续的想法放在一起，试着把它们组合成一个新的想法，对提出这些想法的人给予充分的信任。提出最初想法的人有何反应？
  14. Next time you’re in a meeting, take two consecutive ideas and try putting them together into a new idea, giving full credit to those who offered these ideas. How do the people who brought up the original ideas react?
  15. 列出您现在遇到的一些问题。现在把其中两个问题放在一起，这样每个问题都能解决另一个问题。例如，您可能超重，也没有时间锻炼；您可以通过在午餐时间散步很长一段时间来将它们放在一起。
  16. Make a list of some problems you’re having now. Now take two of those problems and put them together so that each solves the other. For example, you may be overweight and also not have time to exercise; you could put them together by spending your lunch hour taking a long walk.

第 9 章愿景

Chapter 9. The Vision

没有异象，人就灭亡。- 箴言 29:18

Where there is no vision, the people perish. - Proverbs 29:18

尽管想法在解决问题的领导力中占有核心地位，但成为领导者意味着将注意力从你的想法转移到他人的想法上。是什么让创新者想要鼓励或组织其他人进行创新？是什么让其他人愿意采纳领导者的想法？这些是关于您过渡到领导角色的基本问题，而答案需要您对自己的生活有长期的看法。

Although ideas have a central place in problem-solving leadership, becoming a leader means shifting the focus from your ideas to the ideas of others. What makes an innovator want to encourage or organize other people to be innovative? What makes others want to adopt the leader’s ideas as their own? These are essential questions about your transition to a leadership role, and the answers require perspective on your own life over a long period of time.

事业线

THE CAREER LINE

您的日记可以帮助您对自己有一个短期的看法，但有时很难从长远的角度看待自己。下面的练习可以帮助您了解整个职业生涯，从而获得这种观点。

Your journal can help you get a short-run perspective on yourself, but sometimes it’s hard to see yourself in the long term. The following exercise can help you gain that perspective by making a picture out of your entire career.

拿一大张新闻纸和一支记号笔。在纸张中间画一条水平线；这将代表时间，从你的职业生涯开始到现在。在左边缘附近画一条垂直线来代表你的感受，从顶部的高到底部的低。现在画一张代表你职业生涯线的图表，在你职业生涯的起起伏伏中移动。如果可能的话，在讲述你的职业故事时，在别人面前做。完成后，退后一步，查看整个图表并为其命名。然后，尝试将生命线延伸到未来。

Take a large sheet of newsprint and a marker pen. Draw a horizontal line across the middle of the sheet; this will represent time, running from the beginning of your career to the present day. Draw a vertical line near the left edge to represent your feelings, running from high at the top to low at the bottom. Now draw a graph representing your career line, moving through the ups and downs of your career. If possible, do it in front of someone else while you relate the story of your career. When you’re finished, stand back and look at the whole graph and give it a title. Then, try to extend the lifeline into the future.

在进一步阅读之前，您可能想勾勒出自己的职业生命线。

You may want to sketch your own career lifeline before you read further.

我们研究了数百名解决问题的领导者的职业路线。为了理解我们学到的一些知识，让我们从图 9.1 开始，它显示了计算机程序员托尼绘制的一条相当典型的职业线。以下是他在划定职业生涯线时所说的一些话：

We’ve studied the career lines of hundreds of problem-solving leaders. To understand some of what we’ve learned, let’s start by looking at Figure 9.1, which shows a fairly typical career line, drawn by Tony, a computer programmer. Here are some of the things he said as he drew his career line:

图 9.1。托尼的职业生命线。

Figure 9.1. Tony’s career lifeline.

(1) “在整个高中期间，我喜欢数学和物理，但并不太令人兴奋。到大四时，我已经修完了所有的高级课程，所以我只需要社会研究就可以摆脱困境。那一年真的很无聊，所以这条线一直延伸到这里。

(1) “Through high school, I enjoyed math and physics, but it wasn’t too exciting. By my senior year, I’d taken all the advanced courses, so I only had social studies requirements to get out of the way. That year was real boring, so the line’s way down to here.

(2) “在大学里，我参加了我的第一门编程课程，这让我走到了这里。我参加了所有提供的计算机科学课程，但是当我用尽了课程作业的挑战时，我开始逐渐减少。

(2) “In college, I took my first programming course, which shot me way up to here. I took every computer science course that was offered, but I started to taper off when I exhausted the challenge of my course work.

(3) “大三的时候，我在计算机中心找到一份兼职工作，帮助用户解决技术问题。我每天在计算中心度过大约 18 个小时，而且我还看到了招聘人员，这很令人兴奋。

(3) “In my junior year, I got a part-time job in the computer center helping the users solve technical problems. I spent about eighteen hours a day at the computing center, and I was also seeing recruiters, which was exciting.

(4) 我在一家消费电子公司从事编程工作，我以极大的热情开始了。但三年后，我开始相信，我注定要做比维护陈旧的 COBOL 程序更有意义的事情。

(4) I took a programming job with a consumer electronics company, and I started with tremendous enthusiasm. But after three years, I became convinced that I was meant for something more meaningful than maintaining decrepit COBOL programs.

(5) “我辞职了，在树林里游荡了几个月，然后我开始为一家保健食品公司开发会计系统。那是一个工作的好地方，我在那里认识了一个女人，我们认识三个星期后就结婚了。

(5) “I quit and wandered in the woods for a few months, then I went to work developing an accounting system for a health food company. It was a wonderful place to work, and I met a woman there whom I married after we knew each other for three weeks.

(6) “一年后，公司破产了，婚姻也破产了。那时候我真的是跌到谷底了，不过恢复的还挺快的。我知道我再也不能为一家我无法为我所信仰的事情做出贡献的公司工作了。

(6) “After a year, the company went broke, and so did the marriage. I truly hit bottom at that time, but I recovered pretty fast. I knew that I could never again work for a company where I couldn’t contribute to something I believed in.

(7) “我的下一份工作是为我现在工作的公司。他们是一家非常专业的机构，提供一流的在线服务，他们非常尊重我的能力。我在一年内获得了两次晋升，并且是我项目最关键部分的首席程序员。那是一段快乐的时光，有点像乌托邦，我没有让自己看到任何问题。

(7) “My next job was for the company I work for now. They’re a very professional outfit producing a top quality on-line service, and they treated me with real respect for my abilities. I got two promotions in a year, and was chief programmer on the most critical part of my project. It was a happy time, kind of utopian, where I didn’t let myself see any of the problems.

(8) “但事实是，从营销的角度来看，我正在从事的项目非常不稳定。就在我们拥有令我们感到非常自豪的东西时，管理层在一个星期五的下午晚些时候取消了该项目。所以它对我来说就像过山车一样。

(8) “But the fact was that the project I was working on was very shaky from a marketing point of view. Just when we had something we were very proud of, the management scrapped the project late one Friday afternoon. So it was back down the roller coaster for me.

(9) “这一次，只持续了一个周末。周一醒来时，我意识到我们的系统可以用于另一个应用程序，只需稍作修改。我和我的经理谈过，经过几周的努力，我成功地推销了这个想法。

(9) “This time, it lasted only over the weekend. On Monday when I woke up, I realized that our system could be used for another application, with a small modification. I talked to my manager, and after a couple of weeks of struggling, I managed to sell the idea.

(10) “这让我想到了现在。该项目的第一个版本已经发布，现在我们正在进行一些增强。我对我的工作比以往任何时候都更快乐，但我划定的界线更加摇摆不定，因为我现在注意到更多正在发生的事情。我的快乐不是来自盲目，而是来自面对和解决确实出现的问题的满足感。

(10) “That brings me to right now. The first version of the project has been shipped, and now we’re working on some enhancements. I’m happier with my work than I’ve ever been, but I’ve drawn the line wigglier because I now notice more what’s going on. My happiness doesn’t come so much from blindness, but from the satisfaction of facing and solving the problems that do come up.

(11) “虚线是未来。我已经要求离开这个项目并进行一些新的工作。我不知道我接下来要做什么，但我相信它会继续上升，乘坐更多的过山车，但我希望不是那么大。”

(11) “The dotted line is the future. I’ve asked to be taken off this project and put on something new. I don’t know what I’m going to be doing next, but I feel sure it will keep going up, with some more roller coaster rides, though I hope not such big ones.”

事件无关紧要

THE EVENTS DON’T MATTER

托尼将他的生命线命名为“过山车”，因此他惊讶地发现大多数职业都有起伏。许多人感到惊讶，因为在我们的文化中，我们很少谈论这些事情。至少男人不会。但是一旦你真正谈论它们，就会有很多惊喜。-

Tony named his lifeline “The Rollercoaster,” so he was surprised to find that most careers have ups and downs. Many people are surprised because in our culture, we don’t talk much about these things. At least men don’t. But once you do talk about them, there are lots of surprises. -

关于职业线的一个奇怪事实是，“不同人的生活中的相同生活事件与高峰和低谷都相关联。对托尼来说，破裂的婚姻是一件令人沮丧的事情，但对我来说，它让我摆脱了难以置信的负担。我能想到的唯一一个几乎普遍是严重的个人疾病。然而，对于包括我在内的许多人来说，这种疾病的严重抑郁标志着他们职业生涯中一个新的、更高的阶段的开始。其他深度下降也是如此：失去工作、考试不及格、搞砸项目。

One curious fact about career lines is that the same life events in ‘different people’s lives are associated with both peaks and valleys. For Tony the broken marriage was a depressing event, but for me it released me from an incredible burden. The only event I can think of that is almost universally a down is serious personal illness. Yet, for many people including myself, the deep depression of such an illness marks the beginning of a new and much higher period in their career. The same is true for other deep drops: losing a job, failing a test, messing up a project.

研究职业的第一个重要教训是：

The first big lesson from studying careers is this:

重要的不是事件，而是你对事件的反应。

It’s not the event that matters, but your reaction to the event.

成功会导致失败吗？

CAN SUCCESS BREED FAILURE?

低谷可能来自许多来源：例如外部麻烦，或者我们自己的缺点。但是，有时根本没有错；有时我们只是骑得太高了，成功改变了使我们成功的条件。在 Mercury 项目中，这对我来说是真实的：解决大问题让我只剩下无聊的日常工作要做。以下示例说明了出现相同现象的其他一些常见原因：

The dips can come from many sources: external troubles, for example, or our own shortcomings. Sometimes, though, there is nothing wrong at all; sometimes we just ride too high, and success changes the very conditions that made us successful. This was true for me in the Mercury project: Solving the big problems left me with only boring, routine work to do. The following examples illustrate some other common reasons for experiencing the same phenomenon:

对弗兰克来说，成功改变了他的信息系统。“在不到三年的时间里第三次升职，我督导了一百多人。然而，我感觉与外界脱节了。最终，我意识到我已经与以前所有的信息来源隔绝了——在维修区与人们悠闲地共进午餐、喝咖啡休息、在厕所或走廊里偶遇。我是个大人物，有那么多人依赖我，我不能有没有计划的时间。所以我开始在一些未编程的时间编程。和以前不一样了，但比几个月前好多了。”

For Frank, success changed his information system. “After being promoted for the third time in less than three years, I supervised more than a hundred people. Yet, I was feeling out of touch. Eventually, I realized that I’d been cut off from all my former sources of information—leisurely lunches with the people in the pits, coffee breaks, chance meetings in the john or in the corridor. I was such a big shot, with so many people depending on me, that I couldn’t be allowed to have unprogrammed time. So I began to program in some unprogrammed time. It’s not the same as the old days, but it’s better than it was a few months ago.”

对 Iris 来说，成功导致骄傲，而骄傲又导致防御。“我因为创建了一个操作照排机的系统而获得晋升。以前没有人做过这样的事，更不用说女人了，我为自己的成就感到由衷的自豪。但技术在排版业务中发展迅速，一年之内就出现了可以改进系统的新发展。当我小组中的一些人建议更换“我的”系统的一部分时，我对他们非常不满。当时，我认为我是完全理性的。直到很久以后，我才看穿我自己的骄傲造成的烟幕。在一年内，我从解决方案变成了问题。”

For Iris, success led to pride, which led to defensiveness. “I was promoted because of my work in creating a system to operate the phototypesetter. Nobody had ever done anything like that before, let alone a woman, and I was truly proud of my achievement. But technology moves fast in the typesetting business, and within a year there were new developments that could have improved the system. When some of the people in my group suggested replacing part of ‘my’ system, I came down pretty heavily on them. At the time, I thought I was being perfectly rational. It was only much later that I saw through the smokescreen my own pride had created. In one year, I had gone from being the solution to being the problem.”

Walston 的成功让他被诱惑在一个过时的系统上停留太久。“五年时间，我从一个没出息的大学生成长为系统程序员之王。我已经构建了这样一个程序和技术工具包，我可以让那个系统唱“华尔兹玛蒂尔达”并随着曲调跳舞。然后公司买了一个新系统，我被要求在一年的并行操作期间照管旧系统，最终延长到两年。显然，我是最有资格做这件事的人，而且公司给我提供了一份非常有吸引力的财务工作。此外，我真的不想放弃我的最高职位并使用新系统从底部开始。当旧系统最终进入废品场时，问题就来了。除了新系统的底部，我无处可去，其他人对我有两年的开始。我决定在运营中担任非技术职位。虽然薪水不错，但我不是特别喜欢它。”

Walston’s success let him be seduced into staying too long with an obsolete system. “Over a period of five years, I rose from an unwashed college kid to the king of systems programmers. I had built such a toolkit of programs and techniques, I could make that system sing ‘Waltzing Matilda’ and dance to the tune. Then the company bought a new system, and I was asked to tend the old one during a one-year period of parallel operation, which eventually stretched to two. Obviously, I was the best qualified to do it, and the company made me a very attractive financial offer. Besides, I didn’t really feel like giving up my top position and starting over at the bottom with a new system. The problem came when the old system finally went to the scrap yard. I had no place to go but the bottom of the new system, and everybody else had a two-year start on me. I decided to take a nontechnical position in operations. I don’t particularly like it, though the pay is good.”

研究职业生涯的第二个重要教训是：每个人都会失败，即使只是因为他们的成功导致他们失败。

The second big lesson from studying careers is this: Everybody has failures, if only because their success leads them to fail.

愿景的核心作用

THE CENTRAL ROLE OF THE VISION

许多人认为成功的人永远不会经历低谷，但生活对任何人来说都不是童话。美德不需要奖赏。智慧有时会产生错误。没有人每次都能成功。人们不会成为领导者，因为他们从不失败。他们之所以成为领导者，是因为他们对失败的反应方式。

Many people imagine that successful people never experience downs, but life is no fairy tale for anybody. Virtue need not be rewarded. Wisdom sometimes produces blunders. Nobody succeeds every time. People don’t become leaders because they never fail. They become leaders because of the way they respond to failure.

我所知道的成功领导者都有能力振作起来，并利用失败作为跳板获得新的成功。（也许“反弹”过于乐观；有时“爬回来”更贴切。）成为领导者的人不仅要克服逆境，还要将逆境转化为优势。

The successful leaders I’ve known have the ability to bounce back and use their defeats as springboards to new successes. (Perhaps “bounce back” is too optimistic; sometimes “crawl back” would be a better description.) People who become leaders are those who do not just overcome adversity, but turn it to their advantage.

他们是怎么做到的呢？经过 25 年的研究，我得出的结论是，成为创新者的人确实拥有一把秘密钥匙。那把钥匙是一种特殊的视野，一种结合了普通部分和特殊部分的视野。一部分是生活中普通的、普通的使命。任何政治家、传教士或邮政职员都可以有这种远见。然而，第二部分是特殊的，将愿景个性化，将其与领导者对伟大创意的痴迷联系起来。

How do they do it? After twenty-five years of studying the question, I’ve come to the conclusion that people who become innovators do indeed possess a secret key. That key is a special kind of vision, a vision that combines an ordinary part with a special part. One part is the ordinary, run-of-the-mill mission in life. Any politician, preacher, or postal clerk can have this sort of vision. The second part is special, however, in personalizing the vision, tying it to the leader’s obsession with the great idea.

换句话说，一定有值得做的事情，但也一定有只有我能贡献的独特部分。这是实现愿景的关键。加入群众运动可能会让我作为一个人继续前进，但它不会让我作为一个创新者继续前进。

In other words, there must be something worth doing, but it must also have a unique part that only I can contribute. That’s the key to achieving the vision. Joining a mass movement may keep me going as a person, but it won’t keep me going as an innovator.

凯西是一位典型的成功创新者，她描述了她的个人愿景：“我一直想帮助残疾人，因为我的兄弟天生失明。我看到计算机对盲人有巨大的潜力，因此我决定进入计算机科学领域。当我的代数教授告诉我我没有数学能力时，我知道她错了。我知道如果我要和盲人一起做计算机工作，我必须通过考试。第二次上课，跟另外一个教授，一下子就明白了。从那一刻起，我参加的每一门数学课程都得了 A。”

Kathy is a typical, successful innovator, who describes her personal vision: “I always wanted to help the handicapped because my brother was born blind. I could see that computers had terrific potential for the blind, and I determined to go into computer science. When my algebra professor told me I had no mathematical ability, I knew she was wrong. I knew that I had to pass if I was going to do computer work with blind people. The second time I took the course, with another professor, I suddenly got it. From that moment, I got an A in every math course I took.”

凯西可以将她的视力与特定情况联系起来，她的盲人兄弟。但只“知道”什么是值得的史蒂夫可能更典型：“没有人相信这个项目可以完成，但我知道我们可以做到。人们会问我怎么能如此肯定，因为以前从未建造过类似的东西，而我只是说我知道。我不确定为什么管理层一直为我们提供资金，但他们确实这样做了，而且这个系统最终让他们发了大财。然后人们问我是否不生气我已经完成了所有的工作并且公司赚了所有的钱，但我无法理解。在他们花完钱很久之后，我仍然知道我们会完成一些其他人无法完成的有价值的事情。”

Kathy can relate her vision to a particular circumstance, her blind brother. But Steve, who just “knows” what’s worthwhile, may be more typical: “Nobody believed that the project could be finished, but I knew we could do it. People would ask me how I could be so sure, since nothing like it had ever been built before, and I just said that I knew. I’m not sure why management kept funding us, but they did, and the system eventually made them a fortune. Then people asked me if I wasn’t angry that I had done all the work and the company had made all the money, but I couldn’t understand that. Long after they’ve spent the money, I’ll still know that we accomplished something worthwhile that nobody else could have done.”

凯西和史蒂夫，因为他们的个人愿景，以及事情会以某种方式变得更好的未来形象，得到了他们的主要想法的帮助。对他们来说，愿景可能比金钱、权力、声望或帮助特定的人更重要。我从职业生涯研究中学到的第三个重要教训是，每个成功的技术领导者都有这样的个人愿景。

Kathy and Steve, because of a personal vision, an image of a future when things will somehow be better, were helped along by their key ideas. The vision may be more important to them than money, or power, or prestige, or helping specific people. The third big lesson I’ve learned from studying careers is that every successful technical leader has such a personal vision.

为什么愿景造就了创新者

WHY THE VISION CREATES AN INNOVATOR

个人愿景贯穿明星表演者的生活。几乎在任何情况下，个人愿景都会提供一个参考点，一个区分重要与琐碎的规则。每个愿景都可以转化为一个问题，使之分离。“它有助于设计更好的操作系统吗？” “如果我要帮助盲人找到有用的工作，是否需要它？”

A personal vision permeates the lives of star performers. For almost any occasion, the personal vision gives a reference point, a rule for separating the essential from the trivial. Each vision can be translated into a question that makes the separation. “Does it contribute to designing better operating systems?” “Will it be required if I’m to help the blind find useful work?”

愿景使解决问题的领导者对质量产生了必要的痴迷。有了眼光，作品就重要了，是制作人的延伸。如果作品质量差，那么制作人要么漠不关心，要么低劣，要么两者兼而有之。在这种状态下，工人可能无法容忍批评，除非批评者能够证明批评与愿景的完美实现之间的联系。

The vision is what gives the problem-solving leader the necessary obsession with quality. With a vision, the work is important, and it is an extension of the producer. If the work is of poor quality, by extension the producer is either uncaring or inferior, or both. In such a state, the worker may not be able to tolerate criticism, unless the critic can demonstrate the connection between the criticism and the perfect realization of the vision.

例如，计算机程序员倾向于拒绝在他们的程序中发现错误，因为他们想要相信程序是完美的。当他们确信其他人最终会发现这些不完美时，抵抗就会变成热情的合作。事实上，愿景会影响与他人的所有互动。当被愿景驱动的人发现问题时，他们可能会对他们的队友说，“我感觉很糟糕，因为我们没有建立我们可以引以为豪的系统。我们该怎么做才不会有这种感觉？”

For instance, computer programmers tend to resist finding errors in their programs because they want to believe that the programs are perfect. When they become convinced that someone else will eventually find those imperfections, resistance turns to enthusiastic cooperation. In fact, the vision influences all interactions with other people. When people who are driven by a vision see something wrong, they might say to their teammates, “I feel bad because we’re not building the kind of system we can be proud of. What shall we do so we don’t feel this way?”

另一方面，当某人除了完成工作之外还有其他动机时——比如想要权力、金钱或声望——交易就会被扭曲。领导者很难说：“我很难过，因为如果这项工作没有完成，我将无法获得晋升和加薪。你们就不能努力工作，让我变得更富有吗？” 这种没有远见的人通常很聪明，会设法掩饰自己的真实动机，但很少有人被愚弄。研究许多职业教会了我第四课：

On the other hand, when someone has motives besides getting the job done—like wanting power, money, or prestige—the transaction is twisted. A leader can hardly say, “I feel bad because if this job doesn’t get done, I’ll get passed over for promotion and a raise. Couldn’t you people work harder so I can get richer?” Such visionless people are usually clever enough to try to conceal their true motives, but few people are fooled. Studying many careers has taught me a fourth lesson:

没有远见的人对其他人没有太大的影响。

People without vision don’t have much influence on other people.

愿景是会传染的。即使是被误导的愿景，如希特勒的愿景，也能将人们挡在他们的道路上。如果人们分享你的愿景，你们可以互相引导取得伟大的成就。对于人们想成为领导者的大多数其他原因，不能说同样的话。只有你妈妈在乎你是不是发了财或出名了。

Visions are contagious. Even misguided visions, like Hitler’s, can sweep people along in their path. If people share your vision, you can lead each other to great accomplishments. The same cannot be said for most of the other reasons people want to be leaders. Only your mother cares if you get rich or famous.

寻找自己的愿景

FINDING THE VISION IN YOURSELF

没有个人远见，任何领导技能或秘密都不会对你有任何好处。没有远见，在技术的前沿，人和项目都会灭亡。

Without a personal vision, no leadership skill or secret will do you any good. Without a vision, at the leading edge of technology, people and projects perish.

有人曾经问我，如果没有这样的远见，潜在的领导者会怎样。我无法回答，因为我从未见过这样的人。即使那些声称对未来前景愤世嫉俗，或声称只想要金钱或权力的人，也有远见，尽管隐藏在愤世嫉俗的保护壳下。在对金钱和权力的冷酷兴趣的外壳下，人们担心人们会嘲笑像更美好世界的愿景这样理想主义的东西。

Someone once asked me what happens to potential leaders who don’t have such a vision. I couldn’t answer, because I’ve never met such a person. Even those who claim to be cynical about prospects for the future, or claim to want only money or power, have a vision, albeit hidden away under the protective shell of cynicism. Under the crust of callous interest in money and power, there’s a fear that people will laugh at something as idealistic as a vision of a better world.

也许我可以看到这些人中的每一个人的远见，因为我研究了很多人的职业路线，但如果我自己从未去过那里，我将无法看到那些黑暗的地方。我看到我的项目失败了，我看到它们与我的预期目的背道而驰，给我带来了如此多的痛苦，以至于我无法超越自己的生存去思考，更不用说“创造一个更好的世界”了。世界。” 我浪费了很多年的生命，被对金钱和权力的追求席卷而来，试图掩饰我无法实现更高目标的恐惧。我知道这些愚蠢行为，但我不再羞于承认这一点，因为我了解到大多数人都知道这样的愚蠢行为。

Perhaps I can see the vision in each of these people because I’ve studied so many people’s career lines, but I wouldn’t be able to see into those dark places if I’d never been there myself. I’ve seen my projects fail, and I’ve seen them turned to the very opposite of the purpose I had intended, causing me so much pain that I couldn’t think beyond my own survival, let alone think of “making a better world.” I’ve squandered many years of my life being swept up by the quest for money and power, trying to conceal the fear that I couldn’t reach my higher goals. I’ve known these follies, but I’m no longer ashamed to admit it because I’ve learned that most people have known such follies.

我知道愿景在我心中，但我不知道它是如何实现的。精神分析师可能知道，但我不认为我想知道。它对我来说太重要了，而且我知道许多其他解决问题的领导者都有一种神圣的品质。在看电影时，我开始为任何关于一个聪明的年轻人的老生常谈的故事大喊大叫，他相信一个更美好的世界是可能的，并且战胜了所有持怀疑态度的成年人。

I know the vision is in me, but I don’t know how it got there. Psychoanalysts may know, but I don’t think I want to find out. It’s too important to me, and has a sacred quality that I know many other problem-solving leaders share. At the movies, I start bawling over any corny story of a bright young person who believes a better world is possible, and who triumphs over all the skeptical adults.

愤世嫉俗者可能会将解决问题的领导者称为“幼稚”，嘲笑他们的“天真”和“科幻小说心态”。也许只有孩子才会相信他们的所作所为会改变世界，但对我来说，这种品质不是“幼稚”而是“孩子气”。如果您认为这不会产生任何影响，您为什么要做任何事情？

Cynics may label problem-solving leaders as “childish,” scoffing at their “innocence” and “science fiction mentality.” Perhaps only children can believe that what they do makes a difference in the world, but to me that quality is not “childish” but “childlike.” Why would you do anything if you didn’t think it would make any difference?

而且，如果它真的没有任何影响，那么如果你相信它会产生影响，那么它也没有任何影响。

And, if it really doesn’t make any difference, then it doesn’t make any difference if you believe it makes a difference.

如果你缺乏这样的愿景，也许它只是放错了地方。在你过去的某个地方，你一定相信某件事很重要——你能做的某事会改变世界。寻找它，欢迎来到这个任务！

If you lack such a vision, perhaps it is merely misplaced. Somewhere in your past, you must have believed that something was important—that something you could do would make a difference in the world. Look for it, and welcome to the quest!

问题

QUESTIONS

  1. 您是否觉得与人一起工作就像在技术工作中一样自在？如果不是，你打算怎么办？
  2. Do you feel as comfortable working with people as you do in your technical work? If not, what do you intend to do about it?
  3. 您是否见过人们对自己的成功如此着迷以致于变得令人不愉快？你是他们中的一员吗？
  4. Have you ever seen people so carried away with their own success that they became unpleasant to be with? Were you one of them?
  5. 您的成功是否改变了最初促成成功的条件？如果你在你现在努力完成的事情上取得成功，会改变什么？你在做什么？
  6. Has your success ever changed the conditions that fostered the success in the first place? If you succeed at what you are now trying to accomplish, what will change? What are you doing about it?
  7. 你还记得你职业生涯中最糟糕的时候吗？最难的是什么？你是怎么摆脱它的？你从中学到了什么？如果你达到低点，你现在会做些什么不同的事情？
  8. Can you remember the worst time in your career? What was the hardest thing about it? How did you pull out of it? What did you learn from it? What would you do differently now if you reached a low point?
  9. 你现在遇到什么困难？你从他们身上学到了什么？
  10. What difficulties are you now experiencing? What are you learning from them?
  11. 当你与其他人打交道时，他们了解你的动机吗？你想让他们明白吗？你如何检查他们是否理解或不理解？
  12. When you deal with other people, do they understand your motives? Do you want them to understand? How do you check whether they do or don’t understand?
  13. 画出你自己的职业线。如果可能的话，在你画画和说话的时候与一个或多个朋友分享。确保将线路延伸到未来。如果是小说或电影，你会给它起什么名字？你想在电影版中扮演谁？
  14. Draw your own career line. If possible, share it with a friend or friends as you draw and talk. Be sure to extend the line into the future. If it were a novel or a movie, what title would you give it? Who would you like to play you in the movie version?

第三部分：动机

PART THREE: MOTIVATION

有些人成为领导者是为了实现他们的愿景，对许多人来说，他们的愿景是帮助他人。他们会向他们展示、告诉他们、强迫他们、哄骗他们或拖着他们，但不管怎样，他们都会帮助他们。即使是“为了他们好”，其他人也不会轻易被感动。

Some people become leaders to realize their vision, and for many, their vision is that they will help other people. They will show them, tell them, force them, coax them, or drag them, but one way or another, they will help them. Even when it’s “for their own good,” others aren’t easily moved.

在接下来的章节中，我们将了解影响他人的障碍，以及如何提高您的影响力。

In the following chapters, we’ll learn what gets in the way of inï¬‚uencing others, and what may be done to increase your inï¬‚uence.

第 10 章激励他人的第一个大障碍

Chapter 10. The First Great Obstacle to Motivating Others

O wad some Power the giftie 给我们

O wad some Power the giftie gie us

像别人看我们一样看我们自己！

To see oursels as ithers see us!

如果我们犯了一个错误，它就可以免除我们的后顾之忧，

It wad frae monie a blunder free us,

一个愚蠢的想法：

An’ foolish notion:

穿着和步态的风度让我们流连忘返，

What airs in dress an’ gait wad lea’e us,

一个'ev'n奉献！

An’ ev’n devotion!

——罗伯特·伯恩斯，《致一只虱子》

—Robert Burns, “To a Louse”

创新的第一个巨大障碍是自盲：看不到自己。激励的第一个大障碍是另一种盲目：无法像别人看你那样看自己。当罗伯特伯恩斯在教堂里看到一只虱子爬上一位虚荣的年轻女士的脖子时，他完美地表达了这个障碍：我们根本没有可靠的方法来预测其他人的反应。

The first great obstacle to innovation is self-blindness: the inability to see yourself. The first great obstacle to motivation is a different kind of blindness: the inability to see yourself as others see you. When Robert Burns saw a louse crawling up the neck of a vain young lady in church, he perfectly expressed this obstacle: We simply have no reliable way of anticipating the reactions of other people.

测试自己

TESTING YOURSELF

如果有一个简单的问题可以帮助您确定您激励他人的能力，那就太好了，就像这样：

It would be wonderful to have a simple question to help you determine your capacity for motivating others, something like this:

你能看出别人对你的看法吗？

Can you see how you look to other people?

一）是的。

a) Yes.

b) 没有。

b) No.

c) 有时。

c) Sometimes.

d) 我怎么可能知道？

d) How could I possibly know?

这种直接问题的问题是每个创新者都会回答是。即使你可以克服创新的第一个巨大障碍，看到自己的行为，但你仍然可能看不到它对别人来说有多么有趣。创新者需要对自己的行为保持一定的狭隘、利己主义和合理化。崭露头角的创新者需要专注于一个愿景，相信愿景比其他任何事情都更重要，并且忘记其他人的想法。因此，创新者往往没有意识到他们的行为对其他人产生的幽默效果。

The problem with such a direct question is that every innovator will answer yes. Even though you can overcome the first great obstacle to innovation and see your own behavior, you still may not be able to see how funny it is to others. Innovators require a certain narrowness, egoism, and rationalization about their own behavior. The budding innovator needs to concentrate on one vision, to believe that vision is more important than anything else, and to be oblivious to what other people say or think. Innovators, therefore, tend to be unaware of the humorous effect their actions have on other people.

顶尖的问题解决者往往相信他们每个人都在没有其他人帮助的情况下取得了成功。其他人是看不见的，或者即使被看见，也被视为障碍。但是，当明星个人试图成为领导者时，缺乏对他人反应的了解就成了头号障碍。欢迎其他人与天才一起工作，只要他们不妨碍自己，只限于欣赏和管理。

Top problem solvers tend to believe that they each have succeeded without the help of other people. Other people are invisible, or, if seen at all, are seen as obstacles. But when the individual star tries to become a leader, this lack of awareness of other people’s reactions becomes the number one obstacle. Other people are welcome to work with the genius, so long as they stay out of the way and confine themselves to admiration and administration.

这种类型的领导者更像是首席外科医生，而不是运动教练。团队中的人不是来自己解决问题的，而是作为简化外科医生自己工作的工具。他们中的一些人可能会被允许学习有关外科手术的有用知识，前提是他们这样做不会占用外科医生的时间或荣誉。充其量，这是一种师徒关系。在最坏的情况下，它是主/从。

This type of leader is more like a chief surgeon than an athletic coach. People on the team are not there to solve problems themselves, but to serve as tools to streamline the surgeon’s own work. It may be permissible for some of them to learn useful things about surgery, if they do it without stealing the surgeon’s time or glory. At best, this is a master/apprentice relationship. At worst, it is master/slave.

师傅不会理会徒弟和奴隶，除非他们干扰了师傅的工作。不仅如此，师父没有察觉，师父没有察觉。所有这些使得很难创建一个真正的诊断问题来了解你自己的行为对其他人的影响。

The master does not notice apprentices and slaves, unless they interfere with the master’s work. Not only that, the master does not notice that the master does not notice. All of this makes it rather difficult to create a truly diagnostic question about seeing the effect of your own behavior on other people.

幸运的是，有一个更可靠的测试问题：

Fortunately, there’s a much more reliable test question:

你愿意在别人面前装傻吗？

Are you willing to appear foolish in front of other people?

由于您无法可靠地了解您对其他人的影响，因此最好的策略是学会接受您有时会成为笑柄的事实。如果你不能容忍成为一个公众傻瓜，你就不会在一个你的所有行为都被你的崇拜者详细研究的角色中取得成功。问任何父母。

Since you can’t reliably know your effect on other people, the best strategy is to learn to accept that you’ll sometimes be the laughing stock. If you can’t tolerate being a public fool, you’re not going to succeed in a role where all your actions are studied in detail by your admirers. Ask any parent.

交互模型

AN INTERACTION MODEL

我希望我有能力把这个“礼物”送给别人，但我自己几乎没有。我所获得的是与我专业领域以外的人一起工作。我特别受益于家庭治疗师 Virginia Satir 教授的配对互动模型。我发现 Satir 的模型特别适合我作为计算机程序员的技术背景，因为它将看似复杂的过程分解为一系列简单的步骤。这种循序渐进的分析乍一看似乎很乏味，但它可以让您了解人们为什么会以这种方式对您做出反应。

I wish I had the power to grant this “giftie” to others, but I barely have any of it myself. What I have acquired is from working with people outside my areas of expertise. I especially benefited from a model of pair interactions taught by the family therapist Virginia Satir I find Satir’s model particularly appealing to my technical background as a computer programmer, because it breaks down a seemingly complex process into a series of simple steps. This step-by-step analysis may seem tedious at first, but it can give you the power to understand why people react to you the way they do.

让我们从 Yetta 和 Sam 之间的以下交互开始。虽然很简短，但 Yetta 的反应让 Sam 完全摸不着头脑。

Let’s start with the following interaction between Yetta and Sam. It was brief, but Yetta’s reaction entirely mystified Sam.

Yetta：“必须有人来准备咖啡。”

Yetta: “Someone has to set up the coffee.”

山姆：“我愿意这样做。”

Sam: “I’m willing to do it.”

Yetra：（愤怒地）“如果你对此有那种感觉，我会自己做！”

Yetra: (angrily) “If you’re going to feel that way about it, I’ll do it myself!”

这一刻，房间里的所有人都安静了下来。房间里的一个男人看着耶塔，扬起双眉。“你怎么了？” 他问。

At this moment, everyone in the room grew silent. One of the men in the room looked at Yetta and raised both eyebrows. “What’s happening for you?” he inquired.

Yetta 的眼睛蒙上了一层雾气。“我 。. . 我不知道，”她结结巴巴地说。“它刚从我身上冒出来。”

Yetta’s eyes misted over. “I . . . I don’t know,” she stumbled. “It just came out of me.”

当反应“刚刚出现”时，我们都有过这样的经历。当人们甚至不了解自己的反应时，我们怎么知道他们会如何回应我们？这种理解是通过检查在 Sam 说“我愿意做”和 Yetta 尖叫“如果你对此有这种感觉，我会自己做。”之间发生的事情来获得的。 ”

We’ve all had that experience, when a reaction “just came out.” How can we know how people will respond to us when they don’t even understand their own responses? This kind of understanding is acquired by examining what goes on between the time Sam says, “I’m willing to do it” and Yetta screams, “If you’re going to feel that way about it, I’ll do it myself.”

交互的显着部分

THE MANIFEST PART OF AN INTERACTION

在 Yetta 做出反应的那一瞬间，许多事情都在 Yetta 的脑海中浮现。在我和你的任何反馈互动中，都有一部分发生在我身上，一部分发生在你身上，还有一部分是显性的，一部分发生在我们双方之外，理论上可以被其他人观察到甚至录像。

In the fraction of a second it takes to respond, many things go on inside of Yetta’s head. In any feedback interaction between me and you, there is the part that takes place within me, the part that takes place within you, and the manifest part, the part that takes place outside of both of us and that theoretically could be observed by others and even videotaped.

在图 10.1 的反馈图中，清单部分由两条线表示：行为和反馈。它包括口头和非口头的互动。虽然我们经常没有注意到显性相互作用的某些部分，但它们至少在理论上都是可供研究的。它们也可供其他人观察和评论。

In the feedback diagram of Figure 10.1, the manifest part is represented by the two lines: the behavior and the feedback. It includes both verbal and nonverbal interactions. Though we often fail to pay attention to some parts of the manifest interaction, they are all at least theoretically available for study. They are also available for observation and comment by others.

图 10.1。交互模型。

Figure 10.1. A model of interaction.

有时我们会忘记明显部分和隐藏部分之间的区别。那就是当我们说诸如“我知道你想做什么”或“对不起我伤害了你的感情”之类的话时，真实的事情需要我们能够直接看到隐藏的部分。

Sometimes we forget the difference between the manifest and the hidden parts. That’s when we say things like, “I know what you’re trying to do” or “I’m sorry I hurt your feelings,” things that to be true would require the ability for us to see the hidden part directly.

Yetta 真的相信她知道 Sam 内心的感受，但她唯一能知道的方式是通过他们互动的明显部分的线索。如果 Yetta 学会观察，她就可以知道自己内心正在发生什么。

Yetta really believes she knew what Sam was feeling inside, but the only way she could have known was through clues from the manifest part of their interaction. Yetta could know about what’s going on inside herself, if she learns to look.

互动的隐藏部分

THE HIDDEN PARTS OF AN INTERACTION

与您互动后，我内心发生了什么？如果你想克服激励的第一个障碍，这些就是你需要了解的关于我的事情。每一次互动都可以做这样的事情

What does go on inside of me as a result of an interaction with you? These are the things you need to know about me if you are to overcome the first obstacle to motivation. Every interaction can do such things as

• 开启新的可能性

• open new possibilities

• 强化旧的可能性

• reinforce old possibilities

• 激活生存规则

• activate survival rules

• 增加攻击性

• increase assaultiveness

• 增加疼痛

• increase pain

由于这一切都发生在我的内心，所以你不知道，而且——对我们目前的目的来说更重要——甚至对我来说也是如此。你从我身上看到或听到的一切都是我内在过程的结果。同时，我意识到我的内在和外在的混合体，我并不总是意识到内在和外在。

Since all this goes on inside of me, it is unknown to you, and—more important for our present purposes—perhaps even to me. Everything that you see or hear coming out of me is the result of my inner processes. At the same time, I am aware of a mixture of what’s inside of me and what’s outside, and I’m not always aware of what’s inside and what’s outside.

有时，就像 Yetta 一样，我什至不知道自己说了什么，或者我的语气，或者我的手势。甚至我自己的思维也转得如此之快，以至于我经常没有注意到顺序，但我越了解我的内在顺序，我就越容易意识到你在明显的互动中看到的东西。

Sometimes, like Yetta, I’m not even aware of what I said, or my tone of voice, or my gestures. Even my own thinking goes by so fast that I frequently fail to notice the sequence, yet the more I’m aware of my inner sequence, the easier it is for me to be aware of what you’re seeing in the manifest interaction.

萨提亚的交互模型

SATIR’S INTERACTION MODEL

当我使用 Satir 的这种闪电般快速的内部过程模型时，我发现更容易看到我内心正在发生的事情。该模型有七个主要步骤，发生在你表现某事和我回应之间：

I find it easier to see what’s going on inside of me when I use Satir’s model of this lightning-fast inner process. The model has seven major steps that take place between the time you manifest something and I respond:

  1. 感觉输入
  2. sensory input
  3. 解释
  4. interpretation
  5. 感觉
  6. feeling
  7. 关于感觉的感觉
  8. feeling about the feeling
  9. 防御
  10. defense
  11. 评论规则
  12. rules for commenting
  13. 结果
  14. outcome

当你显化某些东西时，一切都开始了，我会对此做出回应。例如，Sam 表示，“我愿意这样做。” 然后事情开始在 Yetta 内部发生。

Everything starts when you are manifesting something, to which I will respond. For instance, Sam manifests, “I’m willing to do it.” Then things start happening inside Yetta.

第一步：感官输入

Step I: sensory input

Yetta 的内部序列从 Sam 的表现形式的感官输入开始。此输入并不完美，但包含“漏洞”。

Yetta’s inner sequence starts with sensory input from Sam’s Manifestation. This input is not perfect but contains “holes.”

Yetta 可能没有仔细注意 Sam 的话，她可能不理解他使用的某些术语，她可能错过了一个重要的手势，或者她可能没有注意到他语调或重音模式中的细微差别，例如“我是愿意去做。” 她可能会错过他想自己做的暗示。山姆无疑认为他的这部分信息是非常清楚的。

Yetta may not be paying careful attention to Sam’s words, she may not understand some term he uses, she may miss a significant gesture, or she may fail to catch a nuance in his tone of voice or stress pattern, such as “I’m willing to do it.” She may miss the implication that he wanted to do it himself. Sam undoubtedly thought that this part of his message was perfectly clear.

我们倾向于认为我们的信息是完全清楚的，但你通常可以假设在每次接收中都会丢失一些东西。当您在接收端时，这同样适用。您永远无法准确了解发送的内容。

We tend to think that our messages are perfectly clear, but you can generally assume something will be lost in every reception. The same applies when you are on the receiving end. You never get exactly what was sent.

所以这一步至少给了你两种方法来提高你理解人们对你的反应的能力：你可以培养你更准确地看和听的能力，你可以选择意识到某人可能没有反应的可能性你认为他们的反应是什么。

So this step gives you at least two ways of improving your ability to understand how people react to you: You can develop your ability to see and hear more accurately, and you can choose to be aware of the possibility that someone may not be reacting to what you think they’re reacting to.

第二步：解读

Step 2: interpretation

假设 Sam 发送消息 A，Yetta 接收消息 B。Yetta 的下一个内部步骤是根据她过去的经验解释B（不是 A，她没有），这可能与 Sam 过去的经验不同。

Let’s say that Sam sends message A and Yetta receives message B. Yetta’s next internal step is to interpret B (not A, which she doesn’t have) according to her past experience, which may be different from Sam’s past experience.

因此，例如，即使 Sam 说：“我愿意这样做”，而 Yetta 实际上听到了完全相同的话，她过去与 Sam 的经历可能会使她将此解释为信息 C：“我愿意去做吧，但我对此并不高兴，除非你强迫我，否则我不会真的去做。”

So, for example, even if Sam said, “I’m willing to do it,” and Yetta actually heard exactly the same thing, her past experience with Sam may cause her to interpret this as message C: “I’m willing to do it, but I’m not happy about it, and I won’t really do it unless you force me to.”

Yetta 可以做出这种过去的解释，即使她以前从未见过 Sam。她可能会想，“我前夫总是说他愿意做，但他从来没有做过，我总是被工作卡住。”

Yetta can make this kind of past interpretation even if she’s never met Sam before. She might think, “My ex-husband always said he was willing to do it, but he never did it, and I always got stuck with the job.”

或者她甚至可能不是在想一个特定的人，而是在想一个普遍的原则，比如，“男人总是这么说，只是为了让我喜欢他们”或“人们总是这么说，但他们讨厌我问。”
