Or she might not even be thinking of a specific person, but a general principle, such as, “Men always say that, just to try to make me like them” or “People always say that, but they resent me asking.”

对解释步骤的觉知会给你两种新的能力：第一，你知道不止一种解释是可能的；其次，即使您想到了多种解释，您也会意识到您的列表可能不包括发件人的解释。

Awareness of the interpretation step gives you two more new abilities: First, you know that more than one interpretation is possible; and second, even when you think of several interpretations, you can be aware that your list may not include the sender’s interpretation.

第三步：感觉

Step 3: feeling

此时，只过去了一小部分秒，但 Yetta 已经从 Sam 的明显行为 A 中移开了两步。她现在所做的不取决于 A，而是取决于 C，她赋予 B 的意义，这部分她观察到的A。

At this point, only a tiny fraction of a second has elapsed, but Yetta is already two steps removed from Sam’s manifest behavior, A. What she does now depends not on A, but on C, the meaning she attached to B, the part of A she observed.

基于她自己的安全需要，Yetta 对 C 有一种感觉。“是支持我还是反对我？” 她问自己。

Yetta reacts with a feeling about C, based on her own security needs. “Is it for me or against me?” she asks herself.

在这种情况下，她可能认为 Sam 不情愿的意愿意味着 D：“我担心你试图阻止我成为这个车间的领导者。”

In this case, she might think Sam’s reluctant willingness means D: “I’m afraid that you are trying to thwart me in my attempts to be a leader in this workshop.”

这一步似乎没有太多选择。一旦你确定了一种解释（C），这种感觉就会在你知道发生了什么之前出现。

There doesn’t seem to be a lot of choice in this step. Once you’ve fixed on an interpretation (C), the feeling just appears before you know what’s happening.

至少你可以选择意识到这种感觉，并将它与其他感觉区分开来。如果你知道自己是生气、受伤、兴奋还是害怕，并且知道其他人也会有这些感受，甚至是对你做出回应，这有助于让事情保持直截了当。

At least you have the choice of being aware of the feeling, and differentiating it from other feelings. It can help keep things straight if you know whether you’re angry or hurt or excited or scared, and to know that other people can have these feelings, even in response to you.

第四步：感受感受

Step 4: feeling about the feeling

Yetta 现在用 E 对 D 做出反应，这是一种关于感觉的感觉。在萨提尔看来，这是一个关键点，因为这种对感觉的感觉取决于 Yetta 的自我价值感。

Yetta now reacts to D with E, a feeling about the feeling. According to Satir, this is a crucial point, for this feeling-about-feeling depends on Yetta’s feeling of self-worth.

如果 Yetta 自我感觉良好，她可以接受她害怕 Sam 试图阻挠她的领导尝试。“我害怕，但这种情况下，害怕也是情理之中的事。” 如果 Yetta 总体上感觉虚弱和脆弱，这样的信息往往会吓到、伤害或激怒她。

If Yetta feels good about herself, she can accept that she is afraid of Sam trying to thwart her attempts at leadership. “I’m afraid, but it’s reasonable to be afraid in such a circumstance.” If Yetta’s feeling weak and vulnerable generally, such a message will tend to frighten, hurt, or anger her.

这种感觉 (E) 可能与Yetta 在生命早期获得的一些生存法则(F) 有关。例如，她可能有潜规则：“如果你表现出你害怕一个男人，他就会利用你。” 这条规则可能会让 Yetta 感到害怕害怕。

This feeling (E) is probably associated with some survival rule (F) that Yetta acquired early in life. For example, she might have the unspoken rule: “If you show you’re afraid of a man, he’ll take advantage of you.” This rule might lead to Yetta feeling afraid of being afraid.

另一个典型的生存规则可能是，“我必须坚强，永远不要害怕任何人。” 这条规则可能会导致 Yetta 为自己的恐惧感到羞耻。

Another typical survival rule might be, “I must be strong, and never be afraid of anyone.” This rule might lead to Yetta feeling ashamed of herself for being afraid.

了解生存法则会让你意识到人们通常会根据他们多年前的经历对你做出反应。这种理解并不会降低他们的反应的真实性，但确实会让你更容易有效地应对他们。

Knowing about survival rules makes you aware that people often respond to you based on experiences they had years ago. This under- standing doesn’t make their responses less real, but it does make it easier for you to deal with them effectively.

第五步：防御

Step 5: defense

如果 Yetta 的生存规则 (F) 说她有这种感觉是可以的，那么她会直接进入第 6 步并开始准备她但是如果她的生存规则 (F) 说她有这种感觉是不好的，然后她会进行一些防御。她可能会通过将问题放在其他地方来计划：“你让我生气，山姆。”

If Yetta’s survival rule (F) says it is okay for her to have this feeling, then she will simply move directly to step 6 and start preparing her But if her survival rule (F) says it’s not okay for her to have this feeling, then she will call some defense into play. She might project by putting the problem somewhere else: “You are making me angry, Sam.”

她可能会无视，例如改变话题：“你认为明天会下雨吗？

She might ignore, as by changing the subject: “Do you think it will rain tomorrow?

她可能会否认她真的有那种感觉：“我没有受伤。谁在乎你对我的领导力的看法？”

She might deny that she’s really having that feeling: “I’m not hurt. Who cares what you think about my leadership?”

她可能会进一步曲解她所听到的：“山姆说的并不是真的。”

She might distort what she heard even further: “Sam didn’t really mean what he said.”

显然，Yetta 在这里有很多选择。首先，她选择是否防御，如果她决定防御，她有多种防御选择。你可能很难相信任何人都需要保护自己免受你的伤害，但如果你想了解为什么你的激励尝试并不总是奏效，你最好相信这一点。

Obviously, Yetta has many choices here. First, she chooses whether to defend at all, and if she does decide to defend, she has many choices of what defense to use. It may be hard for you to believe that anyone needs to defend himself or herself against you, but you’d better believe it if you want to understand why your attempts at motivation don’t always work.

第六步：评论规则

Step 6: rules for commenting

无论她的选择是什么，Yetta 现在都产生了内部响应 (G)，这与 Sam 所说的相差几步。假设她内心的反应是：“山姆，你的抗拒态度让我很生气。”

Whatever her choice, Yetta has now produced an internal response (G), which is several steps removed from what Sam said. Suppose her inner response is, “You’re making me angry, Sam, with your resistant attitude.”

即使是现在，她也有选择，因为这仍然是一种内心的反应。Sam 不会听到 G，因为 Yetta 必须首先应用她的评论规则。

Even now, she has choices to make, for this is still an inner response. It’s not G that Sam will hear, because Yetta must first apply her rules for commenting.

她可能有一条规则说，“永远要有礼貌。” 这条规则可能会导致她将 G 转换为 H1：（微笑并咬牙切齿）“谢谢你的帮助，山姆。”

She might have a rule that says, “Always be polite.” This rule could lead her to transform G into H1: (smiling and gritting her teeth) “Thank you for being so helpful, Sam.”

她可能有一条规则说，“对男人要强硬。” 这可能会让她说 H2，“别以为你能把那些东西拉到我身上。不管你愿不愿意，你都会做的。”

She might have a rule that says, “Be forceful with men.” This might lead her to say H2, “Don’t think you can pull that stuff on me. You’ll do it whether you want to or not.”

另一条规则可能是，“永远不要明确提到愤怒，但不要直接强迫男人。” 在那种情况下，她可能会将 G 翻译成 H3：“如果你对此有那种感觉，我会自己做！”

Another rule might be, “Don’t ever mention anger explicitly, but don’t push men directly.” In that case, she might translate G into H3: “If you’re going to feel that way about it, I’ll do it myself!”

第 7 步：结果

Step 7: outcome

最后一步是实际结果。这包括 Yetta 的口头表达 H，但很可能会混入一些一路积累的残留物。例如，如果从表面上看，这些词听起来可能比伤害或愤怒更温和，但 Yetta 可能会让伤害她的语气和怒火从她的语气中泄露出来，她的手指透过她的手指，用一种略带指责的方式指着山姆。

The final step is the actual outcome. This includes Yetta’s verbal utterance H, but it’s likely that some residues that have accumulated along the way will be mixed in. For example, if taken at their face value, the words may sound more conciliatory than hurt or angry, but Yetta may let hurt leak through in her tone of voice and anger peek through in her finger, pointing at Sam in a slightly accusing manner.

Yetta 对这种泄漏有选择，但通常比她选择的词语更难练习。即便如此，她的行为也不全都属于泄密的范畴。Yetta 可以选择打山姆，或者不打他。她可以选择离开，也可以选择留下。她的动作越露骨，她的选择就越多。

Yetta has choices about this kind of leakage, but they’re generally much harder to exercise than her choice of words. Even so, not all her actions fall into the category of leakage. Yetta can choose to hit Sam, or not to hit him. She can choose to walk out, or to stay. The more explicit her movements, the more choices she has.

那些说“我不得不打他，我无法自拔”的人要么在自欺欺人，要么病得很重。您可能无法很好地控制自己的外表或声音，但可以轻松控制自己的行为。

People who say, “I had to hit him, I couldn’t help myself,” are either lying to themselves or very sick. You may not be in very good control of how you look or sound, but you can readily control what you do.

结果完成了 Yetta 的一个周期，因为它是一种外在表现，它开始了 Sam 的新周期。整个循环可能都发生在不到一秒钟的时间内。

The outcome completes one cycle for Yetta, and because it is an outward manifestation, it starts a new one for Sam. The cycle probably all happened in less than a second.

表面上，山姆诚恳地说：“我愿意。” Yetta 用受伤的语气和愤怒的手指回应道：“如果你对此有那种感觉，我会自己来！”

On the surface, Sam said, sincerely, “I’m willing to do it.” Yetta’s response, in a hurt tone and pointing an angry finger, was, “If you’re going to feel that way about it, I’ll do it myself!”

理解为什么通信会出错

UNDERSTANDING WHY COMMUNICATIONS GO AWRY

从 A 到 H 如此遥远，难怪人们很难理解彼此的反应？如果你检查许多这样的序列，你会发现 A 和 H 之间的转换如此混乱有五个主要原因：

With such a long way from A to H, is there any wonder that people have so much trouble understanding their reactions to each other? If you examine many such sequences, you’ll discover that there are five principal reasons why the transformations between A and H are so confusing:

感知：我们两个人甚至都不会以相同的方式感知明显的部分，因为我们是不同的人，因此有不同的感知。

Perception: Neither of us perceives even the manifest part in the same way, because we are different people and so have different perceptions.

错误的时间：转换是指过去或未来的事物，与现在的交流没有逻辑关系的事物。例如：“你曾经以某种方式行事”；“你可能不会履行那个承诺”；或者“很久以前，我无法对付生气的人，所以现在我也无法对付生气的人。”

Wrong time: The transformation refers to things in the past or future, things that have no logical bearing on the present communication. For example: “You used to behave in a certain way”; “You might not fulfill that promise”; or “A long time ago, I wasn’t able to deal with angry people, so I can’t deal with angry people now.”

错误的地方：转换指的是其他一些上下文。“当没有其他人在场的时候，你可以和我说这些私人的事情，这样当其他人在场的时候你可以说这些。” “你说你午饭前饿了，所以你现在肯定饿了，晚饭前。”

Wrong place: The transformation refers to some other context. “When nobody else is present, you can speak to me about these personal things, so you can speak about them when others are present.” “You said you were hungry before lunch, so you must be hungry now, before dinner.”

错的人：改造指的是另外一个人。“以前我调皮的时候，我妈妈总是对我指指点点，你也是女人。” “我哥老是为了钱骗我，你和我哥一样都是卷发。” “我的前三任老板从来没有兑现过对我的承诺，显然你不可信。”

Wrong person: The transformation refers to some other person. “My mother used to point her finger at me when I had been naughty, and you’re a woman, too.” “My brother always lies to me about money, and you have curly hair just like my brother.” “My previous three bosses never fulfilled their promises to me, so obviously you can’t be trusted.”

自我价值：我对自己的感觉对我的反应有很大的影响，但你无法直接了解我对自己的感觉，在这种情况下，难怪萨提亚估计这要么是普遍的，要么是此时此刻. 百分之九十的沟通都是不一致的，与我们真正想要沟通的内容不一致。不一致的沟通对动机是致命的，而动机取决于关于我们如何回应彼此的信息的自由和准确的流动。

Self-worth: My feelings about myself have a powerful influence on how I respond, but you have no direct access to how I feel about myself, Under the circumstances, it’s no wonder that Satir estimates that s either in general or right at this moment. ninety percent of all communications are incongruent, inconsistent with what we truly want to communicate. Incongruent communication is deadly to motivation, which depends on the free and accurate flow of information about how we respond to one another.

一种开始清除通信的方法

A WAY TO START CLEARING COMMUNICATIONS

弗吉尼亚·萨提亚 (Virginia Satir) 的模型有助于解释人类互动的一大悖论。如果我专注于他们的问题，人们对我的反应会更好，但是专注于他们的问题的一种方式是对我的问题坦诚相待。正如交互模型所表明的那样，他们的许多问题都源于试图与我打交道，试图理解为什么我在这个世界上做着我正在做的疯狂事情。他们不知道我的内部反应序列，除非通过我自己的坦率，所以关于我的一致陈述对他们很有帮助。

Virginia Satir’s model helps to explain one of the great paradoxes of human interaction. People respond better to me if I devote attention to their problems, but one way of devoting attention to their problems is by being candid about my problems. As the interaction model makes clear, many of their problems arise from trying to deal with me, to understand why in the world I’m doing the crazy things I’m doing. They have no knowledge of my internal response sequence except through my own candor, so congruent statements about me become helpful to them.

学生和客户经常提出困扰我的问题。如果我觉得自己很蠢，但我试图通过让他们觉得自己很蠢来掩盖事实，那么我并不是在给他们清晰可靠的信息。假设我只是很幽默地说，“哎呀，我很难弄清楚那个，我觉得在你面前看起来很笨有点尴尬。” 这是对我的内部响应顺序的相当清晰、直接的评论。

Students and clients often pose problems that stump me. If I’m feeling stupid but I try to hide the fact by making them feel stupid, I’m not giving them the gift of clear, reliable information. Suppose I simply say, with good humor, “Gee, I’m having a hard time figuring that one out, and I’m feeling somewhat embarrassed to look dumb in front of you.” This is a reasonably clear, straight comment about my internal response sequence.

使用这些信息，他们现在对我的立场有了更好的了解，并且他们也有了一个模型，说明当他们被难倒时如何作为领导者行事。这为他们打开了许多新的可能性，无论是作为领导者还是作为追随者，他们可以通过给我关于他们自己的内部反应序列或他们对我的反应的看法的准确信息来回报他们的礼物。

Using this information, they now have a better idea of where I stand, and they also have a model of how they can behave as leaders when they are stumped. This opens many new possibilities for them, both as leaders and as followers, a gift that they can reciprocate by giving me accurate information about their own internal response sequences, or about their perceptions of my response.

据我所知，你唯一能得到这份礼物的地方就是从别人那里得到的。如果你没有，他们可能不想麻烦给你，除非你先给他们一些东西。我的送礼技巧几乎可以简化为一个公式：告诉他们你的感受，你对你的感受的感受，如果可能的话，你对那种感受的感受。

As far as I know, the only place you can get this gift is from other people. If you don’t have it, they may not want to bother giving it to you, unless you give them something first. My gift-giving technique can almost be reduced to a formula: Tell them what you perceive, how you feel about what you perceive, and if possible how you feel about that feeling.

这是另一个典型的例子：“当我发现自己问了你三次你什么时候完成这个迟到的任务时，我为自己像个独裁者而不信任你感到羞愧，但我不知道如何处理我对任务的焦虑。项目进度。”

Here is another typical example: “When I find myself asking you three times when you will finish this late assignment, I feel ashamed about acting like a dictator and not trusting you, yet I don’t know how to deal with my anxiety over the project schedule.”

这些话很难对其他人说。实际上，你是在说，“我很脆弱，但我对自己和你有足够的信心来暴露我的脆弱性。” 通过展示你的弱点，你开始打开一个渠道，可以让你获得你需要的关于你自己的信息。

These are difficult things to say to other people. You are saying, in effect, “I am vulnerable, but I am confident enough in myself and in you to expose my vulnerability.” By showing your vulnerability, you begin to open up a channel that might get you the information you need about yourself.

风险是其他人可能会使用此渠道来攻击您。根据我的经验，这不是一个很大的风险，但在您尝试的前几次肯定会感觉非常巨大。它可能会帮助您了解您并不是通过暴露自己来真正暴露自己，因为您越是试图隐藏，其他人就越容易看出您的愚蠢。虱子爬上了一个女人的脖子，看起来更加糟糕，她认为自己是在用华丽的衣服来愚弄教区居民

The risk is that the other person might use this channel to attack you. In my experience, it’s no a great risk, but it definitely feels enormous the first few times you try it. It might help you to know that you’re not really exposing yourself by revealing yourself, because the more you try to hide, the more easily other people can see your foolishness. The louse looked all the more lousy creeping up the neck of a woman who thought she was fooling the parishioners by her fancy dress

我发现工作坊环境是学习以他人眼光看待自己的好地方。不过，像所有参与者一样，我需要一点勇气才能听到我最需要的信息——那些可能会让我从舒适的高原坠入深谷的信息。

I have found workshop settings a fine place to learn to see myself as others see me. Like all participants, though, I need a bit of courage to hear the messages I need the most—those that might drop me off my comfortable plateau into a ravine.

问题

QUESTIONS

  1. 回想一下你最近做过的一些愚蠢的事情。被发现后如何应对？随着你在职业生涯中的进步，你是否变得或多或少地具有防御性？你对你的防御行为做了什么？
  2. Think back to some foolish thing you’ve done recently. How did you respond when you were found out? Are you getting more or less defensive as you advance in your career? What are you doing about your defensive behavior?
  3. 回想一下最近一次您对交互感到困惑的情况。你是怎么参与的？
  4. Recall a recent time when you were puzzled by an interaction. How were you involved?
  5. 你最近做了什么让人们有机会了解他们与你的关系。例如，当您因另一个问题而分心时，您是否让他们知道您的思绪在别处？
  6. What have you done recently that gave people a chance to understand where they are in relationship to you. For example, when you were distracted by another problem, did you let them know that your mind was elsewhere?
  7. 你最近一次嘲笑自己是什么时候？您最近一次与嘲笑您的人一起大笑是什么时候？
  8. What was the most recent time you laughed at yourself? What was the most recent time you laughed with others who were laughing at you?
  9. 在典型的会议中，您会花多少时间观察自己？你组里的其他人？观察小组成员之间的互动？
  10. In a typical meeting, how much time do you spend observing yourself? others in your group? observing the interaction among members in the group?
  11. 想一想您经常与之互动的所有人。他们中的哪一个最有可能与您交换意见？是什么让你远离这样的安排？
  12. Think of all the people you frequently interact with. Which of them would be most likely to exchange observations with you? What’s keeping you from such an arrangement?
  13. 安排对自己进行录像，最好是在其他人对您作出反应而您对他们作出反应的自然环境中。至少观看录像带两次，特别注意那些你第一次没有看到的东西。如果可能，请查看磁带，直到您不再看到新事物为止。然后，将磁带放好六个月，再看一遍，看看你现在注意到了什么。
  14. Arrange to have yourself videotaped, preferably in some natural setting where other people respond to you and you respond to them. Watch the videotape at least twice, noting especially those things you didn’t see the first time. If possible, review the tape until you stop seeing new things. Then, put the tape away for six months and watch it again, to see what you notice now.
  15. 可能使用你在录像带上的一些互动，详细写下你的内部过程的步骤。尝试在同一个交互中与另一个人分享这个，直到你能解释整个清单交互。
  16. Possibly using some interaction you have on videotape, write down the steps of your internal process in detail. Try to share this with another person in the same interaction, until you can account for the entire manifest interaction.
  17. 使用您在某些互动中所说的文字记录，为每个句子写下句子中隐含的感知、时间、地点、人物和自我价值。你有没有看到你的任何特征模式？
  18. Using a transcript of what you said in some interaction, write down for each sentence what perception, time, place, persons, and self-worth were implicit in the sentence. Do you see any characteristic patterns of yours?

第 11 章激励他人的第二大障碍

Chapter 11. The Second Great Obstacle to Motivating Others

*当我负责一个有任务要做的团队，而任务的成功受到威胁时，我很可能

*When I’m in charge of a team that has a task to do, and the success of the task is threatened, I am likely to

  1. 把任务摆在人民面前
  2. put the task ahead of the people
  3. 以人为先
  4. put the people ahead of the task
  5. 平衡人员和任务
  6. balance the people and the task
  7. 逃离困境
  8. escape from the situation
  9. 以上都不是*
  10. none of the above*

前面的问题改编自一本管理学教科书。用简单的英语来说，它提出了这位熟悉的领导者的困境：一项任务必须以特定的结果或在特定的时间完成，否则将发生只有你知道的可怕事情。如果你要求每个人都加班或做任何其他必要的事情来完成任务，你就是把任务放在了人的前面。如果你与你的员工分享任务的需要，从而允许他们自己决定如何完成任务，甚至他们是否会完成任务，你就是在把人放在任务的前面。

The preceding question is adapted from a textbook on management. Put in plain English, it poses this familiar leader’s dilemma: A task must be completed with certain results or by a certain time, or else something dire, known only to you, will happen. If you require everybody to work overtime or do anything else necessary to complete the task, you are putting the task ahead of the people. If you share with your employees the need for the task, thereby allowing people to decide for themselves how, or even if they’ll accomplish the task, you are putting the people ahead of the task.

您可能会成为个人成功者，而不必面对这种困境。作为个人成就者，您是唯一参与的人，因此您必须平衡任务和您的需求，尽管您可能没有意识到自己正在这样做。在这种情况下，当你开始与他人合作时，冲突会让你感到震惊，你可能无法很好地处理它。

You may become an individual success without ever having to face this dilemma. As an individual achiever, you are the only person involved, so you necessarily balance the task and your needs, though you may be unaware that you are doing so. In that case when you start working with others, the conflict comes as a shock to you, and you may not handle it well.

事实上，您可能会不知所措，以至于您没有意识到实际上没有冲突。如果是这样，你就会有很好的陪伴。许多人，甚至一些教科书作者，都认为人与任务之间存在冲突。他们陷入了激励他人的第二大障碍。

In fact, you may be so overwhelmed that you fail to realize that there actually is no conflict. If so, you’ll be in good company. Many people, even some textbook authors, think there is a conflict between people and task. They are trapped by the second great obstacle to motivating others.

这是一个容易掉入的陷阱。在本章中，我将探讨一些人与任务相关的方式，从我必须在截止日期前独自完成的一项不愉快的任务开始。

It’s an easy trap to fall into. In this chapter, I’ll explore some ways in which people and task are related, starting with an unpleasant task I had to do alone, by a deadline.

##一项不愉快的任务

##AN UNPLEASANT TASK

当我结束为期一个月的旅行回来时，我看到了一大堆邮件。一个信封里装着一批提交给管理期刊发表的文章，还有一封编辑邀请我担任审稿人的来信。这些文章试图回答这个问题：哪些领导者更有效，是那些以人为本的领导者，还是那些把任务放在首位的领导者？虽然我对这个问题非常感兴趣，但论文很无聊，所以我把评论推迟到截止日期的早上。

Upon my return from a month-long trip, I faced a huge stack of mail. One envelope contained a batch of articles submitted for publication in a management journal, along with a letter from the editor asking me to act as a reviewer. The articles were trying to answer the question: Which leaders are more effective, those who put people first or those who put the task first? Although I was vitally interested in the question, the papers were boring, so I put off writing my reviews until the morning of the deadline day.

我起得太早了，一只老鼠在我的浴室里转来转去，这让我大吃一惊。她也让我吃惊。如果有一项任务比审阅枯燥的论文更让我不喜欢，那就是捉老鼠。所以我把猫贝弗莉叫来，把工作委托给她。我提着那叠文章退到我妻子的办公室。

I woke up so early that I surprised a mouse making her rounds of my bathroom. She surprised me, too. If there’s one task I like less than reviewing boring papers, it’s catching mice. So I fetched Beverly, the cat, and delegated the job to her. I withdrew to my wife’s office with the stack of articles.

面向任务的风格的教训

Lessons from a task-oriented style

第一篇文章的作者偏爱任务优先。我的任务是阅读它，这是我很难把它放在首位的任务。我的思绪不断飘回到鼠标和我的人身安全上。我发现自己读了又读同一个句子，无法理解它的意思。

The author of the first article favored task first. My task was reading it, a task I was having great difficulty putting first. My mind kept drifting back to the mouse and my personal safety. I found myself reading and rereading the same sentence, unable to grasp its meaning.

我突然想到那里有一个教训，所以我给自己写了一张纸条：

It occurred to me that there was a lesson there, somewhere, so I wrote myself a note:

*第一课：

*Lesson Number One:

关乎生存，唯有以人为本。 *

When survival is concerned, there’s no choice but to put people first.*

对自己的生命感到恐惧的人无法完成任何任务，除了致力于他们个人安全的任务。

People in fear for their lives can’t do any task well, except a task devoted to their personal safety.

我的部分问题是对贝弗利缺乏信心。她没有守在门口，而是爬到我的腿上，想要被抚摸。贝弗利不会捉老鼠，但老鼠不知道这一点，所以她是老鼠的好领袖。我注意到另一个关于动机的教训：

Part of my problem was lack of confidence in Beverly. Instead of guarding the door, she climbed into my lap and wanted to be stroked. Beverly can’t catch mice, but mice don’t know that, so she makes a good leader of mice. I noted another lesson about motivation:

*第二课：

*Lesson Number Two:

如果工作不是高度技术性的，领导者不需要有能力，但可以通过恐惧来领导。*

If the job isn’t highly technical, the leader need not be competent, but can lead by fear.*

当任务是将老鼠赶出我的房间时，贝弗利是一个很好的老鼠领袖，但如果任务是训练老鼠做杂技，他就会失败。

Beverly makes a good mouse leader when the task is to keep the mice out of my room, but would fail if the task was to train the mice to do acrobatics.

把贝弗利放回她可以更好地完成任务的地板上，我又回到了这篇文章，但我仍然无法集中注意力。为了寻找避免这项繁重任务的方法，我开始分析 X 的写作风格，而不是他的内容。当我意识到自己在做什么时，我写了第三张纸条：

Putting Beverly back on the floor where she could do her task better, I returned to the article, but I still couldn’t concentrate. Searching for some way of avoiding this onerous task, I proceeded to analyze X’s writing style, rather than his content. When I realized what I was doing, I wrote a third note:

*第三课：

*Lesson Number Three:

具有强大技术背景的人可以将任何任务转化为技术任务，

People with strong technical backgrounds can convert any task into a technical task,

从而避免他们不想做的工作。 *

thus avoiding work they don’t want to do.*

为了防止这种逃避任务的方法，领导者必须有足够的敏锐度来发现他们正在做的事情并阻止它。

To prevent this method of avoiding the task, leaders must have enough acumen to spot what they’re doing and stop it.

我用来逃避复习任务的技术技巧叫做“迷雾指数”。它根据平均长度和较长单词的出现频率来衡量写作难度。雾指数大约为 12 被认为是大多数技术写作的极限，但 X 始终超过 30。催眠效果比两颗安眠药，一杯温牛奶，再晚点晚点重跑还要好。

The technical trick I used to avoid my reviewing task is called the “Fog Index.”s It measures writing difficulty based on the average length and the frequency of longish words. A Fog Index of about twelve is considered to be the limit for most technical writing, but X consistently surpassed thirty. The soporific effect was better than two sleeping pills, a glass of warm milk, and a late-late rerun.

作家是领导者。作家的任务是引导您完成主题，因此写作风格可以被视为一种特殊的领导风格。我相信 X 的模糊风格表明他完全不关心我，他的读者；这是一个完全以任务为导向的领导者的风格。有了雾指数的证据，我决定放弃 X 的论文，并写下另一条注释：

Writers are leaders. A writer’s task is to lead you through the subject matter, so writing style can be considered a particular kind of leadership style. I believe X’s foggy style shows a total lack of concern for me, his reader; it was the style of a leader who is totally task-oriented. Armed with the evidence of the Fog Index, I decided to give X’s paper the bum’s rush and to write another note:

*第四课：

*Lesson Number Four:

不关心人民的领导者没有人可以领导，

Leaders who don’t care about people don’t have anyone to lead,

除非他们的追随者别无选择。 *

unless their followers don’t have a choice.*

敏锐的问题解决者通常有一个选择，很多选择，所以他们不会留下来被一个冷漠的领导虐待。

Sharp problem solvers usually have a choice, lots of choices, so they won’t stay around to be abused by an uncaring leader.

以人为本的风格更好吗？

Is a people-oriented style better?

作家 X 是不关心读者的任务导向型作者的完美例子。Y 的第二篇文章更容易阅读，雾指数约为 8。Y 真的很关心传达他的信息。问题是他无话可说。我会仁慈地为您提供 Y 的写作样本，就像我在两页后停下来对我自己仁慈一样。

Writer X was a perfect example of the task-oriented author who didn’t care about his readers. The second article, by Y, was a lot easier to read, with a Fog Index of around eight. Y really cared about getting his message across. The problem was he had nothing to say. I will be merciful and spare you a sample of Y’s writing, just as I was merciful to myself by stopping after two pages.

正如 Kenneth Boulding 曾经说过的，世界上有两种人：一种将世界上的一切都分为两种，另一种则不这样做。Y 是第一种，典型的分叉器。使用这种风格，他可以写出任何主题的卷——什么都不说。

As Kenneth Boulding once remarked, there are two kinds of people in the world: those who divide everything in the world into two kinds and those who don’t. Y is one of the first kind, your typical bifurcator. Using this style, he can write volumes on any subject—and say nothing.

“分叉”听起来像是性变态，也许确实如此。对我来说，这是一种扭曲的思想，让我厌恶地离开。这也让我写了另一个笔记：

“Bifurcation” may sound like a sexual perversion, and perhaps it is. To me, it’s a perversion of thought that turns me away in disgust. It also led me to write another note:

*第五课：

*Lesson Number Five:

再多的关怀也无法留住你的听众

No amount of caring for people will hold your audience

如果你没有什么可以提供，但假装你有。*

if you have nothing to offer but pretend you do.*

现在，谁的风格更差，X 还是 Y？个人认为Y更差。每当我读到 X 之类的东西时，我都会对作者产生不好的品味，但是从 Y 之类的东西中，我会对主题产生不好的品味。X 使我远离目前的工作，但 Y 使我反对进一步参与。对我来说，这是作家、教师、教练或任何其他领导者的终极罪过。

Now, whose style is worse, X or Y? Personally, I think Y is worse. Whenever I read something like X’s, I acquire a bad taste for the author,but from something like Y’s, I acquire a bad taste for the subject. X turns me away from the present work, but Y turns me against further participation. To me, that’s the ultimate sin of a writer, teacher, coach, or any other leader.

温伯格的目标

WEINBERG’S TARGET

当我写一本书、一篇论文或一部小说，或者举办研讨会时，我通过一个简单的测试来衡量我的成功：

When I write a book or an essay or a novel, or give a workshop, I measure my success by one simple test:

当我完成他们的工作时，他们是否不太关心这个主题？

When I’m finished with them, do they care less about the subject?

如果答案是肯定的，那我就失败了。如果不是，我就成功了，我很高兴。这就是 Weinberg 的目标，我所面向的任务。你觉得我的任务太微不足道了吗？回想一下你自己的教育经历：你学过的课程、读过的书、看过的电影。有多少人达到了温伯格目标？十分之一？从这个答案中，我得出另一个教训：

If the answer is yes, I’ve failed. If it’s no, I’ve succeeded and I’m happy for it. This is Weinberg’s Target, the task to which I am oriented. Do you think my task is too modest? Think back over your own educational experiences: the courses you’ve taken, the books you’ve read, the films you’ve watched. How many have met Weinberg’s Target? One in ten? From this answer, I derive another lesson:

*第六课：

*Lesson Number Six:

以任务为导向的领导者往往会高估自己的成就。 *

Task-oriented leaders tend to overestimate their own accomplishments.*

这是所有领导者的道德准则，无论他们是在教授一个 20 人的班级，指导一个四人团队，还是为数百万人的生活制定计划。

This is a moral for all leaders, whether they’re teaching a class of twenty, coaching a team of four, or laying plans for the lives of millions.

规划与未来

PLANNING AND THE FUTURE

当我写下那张纸条时，老鼠冲出浴室，从熟睡的贝弗利身边掠过。我突然想起了罗伯特伯恩斯，他在 200 年前遇到了另一只老鼠。当他用犁翻起她的巢穴时，他告诉她那些我们都从他的诗《致老鼠》中熟知的话：

As I wrote that note, the mouse darted out of the bathroom, past the sleeping Beverly. I suddenly thought about Robert Burns, who two hundred years ago came upon another mouse. When he turned up her nest with a plough, he told her those words we all know from his poem, “To a Mouse”:

*但是 Mousie，你不是你的车道，[不是一个人]

*But Mousie, thou art no thy lane, [not alone]

在证明先见之明可能是徒劳的，

In proving foresight may be vain,

老鼠和人的最佳计划

The best-laid schemes o’ mice an’ men

船尾敏捷，

Gang aft agley,

让我们只剩下悲伤和痛苦，

And lea’e us nought but grief an’ pain,

为了承诺的快乐！ *

For promis’d joy!*

领导者往往对其工作的积极社会影响过于乐观。相信他们的愿景向世界承诺了如此欢乐，他们不能让自己担心他们的领导对工人做了什么。根据我自己的经验，我发现工程师和计算机程序员都被这种乐观主义所感染，这种乐观主义推动了信息爆炸。不过，到最后，这个愿景似乎并不那么重要，留给我们的“只有悲伤和痛苦，为了应许的快乐”。即使在 200 年里，老鼠和人也没有太大改变。

Leaders tend to be overly optimistic about the positive social impact of their work. Believing that their vision promises such joy to the world, they can’t allow themselves to worry about what their leadership is doing to the workers. In my own experience, I find engineers and computer programmers are infected with this optimism, which drives that the information explosion. In the end, though, the vision doesn’t seem that important, leaving us “nought but grief an’ pain, For promis’d joyl” Even in two hundred years, mice and men haven’t changed that much.

很少有经典的领导力研究是在与技术工作情境有些相似的环境中进行的。许多是在军事情况下完成的，士兵们应该愿意为事业而死。对于一个程序员或者工程师，甚至对于一个学校的老师来说，这都有些不现实。按照这个想法得出自然的结论，我得出了另一个教训：

Very few of the classic leadership studies have been done in environments even vaguely resembling the technical work situation. Many have been done in military situations, where the soldiers were supposed to be willing to die for the cause. For a programmer or engineer, even for a school teacher, this is a bit unrealistic. Following this idea through to its natural conclusion, I derived another lesson:

*第七课：

*Lesson Number Seven:

我们所做的很少的工作真的如此重要以至于它证明了

Very little work we do is really so important that it justifies

牺牲从事这项工作的人未来的可能性。 *

sacrificing the future possibilities of the people doing the work.*

如果你不能在不剥削他人的情况下完成任务，也许你根本不应该这样做。

If you can’t do the task without exploiting people, perhaps you shouldn’t do it at all.

我们不能否认这枚硬币有两个方面。如果你把人放在工作之前，你可能会损害你的项目成功的机会；但是在项目被遗忘、从事其他项目并影响其他人的生活之后，人们将长期存在。

We can’t deny that there are two sides to this coin. If you put people ahead of the work, you may hurt your chances of succeeding with your project; but the people will be around long after the project has been forgotten, doing other projects and affecting the lives of other people.

话又说回来，您可能不会损害成功的机会。许多关于领导风格的研究都是针对日常工作进行的，比如流水线。也许任务至上的人对装配线的看法是正确的，但任务与人的分叉并不适用于复杂的技术工作。当事情没有按计划进行时，只有人的适应能力才能挽救行动。我已经吸取了数百次这个教训：

Then again, it’s just possible that you may not be hurting the chances for success. Many of the studies of leadership style have been done on routine work, like assembly lines. Possibly the task-first people are correct about assembly lines, but the bifurcation of task versus people doesn’t work for complex technical work. When things don’t go according to plan, only the adaptability of people can rescue the operation. I’ve learned this lesson hundreds of times:

*第八课：

*Lesson Number Eight:

当工作复杂时，没有领导可以

When the work is complex, no leader can be

绝对确定计划不会“一团糟”。 *

absolutely sure that plans won’t “gang aft agley.”*

在复杂的环境中，即使是最注重任务的领导者也不得不以人为本，否则任务将无法完成。

In a complex environment, even the most task-oriented leader is forced to put people first, or the task won’t get done.

第二大障碍

THE SECOND GREAT OBSTACLE

所有这些教训让我相信，在谈论技术领导力时，对本章开头提出的问题的最佳答案是 (d) 以上都不是。当任务很复杂时，我无法在人和任务之间做出选择，因为那时我根本无法将人和工作分开。

All of these lessons led me to believe that when talking about technical leadership, the best answer to the question posed at the beginning of the chapter is (d) none of the above. When the task is complex, I can’t choose between people and task, because then I simply cannot separate people and work.

每当有一项任务，甚至是一项个人任务时，都会有人参与。我们不为抽象的利润工作；我们工作是为了某些人的利益。我们不是为了和平而工作；我们努力使某些人能够享受和平生活的好处。参与我们任务的人可能是我们的客户、我们的经理、我们的选民或我们的董事会，但即使他们不直接可见，他们也是人。

Whenever there is a task, even an individual task, there are people involved. We don’t work for abstract profits; we work to make profits for certain people.We don’t work for peace; we work so that certain people can enjoy the benefits of living in peace. The people involved in our task may be our customers, or our managers, or our constituents, or our board of directors, but even though they are not directly visible, they are people.

如果我们相信选择是在人和任务之间进行的，那么我们就是在否认或扭曲真正的选择：在一组人和另一组人之间。我们有时会做出这种否认，因为这比面对不同群体之间的真正选择要容易。说“我们不能实施你的想法，因为我们必须控制在预算范围内”比说“我们不能实施你的想法，因为我们的股东对他们的股息不满意”要容易得多。

If we believe that the choice is between people and task, we are denying or distorting the real choice: between one group of people and another. We sometimes make that denial because it’s easier than facing the true choice between groups. How much easier it is to say, “We can’t implement your idea, because we have to stay within budget” than “We can’t implement your idea, because our stockholders are dissatisfied with their dividends.”

不幸的是，这种扭曲剥夺了人们解决问题的能力，剥夺了他们正确定义问题的可能性。所以，如果你否认工作背后的人性现实，你永远不会成为一个非常成功的解决问题的领导者。这确实是另一堂课，也是最重要的课之一：

Unfortunately, this kind of distortion usurps people’s problem- solving power by denying them the possibility of a correct definition of their problem. So, if you deny the human reality behind your work, you’ll never be a very successful problem-solving leader. Which is really another lesson, one of the most important:

*第九课：

*Lesson Number Nine:

要成为成功的解决问题的领导者，

To be a successful problem-solving leader,

你必须把每个人的人性放在首位。*

you must keep everybody’s humanness at the forefront.*

对任务/人员二分法的信念是成为成功激励者的第二大障碍，因为它假装任务与人一样真实，而不是源于人。你可能偶尔会用这种错误的二分法来激励人们，但最终他们会看穿它，即使你看不出来。一旦他们意识到你一直在愚弄他们，你就不再是一个激励者。

The belief in the task/people dichotomy is the second great obstacle to being a successful motivator because it pretends that tasks are as real as people, rather than deriving from people. You may motivate people once in a while with that false dichotomy, but eventually they will see through it, even if you don’t. Once they realize you’ve been fooling them, you’re finished as a motivator.

作为一个人的领导者

THE LEADER AS A PERSON

到了这个时候，我发现我已经有足够的功课来写一篇自己的文章了，所以我决定不再否认自己的人性，放弃复习，照顾好自己的需要。我查阅了弗吉尼亚·萨提亚 (Virginia Satir) 对领导者人性的看法。她正在为治疗师写作，但你可以用“领导者”这个词代替“治疗师”：

By this time, I noticed that I had enough lessons to write an article of my own, so I decided to stop denying my humanness, drop the reviewing, and take care of my own needs. I looked up what Virginia Satir had to say about the humanness of leaders. She was writing for therapists, but you can substitute the word “leader” for “therapist”:

*把自己当作治疗师是一项了不起的任务。为了胜任这项任务，一个人需要继续发展自己的人性和成熟度。我们正在处理人们的生活。在我看来，学习成为一名治疗师不同于学习成为一名水管工。管道工通常可以满足于技术。治疗师需要做更多。您不必爱上管道即可修复它。无论我们属于什么技术、哲学或学派，无论我们实际与他人做什么，都必须通过我们自己作为人来传递。

*Using oneself as a therapist is an awesome task. To be equal to that task, one needs to continue to develop one’s humanness and maturity. We are dealing with people’s lives. In my mind, learning to be a therapist is not like learning to be a plumber. Plumbers can usually settle for techniques. Therapists need to do more. You don’t have to love a pipe to fix it. Whatever techniques, philosophy or school we belong to, whatever we actually do with others has to be funneled through ourselves as people.

在我的教学中，我深入关注治疗师的人格。我们是与人打交道的人。我们需要能够理解和爱自己，能够看、听、摸和理解我们所看到的。我们需要能够创造条件，让我们可以被观看、聆听、触摸和理解。*（Satir 和 M. Baldwin，Satir：Step by Step（Palo Alto，CA：Science and Behavior Books，1983 年） , 第 227-28 页

In my teaching, I focus in depth on the personhood of the therapist. We are people dealing with people. We need to be able to under- stand and love ourselves, to be able to look, listen, touch and understand those we see. We need to be able to create the conditions by which we can be looked at, listened to, touched and understood.* (Satir and M. Baldwin, Satir: Step by Step (Palo Alto, CA: Science and Behavior Books, 1983), pp.227-28

不知何故，这段话让我意识到那些文章另一端的人，正焦急地等待着他们的评论。我决定再试一次审查任务。

Somehow this passage made me aware of the people on the other end of those articles, waiting anxiously for their reviews. I decided to give the reviewing task one more try.

我重读了文章随附的求职信，编辑在信中解释说作者和我都是匿名的，这样“评论才能准确和公正”。我突然想到，这个设备确保我们不能看、听、摸或理解对方——在将人与任务分开的幌子下，将人与人完全分开。任务是什么？在编辑看来，我是要评判报纸，看看哪些是“赢家”，哪些是“输家”。

I reread the cover letter that came with the articles, in which the editor explained that the authors and I were being kept anonymous so “the reviews can be accurate and impartial.” It suddenly struck me that this device ensured that we could not look, listen, touch, or understand each other—a complete separation of people from people under the guise of separating people from task. What was the task? In the editor’s view, I was to judge the papers, to see which were the “winners” and which were the “losers.”

这种匿名审查源于人类互动的威胁/奖励模型。作者应该非常报复，如果我给他们一个不利的评论，他们会试图以某种方式伤害我；我应该非常害怕可能的反响，除非我是匿名的，否则我不能说实话；而且我太有偏见了，如果我知道作者是谁，我就不能给出诚实的评论。

Such anonymous reviewing grows out of the threat/reward model of human interaction. The authors are supposed to be so vindictive that they will try to damage me in some way if I give them an unfavorable review; I’m supposed to be so afraid of possible repercussions that I can’t be honest unless I am anonymous; and I’m so biased that I can’t give an honest review if I know who the authors are.

我很快意识到我遇到了麻烦，因为我无法接受问题的这种威胁/奖励定义。我没有继续玩猫捉老鼠的游戏，而是写了一封信给编辑，告诉他在这种情况下我不可能做好我的工作。如果作者需要我的帮助来改进他们的论文，我主动提出与他们进行私下交流。其中两人接受了我的提议。他们发表了两篇改进后的论文，我得到了两位感激的同事以及一个进一步的教训：

I quickly realized that I had been having trouble because I couldn’t accept this threat/reward definition of the problem. Rather than continuing to play cat-and-mouse, I wrote a letter to the editor telling him that it was impossible for me to do my job under these circumstances. I offered to communicate personally with the authors if they wanted my help in making their papers better. Two of them accepted my offer. They produced two improved papers, and I gained two grateful colleagues as well as one further lesson:

*第十课：

*Lesson Number Ten:

如果您是领导者，那么人民就是您的工作。

If you are a leader, the people are your work.

没有其他工作值得做。 *

There is no other work worth doing.*

还有一件事：老鼠也巧妙地逃脱了。

One more thing: The mouse also made a clever escape.

问题

QUESTIONS

  1. 你最后一次试图通过恐惧来领导是什么时候？发生了什么？
  2. When was the last time you tried to lead by fear? What happened?
  3. 您最后一次尝试将某项任务转换为您更有能力完成的技术任务是什么时候？发生了什么？
  4. When was the last time you tried to convert some task to a technical task of a type you were better equipped to do? What happened?
  5. 你有没有选择过你现在后悔的任务而不是人？您是否曾经选择过人而不是任务而现在后悔了？
  6. Have you ever made a choice of task over person that you now regret? Have you ever made a choice of person over task that you now regret?
  7. 与您合作后，人们有何变化？
  8. How have people changed after they’ve worked with you?
  9. 人们为你工作后有何变化？
  10. How have people changed after they’ve worked for you?
  11. 你通常会逃避什么类型的情况？你典型的逃避模式是什么？
  12. What type of situation do you typically escape from? What is your typical escape pattern?
  13. 一个像你那样行事的人会激励你吗？
  14. Would you be motivated by a person who acts the way you do?

第十二章帮助他人的问题

Chapter 12. The Problem of Helping Others

*人们不能为别人做他们想做的一切，这似乎是一个公认的生活事实；尽管如此，对于许多辅导员来说，接受并在他们自己的活动中发挥作用是一个非常困难的现实。

*That people cannot do everything they want to do for others seems to be a well-established fact of life; it is nonetheless a very difficult reality for many counselors to accept and make operational in their own activities.

为了他们自己的利益，辅导员需要对他们在帮助客户时可以取得的成就做出简单而可靠的估计。 *

Counselors need to develop, for their own good, simple and reliable estimates of what they can accomplish in trying to help their clients.*

——尤金·肯尼迪，关于成为一名顾问

—Eugene Kennedy, On Becoming a Counselor

有机模型认为，领导力是创造一个赋予人们权力的环境的过程。这是一个吸引人的定义，因为大多数人都想帮助别人，而创造一个赋权的环境是帮助别人的一种方式。然而，创建有用的环境并非易事，在本章中，我们将探讨其中的一些原因。

The organic model says that leadership is the process of creating an environment in which people are empowered. This is an appealing definition because most people want to help people, and creating an empowering environment is one way of helping people. Creating helpful environments, however, is no easy task and in this chapter, we’ll examine some of the reasons why.

帮助应该是自然的

HELP SHOULD BE NATURAL

我们已经看到解决问题的领导风格是如何与过程相关的，即做事的方式。在我们的社会中，工作的内容应该是困难的，并且是专为专家保留的。另一方面，这个过程是每个人都应该知道的。聘用教师的依据是他们对学科知识的了解，而不是他们的课堂技能。编程团队负责人根据他们的技术技能得到晋升，并假设任何正常人都能在此过程中掌握领导技能。

We’ve seen how the problem-solving leadership style is concerned with process, the way things are done. In our society, the content of work is supposed to be difficult and reserved for specialists. The process, on the other hand, is something that everyone should know. Teachers are hired on the basis of their knowledge of subject matter rather than their classroom skills. Programming team leaders are promoted according to their technical skills, with the assumption that any normal person will be able to pick up leadership skills along the way.

这个神话一直存在是因为偶尔有人似乎不费吹灰之力就掌握了流程技能。正如有些人未经直接培训就学会了计算机编程一样，其他人似乎总是知道如何提供最大帮助。这对那些人来说很棒，但这是否使他们比必须通过有意识的努力学习的人更好？威胁/奖励模型导致我们做出这样的判断，因此我们为需要学习的努力感到羞耻。

This myth persists because once in a while, someone does seem to acquire process skills without visible effort. Just as some people learn to program computers without direct training, others seem always to know just how to be most helpful. It’s wonderful for those people, but does that make them better than people who have to learn by conscious effort? The threat/reward model leads us to make such judgments, so we are ashamed of the effort we need to learn.

我们的耻辱也许可以解释为什么人们开始相信工作可以与人分离。正如尤金·肯尼迪所说，当我们开始意识到“人们无法为他人做他们想做的一切”时，我们就会为自己的不足感到羞愧。通过假装工作以某种方式从人身上抽象出来，我们可以将人际关系失败转化为机械故障。例如，说“我们无法让程序按时运行”比说“我不够熟练，无法帮助杰克成为更好的程序员”要容易得多。

Our shame may explain why people start to believe that work can be separated from people. When we begin to realize, as Eugene Kennedy says, that “people cannot do everything they want to do for others,” we are ashamed of our inadequacy. By pretending the work is somehow abstracted from the people, we can transform our interpersonal failure into a mechanical failure. It’s much easier to say, for example, “We couldn’t get the program working on time” than “I wasn’t skilled enough to help Jack become a better programmer.”

“天生乐于助人”的神话造成了一个恶性循环。想要与他人合作的人通常很少练习，所以他们对帮助知之甚少，这有什么奇怪的吗？在本章中，我希望通过介绍技术领导者在创建有用环境方面需要学习的一些经验教训来打破这个迷思。

The myth of “natural helpfulness” creates a vicious circle. People who want to work with others generally get little practice, so is it any wonder that they know so little about helping? In this chapter, I hope to fight that myth by presenting some lessons that technical leaders need to learn about creating helpful environments.

努力提供帮助：一项练习

TRYING TO BE HELPFUL: AN EXERCISE

为了让潜在的领导者练习为他人创造有益的环境，我们设计了以下练习：分配两个团队来创建一个创新设计，并使用 Tinkertoys 来执行，然后记录他们的设计。团队不知道的是，他们之后必须交换纸质设计（不能包含图片），每个团队都试图重新创建对方的设计。团队成员只能通过书面信息进行交流，以便在演练之后，我们可以分析各种尝试来帮助其他团队。

To give potential leaders practice at creating helpful environments for others, we designed the following exercise: Two teams are each assigned to create an innovative design and use Tinkertoys to execute, and then document, their design. What the teams don’t know is that they later must exchange the paper designs (which can contain no pictures), each trying to recreate the other’s design. Team members can communicate only through written messages, so that after the exercise, we can analyze the various attempts to help the other team.

我记得在一次这样的练习中，蓝队编造了一个如此抽象的结构，我担心绿队没有机会。我想让它在 Green 上更容易一些，但又不想放弃整个练习：

In one such exercise, I recall, the Blue team had concocted such an abstract structure I worried that the Green team wouldn’t have a chance. I wanted to make it easier on Green, without giving the whole exercise away:

“也许一个名字会有所帮助，”我冒险道。Blue 团队想出了一个名字，最终想出了 Inert Toky，这是 Tinkertoy 的变位词。这当然很有创意，但我怀疑 Inert Toky 是否会改善其他团队的环境。一想到格林的反应我就不寒而栗。

“Perhaps a name would be helpful,” I ventured. The Blue team worked on a name, eventually coming up with Inert Toky, an anagram of Tinkertoy. It was certainly creative, but I doubted that Inert Toky would enhance the environment for the other team. I shuddered to think what Green’s reaction would be.

后来，当Blue和Green交换了设计，Blue正在重建Green的Hurdy Gurdy Banjo设计时，Blue团队的Alex突然宣布：“他们无法制造我们的Toky。”

Later, after Blue and Green exchanged designs and Blue was working to reconstruct Green’s Hurdy Gurdy Banjo design, Alex of the Blue team suddenly announced, “They’re not going to be able to build our Toky.”

“为什么不？” 卡西问。

“Why not?” asked Cassie.

“因为我们没有把模具留下的这个废料上的绿色和黄色小塑料刀片折断。”

“Because we didn’t break the little green and yellow plastic blades off this waste piece left over from the mold.”

“所以？” “好吧，如果你看看他们的班卓琴，你会发现他们已经把刀片从废品上折断了。没有办法让他们重新开始，所以他们无法建造 Toky。”

“So?” “Well, if you look at their banjo, you see that they’ve already broken their blades off the waste piece. There’s no way to get them back on, so they can’t build the Toky.”

“也许我们可以把我们的刀片寄给他们，以换取他们的。”

“Maybe we could send them our blades, in exchange for theirs.”

卡西转向我，寻求许可。

Cassie turned to me, seeking permission.

“你知道规则，”我提醒她。“你们只能交换书面信息。”

“You know the rules,” I reminded her. “You can exchange written messages only.”

“但是我们可以交换工具包吗？否则，知道他们没有合适的部件来构建 theToky 对他们来说不会有太大帮助。”

“But can we exchange kits? Otherwise, it isn’t going to be too helpful for them to know they don’t have the right parts to build theToky.”

“好吧，”我同意，想帮忙。“如果格林同意交换

“All right,” I agreed, wanting to be helpful. “If Green agrees to the exchange

Alex 和 Cassie 写下了这条消息，我把它带到 Green 团队时读到了：

Alex and Cassie wrote the message, which I read as I carried it to the Green team:

*亲爱的格林：

*Dear Green:

如果可以，我们愿意与您交换零件。Inert Toky 需要一些深奥的零件。爱，蓝色*

We are willing to swap parts with you, if we can. The Inert Toky requires some esoteric parts. Love, Blue*

当格林收到这条信息时，他们有些不明白其中的含义。他们并不特别反对交换零件，但他们已经开始研究 Inert Toky，并不急于打破他们的辛勤工作。

When Green received this message, they were a bit unsure of its meaning. They had no particular objection to swapping parts, but they had already started to work on the Inert Toky and weren’t eager to break up their hard work.

尼尔斯转向我问道：“他们为什么要求交换？”

Nils turned to me and asked, “Why are they asking for a swap?”

“我只是一个使者。我知道什么？”

“I’m just a messenger. What do I know?”

“也许这是我们的‘不明部件’，”Vinetta 提出。“我告诉过你，他们无法弄清楚那些是盒子顶部和橡皮筋。”

“Maybe it’s our ‘unidentified parts,’ “ Vinetta offered. “I told you they wouldn’t be able to figure out that those were the box top and the rubber band.”

“我们不要再谈这个了。一旦他们意识到这是班卓琴，这对他们来说应该是显而易见的。”

“Let’s not get into that again. It should be obvious to them once they realize it’s a banjo.”

“也许他们没有橡皮筋？小布景没有。”

“Maybe they don’t have a rubber band? The little sets didn’t have them.”

“好吧，”尼尔斯说，“我认为没有必要，但我想我们应该愿意帮助他们。” 他在 Blue 的原始消息下方写下了回复：

“Okay,” Nils said, “I don’t think it’s necessary, but I guess we should be willing to help them out.” He wrote his reply below Blue’s original message:

*亲爱的蓝：

*Dear Blue:

我们很高兴交换零件。1 个组件的零件在罐子里。

We are delighted to swap parts. The parts for 1 Assembly are in the can.

绿色的*

Green*

在他们对信息的含义感到困惑时，Blue 似乎没有注意到 Green 在他们的信息中省略了“爱”。“他们想交换一些单独的零件吗？” 卡西问。“我们可以这样做吗？”

In their confusion over the meaning of the message, Blue didn’t seem to notice that Green had left “Love” off their message. “Do they want to swap some individual parts?” Cassie asked. “Are we allowed to do that?”

“我认为他们要求交换已完成的模型，”亚历克斯说。

“I think they’re asking to swap the completed models,” said Alex.

“这样可以吗？”

“Is that allowed?”

“不，”我回答说，“交换模型会破坏练习的目的。”

“No,” I replied, “swapping models would defeat the purpose of the exercise.”

“好吧，”卡西说，“但时间越来越紧迫了。” 她在格林的留言下方又写了一条留言，然后递给了我。然后，她从她的钱包里拿出一个硬币，在我的鼻子前闪了一下。“如果你在不到三分钟的时间内交付并带着他们的工具包回来，这对你来说是一个很大的提示。” 这不是一个足够大的提示来阻止我在走廊停下来阅读消息：

“Okay,” said Cassie, “but time is growing short.” She scribbled another message below Green’s message and handed it to me. Then, she took a quarter from her purse and flashed it in front of my nose. “There’s a big tip for you if you deliver this and get back with their kit in less than three minutes.” It wasn’t a big enough tip to prevent me from stopping in the corridor to read the message:

*以上信息不清楚。我们希望交易 Tinkertoy Kits。

*Above messages not clear. We wish to trade Tinkertoy Kits.

你是否愿意？如果是这样，请发送您的工具包。*

Are you willing? If so, send your kit.*

显然，卡西在匆忙中也忘了写“爱”，但格林没有注意到。“你看，”维内特说，“他们不明白他们已经得到了他们需要的所有零件。我们现在不能交易整套装备。我们没有时间打破所有这些工作并重新开始。”

Evidently, Cassie in her haste had also forgotten to write “Love,” but Green didn’t notice. “See,” said Vinette, “they don’t understand that they’ve got all the parts they need. We can’t trade whole kits now. We haven’t got time to break up all this work and start over.”

“在这里，”尼尔斯说。“你继续工作，我会向他们解释清楚。” 在之前的消息下，他写道：

“Here,” said Nils. “You keep working and I’ll make it perfectly clear to them.” Under the previous messages, he wrote:

我们的 Tinkertoy 套件包含 Hurdy Gurdy Banjo 的所有部件，而且仅包含这些部件。我们只对 Inert Toky 的部分感兴趣。

Our Tinkertoy Kit contains parts for Hurdy Gurdy Banjo, all & only. We are interested in parts of Inert Toky, all & only.

Vinette 阅读了消息，然后添加了一个脚注：

Vinette read the message, then added a footnote:

注：盒顶和橡皮筋为“未识别部件”。

Note: Box top and rubber bands are “unidentified parts.”

她可能应该把它关掉，因为亚历克斯的反应很糟糕。“他们认为我们是白痴吗？我们在两分钟内就想通了，但如果我们不告诉他们，他们永远不会发现我们的刀片没有分开。”

She probably should have left it off, because Alex reacted badly. “Do they think we’re idiots? We figured that out in two minutes, but they’ll never figure out that our blades aren’t separated if we don’t tell them.”

“也许我们应该放弃它，”卡西大胆地说。

“Maybe we should just give it up,” Cassie ventured.

“不，不帮助他们是不公平的。我会再试一次，”亚历克斯一边说一边潦草地写下了系列中的下一条消息：

“No, it wouldn’t be fair not to help them. I’ll try one more time,” Alex said as he scrawled the next message in the series:

不可能在此时分开。如果没有这种交换，您将无法完成惰性 Toky。

Impossible to separate at this point. You will not be able to complete the Inert Toky without this exchange.

我不明白这是什么意思，尼尔斯也不明白。

I couldn’t figure out exactly what this meant, and neither could Nils.

“他们在威胁我们吗？”

“Are they threatening us?”

“我不这么认为，”维内特说。“也许他们认为我们不够聪明，无法建造它？”

“I don’t think so,” said Vinette. “Maybe they don’t think we’re smart enough to build it?”

“那样的话，”尼尔斯说，转向我，“你可以告诉他们把他们的小工匠玩具盒贴上去。”

“In that case,” Nils said, turning to me, “You can tell them to stick their box of Tinkertoys .”

“对不起，”我回答说，“传递口头信息不是我的工作。你得把它写下来。”

“I’m sorry,” I replied, “but it’s not my job to deliver verbal messages. You’ll have to put it in writing.”

显然，尼尔斯认为最好把脏话白纸黑字，这样就没有更多的书面信息了。我确实听到了更多关于血统和解剖学的意见，但它们对故事的意义不大。

Evidently, Nils thought better of putting obscenities in black and white, so there were no more written messages. I did hear a few more opinions about ancestry and anatomy, but they wouldn’t add greatly to the story.

关于帮助的一些教训

SOME LESSON•S ABOUT HELPING

作为一个孤立的事件，这些事件只是幽默，尽管沮丧和愤怒的情绪是真实的。类似的顺序每天都会发生在潜在的解决问题的领导者身上。细节不同，但模式永远不会改变。它始于真诚的帮助愿望，经过一些混乱的沟通，退化为情绪化的辱骂，最后让事情变得更糟。

As an isolated incident, these events are merely humorous, though the emotions of frustration and anger were real enough. Similar sequences happen to would-be problem-solving leaders every day. The details differ, but the pattern never varies. It begins with a sincere desire to help, progresses through some muddled communications, degenerates into emotional name calling, and finishes by making things worse.

这个案例唯一的独特之处在于观察者的存在和书面记录，让我们有机会回顾事件。当那些寻求金钱、权力和其他形式的个人利益的人受挫时，很容易对他们的动机进行道德化。我们可以从只是想提供帮助的蓝色和绿色的困境中学到什么？

The only thing unique about this case is the existence of the observer and the written record, giving us the opportunity to review the incident. When those who seek money, power, and other forms of personal gain are thwarted, it’s easy to moralize about their motives. What can we learn from the plight of the Blue and the Green, who were only trying to be helpful?

第一课是这样的：

Lesson number one is this:

想要帮助别人可能是一个崇高的动机，但这并没有使它变得更容易。

Wanting to help people may be a noble motive but that doesn’t make it any easier.

蓝蓝心想，既然他们是来帮忙的，那倒是很容易成功。结果，他们对最初的信息很粗心，导致整个事件走上歧途。

Blue thought that since they were trying to help, it would be easy to succeed. As a result, they were careless with their initial message, which got the whole affair off on the wrong foot.

要吸取的第二个教训是，

The second lesson to be learned is,

*如果人们不需要你的帮助，你将永远无法提供帮助

*If people don’t want your help, you’ll never succeed in helping

他们，无论你有多聪明或多棒。*

them, no matter how smart or wonderful you are.*

如果他们了解问题的本质，Green 可能会欢迎 Blue 的帮助。缺乏这种理解，他们误解了 Blue 的提议，首先是请求帮助 Blue，然后是对他们能力的侮辱。

Had they understood the nature of their problem, Green might have welcomed help from Blue. Lacking that understanding, they misinterpreted Blue’s offer, first as a request to help Blue, then as an insult to their abilities.

Blue 的失误开始说明了第三个教训：

Blue’s blundering start illustrates a third lesson:

*有效的帮助只能从

*Effective help can only start with

就问题的明确定义达成共识。 *

mutual agreement on a clear definition of the problem.*

有趣的是，Blue 认为 Green 的报价具有侮辱性，但他们做梦也没想到自己的报价会被这样解读。格林也没有。这是明星问题解决者中最常见的动机错误，他们发现自己在监督他人，提供的帮助如果被提供给他们，他们会认为是侮辱。

Interestingly enough, Blue considered Green’s offer insulting, but they never dreamed that their own offer could be interpreted that way. Neither did Green. This is the most common motivational mistake among star problem solvers who find themselves supervising other people, offering help that they would consider insulting if offered to them.

您可以通过询问自己是否需要此帮助来防止此错误，但如果您不确定他们是否需要帮助，最好检查一下。由于您永远无法完全确定他们是否需要您的帮助，甚至无法完全确定他们是否理解问题，所以第四课说：

You can prevent this error by asking yourself if you would want this help, but if you’re not absolutely sure they want help, it’s best to check it out. Since you can never be entirely sure that they want your help, or even that they understand the problem, lesson number four says:

始终检查他们是否需要您的帮助。

Always check whether they want your help.

最简单的检查方法是询问他们是否需要帮助，但两个团队都不愿意这样做。

The simplest way to check is by asking them if they want help, which neither team ever bothered to do.

随着两个团队一起解决这个问题，越来越深入地构建，情况发生了变化，问题的定义也发生了变化。此外，当他们发现帮助会花费他们多少钱时，他们改变了主意。所以，第五课是，

As the two teams worked on the problem, getting further and further into the construction, the situation changed, and so did the problem definition. Also, when they discovered what the help would cost them, they changed their minds. So, a fifth lesson is,

*即使人们同意他们需要你的帮助，那个协议

*Even when people agree that they want your help, that agreement

通常不是终身合同。 *

is not usually a lifetime contract.*

承认你的慷慨失败是没关系的，特别是如果它能帮助你在事情变得更糟之前停下来。

It’s all right to admit you failed in your generosity, especially if it helps you stop before you make things a lot worse.

这第六课似乎对一些领导人来说尤其难以接受。一旦他们主动提出帮助，他们似乎觉得违约，即使没有合同，也是自私的。他们似乎没有意识到每一次提供帮助都是为了帮助帮助者。活着的圣人很少。

This sixth lesson seems especially hard for some leaders to accept. Once they offer to help, they seem to feel that breaking the contract, even when there was no contract, is selfish. They don’t seem to realize that every offer to help is intended to do something for the helper. There are very few living saints.

想要帮助别人的人通常希望自己得到一些东西，尽管他们可能没有意识到这一点。当我们讨论这件事时，Blue 意识到他们担心 Green 不会重建他们的杰作，这意味着一半的班级都看不到它。如果他们最初的信息是这样的话，事情可能会完全不同：

People who want to help other people generally expect to get something for themselves, though they may not be aware of it. When we discussed the incident, Blue realized they were afraid Green wouldn’t rebuild their masterpiece, which meant that half the class wouldn’t see it. Things might have gone rather differently had their initial message been this:

我们在构建 Inert Toky 时犯了一个错误。我们的失误可能会给您带来很多麻烦，因为您需要一件您没有的特殊物品。我们当然希望看到 Toky 重建，但规则规定，除非您同意交换套件，否则我们不能将这件作品寄给您。您想通过交换来帮助我们吗？

We made a blunder when we built Inert Toky. Our blunder could cause you much difficulty because you’ll need a special piece that you don’t have. We’d sure like to see the Toky rebuilt, but the rules say we can’t send you the piece unless you agree to swap kits Do you want to help us out by swapping?

当然，事后我很容易这么说，但我自己尝试提供帮助呢？我曾建议他们给 Inert Toky 起个名字，我也改变了规则允许他们交换工具包，但我从来没有想过我自己的动机，这本身就是一个教训：

Of course, it’s easy for me to say this, after the fact, but what about my own attempts to help? I had suggested they give the Inert Toky a name, and I had bent the rules to allow them to exchange kits, but I had never thought much about my own motives, which is a lesson in itself:

*大多数人明白帮助者是自私的，但也认为

*Most people understand that helpers are selfish, but also think

他们是规则的例外。 *

they are exceptions to the rule.*

在讨论中，Green 透露是 Inert Toky 这个名字导致了这个问题。尼尔斯说：“我们以为他们装可爱是想给我们添麻烦。” 他的反应完全是典型的。

During the discussion, Green revealed that the name Inert Toky had contributed to the problem. “We thought they were trying to give us a hard time by being cute,” Nils said. His reaction was completely typical.

试图提供帮助通常被解释为试图干涉。

Attempts to help are often interpreted as attempts to interfere.

您可以通过两种方式使用本课程：一种是当您试图提供帮助但遇到阻力时，另一种是当您认为有人试图干扰您的工作时。这可能是最重要的一课：

You can use this lesson two ways: one when you’re trying to help and are meeting resistance, and another when you think someone is trying to interfere with your work. This may be the most important lesson of all:

*不管它看起来多么奇怪，

*No matter how strange it may look,

大多数人实际上是在努力提供帮助。*

most people are actually trying to be helpful.*

这并不意味着他们成功了，或者你必须接受他们的帮助，而只是说你最好了解它的来源。

That doesn’t mean that they are succeeding, or that you must accept their help, but only that you might be better off understanding where it’s coming from.

帮助和自尊

HELPING AND SELF-ESTEEM

当我回顾消息的顺序时，我对“爱”在第一条消息之后消失的方式感到震惊，无论是字面上还是比喻上。它让我想起了黄金法则，我看到它以两种不同的方式表达：

As I look back over the sequence of messages, I’m struck by the way that “Love” dropped off after the first message, both literally and figuratively. It reminded me of the Golden Rule, which I’ve seen expressed in two different ways:

*己所不欲，勿施于人。

*Do unto others as you would have others do unto you.

爱人如己。 *

Love thy neighbor as thyself.*

第一个版本通常被解释为意思，

The first version is often interpreted as meaning,

帮助你的邻居。

Help your neighbor.

根据我自己帮助和被帮助的经验，我更愿意做出不同的解释：

Based on my own experience with helping and being helped, I prefer to interpret it differently:

*只有在您愿意在以下方面得到帮助时才愿意帮助您的邻居

*Offer to help your neighbor only if you would want to be helped in

同样的情况，并按照你想要的方式去做

the same situation, and do it in the way you would want to be

有帮助。*

helped.*

我们希望得到怎样的帮助？我不想因为怜悯而被帮助。我不想因为自私而被帮助。在这些情况下，帮助者真的不关心我这个人。我希望别人对我做的是爱我——当然不是浪漫的爱，而是真正的人文关怀。

How do we want to be helped? I don’t want to be helped out of pity. I don’t want to be helped out of selfishness. These are situations in which the helper really cares nothing about me as a human being. What I would have others do unto me is to love me—not romantic love, of course, but true human caring.

所以，如果你想直接或通过创造一个帮助环境来激励人们，你必须首先让他们相信你关心他们，而唯一可靠的说服他们的方法就是真正关心他们。人们可能会因为关心而被愚弄，但不会长久。这就是为什么黄金法则说的是“爱你的邻居”，而不是“假装你爱你的邻居”。不要自欺欺人。如果你不真正关心你所领导的人，你将永远无法成为他们的领导者。

So, if you want to motivate people, either directly or by creating a helping environment, you must first convince them that you care about them, and the only sure way to convince them is by actually caring. People may be fooled about caring, but not for long. That’s why the Golden Rule says, “Love thy neighbor,” not “Pretend you love thy neighbor.” Don’t fool yourself. If you don’t really care about the people whom you lead, you’ll never succeed as their leader.

我无法教你去关心别人，无论是一般的人还是特定的人，但我了解到，如果你不关心自己，就不可能关心别人。黄金法则并没有说，“即使你认为自己是卑鄙的蠕虫，也要爱你的邻居。” 爱他人的能力——从而帮助他人，从而领导他人——始于爱自己的能力。

I cannot teach you to care about people, neither people in general nor particular persons, but I have learned that caring about other people is impossible if you don’t care about yourself. The Golden Rule doesn’t say, “Love thy neighbor even though you think you’re a despicable worm.” The ability to love others—and thus to help others, and thus to lead others—starts with the ability to love yourself.

正如我们刚刚看到的，帮助他人的尝试并不能保证成功。当你作为帮助者遇到困难时，你对自己的感受将决定你的反应。如果你关心自己，你就能在困难中坚持下去，如果有必要，你可以在不毁掉自己的情况下放弃这个项目。如果你的自我价值低下，你将不得不保护自己。你可能会在需要坚持的时候放弃帮助。在尝试证明对另一方甚至对您有害之后，您可能会坚持很长时间。或者你可以将失败从你自己身上转移开，而责怪你开始帮助的那个人。

As we’ve just seen, the attempt to help others is not guaranteed to succeed. When you run into difficulty as a helper, your own feelings about yourself will determine how you react. If you care about yourself, you’ll be able to persist through difficulties and, if necessary, to abandon the project without destroying yourself. If your self-worth is low, you’re going to have to protect yourself. You may abandon the help just when it needs persistence. You may persist long after the attempt has proved harmful to the other party, or even to you. Or you may project the failure away from yourself, blaming the very person you started out to help.

如果你自卑，就会阻碍你激励他人的一切努力。在你着手帮助别人之前，你最好先做好自己，这是下一章的主题。

If you hold yourself in low esteem, it will thwart your every attempt to motivate others. Before you set out to help others, you’d better work on yourself first, which is the subject of the next chapter.

问题

QUESTIONS

  1. 尤金·肯尼迪 (Eugene Kennedy) 在《论成为一名顾问》（第 14 页）中谈到“在整个历史上，坚决的行善者造成了多少伤害——这个坚强的部落对人类的粗暴对待无法用他们眼中的善良光芒来弥补. 行善者被定义为那些根据自己的需要对他人采取行动的人。因此，很明显，做好事与不惜一切代价有目的地对他人造成好处一样是不可避免的。” 您想成为领导者以便为他人做好事吗？你知道什么时候你是在做好事而不是在造成伤害？
  2. Eugene Kennedy in On Becoming a Counselor (p. 14) talks about “how much harm has been done all through history by resolute do-gooders—that sturdy tribe whose heavy handedness with human beings cannot be compensated for by the virtuous gleam in their eyes. Do-gooders are defined as those persons who act on others in response to their own needs. It is clear, therefore, that doing good is not to be avoided as much as setting out purposefully to inflict good on others at all costs.” Do you wish to be a leader so you can do good for others? In what ways do you know when you are doing good rather than inflicting good?
  3. 回想一下有人试图强迫您提供帮助的时候。你感觉怎么样？你有什么反应？
  4. Recall some time when someone tried to force some help on you. How did you feel? How did you react?
  5. 你现在处于帮助关系中吗？如果是这样，你的合同是什么？别人的合同是什么？
  6. Are you in a helping relationship now? If so, what is your contract? What is the other person’s contract?
  7. 对于你最近经历过的一些帮助情况，你想从中得到什么？如果可能的话，回忆几种情况，看看你是否能发现一种模式。
  8. For some helping situation you’ve experienced in the recent past, what were you trying to get out of it? If possible, recall several situations and see if you can detect a pattern.
  9. 想一想与你共事的某个人，你发现对他应用黄金法则很困难。你觉得那个人有什么难以照顾的地方？同样的品质如何体现在你身上？
  10. Think of some person you work with to whom you find it difficult to apply the Golden Rule. What is it in that person that you find hard to care for? How is that same quality manifest in you?
  11. 回想一下最近人们似乎故意阻挠你工作的情况。试着想象他们可能是多么真诚地试图帮助你，即使这种尝试可能被误导了。如果可能的话，与相关人员交谈并听取他们对他们尝试做的事情的看法。
  12. Recall a recent occasion when people seemed to be deliberately thwarting your work. Try to imagine how they might have been sincerely attempting to help you, even though the attempt may have been misguided. If possible, speak to the people involved and listen to their version of what they were trying to do.
  13. 白金法则说：“己所不欲，勿施于人。” 这与作为帮助的实用指南的黄金法则相比如何？如果你不想做他们想做的事怎么办？
  14. The Platinum Rule says, “Do unto others as they would have you do unto them.” How does this compare with the Golden Rule as a practical guide to helping? What if you don’t want to do what they want?

第 13 章学习成为激励者

Chapter 13. Learning to Be a Motivator

为什么要阅读这本书来了解如何赢得朋友？为什么不研究世界上最伟大的朋友赢家的技术呢？他是谁？你明天可能会在街上遇见他。当你离他不到十英尺时，他会开始摇尾巴。如果你停下来拍拍他，他几乎会跳出他的皮肤来告诉你他有多喜欢你。而且你也知道，他这秀恩爱的背后，并没有什么别有用心的：他不想卖给你任何房产，也不想娶你。——戴尔·卡耐基，《如何赢得朋友和影响他人》

Why read this book to find out how to win friends? Why not study the technique of the greatest winner of friends the world has ever known? Who is he? You may meet him tomorrow coming down the street. When you get within ten feet of him, he will begin to wag his tail. If you stop and pat him, he will almost jump out of his skin to show you how much he likes you. And you know that behind this show of affection on his part, there are no ulterior motives: he doesn’t want to sell you any real estate, and he doesn’t want to marry you. —Dale Carnegie, How to Win Friends and Influence People

谈论自尊在影响他人方面的重要性固然很好，但技巧呢？即使你不太喜欢自己，难道你不认为有一些指导方针可以让你有能力让其他人做你想做的事吗？

It’s all very nice to talk about the importance of self-esteem in influencing other people, but what about techniques? Even if you don’t like yourself very much, wouldn’t you think there are guidelines that will give you power to make other people do what you want?

也许有，但当我坐下来写这一章时，我很难想出任何看起来重要的事情。不仅如此，我还很难弄清楚为什么会遇到麻烦。我尝试了十几种技巧来让章节滚动，但似乎没有一个起作用。最后，我决定首先向自己承认我遇到了很多麻烦。那打破了大坝，你正在阅读的章节就是结果。

Perhaps there are, but as I sat down to write this chapter, I had trouble thinking of any that seemed important. Not only that, but I had trouble figuring out why I was having trouble. I tried a dozen tricks to get the chapter rolling, but none of them seemed to work. Finally, I decided to start by confessing to myself that I was having a lot of trouble. That broke the dam, and the chapter you are reading is the result.

始终保持真诚（无论您是否有意）

ALWAYS BE SINCERE (WHETHER YOU MEAN IT OR NOT)

在为本章寻找灵感时，我看了看戴尔·卡内基 (Dale Carnegie) 的《如何赢得朋友和影响他人》。毫无疑问，它是有史以来最受欢迎的自助书籍，四十多年来一直是畅销书，催生了数十种不太成功的仿制品。还有什么更好的灵感来源？

While searching for ideas for this chapter, I took a look at Dale Carnegie’s How to Win Friends and Influence People. Undoubtedly the most popular self-help book of all time, it has been a best seller for more than forty years, spawning dozens of less successful imitations. What better place to turn for inspiration?

我十岁的时候，我们家里就有这本书的副本。那个年纪，屋里什么都看，所以就看。我没有留下深刻的印象。我不仅没有留下深刻印象，但我完全厌恶。四十年来，每当有人提到戴尔卡内基或他的书时，我都会无缘无故地表达我的蔑视。

There was a copy of the book in our house when I was ten years old. At that age, I read everything in the house, so I read it. I wasn’t impressed. Not only wasn’t I impressed, but I was utterly disgusted. For forty years, I gratuitously expressed my contempt when anyone even mentioned Dale Carnegie or his book.

几年前，当我躲在科罗拉多州拉瓦荒野附近的一个狩猎小屋里，等待一场为期三天的暴雨时，我找到了一本破旧的《如何赢得朋友和影响他人》。我决定把它作为衡量我四十年来变化的标准来阅读会很有趣。

A few years ago, while holed up in a hunting lodge near the Rawah Wilderness of Colorado, waiting out a three-day rainstorm, I found a battered copy of How to Win Friends and Influence People. I decided it would be amusing to read it as a measure of how I had changed in forty years.

急于获得一些快速结果，我立即转向总结本书各部分的原则列表。当我看到诸如“微笑”或“让对方觉得自己很重要——并且这样做的时候”这样的规则 为什么会生气？这些规则让我想起了我应该从我所认识的最伟大的伪君子那里学到的一条规则：永远要真诚（不管你是不是真心的）。

Eager for some quick results, I turned immediately to the lists of principles that summarize sections of the book. When I saw rules such as “Smile” or “Make the other person feel important—and do it Why become angry? Those rules reminded me of a rule I was supposed to learn from the greatest hypocrite I’ve ever known: always be sincere (whether you mean it or not).

对我来说，那些通过死记硬背的规则来赢得朋友或影响他人的人是在进行最低级的虚伪欺骗，我不想与他们有任何关系。对我来说，世界上第二糟糕的事情是读一本教授这些规则的书。当然，最糟糕的事情莫过于写这样一本书。

To me, people who learn to win friends or influence other people through memorized rules are practicing the lowest form of hypocritical deception, and I want nothing to do with them. The second worst thing in the world for me was to read a book that taught such rules. The worst thing, of course, would be to write such a book.

生存规则

SURVIVAL RULES

我气得差点把这本书搁置四十年；但因为这是小屋里唯一的一本书，所以我坚持了下来。我很高兴我做到了。改变我想法的是按照作者的意图从正面阅读这本书。我注意到的第一件事是，不管他的 15,000,000 名读者的动机如何，卡内基本人显然是真诚的。尽管他从未见过我，但他给我的感觉是他真的很想帮助我赢得朋友并影响他人。

I was so angry, I almost put the book down for another forty years; but since it was the only book in the lodge, I persisted. I’m glad I did. What changed my mind was reading the book from the front, as the author had intended. The first thing I noticed was that regardless of the motives of his 15,000,000 readers, Carnegie himself was obviously sincere. Even though he never met me, he gave me the feeling that he really did want to help me win friends and influence people.

很明显，四十年来我的内心发生了一些变化，因为我十岁时没有那种感觉。到我十岁的时候，我已经制定了一个强有力的规则，它说，

Apparently something in me had changed in forty years, because I didn’t feel that way when I was ten years old. By the time I was ten, I had developed a powerful rule that said,

不要相信那些说他们想帮助你的人。[不再是规则]

Don’t trust people who say they want to help you. [no longer a rule]

规则从何而来？我不太清楚，但我很惊讶我有这样的规则。正如我们在第 10 章分析交互动态时所看到的，每个人都有一些与他人打交道的规则。其中一些“生存法则”是我们成年后明确学到的，比如如果你是银行家该穿什么样的西装，或者如果你是军人该向谁敬礼。但我们的大部分生存规则都源于幼年，有些太早以至于我们不记得明确地学习过它们。

Where did the rule come from? I don’t know exactly, but I’m surprised I had such a rule. As we saw in Chapter 10 when we analyzed the dynamics of an interaction, everyone has some rules for dealing with other people. Some of these “survival rules” are learned explicitly when we are adults, like what sort of suit to wear if you’re a banker, or whom to salute if you’re a soldier. But most of our survival rules stem from an early age, some so early that we cannot remember learning them explicitly.

规则来自我们的生存确实依赖于适当互动的时代，因此它们伴随着强烈的情绪。而且因为它们源自我们对世界运作方式还不是很清楚的时代，所以我们倾向于认为它们不是习得的规则，而是普遍的真理。因此，很容易理解为什么有另一条规则说，

Rules come from a time when our survival really did depend on interacting properly, and so they are accompanied by strong emotions. And because they derive from a time when we weren’t terribly conscious of the way the world worked, we tend to think of them not as learned rules, but as universal truths. Thus, it’s easy to understand why had another rule that said,

提防任何试图教你普遍真理的人。[不再是规则]

Beware of anybody who tries to teach you universal truths. [no longer a rule]

这条规则是许多愤怒的根源，阻碍了我接受戴尔卡内基的领导。

This rule was the source of much of the anger that interfered with my acceptance of Dale Carnegie’s leadership.

元规则

META-RULES

解决问题的领导者关注规则，因为它们对思想的流动有很大的影响。如果你试图激励某人以一种新的方式工作，而那个人有一个关于怀疑权威的规则，你就会遇到麻烦。了解规则将帮助您弄清楚如何联系到那个人。

A problem-solving leader is concerned with rules because they exert a strong influence on the flow of ideas. If you are trying to motivate someone to work in a new way, and that person has a rule about suspecting people in authority, you are going to run into trouble. Understanding about rules will help you figure out how to reach that person.

某些规则是双重重要的，就像我的规则说要提防任何试图教你普遍真理的人。它非常重要，因为它是关于规则的规则，或者说是元规则。元规则控制关于规则的想法的流动，从而决定我们学习新规则或忘记旧规则的容易程度。因此，元规则决定了我们改变与他人互动方式的难易程度。

Certain rules are doubly important, like my rule that says to beware of anybody who tries to teach you universal truths. It’s doubly important because it is a rule about rules, or a meta-rule. Meta-rules control the flow of ideas about rules, and thus determine how readily we can learn new rules, or unlearn old ones. Therefore, the meta-rules determine how easily we can change the way we interact with others.

到十岁时，我已经掌握了我的元规则，所以我从来没有喜欢过 Dale Carnegie 的书、他的研讨会，或者任何其他人处理人类互动的书籍或研讨会。在我改变与他人互动的方式之前，我必须学习如何挖掘我的生存规则和元规则，以及如何改变它们——如果我愿意的话。

By age ten, I was already in the grips of my meta-rule, so I never took well to Dale Carnegie’s book, or his workshops, or to anybody else’s books or workshops that deal with human interaction. Before I could change the way I interacted with other people, I had to learn how to unearth my survival rules and my meta-rules, and how to transform them—if I wanted to.

即使我们没有明确地处理生存规则，生存规则也会发生变化，但如果没有这种明确的知识，这种变化可能会花费难以忍受的长时间。我花了将近四十年的时间来修改我的规则，说，

Survival rules can change even when we don’t work on them explicitly, but without this explicit knowledge, the change can take an unbearably long time. It took me close to forty years to modify my rule that said,

不要相信那些说他们想帮助你的人。

Don’t trust people who say they want to help you.

与其他人的长期经验逐渐将此规则转化为，

Long experience with other people gradually transformed this rule into,

*你可以相信一些说他们想帮助你的人，

*You can trust some people who say they want to help you,

因为即使你错误地信任了某人，

because even if you trust someone by mistake,

您可能可以照顾好自己并生存下来。*

you can probably take care of yourself and survive.*

这条新规则让我可以继续阅读戴尔卡耐基的作品。

This new rule allowed me to continue reading Dale Carnegie.

将规则转化为指南

TRANSFORMING RULES INTO GUIDES

假设你最近被提升为领导一组计算机程序员。你认为自己做得很好，直到管理层进行的一项调查显示你的团队中的人认为你“专横”、“干涉”并且你“不能委派任务”。你感到受伤和愤怒，然后对自己说，我只是想帮忙。”

Suppose you have recently been promoted to lead a group of computer programmers. You think you’re doing rather well until a survey conducted by management shows that the people in your group think you are “overbearing,” “interfering,” and that you “can’t delegate a task.” You are hurt and angry, and say to yourself, I’m just trying to be helpful.”

您可以与小组成员交谈并向他们解释您只是想提供帮助，但您意识到他们也可能将此解释为更多干扰。也许你可以为自己做点什么。也许你的伤害和愤怒意味着你正在触及某种深刻的生存法则，它要求你对人们施加帮助。这个生存法则似乎阻碍了你成为一个有效的领导者，所以你不想等待四十年让它自我转变。相反，您可以应用逐步转换来生成更有效的版本，即指南而不是规则。

You could talk to the group members and explain to them that you’re just trying to be helpful, but you realize that they could also interpret this as more interference. Perhaps there’s something you can do about yourself. Perhaps your hurt and anger means that you are touching on some sort of deep survival rule that requires you to inflict help on people. This survival rule seems to be blocking you from being an effective leader, so you don’t want to wait forty years for it to transform itself. Instead, you can apply a step-by-step transformation to produce a more effective version, a guide rather than a rule.

第 1 步：清楚明确地陈述规则。

Step 1: State the rule clearly and explicitly.

对于某些男人来说，这可能是，

For certain men, this might be,

我必须帮助所有的女人。

I must help all women.

对于其他人来说，可能是，

For other people, it might be,

我必须帮助所有的年轻人。

I must help all young people.

假设您的规则是，

Let’s suppose that your rule is,

我必须总是帮助每个人。

I must always help everybody.

此语句成为转换的起点。

This statement becomes the starting point for the transformation.

第 2 步：承认规则的生存价值并与你的潜意识达成交易。

Step 2: Acknowledge the rule’s survival value and strike a bargain with your unconscious mind.

一旦你陈述了规则，“我必须总是帮助每个人”，你的第一个冲动可能是试图摆脱它，但即使你能做到，也将是一个严重的错误。让我们看看为什么。

Once you’ve stated the rule, “I must always help everybody,” your first impulse might be to try to get rid of it, but even if you could do this, it would be a serious mistake. Let’s see why.

对于每条规则，都有一个原因。当您可以清楚、轻松地找出原因时，更改规则就相对容易了。例如，我有一个驾驶规则，“靠右行驶”。当我在英国或澳大利亚租车时，我很难将规则更改为“靠左行驶”。但是，因为生存价值是显而易见的，所以几天后我就可以像本地人一样开车了。

For every rule, there is a reason. When you can clearly and easily identify the reason, it’s relatively easy to change the rule. For instance, I have a driving rule, “Keep to the right.” When I rent a car in England or Australia, I have some difficulty changing that rule to, “Keep to the left.” Because the survival value is obvious, however, I’m driving like a native in a few days.

你会认为改变道路的两侧比改变其他一些生存规则更苛刻，因为即使在某些时候在错误的一侧驾驶也可能是致命的。对于大多数行为，部分更改的惩罚并没有那么严重，但是许多人通过应用元规则来挫败他们更改规则的尝试：

You would think that changing sides of the road would be more demanding than changing some other survival rules because driving on the wrong side even some of the time could be deadly. For most behaviors, the penalties for a partial change aren’t nearly that severe, yet many people defeat their attempts to change rules by applying a meta-rule:

我必须全部改变或什么都不改变。

I must change all or nothing.

如果规则背后的原因不明确，则此元规则会使更改风险太大。规则背后的原因可能埋藏在过去，在它真正意味着生存的时候。我们不会明确地想，“如果我不帮妈妈收拾玩具，她就不会爱我，然后我就会饿死。” 所有那些童年的原因都在模糊的过去中消失了，取而代之的只是围绕着它们的强烈情绪，比如害怕如果我们不一直帮助每个人就会发生可怕的事情。

If the reason behind the rule is not clear, this meta-rule makes change too risky. The reason behind the rule may be buried in the past, at a time when it really did mean survival. We don’t think explicitly, “If I don’t help Mommy put away the toys, she won’t love me and then I’ll starve to death.” All those childhood reasons have been lost in the fuzzy past, replaced only by the strong emotions surrounding them, like the fear that something terrible will happen if we don’t help everybody all the time.

因此，与其花七年的时间进行精神分析来挖掘规则背后的原因，你可以简单地对自己说：“这条规则对我的生存很有价值，所以我无意摆脱它。我会把它放在身边，以便在适当的时候使用。我可能会添加一些新规则，但如果我需要的话，旧规则仍然存在。”

So instead of spending seven years in psychoanalysis to unearth the reason behind the rule, you can simply say to yourself, “This rule has been valuable in enabling me to survive, so I have no intention of getting rid of it. I will keep it around for use when the proper occasion arises. I may add some new rules, but the old one will still be there if I need it.”

这个新想法成为你潜意识的一部分，它一直在工作，照顾你。例如，它可以在您说话或想别的事情时为您开车。如果你曾经有过在没有意识到自己开车到达目的地的情况下到达目的地的经历，你就会知道你的潜意识确实可以听到并理解来自你意识的指示。但它永远不会影响您的安全。

This new idea becomes part of your unconscious mind, which is constantly at work taking care of you. For example, it can drive the car for you while you are talking or thinking of something else. If you’ve ever had the experience of arriving at your destination without being consciously aware of driving there, you know that your unconscious can indeed hear and understand directions from your conscious mind. But it won’t ever compromise on your safety.

当您进行规则转换时，重要的是每一步都尽可能安全；否则你的潜意识会为了你好而反抗。当你经历这个转变的每一步时，你的潜意识可能不会用语言告诉你，但你的身体会用感觉来回应，告诉你是否可以继续下一步。

As you go through a rule transformation, it’s important that every step be as safe as possible; otherwise your unconscious mind will resist, for your own good. As you go through each step in this transformation, your unconscious probably won’t talk to you in words, but your body will respond with feelings that tell you whether it’s okay to proceed to the next step.

第三步：给自己一个选择。

Step 3: Give yourself a choice.

一旦你的潜意识确信总有一条安全路径回到旧规则，你就已经建立了旧规则是你自己选择的想法的一半，因此在任何情况下是否应用它也取决于你。这意味着您现在可以将规则的陈述从强制转变为选择。例如，这条规则，我必须总是帮助每个人，它带有无所不能的含义，事实上它总是可以被遵守的。对一些人来说，这条规则意味着，“既然我必须总是帮助每个人，如果我不能帮助某人，那一定是我出了问题。”

Once your unconscious is assured that there is always a safe path back to the old rule, you are halfway to establishing the idea that the old rule was your own choice, so that applying it or not in any circumstance is also up to you. What this means is that you now can transform the statement of the rule from one of compulsion to choice. For instance, the rule, I must always help everybody, carries with it the implication of omnipotence, that it could in fact always be followed. To some people, this rule implies, “Since I must always help everybody, if I can’t help somebody, something must be wrong with me.’

在第 3 步中，将规则从强制转换为选择：

In step 3, transform the rule from compulsion to choice:

我总是可以帮助每个人（如果我选择的话）。

I CAN always help everybody (IF I CHOOSE TO).

第 4 步：从确定性变为可能性。

Step 4: Change from certainty to possibility.

当您完成一个规则的转换步骤时，您经常会遇到第一个规则所暗示的其他规则。当然，没有人是无所不能的，也没有人能期望成为无所不能的人，但有些人有规则，

As you go through the steps in the transformation of one rule, you’ll often come upon some other rule implied by the first. Nobody is omnipotent, of course, nor can anyone expect to be, but some people have the rule,

我必须总是非常有效。

I must always be perfectly effective.

如果你有一个“完美”的规则，你就不会在你的“帮助”规则的转变中越过这一步。在这种情况下，中断该过程并根据您的“完美”规则或任何其他阻碍您进步的规则启动新过程。

If you have a “perfection” rule, you’re not going to get past this step in the transformation of your “helping” rule. In that case, interrupt the process and start a new process on your “perfection” rule, or whatever other rule is blocking your progress.

一旦处理了完美的隐含规则，转换的下一步就会从，

Once the implicit rule of perfection is dealt with, the next step of the transformation changes from,

我总是可以帮助每个人（如果我愿意的话）。

I can always help everybody (if I choose to).

到

to

我有时可以帮助每个人（如果我选择的话）。

I can SOMETIMES help everybody (if I choose to).

再一次，这个陈述提出了完美的问题，这导致了下一步。

Again, this statement raises the issue of perfection, which leads to the next step.

第 5 步：将规则从总体性更改为非总体性。

Step 5: Change the rule from totality to non-totality.

没有人总是完美的，所以摆脱普遍性，将最新规则更改为，

Nobody’s perfect all the time, so get rid of the universals, changing the latest rule to,

有时我可以帮助其他人（如果我愿意的话）。

I can sometimes help some others (if I choose to).

一旦你完成了这么多，你就可以继续进行相对良性的任务，即决定哪些人以及何时。

Once you’ve accomplished this much, you can proceed to the relatively benign task of deciding which people, and when.

到目前为止的转变让你意识到你有选择，所以要小心，以防你有另一条规则，即创新的第三大障碍，即“只有一种正确的方法”。你想避免用一个严格的规则替换另一个，所以尽量确保你至少给出三个例子，如

The transformation up to this point has served to make you aware that you have choices, so be wary in case you have another rule, the third great obstacle to innovation, that says, “There is only one right way.” You want to avoid replacing one rigid rule with another, so try to make sure you give at least three examples, as in

我可以帮助其他人的时候。. .

I can help other people when . . .

*他们清楚地向我寻求帮助

*they ask me clearly for help

我有能力帮助他们

I have the skills to help them

我有资源帮助他们

I have the resources to help them

我适合帮助他们

it fits me to help them

我选择帮助他们

I choose to help them

我将能够容忍无法提供帮助*

I will be able to tolerate failure to help*

自从我从弗吉尼亚·萨提亚那里学到了这个方法，我自己就经历了这个特殊的转变，我也帮助了几十个领导者做到了。结果总是减少了对干涉的指控，并提高了有效授权的能力。我还将转换方法应用于许多其他通常会阻止领导者有效地与他人相处的生存法则。它几乎总是有帮助，但尽管这种转换方法非常强大，但它并非完美无缺。我们都有忘记事情的能力。

Since I learned this method from Virginia Satir, I’ve gone through this particular transformation myself, and I’ve also assisted dozens of leaders to make it. The results have always been a reduction in the accusations of interference and an increase in the ability to delegate effectively. I’ve also applied the transformation method to many other survival rules that commonly stop leaders from being effective with other people.
