• 如有必要，请使用您的直觉，但要明确表示您正在这样做。直觉是添加到论点中的有效事实。

• If necessary, use your intuition, but make clear that you’re doing it. Intuition is a valid fact to add to an argument.

一旦一个团队学会了以这种方式工作，共识方法就可以产生非常好的结果。通常在练习中，共识得分超过他们团队的平均分 30 分或更多。

Once a group learns to work in this way, the consensus approach can produce very fine results. Typically in the exercise, consensus scores exceed their team average by thirty points or more.

然而，偶尔，共识过程会崩溃，产生非常糟糕的分数。在这种情况下，并没有真正的共识，而是为了按时完成大量的争论、权衡和让步。然而，当这种情况发生时，对每个人来说都是显而易见的。一些团队明智地决定放弃在这种情况下做出的任何决定，即使这意味着不完成任务。在崩溃的情况下做出的决定不仅是错误的，而且不会被所有人接受，因此很容易被破坏。

Once in a while, though, the consensus process breaks down, producing a very poor score. In such cases, there is no actual consensus, but a lot of arguing, trading off, and backing down, just to get finished on time. When this happens, however, it’s obvious to everyone. Some teams wisely decide to discard any decision that is made under such conditions, even if it means not completing the assignment. Decisions made under breakdown conditions will not only be faulty, but will not be accepted by everyone, and thus will be easily undermined.

共识方法的一大潜在优势是信息共享。每个人都能听到其他人的意见，并可以提出问题。参与者了解实质性问题并相互了解，这为未来的团队合作做好了很好的准备。共识团队在刚成立时可能会很慢，但一旦团队成员相互了解，就会变得非常快。

One great potential advantage of the consensus approach is the sharing of information. Everyone hears what everyone else has to say and can ask questions. Participants learn about substantive matters and about each other, which is excellent preparation for future teamwork. Consensus teams may be slow when they first form, but tend to become super-fast once team members get to know each other.

绩效提高的原因之一是达成共识的团队成员分担工作责任。投票也分担责任，但以匿名方式进行，可能不会鼓励个人承担责任（“好吧，我投了反对票！”）。强有力的领导者也可能鼓励负责任的行为，但团队成员很容易坐视不管，让领导者承担功劳或责备。当达成真正的共识时，团队更有可能继续解决下一个问题，并完全接受对已完成的事情的责任。如果不是，团队可能没有达成真正的共识。

One reason for this improved performance is that consensus team members share responsibility for the work. Voting also shares responsibility, but in an anonymous way that may not encourage individual responsibility (“Well, I voted against it!”). The strong leader may also encourage responsible behavior, but it’s easy for the team members to sit back and let the leader take the credit—or the rap. When a true consensus has been reached, the team is much more likely to proceed to the next problem fully accepting responsibility for what has been done. If not, the team has probably not achieved a true consensus.

混合组织形式

MIXED ORGANIZATIONAL FORMS

我们很少看到真正的团队使用我们组织范围内的纯形式。最典型的是，团队用一个或多个其他方面的一个方面来修饰一种形式，以消除该形式的弱点。如果团队成员担心讨论会变成竞选活动，导致决策受到某个人的过度影响，他们可能会先投票，然后再竞选。

We rarely see a real team using a pure form from our organizational spectrum. Most typically, the team embellishes one form with an aspect from one or more of the others to eliminate the form’s weaknesses. If team members are afraid the discussion might turn into campaigning that could result in a decision overly influenced by one individual, they might vote first and campaign afterward.

显然，强有力的领导者形式的成功取决于被选为领导者的想法和动机的正确结合。为了让这种形式持续成功，必须有一种一致的方法来选择具有合适品质的领导者。投票是选择领导者的一种快速且适度安全的方法。共识更可靠，但需要更长的时间。

Obviously, the success of the strong leader form depends on the correct combination of ideas and motivation within the person chosen as leader. For this form to succeed consistently, there must be a consistent method of choosing a leader with the right qualities. Voting is a swift and moderately safe method of choosing a leader. Consensus is much more reliable, but takes longer.

继任之后，最糟糕的选择领导者的方法可能是让一位强大的领导者选择另一位领导者，尽管这是大多数组织使用的方法。只有当你从一个真正有效率的高层开始时，这个人才能奏效，这个人也对将要领导的人充满信心。

After inheritance, probably the worst method of choosing a leader is to have one strong leader choose another, though that is the method used in most organizations. It works only if you start with a truly effective person at the top, someone who also has the full confidence of the people to be led.

偶尔，使用共识方法的团队中的专业知识会非常低，以至于没有足够的信息来使共识可行。当由于参与者之间缺乏专业知识而无法达成共识时，我们可能不会观察到它，除非我们注意到人们提供信息时缺乏信心。在这种情况下，可以通过在所有团队成员独立工作以了解自己的阶段之前，或者通过组建一个更专业的团队来改进共识组织。

Once in a while, the expertise in a group using the consensus approach will be so low that there is not enough information to make consensus viable. When consensus fails for lack of expertise among the participants, we may not observe it, unless we notice how little confidence exists when people offer information. In such cases, the consensus organization can be improved either by preceding it with a phase in which all team members work independently to inform themselves, or by putting together a more expert team.

即使在一个纯粹的组织形式中，也有几十种变体。例如，尽管投票是一种机械程序，但小组可能会决定采用加权方案，或三分之二或四分之三的投票规则。

Even within a pure organizational form, there are dozens of variations. For example, even though voting is a mechanical procedure, the group may decide to adopt a weighting scheme, or a two-thirds or three-quarters voting rule.

我们已经看到不同的任命领导者如何解释他们的角色，并且在每种形式中，个人可以扮演不同的角色。例如，一些团体与官方记录员合作，记录下团体的部分决定。其他小组使用非官方录音机，或让组长录音，而其他小组则根本不使用录音机。

We’ve already seen how different appointed leaders interpret their role, and within each form, there are different roles that individuals can adopt. For instance, some groups work with official recorders to write down the group’s partial decisions. Other groups use unofficial recorders, or let the leader record, while others use no recorders at all.

当然，根据问题、参与者和条件，共识为组织变化提供了最大的空间。例如，一个经验丰富的人可能会在一个不熟悉共识决策的团队中担任强有力的领导角色。共识并不意味着“没有领导”。这意味着任何领导都受到共识指南的约束，关注的不是讨论的内容，而是共识过程本身。在另一个极端，整个会议可能会在没有任何可识别的领导者的情况下举行，只要遵循共识准则即可。

Consensus, of course, offers the most scope for organizational variation according to the problem, the participants, and the conditions. For instance, an experienced person might emerge to take a strong leadership role in a team that was not familiar with consensus decision making. Consensus does not mean “leaderless.” It means that any leading is constrained by consensus guidelines, concentrating not on the content of the discussion, but on the consensus process itself. At the other extreme, the entire meeting might take place without any identifiable leader, as long as the consensus guidelines are followed.

请注意，要使这些混合组织方法中的任何一种起作用，受影响的人必须同意他们将使用该组织。从本质上讲，该协议必须以协商一致的方式达成，尽管协商一致可能永远不会被公开讨论。在大多数组织中，大多数时候，共识是惯性的。有人可能会建议投票决定某些问题，并且由于该组对这种类型的投票很熟悉，所以他们默许了。

Notice that for any of these mixed organizational methods to work, the people who are affected must agree they will use that organization. Essentially, this agreement must be by consensus, though the consensus may never be openly discussed. In most organizations, most of the time, the consensus is by inertia. Someone may suggest a vote to decide some issue, and because this type of vote is familiar to the group, they acquiesce.

为了让组织尝试一种新的形式，有人不得不重新讨论这种隐含共识的问题。这样的行为很可能会引起轰动，所以没有人会尝试这样的改变，除非那个人认为新组织会更好。这让我们回到最初的问题：组织团队解决问题的最佳方式是什么？

For the organization to attempt a new form, someone has to reopen the question of this implicit consensus. Such an act is more than likely to cause a stir, so nobody would even attempt such a change unless that person thought the new organization would be better. Which brings us back to the original question: What is the best way to organize a team for solving problems?

形式服从功能

FORM FOLLOWS FUNCTION

对于“排名世界纪录”练习的参与者来说，最重要的教训是，没有一种组织总是最适合团队用来做决定的。尽管该练习模拟决策制定，但同样的教训适用于组织任何解决问题的任务。该集团的建筑以弗兰克劳埃德赖特的建筑格言为指导：“形式服从功能。”

For participants in the “Ranking World Records” exercise, the most important lesson is that there is no organization that is always best for a team to use in making a decision. Although the exercise simulates decision making, the same lesson applies to organizing for any problem- solving task. The architecture of the group is guided by Frank Lloyd Wright’s architectural dictum: “Form follows function.”

问题解决型组织的职能是什么？组织的目的是为人们创造适当的环境

What is the function of a problem-solving organization? The purpose of the organization is to create the proper environment for people to

• 理解问题

• understand the problem

• 管理思想流

• manage the flow of ideas

• 保持质量

• maintain quality

如果当前的组织没有推进这些目标，解决问题的领导者就会开始寻找一些新的形式。

If the current organization isn’t furthering these goals, the problem-solving leader starts looking for some new form.

为了在我们的研讨会中提高这种意识，我们有时会用十个新项目重复之前的练习以进行排名。我们还引入了目标的微小变化，让团队选择自己最好的组织结构。例如，我们可能会告诉他们，如果排名超过 10 分钟或他们的得分低于 60，则排名毫无价值。这个目标可能会使他们倾向于某种形式的投票。

To raise this awareness in our workshops, we sometimes repeat the previous exercise with ten new items to rank. We also introduce a slight change in goal and let the teams choose their own best organizational structure. For instance, we might tell them that the ranking is worthless if it takes longer than ten minutes or they score less than 60. This goal might bias them toward some form of vote.

如果将最低分数提高到 75，他们以前的经验告诉他们，投票几乎肯定会落空。这些团队经常赌一把，任命一位在之前的练习中表现出色的领导者，然后回答这位领导者对信息的明确要求。这个组织大约有一半成功，但投票几乎总是失败。

If the minimum score is raised to 75, their previous experience tells them that a vote is almost certain to miss the mark. The teams often gamble on appointing a leader who did well on the previous exercise, then answering this leader’s explicit requests for information. This organization succeeds about half the time, but voting almost always fails.

如果最低分提高到90分，但时间限制放宽到三十分钟，各队一般采用共识形式。利用他们对共识的更好理解，他们可以在大约 70% 的时间里获得 90 分。当然，如果他们觉得自己有世界纪录方面的专家，他们可能会选择一个强有力的领导者，并就一些薄弱环节进行深入讨论，但很少有这样的专家。

If the minimum score is raised to 90, but the time limit is relaxed to thirty minutes, the teams generally adopt a consensus format. Using their improved understanding of consensus, they can achieve a score of 90 about seventy percent of the time. Of course, if they felt they had an expert in world records, they might opt for a strong leader, with extended discussion on a few weak points, but there seldom is such an expert.

有时，我们会在团队已经开始解决问题后引入新目标。诀窍不是知道最好的方法，而是知道在当前情况下最好的方法。如果情况发生变化，组织就需要改变。没有一个组织是永远最好的，也没有一个组织能长期保持最佳状态。最有效的领导者是那些帮助团队识别环境何时发生变化并找到适合的新组织的领导者。

Sometimes, we introduce the new goal after the teams have already begun to work on the problem. The trick is not to know the best method, but the best method under the present circumstances. If circumstances change, the organization needs to change. No organization is best all the time, and no organization even remains best for very long. The most effective leaders are the ones who help the team to recognize when circumstances change and to find a new organization that fits.

附录：评分排名

APPENDIX: SCORING THE RANKING

如果您自己尝试排名，这里是吉尼斯世界纪录中的实际排名。它们以计分表的形式排列，因此您可以将您的排名技巧与本章中给出的数字进行比较。

In case you tried the ranking yourself, here are the actual rankings as found in The Guinness Book of World Records. They’re arranged in the form of a scoring sheet, so you can compare your ranking skills with the numbers given in the chapter.

排名世界纪录——计分表

Ranking World Records—Scoring Sheet

通过将您自己的排名发布到 B 列开始计算。然后计算每个平方差并在右侧列中发布。添加这些方块以获得 x 的值，您可以使用它来推导 y、z 和 q，即您的质量得分。

Begin the computation by posting your own rankings to column B. Then compute each squared difference and post in the right-hand column. Add these squares to obtain the value of x, which you can use to derive y, z, and q, your quality score.

第 4 棵 366 英尺的树

1st 197ft Ferris wheel

7th 663ft Mobile crane

5th 444ft Frisbee throw

8th 935ft Dam

3rd 298ft Bar

计算

Computation

  1. 计算得分排名和实际排名之间的差异
  2. compute the differences between scored-rank and actual
  3. 平方这些差异
  4. square those differences
  5. x = 这些平方差的总和
  6. x = sum of those squared differences
  7. y = x/165
  8. y = x/165
  9. z = 1.00–y
  10. z = 1.00–y
  11. q = 100 z
  12. q = 100 z

q 是质量得分

q is the QUALITY SCORE

问题

QUESTIONS

  1. 想一想你所属的某个群体。这个小组使用多少种不同的组织形式？每种形式如何与其所使用的任务相关联？
  2. Think of some group to which you belong. How many different forms of organization does this group use? How does each form relate to the tasks it’s used for?
  3. 回想一下您上次使用投票来做出集体决定的情况。它是避免冲突的工具吗？有用吗？如何修改投票算法以使其更好地工作？
  4. Recall the last time you used voting to make a group decision. Was it a device for avoiding conflict? Did it work? How could the voting algorithm have been modified to work better?
  5. 当你为团队中的其他人做决定时，你怎么知道自己做得好不好？你会写下你的决定并在以后回顾它们是否被证明是明智的吗？你问小组中的人这些决定对他们有何影响？如果不是，为什么不呢？
  6. When you are making decisions for other people in a group, how do you know if you are doing a good job? Do you write down your decisions and review them later to see if they proved to be wise? Do you ask the people in the group how the decisions affected them? If not, why not?
  7. 在没有任命领导者的团队中工作时，您通常是隐式还是明确选择领导者？用什么方法？尝试以鼓励更充分参与的方式改变该方法。记下发生的事情。
  8. When working in a group without an appointed leader, do you ordinarily select a leader implicitly or explicitly? by what method? Try to alter that method in a way that will encourage fuller participation. Make notes of what happens.
  9. 下次你在小组中工作时，请征得其他成员的同意，让他们成为小组的过程观察员。如果他们同意，您将记录该小组在其任务的各个阶段中不断发展的组织。每隔一段时间，按照商定的时间间隔，您将向他们报告您观察到的情况。如果他们不同意，问问自己他们为什么不同意。无论如何都要记下笔记，但不要报告它们，除了你的日记。然后，再找一个你可以得到小组许可的机会。
  10. Next time you’re working in a group, ask the other members’ permission to be a process observer to the group. If they agree, you will take notes on the evolving organization of the group as it works through various phases on its task. Every so often, at agreed-upon intervals, you will report to them on what you observed. If they don’t agree, ask yourself why they didn’t. Take the notes anyway, but don’t report on them, except to your journal. Then, find another occasion when you can get the group’s permission.
  11. 下次你负责一个小组时，选择一个人作为过程观察员，就像在上一个练习中一样。特请观察员作为指定领导，对您的工作进行观察和汇报。
  12. Next time you’re in charge of a group, select someone to be a process observer, as in the previous exercise. Ask the observer particularly to observe and report on your work as appointed leader.
  13. 组织使用不同的术语来指定主持会议的人。一些常用术语是领导者、主席、主持人和促进者。您的组织使用哪个术语？为什么？这个词对你意味着什么？
  14. Organizations use different terms to designate the person who leads a meeting. Some common terms are leader, chair, moderator, and facilitator. Which term does your organization use? Why? What does the term mean to you?

第 18 章有效组织的障碍

Chapter 18. Obstacles to Effective Organizing

*在日常生活中的任何普通紧急情况下，您更愿意

*In any of the ordinary emergencies of everyday life, do you prefer to

  1. 发号施令负责
  2. give orders and be responsible
  3. 接受命令并提供帮助
  4. take orders and be helpful

—性格测试题*

—Personality Test Question*

假设您想知道自己是否可以成为出色的组织者。解决这个问题的一种方法是进行性格测试。我最近做了这样一个测试，我发现了前面的问题。这让我开始思考有效组织的真正障碍是什么。测试开始时的说明说

Suppose you wanted to know if you could be a great organizer. One way of approaching this problem would be to take a personality test. I recently took such a test, where I found the preceding question. It led me to think about what were the true obstacles to effective organizing. The instructions at the start of the test said

*不要对任何问题考虑太久。

*Do not think too long about any question.

如果您不能决定问题的答案，请跳过它。*

If you can’t decide the answer to a question, skip it.*

牢记这些指示，我跳过了 166 个问题中的 114 个。我本来可以跳过更多，但我不想让心理学家认为我优柔寡断。当然没关系。我会被贴上“软弱无力”或更糟的标签，比如“不合作”。

Taking those instructions to heart, I skipped 114 questions out of 166. I would have skipped more, but I didn’t want the psychologist to think I was indecisive. It didn’t matter, of course. I would be labeled “wishy-washy” or something worse, like “uncooperative.”

事实上，我脾气暴躁，讨厌做决定，所以也许心理学家知道他们在做什么。作为一个潜在的组织者，“不合作”肯定会让我排在最后；wishy-washy”会让我完全退出比赛。

Actually, I am rather cantankerous and do hate making decisions, so perhaps the psychologists know what they’re doing. As a potential organizer, “uncooperative” would certainly put me in last place; wishy-washy” would put me out of the race entirely.

第一个障碍：玩大游戏

FIRST OBSTACLE: PLAYING THE BIG GAME

有些人认为，要成为组织者，您必须成为管理者。如果您相信这一点，并且您不是经理，那么您已经遇到了您的第一个大障碍。这些人说，要成为一名经理，你必须成为一名决策者。正如管理文本可能所说：

To be an organizer, some people believe, you have to be a manager. If you believe that, and if you’re not a manager, you’ve already encountered your first big obstacle. To be a manager, so these people say, you have to be a decision maker. As a management text might put it:

管理者的全部方向是解决问题和做出决策，而不是亲自执行实施所需的行动；通常这些动作是由其他人执行的。

A manager’s entire orientation is toward solving problems and making decisions rather than toward personally performing the actions necessary for implementation; usually the actions are carried out by others.

在这种管理模式中，有两种人：

In this model of management, there are two kinds of people:

  1. 组织者
  2. those who organize
  3. 那些被别人组织的
  4. those who are organized by others

如果你想成为组织者，你最好回答（a）本章开头心理学家提出的问题，因为你必须“发号施令，负责”。另一方面，你最好在大多数其他人回答 (b)“喜欢接受命令并乐于助人”的组织中工作。这种组织可能很难找到。我见过很多组织都有乐于助人的人；我也见过大多数人接受命令的组织；但我从来没有找到一个人们喜欢接受订单的地方。

If you want to be an organizer, you’d better answer (a) to the psychologist’s question posed at the beginning of the chapter, for you’ll have to “give orders and be responsible.” On the other hand, you’d better work in an organization where most of the other people answer (b), “like to take orders and be helpful.” This kind of organization might be difficult to find. I’ve seen many organizations full of people who like to be helpful; I’ve also seen organizations where most of the people take orders; but I’ve never found one where people like to take orders.

事实上，我很少遇到喜欢发号施令的人。我发现有些人不喜欢接受命令，他们认为只有两种选择：命令或被命令。这种错误的二分法导致了弗吉尼亚·萨提亚所说的“大游戏”：

In fact, I’ve rarely found people who like to give orders. What I have found is people who don’t like to take orders, and who believe there are only two choices: order or be ordered. This false dichotomy leads to what Virginia Satir calls “the Big Game”:

谁有权告诉谁该做什么？

Who’s got the right to tell whom what to do?

大游戏没有任何规则，除了“做任何你能逃脱的事情”。有些人通过试图让别人感到内疚或粉碎他们的自尊来玩。其他人则通过讨好强手来玩游戏，希望他们的一些“发言权”能够得到影响。有些玩“我很无助，你必须照顾我”的策略；一些游戏“我很聪明（老的，有经验的，男性的，白人的，有钱的，......）你必须听我说”；和其他人玩“我是如此不可预测，你一定会后悔给我发号施令的。” 最有效的 Big Game 策略之一是心理学家的策略：“如果你没有按照我认为你应该的方式回答问题，我可以给你贴上‘优柔寡断’、‘不合作’或‘社会心理变态’的标签。”

The Big Game is played without any rules except “do anything you can get away with.” Some people play by trying to make others feel guilty, or shattering their self-esteem. Others play by rubbing up to strong players, hoping some of their “right to tell” will rub off. Some play the “I’m so helpless you have to take care of me” strategy; some play “I’m so smart (old, experienced, male, white, rich, …) you must listen to me”; and others play “I’m so unpredictable, you’re sure to regret giving me orders.” One of the most effective Big Game strategies is the psychologist’s ploy: “If you don’t answer the questions the way I think you should, I can label you ‘indecisive’ or ‘noncooperative’ or ‘sociopsychopathic’.”

大型游戏是有效组织的一大障碍。在许多组织中，就像在许多家庭和其他人际关系中一样，人们忙于玩大游戏，没有时间去做组织设计的任务。

The Big Game is a big obstacle to effective organizing. In many organizations, as in many families and other human relationships, people are so busy playing the Big Game that they don’t have time to do what the organization was designed to do.

第二个障碍：像机器一样组织人

SECOND OBSTACLE: ORGANIZING PEOPLE AS IF THEY WERE MACHINES

Big Game 的基础是一种模型，它以程序员看待计算机的方式看待人，而不是将其视为可以以各种方式解释命令的智能决策者。你不问一台新电脑

Underlying the Big Game is a model that regards people the way programmers regard computers, not as intelligent decision makers who can interpret an order in various ways. You don’t ask a new computer

执行程序时，您更喜欢

When executing a program, do you prefer to

  1. 告诉程序员该做什么
  2. tell the programmer what to do
  3. 听从程序员的命令
  4. follow the programmer’s orders

这个问题当然很重要，但你希望所有的机器都听从命令。机器总是输掉大比赛。这就是为什么我们喜欢使用机器。

The question is certainly important, but you expect that all machines will follow orders. Machines always lose the Big Game. That’s why we like to use machines.

如果您是一名计算机程序员，您会习惯于让您的命令始终如一地执行。如果它们没有被执行，您从痛苦的经历中了解到问题通常不是机器坏了。更有可能的是，您的订单不正确。你说了一些并不意味着它似乎在说什么，但机器按字面意思解释了它。也许你说的是 STOP，但并不是说“立即停止”，而是“一到不会破坏我的文件的地方就停止”。

If you’re a computer programmer, you’re accustomed to having your orders carried out invariably. If they are not carried out, you’ve learned through bitter experience that the problem is usually not a broken machine. More likely, your orders are not correct. You’ve said something that didn’t mean what it seemed to say, but the machine interpreted it literally. Perhaps you said STOP, but didn’t mean “stop instantly,” but, rather, “stop as soon as you come to a place where my files won’t be destroyed by stopping.”

与机器不同，人类能够以各种方式解释 STOP 命令。这在保存文件的情况下是幸运的，而在 STOP 太慢的情况下则令人沮丧。当有人理解并服从他们的命令时，玩大游戏的人就会认为他们的好运是理所当然的。另一方面，当他们被误解时，他们确实注意到了挫败感，这让他们觉得其他人正试图在大赛中击败他们。

Unlike machines, humans are capable of interpreting a STOP command in various ways. This is fortunate in those circumstances where your files are saved, and frustrating in those situations where the STOP is too slow. People who play the Big Game take their good fortune for granted when someone understands and follows their orders. On the other hand, they do notice the frustration when they are misinterpreted, which makes them feel that other people are trying to beat them at the Big Game.

不玩大游戏的人可以接受别人不能总是完全理解他们的想法，这是为识别愚蠢命令并拒绝执行它们的能力付出的小代价。如果他们需要完美而不是良好的判断力，他们会得到一台机器来完成这项工作，如果一台机器可以通过编程来完成这项工作的话。

People who don’t play the Big Game can accept the idea that other people can’t always understand them perfectly, which is a small price to pay for the ability to recognize stupid orders and refuse to follow them. If they need perfection rather than good judgment, they get a machine for the job, if a machine can be programmed to do it.

将大游戏作为组织指南的问题在于，玩家可能会像对待机器一样对挫折做出反应——试图让他们的决定和命令更加严格和精确。将人视为机器是有效组织的另一大障碍。

The problem with the Big Game as a guide to organizing is that players are likely to respond to frustration the way they would with machines—by trying to make their decisions and orders even more rigid and precise. Regarding people as if they were machines is another big obstacle to effective organization.

虽然这种方法有时可能是有效的，但如果走得太远，它会导致不断扩展的标准和程序书籍作为组织的基础；随着这些书籍的精确度提高，它们的有效性也会降低。没有人花时间阅读它们，更不用说跟随它们了。忙于通过书面程序和备忘录进行组织的领导者很快就会与本应跟随他们的人失去联系。

Although this approach may sometimes be effective, when carried too far it leads to ever-expanding books of standards and procedures as the basis for organization; and as these books grow in precision, they diminish in effectiveness. Nobody ever takes the time to read them, let alone follow them. The leader who is busy organizing through written procedures and memos soon loses touch with the people who are supposed to be following them.

第三个障碍：自己做

THIRD OBSTACLE: DOING THE WORK YOURSELF

许多人认为大游戏是有效解决问题领导力的障碍，但一些创新的问题解决者走得更远，实际上蔑视大游戏玩家。这些独立的灵魂认为接受命令对他们的创造精神有害，如果他们足够创新，他们可以不玩。这让 Big Game 玩家陷入盲目愤怒，就像我的高尔夫朋友拒绝数杆数一样。

Many people see the Big Game as an obstacle to effective problem-solving leadership, but some innovative problem solvers go much further and actually hold Big Game players in contempt. These independent souls regard taking orders as harmful to their creative spirit, and if they are sufficiently innovative, they can get away with not playing. This drives Big Game players into a blind rage, much like my golfing friends when I refuse to count my strokes.

这样的创新者通常很难创造出让其他人从事这项工作的环境。一个原因是他们拒绝提供建议，以免他们的想法被误认为是大游戏中的命令。他们可能无法理解人们不会像计算机或混凝土搅拌机那样服从命令。人们响应命令，但他们也响应建议、表扬、提示、想法、鼓励、建议以及对他们的进展的明确反馈。为其他人组织工作需要不同的决策和发布命令的方法。

Such innovators often have a difficult time creating environments in which others do the work. One reason is that they refuse to offer suggestions, lest their ideas be mistaken for orders in the Big Game. They may fail to understand that people don’t follow orders the way computers or concrete mixers do. People respond to orders, but they also respond to suggestions, praise, hints, ideas, encouragement, recommendations, and clear feedback on their progress. Organizing work for other people requires different methods of making decisions and issuing orders.

缺乏有效的沟通方法是一个相对较小的障碍，可以通过培训和经验来克服。当创新者发现其他人在完成某项任务时遇到困难时，就会出现更大的障碍。无论原因是沟通不畅、缺乏技能、动力不足，还是对如何完成工作有不同的想法，创新者的第一个也是最强烈的冲动就是介入并为他们完成工作。为什么？因为，事实上，创新者可能会做得更好。在这种情况下，组织其他人解决问题的最大障碍是你自己以前作为问题解决者的成功。

The lack of effective communication methods is a relatively minor obstacle, one that can be overcome by training and experience. A bigger obstacle arises when the innovator perceives that the other people are having trouble doing some task. No matter whether the reason is poor communications, lack of skill, inadequate motivation, or a different idea of how the job should be done, the innovator’s first and strongest impulse is to step in and do the work for them. Why? Because, in fact, the innovator can probably do it better. In such cases, the greatest obstacle to organizing other people for problem solving is your own previous success as a problem-solver.

为什么这种干预会造成如此大的障碍？问题解决型领导的目的不就是想尽一切办法解决问题吗？这种观点的谬误在于“工作”的定义。领导者的工作通常不是解决单个问题，而是创造一个解决许多问题的环境，不仅为了今天，而且为了未来。

Why is this kind of intervention such a great obstacle? Isn’t it the purpose of problem-solving leadership to get the problem solved by whatever means necessary? The fallacy in this view lies in the definition of “the job.” The leader’s job is usually not to solve a single problem, but to create an environment in which many problems will be solved, not just for today, but for the future.

当然，这个问题不解决可能就没有未来，那么可能就需要介入解决。培训实习生的外科医生显然不能袖手旁观，让病人死去；实习生可以在下一个病人身上学习。同样，如果公司破产，项目经理也不能简单地让项目失败。如果发生这种情况，无论如何都没有人会找到工作，那么为下一个项目创建一个优秀的团队有什么意义呢？这个问题真的生死攸关吗？这可能是领导者最艰难的决定。

Of course, there may be no future unless this problem is solved, in which case it may be necessary to step in and solve it. A surgeon who is training an intern obviously cannot just stand by and let the patient die; the intern can learn on the next patient. Similarly, a project manager cannot simply let the project fail if the company will go bankrupt. If that happens, nobody will have a job anyway, so what’s the point of creating a great team for the next project? Is the problem really life-and-death? That may be the leader’s most difficult decision.

第四个障碍：奖励无效的组织

FOURTH OBSTACLE: REWARDING INEFFECTIVE ORGANIZING

也许我优柔寡断，因此缺乏领导才能，但我相信最好的领导者是很少（如果有的话）不得不做出决定的人，更不用说生死攸关的决定了。当面临移动或不移动流血患者的选择时，总是存在做出错误决定的风险。

Perhaps I’m indecisive and consequently poor leadership material, but I believe the best leader is the one who rarely, if ever, gets into a position of having to make a decision, let alone a life-and-death decision. When faced with the choice of moving or not moving the bleeding victim, there’s always the risk of a poor decision.

当然，如果你一开始就避免了事故，那么任何人都不需要做出决定，因此也就没有任何风险。所以也许一个更好的领导问题是：

Of course, if you’ve avoided the accident in the first place, there’s no decision to be made by anybody, and thus no risk at all. So perhaps a better leadership question is this:

如果你不得不和别人一起开车旅行，你会更喜欢这样的司机吗？

If you had to take a trip with someone else driving, would you prefer a driver who

  1. 从未发生过事故，但如果发生事故可能会优柔寡断
  2. has never had an accident, but would likely be indecisive if an accident occurred
  3. 平均每周发生一次事故，但非常善于在紧急情况下做出决定
  4. has had an average of one accident a week, but was very adept at making decisions in emergency situations

遗憾的是，很多人似乎更喜欢（b）。这就是为什么停战日被退伍军人节取代的原因。和平更难组织，战争更英勇。真正好的组织似乎缺乏戏剧性。

Sad to say, many people seem to prefer (b). That’s why Armistice Day has been replaced by Veterans Day. Peace is more difficult to organize, but war is more heroic. Really good organizing seems to lack drama.

为什么我们奖励那些通宵工作以消除他们在程序中犯下的错误的程序员，或者奖励那些为解决管理不善造成的危机而进行重大组织变革的经理？为什么不奖励那些设计得很好以至于没有出现严重错误的程序员，以及那些组织远离危机模式的经理呢？

Why is it that we reward programmers who work all night to remove the errors they put into their programs, or managers who make drastic organizational changes to resolve the crises their poor management has created? Why not reward the programmers who design so well that they don’t have dramatic errors, and managers whose organizations stay out of crisis mode?

组织不是解决问题，而是避免问题。一旦你陷入问题的痛苦之中，再进行真正有效的组织就太晚了。也许有效组织的最大障碍是我们渴望奖励无效组织。

Organizing is not about solving problems, but avoiding them. Once you’re in the throes of the problem, it’s too late to do really effective organizing. Perhaps the biggest obstacle to effective organizing is our eagerness to reward ineffective organizing.

有机组织

ORGANIC ORGANIZING

在一个典型的心理测验中，有很多像本章开头的问题。显然，如果每个人都给出相同的答案，这样的问题就不会很有用，并且心理学家设计他们的测试，以便大约一半的人会选择每个选项。因此，问题的存在表明，大约一半参加考试的人会说他们更喜欢发号施令，而另一半会说他们更喜欢接受命令。

In a typical psychological test, there are many questions like the one that heads this chapter. Obviously, such a question wouldn’t be very useful if everyone gave the same answer, and psychologists design their test so that approximately half the people will select each alternative. Therefore, the existence of the question says that approximately half the people who take the test will say they prefer to give orders and the other half will say they prefer to take orders.

如果您相信大型游戏，那么这个结果会令人惊讶。不是每个人都想发号施令，成为赢家吗？也许人们是随机回答的，因为他们不相信大游戏。也许他们甚至不知道这个问题是什么意思。摆脱这种困境的一种方法是注意这个问题还有第三种可能的答案：

This result is surprising if you believe in the Big Game. Doesn’t everyone want to give orders, to be a winner? Perhaps people are answering at random, because they don’t believe in the Big Game. Perhaps they don’t even know what the question means. One way out of this dilemma is to notice that there’s a third possible answer to the question:

*C。停止玩游戏并处理紧急情况。

*c. stop playing games and take care of the emergency.

我希望我永远不会在车祸中受伤，但如果我受伤了，我当然希望观众不要站在一旁看这场大比赛。相反，我希望他们足够成熟和训练有素，可以做必要的事情。我可能不太适合自己管理流程，所以我更喜欢能够自行管理的人，而无需我的命令。

I hope I’m never hurt in an auto accident, but if I am, I sure hope the spectators won’t stand around playing the Big Game. I hope, instead, that they’re sufficiently mature and trained to do what’s necessary. I may not be in very good shape to manage the process myself, so I’d much prefer people who are capable of managing themselves, without orders from me.

解决问题的领导力是基于这种组织模式。根据有机模型，大多数人都喜欢组织一些时间，也许有些时间被组织起来，视情况而定。发号施令和接受命令只是达到目的的手段，而不是目的本身。在最有效的组织中，每个人都根据完成工作的需要解决问题和做出决策。在这个模型中，组织由带头的任何人完成，领导者工作的更好定义是

Problem-solving leadership is based on this sort of model of organization. According to the organic model, most everyone likes to organize some of the time, and perhaps be organized some of the time, depending on the situation. Giving orders and taking orders are merely means to an end, not an end in themselves. In the most effective organizations, everyone is solving problems and making decisions, as required to get the job done. In this model, organizing is done by anyone who takes the lead, and a better definition of the leader’s job is

一个解决问题的领导者的整个方向是创造一个环境，在这个环境中每个人都可以解决问题、做出决定并执行这些决定，而不是亲自解决问题、做出决定并执行这些决定。

A problem-solving leader’s entire orientation is toward creating an environment in which everyone can be solving problems, making decisions, and implementing those decisions, rather than person- ally solving problems, making decisions, and implementing those decisions.

在这个模型中，组织不是创建一套严格的规则；而是组织。组织不是发号施令或接受命令；组织就是完成工作。

In this model, organizing is not creating a set of rigid rules; organizing is not giving orders or taking orders; organizing is getting the job done.

问题

QUESTIONS

I. 当你实际上试图通过指出一个团体组织不善来提供帮助时，你是否曾经被贴上“不合作”的标签？感觉如何？它对提升团队绩效是否有效？

I. Have you ever been labeled “uncooperative” when you were actually trying to be helpful by pointing out that a group wasn’t organized well? How did it feel? Was it effective in advancing the group’s performance?

  1. 当某人阻止您的团队继续工作时，您是否曾给某人贴上“不合作”的标签？这种方法的效果如何？还有什么其他方法可能更有效？
  2. Have you ever labeled someone “uncooperative” when that person was preventing your group from getting on with its work? How effective was this approach? What other approach might have been more effective?
  3. 你最后一次玩 Big Game 是什么时候？你是赢家、输家还是学习者？你喜欢吗？它是否推进了手头的任务？
  4. When was the last time you played the Big Game? Were you a winner, a loser, or a learner? Did you enjoy it? Did it advance the task at hand?
  5. 你是否曾经写过备忘录而不是与相关人员面对面交流？它会让你感觉好些吗？这会让他们感觉好些吗？你的备忘录有多有效？
  6. Do you ever write a memo rather than deal face-to-face with the people involved? Does it make you feel better? Does it make them feel better? How effective are your memos?
  7. 你能做些什么来改变订单、备忘录、不合作的员工和大游戏的其他症状的环境？你在做什么？
  8. What can you do to alter an environment of orders, memos, uncooperative workers, and other symptoms of the Big Game? What are you doing?
  9. 你最后一次接手你委派给别人的工作是什么时候？它让你感觉如何？这让对方感觉如何？它完成工作了吗？下次您将某事委托给那个人时，它会产生什么影响？
  10. When was the last time you stepped in to take over some job you had delegated to someone else? How did it make you feel? How did it make the other person feel? Did it get the job done? What effect did it have the next time you delegated something to that person?
  11. 你以什么方式奖励或因组织不力而受到奖励？你如何创造一个扭转这种趋势的环境？
  12. In what ways are you rewarding or being rewarded for ineffective organizing? How can you create an environment that reverses this tendency?

第 19 章学习做一个组织者

Chapter 19. Learning to Be an Organizer

对于我们所有在系统中工作的人来说，这是权力的头等奖：能够以增强我们系统在其环境中生存和发展的能力的方式行事。当我们能够做到这一点时，我们就知道我们是强大的。如果我们不能以这种方式影响系统，那么所有其他的权力陷阱——控制、支配、额外津贴、恐吓、报复、强硬路线、底线——所有这些都是无稽之谈；它们是权力的二等奖（或诱杀奖）；他们试图感觉强大或看起来强大；它们是不强大的后果。但系统权力的真正底线是：你能影响系统吗？您是否能够以帮助系统在其环境中更有效地应对和繁荣的方式行事？你可能是你所在组织的首席执行官，—Barry Oshry，电源和系统实验室

This is the first prize of power for all of us who work in systems: to be able to act in ways which enhance the capacity of our systems to survive and develop in their environment. When we’re able to do that, we know we’re powerful. If we cannot influence systems in this way, then all the other trappings of power—control, dominance, perquisites, intimidation, revenge, hard-lining, bottom-lining—all of these are nonsense; they are power’s second prizes (or booby prizes); they are attempts to feel powerful or look powerful; they are consequences of not being powerful. But the true bottom line of system power is this: Are you able to influence the system? Are you able to act in ways which help the system cope and prosper more effectively in its environment? You may be the chief executive of your organization, you may enjoy an astronomical salary and luxurious perquisites, you may be King or Queen of the hill intimidating and dominating all comers, but if you cannot influence the system so that it is better able to cope and prosper, you are working on second prize. —Barry Oshry, Power and Systems Laboratory

拿到一等奖怎么办？

How do you go after first prize?

实践

PRACTICE

第一个想法太明显了，我应该羞于把它写下来。我应该，但我不是，因为我们百分之九十的学生似乎不知道学习成为有效的组织者需要练习。还有更多的练习。也许他们害怕与别人的生活发生错误，所以他们想先掌握理论。理论固然重要，但不能避免所有的错误。关于如何组织人有许多相互矛盾的理论，如果没有实践经验作为指导，甚至无法理解它们。

The first idea is so obvious I should be ashamed to write it down. I should, but I’m not, because ninety percent of our students don’t seem to know that learning to be an effective organizer takes practice. And more practice. Perhaps they are afraid to make mistakes with other people’s lives, so they want to master the theory first. Theory is important, but it can’t prevent all mistakes. There are many conflicting theories about how to organize people, and they cannot even be understood without practical experience as a guide.

除非你已经有足够的实践经验来胜任那种你有权影响组织的工作，否则你怎么能获得实践经验呢？如果您发挥想象力，您可以在上百万种情况下与不同组织形式的人一起工作。在一次简短的头脑风暴会议中，一组客户从他们自己的活动中得出了以下列表：

How can you get practical experience unless you already have enough practical experience to qualify for the kind of job where you’re given the power to affect the organization? If you use your imagination, there are a million situations where you can work with people in different organizational forms. In a single brief brainstorming session, a group of clients came up with the following list from their own activities:

• 社区工作：为慈善机构筹款、经营旧货店、参与城市使命、经营回收中心、在动物控制中心工作、担任志愿消防员

• community work: raising money for charity, running a thrift store, working in the city mission, operating a recycling center, working in the animal control center, being a volunteer firefighter

• 政治工作：支持候选人、发起请愿活动、组织抗议活动

• political work: supporting a candidate, running a petition drive, organizing a protest

• 专业活动：计算机用户团体、专业协会、校友会

• professional activities: computer user groups, professional societies, alumni associations

• 与儿童一起工作：童子军领袖、少儿联盟教练、青年旅舍旅行领袖

• work with children: scout leader, little league coach, youth hostel trip leader

• 与体弱者一起工作：医院志愿者、疗养院志愿者、残疾人伴侣

• work with the infirm: hospital volunteer, nursing home volunteer, companion for the disabled

• 志愿协会：Lions、Elks、Toastmasters、Masons

• voluntary associations: the Lions, Elks, Toastmasters, Masons

• 教会工作：传教工作、外行咨询、委员会工作

• church work: missionary work, lay counseling, committee work

• 运动队：垒球、保龄球、篮球

• athletic teams: softball, bowling, basketball

在工作中也有类似的丰富机会：

On the job there is a similar wealth of opportunities:

• 参加课程并组织会议向您的工作组汇报

• attend courses and organize a meeting to report back to your work group

• 参加实验室培训

• attend laboratory-based training

• 成为某人的学徒，或收自己的学徒，或两者兼而有之

• become an apprentice to someone, or take an apprentice of your own, or both

• 成为一名培训师，这样您就可以练习组织课程

• be a trainer so you can practice organizing courses

• 成为一名导师，这样你就可以在一个人身上练习

• be a tutor so you can practice on one person

• 主持技术审查会议

• lead technical review meetings

• 领导团队会议，尝试不同的风格

• lead team meetings, trying different styles

简而言之，参与任何类型的活动，让您体验在不同组织形式中担任不同职位的感觉。如果您发现自己说，“呃，我无法忍受那样做”，那么这就是最好的起点。您将填补您体验中最大的漏洞。

In short, get involved in any sort of activity that lets you experience what it feels like to be in different positions within different organizational forms. If you find yourself saying, “Ugh, I couldn’t stand doing that,” then that’s the best place to start. You’ll be filling up the biggest hole in your experience.

观察和实验

OBSERVE AND EXPERIMENT

实践是最古老的学习方法，即使不特别努力去寻找组织经验，你也会发现自己每天都身处许多不同的组织中。如果这些看起来太熟悉而无法教给你任何东西，你需要培养一套更现代的学习技能：科学方法。

Practice is the oldest method of learning, and even without special efforts to find organizational experiences, you’ll find yourself involved in many different organizations every day. If these seem too familiar to teach you anything, you need to cultivate a more modern set of learning skills: the scientific method.

科学方法是一种奇特的方式，它告诉你不要满足于被动地体验组织：科学意味着观察和实验，它可以意味着像参加每周一次的小组会议一样简单，观察每个人坐在哪里，数一数每个人坐了多少次人说话，并注意谁提出问题以及谁（如果有人）回答问题。你将从这些观察中学到什么？我真的不知道，但我可以向你保证，所有尝试过对日常工作环境进行这种简单观察的人都学到了一些有用的东西，了解人们为不同的活动组织自己的方式。

The scientific method is a fancy way of telling you not to be content to experience organizations passively: Science means observe and experiment, and it can mean something as simple as going to your weekly group meeting and observing where everyone sits, counting how many times each person talks, and noting who asks questions and who, if anybody, answers them. What will you learn from such observations? I really don’t know, but I can assure you that everyone else who has tried this kind of simple observation of their everyday work environment has learned something useful about the way people organize themselves for different activities.

观察可以养成习惯。一旦你在工作中尝试过，你就会开始随时随地练习：等电梯、逛超市、看超级碗都会成为新的、更丰富的体验。

Observing can be habit forming. Once you’ve tried it on the job, you’ll begin to practice everywhere you go: Waiting for the elevator, shopping in the supermarket, and watching the Superbowl will all become new and richer experiences.

一旦你掌握了一些观察技巧，就可以尝试一些小实验。您可能会提前参加下一次会议并坐在不同的椅子上。这会给你一个不同的观察点，也会让你有机会看到什么样的“音乐椅”正在发展。或者，您可以尝试按照通常的模式重新布置房间。转动桌子。添加一把椅子，或删除一个。带上活动挂图，或者拿走所有的笔。

Once you’ve acquired some skill at observing, try some little experiments. You might come early to the next meeting and sit in a different chair. This will give you a different vantage point from which to observe, and will also give you a chance to see what kind of “musical chairs” develops. Or you might try rearranging the room from its usual pattern. Turn the table. Add a chair, or remove one. Bring in a flipchart, or take away all the pens.

这些实验是完全安全的，因为没有人需要知道你做过它们。随着您变得更加大胆，您可能想要出柜。在会议期间，您可能会拿起笔，开始在一直在那里但从未使用过的活动挂图上写下要点。或者，如果您始终是书写者，请将笔递给其他人。建议在立交桥变热时休息一下，或者建议在立交桥耗尽时更换座位。

These experiments are completely safe, because nobody need know you’ve done them. As you grow more bold, you may want to come out of the closet. During the meeting, you might grab a pen and start writing important points on the flip chart that’s always been there but never been used. Or if you’re always the one who’s writing, offer the pen to someone else. Suggest a break when the interchange gets heated, or suggest that the seating be changed when the interchange is running out of steam.

实验和观察一样，是习惯的形成。当你等电梯的时候，你可能会看看你是否可以引导你的同行者按顺序登机，以避免后面的人想先出去的问题。当您为下一次家庭出游打包汽车时，您可能会看到如果让孩子们做出一些决定会发生什么。

Experimentation, like observation, is habit forming. When you wait for the elevator, you might see if you can induce your fellow travelers to board in a sequence that will avoid the problem of the people in back wanting to get out first. When you pack the car for the next family outing, you might see what happens if you let the kids make some of the decisions.

寻找不一致之处：他们正在尽力而为

LOOK FOR INCONGRUENCE: THEY’RE DOING THE BEST THEY CAN

当您观察一个团体的组织方式时，您会寻找什么？要遵循的最佳指南之一是寻找不一致之处，即事物的本来面目与外观之间的差异。例如，新手观察者通常会将正式的权力结构误认为是真正的组织。他们看到了他们应该看到的东西，而不是观察和假设之间的不一致。

What do you look for when you observe the way a group is organized? One of the best guides to follow is to look for incongruence, a difference between the way things are and the way they look. For instance, novice observers usually mistake the formal power structure for the real organization. They see what they’re supposed to see, rather than the incongruence between what is and what is supposed to be, between observation and assumption.

例如，许多人认为组织就是组织结构图上的内容。通过注意人们如何真正互动来检验这个假设，而不是图表上所说的。会议的领导者真的决定了谁将发言，还是其他成员默默点头做出选择？人们是真的通过渠道获取信息，还是有自己的非正式渠道？

For instance, many people assume that the organization is what’s on the organization chart. Test this assumption by noticing how the people really interact, as opposed to what the chart says. Does the leader of the meeting really decide who will speak next, or does some other member make the choice with a silent nod of the head? Do people really go through channels to get information, or do they have their own informal channels?

基于种子模型，我对组织有一些最喜欢的假设。这些假设中最重要的是每个人都希望感到自己有用——为组织做出贡献。根据实际观察，这可能是一个难以成立的假设。如果一个组织中有这么多人看起来冷漠，甚至具有破坏性，那么这个假设肯定有问题。如果每个人都那么热切地想要做好事，为什么那么多人总是那么痛苦呢？

I have some favorite assumptions of my own about organization, based on the seed model. The most important of those assumptions is that everyone wants to feel useful—to make a contribution to the organization. This can be a difficult assumption to hold, in the light of actual observations. If so many people within an organization seem apathetic, or even destructive, something must be wrong with the assumption. If everyone wants so intensely to do good, how come so many people are so miserable all the time?

我的朋友斯坦格罗斯有一个有用的方法来处理他的感受，即人们没有试图做出贡献。他对自己说：“在这种情况下，他们都在尽力而为。如果我认为他们没有尽力而为，那我就不明白当时的情况。”

My friend Stan Gross has a useful device for dealing with his feelings that people are not trying to contribute. He says to himself: “They’re all doing the best they can, under the circumstances. If I don’t think they are doing the best they can, then I don’t understand the circumstances.”

这些“情况”通常是组织与要完成的任务之间的一些不一致。

These “circumstances” are usually some incongruence between the organization and the task to be accomplished.

但是出了什么问题？当你身处其中时，并不总是很容易清楚地看到原因。当我密切参与时，我会尝试假设问题解决领导力的三个基本功能之一出了问题：

But what has gone wrong? It’s not always easy to see the reason clearly when you’re involved in the situation. When I’m closely involved, I try to look at things assuming that something has gone wrong with one of the three essential functions of problem-solving leadership:

• 定义问题

• defining the problem

• 管理思想流

• managing the flow of ideas

• 控制质量

• controlling the quality

团体可能会功能失调，就像个人一样，因为他们当前的问题采用了错误的组织方式。我通常可以通过假设组织的存在是为了解决一些问题来理解功能失调的群体，尽管这可能不是手头的问题。

Groups can be dysfunctional, just like individuals, with the wrong kind of organization for their current problem. I can often understand a dysfunctional group by making the assumption that the organization exists to solve some problem, though it may not be the problem at hand.

通常，问题就像小组之前解决过的问题，因此进行一些历史研究可能会发现他们真正组织起来要解决的问题。例如，一个需要高质量共识决策的团队可能会围绕一个专制领导者组织起来，因为该团队以前在一个有利于快速决策而不是高质量决策的环境中解决问题。一旦有人意识到群体结构如何受到其历史的影响，他们就会明白需要某种其他形式的组织，至少对于目前的问题而言。

Frequently, the problem is like one that the group solved previously, so a little historical research may unearth what they’re really organized to solve. For example, a group that needs a high-quality consensus decision might be organized around an autocratic leader because the group formerly solved problems in an environment that favored fast decisions over high-quality decisions. Once someone realizes how the group structure has been affected by their history, they can see that some other form of organization is needed, at least for the present problem.

寻找交叉线

LOOK FOR CROSSED WIRES

你显然不能研究一个新成立的团体的历史。在一个新的团队中，组织可能只是错误的结果，如以下类比所示。

You obviously cannot study the history of a newly formed group. In a new group, the organization can simply be the result of a mistake, as the following analogy demonstrates.

在经历了内布拉斯加州十个寒冷的冬天后，丹妮和我忍无可忍，买了一条电热毯。我们等了这么久的原因之一是 Dani 和我对最佳睡眠温度的概念截然不同。双重控制的可用性使 Dani 相信我们每个人都可以拥有自己的私人温度，因此她为我们买了第一条电热毯。即便如此，仍然怀疑这种新奇的东西永远不会奏效。

After suffering needlessly through ten cold Nebraska winters, Dani and I broke down and bought an electric blanket. One of the reasons we waited so long was that Dani and I have very different concepts about what is the optimal sleeping temperature. The availability of dual controls convinced Dani that each of us could have our own private temperature, so she bought our first electric blanket. Even so, was still suspicious that the newfangled thing would never work.

果然，我们第一次使用它时，Dani 在烤的时候冻住了。我整晚都在摆弄控制器，当天亮时，我发现丹妮也做了同样的事情。我们愤愤不平地把那条破毯子带回西尔斯，要求我们换一条没有破损的新毯子。推销员向我们解释说，就像其他许多人一样，我们已经交叉连接了控件，他表现得非常克制。

Sure enough, the very first time we used it, Dani froze while roasted. I fiddled with the controls all night, and when morning finally came, I discovered that Dani had done the same thing. We indignantly brought the confounded blanket back to Sears and demanded that we get a new one that wasn’t broken. The salesman showed remarkable restraint when he explained to us that, like so many other people, we had cross-connected the controls.

当 Dani 和 Jerry 试图在交叉控制的电热毯下睡觉时会发生什么？控制设置范围从 1 到 10。我们开始睡觉时将控制设置为 5。在夜间的某个时候，Dani 觉得这有点酷，所以她伸出手将控制从 5 调到 6。不幸的是，她的控制连接到我这边的毯子上，所以我开始觉得有点暖和。最终，我伸出手，将控制旋钮调低到 4，这样可以让 Dani 的床边冷却下来。然后她将控制旋钮调到 7。我变得更热了；我把我的降到 3。她变冷了；她把她的调到 8。最终，她的控制是 10，而我的是关闭的。我们已经到了最痛苦的地步。

What happens when Dani and Jerry try to sleep under an electric blanket with crossed controls? The control settings ranged from 1 to 10. We started sleeping with the controls set at 5. At some time during the night, Dani decided that it was a trifle cool, so she reached out and turned up her control from 5 to 6. Unfortunately, her control was connected to my side of the blanket, so I started to feel a little warm. Eventually, I reached out and turned my control down to 4, which had the effect of cooling Dani’s side of the bed. She then turned her control up to 7. I got hotter; I turned mine down to 3. She got colder; she turned hers up to 8. Eventually, her control was at 10, and mine was turned off. We had reached the point of maximum suffering.

总的来说，情况可以这样描述：Dani 和 Jerry 都想在晚上过得更舒服。他们同意购买电热毯来尝试解决这个问题。他们每个人都尽自己最大的能力操作新毯子，但结果却是双方都痛苦不堪。在这个故事中，你找不到丝毫动力不足的暗示。相反，你可以争辩说，正是他们强烈的追求舒适的共同动机导致了所有的不适，因为毯子连接错误。

Overall, the situation can be described this way: Dani and Jerry both want to be more comfortable at night. They agree to buy an electric blanket to try to solve this problem. They each operate the new blanket to the best of their ability, yet the result is misery for both. Nowhere in this story do you find the slightest hint of poor motivation. On the contrary, you could argue that it was their intense shared motivation for comfort that led to all the discomfort, given that the blanket was connected wrong.

这也不是问题定义不同的情况。丹妮和杰瑞想要同样的东西。出了问题的是他们对毯子的实际连接方式以及他们工作的组织的理解不足。换句话说，他们的电线交叉了，推销员可以通过假设他们正在尽力而为并观察他们是否成功来确定这一点。

Nor was this a case of differing problem definitions. Both Dani and Jerry wanted the same thing. What went wrong was their inadequate understanding of how the blanket was actually connected, the organization under which they were working. In other words, they had their wires crossed, which the salesman could determine by assuming that they were trying to do the best they could and observing their lack of success.

合法化差异

LEGITIMIZE DIFFERENCES

人们不必为了交叉电线而被电热毯连接，也不必进入他们的卧室观察他们在不知不觉中交叉工作。很多问题的产生是因为人是不同的，但他们的组织形式并没有考虑到这些差异。例如，基于荣格的 Myers-Briggs 人格理论（见参考书目）指出，人们在四个主要领域有不同的偏好：

People don’t have to be connected by electric blankets for their wires to be crossed, nor do you have to enter their bedrooms to observe them unknowingly working at cross purposes. Much of the trouble arises because people are different, yet their organizational forms are not designed to take those differences into account. For instance, the Jungian-based Myers-Briggs personality theory (see the Bibliography) says that people have different preferences in four major areas:

• 社会的

• social

• 信息性的

• informational

• 决策

• decision making

• 行动

• action

例如，在信息维度上，该理论认为一些人（N 人）更喜欢以直观的方式获取信息，而其他人（S 人）更喜欢具体的、具体的数据。在 S 组织的会议中，N 可能会因为“过度使用数据”而感到厌烦。如果 S 将 N 的反应解释为缺乏理解，他们可能会通过提供更多数据来证明他们的观点。N 一家变得更无聊了，他们开始骑电热毯了。

On the informational dimension, for instance, the theory says that some people (the N’s) prefer to get their information in an intuitive way, while others (the S’s) prefer to have specific, concrete data. In a meeting organized by S’s, the N’s may be bored by “an overkill of data.” If the S’s interpret the N’s reaction as poor understanding, they may respond by presenting even more data to prove their point. The N’s become even more bored, and they’re off on an electric blanket cycle.

类似的循环可以从 N 组织的会议开始。S 会不断恳求“更多的事实来支持你的结论”，N 可能会将其解释为试图阻挠会议。摆脱这种循环的唯一有效方法是承认它们的存在，然后发展一个承认这两种需求都是合理的组织。例如，你可能会组织下一次会议，让 S 在会议开始前手上就有成堆的事实和数据。

A similar cycle can start in a meeting organized by N’s. The S’s will constantly plead for “more facts to support your conclusions,” which the N’s may interpret as attempts to obstruct the meeting. The only effective way out of such cycles is to recognize their existence, then develop an organization that acknowledges both needs as legitimate. For instance, you might organize the next meeting so that the S’s will have piles of facts and figures in their hands before the meeting ever starts.

Myers-Briggs 模型可以有效地帮助观察组织中人与人之间发生的事情，并指导进行调整以使组织更有效。潜在的组织者最好研究一下请理解我或其他关于此模型的优秀书籍之一。

The Myers-Briggs model is an effective aid to observing what’s going on between people in an organization, and guide to making adjustments that render the organization more effective. The would-be organizer would do well to study Please Understand Me or one of the other excellent books about this model.

以自己为团队的榜样

USE YOURSELF AS A MODEL OF THE TEAM

另一个可以洞察组织的地方是你自己的头脑：现代大脑研究告诉我们，我们的大脑不是一个单一的整体器官，而更像是一组相互依存的大脑，每个大脑都有自己的才能、偏好和弱点。您对自己的内部流程了解得越多，您就越能够将它们用作团队中正在发生的事情的模型。

Another place to go for insights on organization is inside your own head: Modern brain research tells us that our brain is not a single monolithic organ, but more like a group of interdependent brains, each with its own talents, preferences, and weaknesses. The more aware you become of your own internal processes, the more you’ll be able to use them as a model of what’s going on in groups.

例如，Myers-Briggs 模型表明，人们喜欢在何处给电池充电——从内部来源（I 型）或外部来源（E 型）。但如果你注意自己的大脑，就不需要理论来告诉你这一点。独处的需要和与他人相处的需要之间的冲突，你难道没有感到过吗？如果是这样，您将能够更好地识别希望自己解决问题的人与希望延长问题解决会议戏剧性的人之间的这种冲突。

For instance, the Myers-Briggs model says that people differ in where they prefer to recharge their batteries—from internal sources (I type) or external sources (E type). But you don’t need the theory to tell you this if you pay attention to your own brain. Haven’t you ever felt a conflict between the need to be alone and the need to be with other people? If so, you’ll be better able to recognize such a conflict between people who want to work on a problem by themselves and those who wish to prolong the drama of a problem-solving meeting.

随成功而改变

CHANGE AS YOU SUCCEED

一旦开始观察周围发生的事情，就会有很多方法可以学习如何成为有效的组织者。您如何才能找到最适合您的方式？一种方法是将大脑的群体模型与群体解决问题历史的想法一起应用。与其试图重组你的大脑以适应任务，不如问问你自己，你的大脑最适合做什么类型的任务。然后选择适合自己才能和喜好的方法。

Once you start observing what is going on around you, there is no shortage of ways to learn how to be an effective organizer. How can you discover those ways that suit you best? One way is to apply the group model of brains along with the idea of a group’s problem-solving history. Instead of trying to reorganize your brain to suit the task, ask yourself what kinds of tasks your brain is best organized to do. Then choose a method that suits your own talents and preferences.

团队需要随着问题的变化而变化，你也一样。了解组织可以为您提供强大的变革力量，但变革会产生差异。有一天你可能会醒来发现你必须改变你无能为力的旧形象。

A group needs to change as problems change, and so do you. Learning about organizations can offer you great power to make changes, but change produces difference. One day you may wake to discover that you must revise your old image of being powerless.

现在你将面临在靠自己的力量创造的新环境中生存的问题。随着你个人力量的增长，你每一个小行为的后果也会随之增长。当你提出建议时，人们可能会把它当作一项任务。当您表示怀疑时，他们可能会将其解释为否决权。当你微笑时，是认可；当你皱眉时，毁灭。

Now you will be faced with the problem of living in the new environment created by your own power. As your personal power grows, so grow the consequences of your every little act. When you make a suggestion, people may take it as a mandate. When you express doubt, they may interpret it as a veto. When you smile, it’s approval; when you frown, devastation.

随着新的权力而来的是学习使用这些权力的新方法的责任。矛盾的是，你拥有的权力越大，就越难做你必须做的事情来学习这些新方法。如果人们认为你很有权势，就很难在不影响的情况下进行观察。小实验不再安全，因为你所做的一切都有可能产生巨大影响。即使你认为自己什么都没做，人们也会注视着你的一举一动。并测试你的反应。

With new powers comes the responsibility to learn new ways to use those powers. Paradoxically, the greater the power you have, the harder it becomes to do what you have to do to learn those new ways. If people consider you powerful, it becomes difficult to observe without influencing. Little experiments are no longer safe, because everything you do threatens to have great effects. Even when you think you aren’t doing anything, people will be watching your every move. And testing your reactions.

当你的力量增长时，你就关闭了这个圈子。观察者成为被观察者；实验者，受试者。你可能不喜欢去想它，但如果你不去想，权力就会变得瘫痪。第 20 章将介绍您可以对此做些什么。

As you grow in power, you close the circle. The observer becomes the observed; the experimenter, the subject. You may not like to think about it, but if you don’t, power can become paralysis. Chapter 20 will look at what you can do about it.

问题

QUESTIONS

  1. 你有多少时间花在思考权力的陷阱上或根据权力的陷阱采取行动？你喜欢 Oshry 所说的诱杀奖品足以证明这段时间是合理的吗？
  2. How much of your time do you spend thinking about or acting upon the trappings of power? Do you enjoy what Oshry calls booby prizes enough to justify this amount of time?
  3. 列出所有你练习组织能力的机会。再列出一份你可能拥有但没有使用的所有机会。有什么理由让你不能从这个列表中选择一项并去做吗？可以通过有效地组织自己来克服这个原因吗？
  4. Make a list of all the opportunities you have to practice your organizing skills. Make another list of all the opportunities you could have, but aren’t using. Is there some reason you can’t pick one item from this list and go for it? Could that reason be overcome by effectively organizing yourself?
  5. 列出所有你必须观察和尝试组织的机会。再列出一份你可能拥有但没有使用的所有机会。有什么理由让你不能从这个列表中选择一项并去做吗？
  6. Make a list of all the opportunities you have to observe and experiment with organizing. Make another list of all the opportunities you could have, but aren’t using. Is there some reason you can’t pick one item from this list and go for it?
  7. 回想一下你尽力而为的情况，但这被解释为“不想做出贡献”。你感觉怎么样？你试图解释自己吗？发生了什么？
  8. Recall some occasion when you were doing the best you could, but this was interpreted as “not trying to contribute.” How did you feel? Did you try to explain yourself? What happened?
  9. 回想一下您认为某人没有做出贡献的时间之一。试着用这个人在这种情况下尽最大努力来解释这种情况。那个人试图解决什么问题？下次发生这种情况时，您是否有某种原因无法与此人核实？
  10. Recall one of the times you thought someone wasn’t contributing. Try to explain that situation in terms of the person’s doing the best possible job, under the circumstances. What problem was that person trying to solve? Is there some reason you can’t check that out with the person the next time it happens?
  11. 你最后一次与工作中的人发生真正激烈的争论是什么时候？有没有可能你的电线交叉了？您将来如何防止这种情况发生？（什么？你从来没有在工作中与某人进行过真正好的争论？你要么是一个伟大的领导者，要么你已经死了。）
  12. When was the last time you got into a really good argument with someone at work? Is it possible that you got your wires crossed? How could you prevent that in the future? (What? You never got into a really good argument with someone at work? You’re either a great leader or you’re dead.)

7 采用某种形式的 Myers-Briggs 类型指标，例如请理解我中的自测。如果可能，找出你的同事的类型并讨论你的不同偏好如何给你在组织工作时带来问题和机会。

7 Take some form of the Myers-Briggs Type Indicator, such as the self-test found in Please Understand Me. If possible, find out the types of your co-workers and discuss how your different preferences give you problems and opportunities in organizing your work.

  1. 你和自己有过争论吗？下次你这样做时，写下论点。不同的方面代表谁？同样的争论是否发生在其他人身上？
  2. Do you ever have arguments with yourself? Next time you do, write down the argument. Whom do the different sides represent? Does this same argument ever happen with other people?

第五部分：转型

PART_FIVE: TRANSFORMATION

要成为一名技术领导者，仅仅了解什么是领导力是不够的。无论你在智力上知道多少，你都会发现自己在改变自己的实际现实中受到了严峻的考验。要想在转型中幸存下来，您必须自然而然地具有积极性、组织性和创新性。

To become a technical leader, it’s not sufficient to know what goes into leadership. No matter how much you know intellectually, you will find yourself sorely tested by the practical realities of changing yourself. To survive the transformation, you must, quite naturally, be motivated, organized, and innovative.

在这最后一节中，我们将研究当您尝试改变自己的行为时，其他人将如何对您进行测试，如何测试自己作为预测困难的一种方式，如何计划改变，如何抽出时间来实施您的计划，以及当计划出现问题时如何获得他人的支持。

In this final section, we’ll examine how other people will put you to the test as you try to change your behavior, how to test yourself as a way of anticipating difficulties, how to plan for change, how to find time to implement your plan, and how to obtain the support of other people when the plan goes awry.

第 20 章你将如何被评为领导者

Chapter 20. How You Will Be Graded as a Leader

忧虑和忧虑的鸟儿飞过你的头顶，这是你无法改变的；但是它们会在你的头发上筑巢，这是你可以预防的。——中国谚语

That the birds of worry and care fly above your head, this you cannot change; but that they build nests in your hair, this you can prevent.—Chinese Proverb

过渡总是困难的。游戏有一套全新的规则，您没有规则手册。例如，我们一生都在学校接受评估，因此我们研究规则并学习如何应对糟糕的评估。然后我们离开学校。得到不好的评价已经够糟糕了，但是当我们试图改进时，我们会沮丧地发现一套不同的规则。

Transitions are always difficult. There’s a whole new set of rules to the game, and you don’t get a rule book. For instance, we spend a lifetime being evaluated in school, so we study the rules and we learn how to cope with poor evaluations. Then we leave school. It’s bad enough to get poor evaluations, but when we try to improve, we are devastated to find a different set of rules.

以几乎相同的方式，我们花了一生作为追随者来评价，只是为了了解其他人使用非常不同的规则来评价领导者。回想一下您的学生时代以及您如何害怕上课的第一天。然后乘以 100，您就会知道当您开始展示自己作为潜在领导者时将面临的问题。

In much the same way, we spend a lifetime being evaluated as followers, only to learn that other people use very different rules to evaluate leaders. Think back to your student days and how you dreaded the first day of class. Then multiply by 100, and you’ll have some idea of the problems you’ll face when you start putting yourself forward as a potential leader.

教授上课的第一天

THE PROFESSOR’S FIRST DAY OF CLASS

为什么这么难？首先，你的新角色更像是教授而不是学生。每个学生对一个人的问题负责，但每个教授都觉得对许多人负责。这就是球员和教练的区别。如果你认为学生害怕上课的第一天，你应该和教授住在一起。

Why is it so difficult? In the first place, your new role is more like the professor’s than the student’s. Each student is responsible for one person’s problems, but each professor feels responsible for many. This is the difference between the player and the coach. If you think students dread the first day of classes, you should live with a professor.

以下是丹妮 (Dani) 担任大学人类学教授时的情况。每个学期，当丹妮回到常春藤覆盖的大厅时，都是同样的故事：学生进错教室，学生拿掉卡片，学生拿添加卡片，学生买错教科书并在上面写下自己的名字，学生谁没有先决条件，但想通过特别许可被允许参加课程，教室太小，座位不够，窗户紧闭，朝南。

Here’s how it was for Dani, when she was a university professor of anthropology. Each semester when Dani heads back to the ivy-covered halls, it’s the same story over again: students in the wrong classroom, students with drop cards, students with add cards, students who bought the wrong textbook and wrote their name in it, students who don’t have the prerequisites but want to be allowed in the course by special permission, classrooms too small with not enough seats, and windows shut tight with a southern exposure.

我总是尽量在 Dani 上课的第一天安排一次旅行。然而，有一年，在那个艰难的日子里，我犯了一个错误，从咨询任务中返回家中。除了所有其他问题外，丹妮还必须在机场接我。我并不期待她的心情，尤其是我自己的一天并不完美。半天时间里，一群新任命的项目负责人似乎沉浸在自怜之中。

I always try to arrange a trip on Dani’s first day of classes. One year, however, I made the mistake of returning home from a consulting assignment on that difficult day. In addition to all her other problems, Dani had to meet me at the airport. I wasn’t looking forward to the mood she would be in, especially as my own day hadn’t been exactly perfect. Half of the day had been spent with a group of newly appointed project leaders who seemed to be wallowing in self-pity.

当飞机接近登机口时，我努力想出一些聪明而舒缓的开场白，但我疲惫的大脑无法胜任这项任务。当我们争先恐后地张开双臂互相问候时，我只能说：“你在学校过得愉快吗？” 显然，这个空洞的评论并没有起到作用。我们一路开车回家，没有再说一句话。

As the plane approached the gate, I struggled to think of some clever and soothing first words, but my weary brain wasn’t equal to the task. As we raced to greet each other with open arms, all I could utter was, “Did you have a nice day at school?” Evidently, this inane remark didn’t do the job. We drove all the way home without exchanging another word.

致命的问题

The fatal question

大约半个小时后，丹妮倒在沙发上喝了一口酒，长长地叹了一口气，说道：“你知道的，我想我可以忍受第一天发生的所有其他事情。”学校，要是他们不问那个致命的问题就好了。”

About half an hour later, after collapsing on the sofa, Dani took a sip of her wine, gave a long, deep sigh, and said,”You know, I think I could tolerate all the other things that happen on the first day of school, if only they didn’t ask that one fatal question.”

“那是什么问题？” 我问道，很高兴听到她没有失去说话的能力。

“And what question is that?” I asked, glad to hear she had not lost her powers of speech.

“你和我一样清楚。无论课程是什么，无论级别如何，总会有人说‘温伯格教授，我们将如何评分？

“You know as well as I do. No matter what the course, no matter what the level, someone always says ‘Professor Weinberg, how will we be graded?”

“那为什么这么可怕？”

“And why is that so terrible?”

“因为他们似乎只考虑成绩。难道他们不关心他们将得到什么样的教育吗？”

“Because they don’t seem to think of anything but grades. Don’t they care about what kind of education they’re going to get?”

“哦，我不知道。我认为关注成绩是完全合理的。如果你不想让他们关心成绩，你为什么要给他们打分？”

“Oh, I don’t know. I think it’s perfectly reasonable to be concerned about grades. If you don’t want them to be concerned about grades, why do you grade them?”

“哦，不是我觉得不合理，只是质疑从未停止过。如果我让他们知道他们将如何评分，他们会想要更详细的想法。如果我告诉他们成绩部分取决于期末考试，部分取决于论文，部分取决于家庭作业，部分取决于出勤率，他们想知道这对每件事有多大影响。”

“Oh, it’s not that I don’t think it’s reasonable, it’s just that the questioning never stops. If I give them an idea of how they’ll be graded, they want a more detailed idea. If I tell them the grade depends partly on the final exam, partly on the paper, partly on the homework, and partly on attendance, they want to know how much does it count for each thing.”

“那你怎么跟他们说？”

“So what do you tell them?”

“我告诉他们我不知道。我不能说这个正好是 30%，其他东西是 20%。再说了，我也加不上那么好，所以估计也达不到百分之一百。”

“I tell them I don’t know. I can’t say it’s exactly thirty percent for this, and twenty percent for something else. Besides, I can’t add that well so it probably wouldn’t come out to a hundred percent.”

“也许那是你的问题。”

“Maybe that’s your problem.”

“也许我的问题是什么。”

“Maybe what’s my problem.”

“您尝试通过添加来获得他们的成绩。”

“That you try to get their grades by adding.”

“好吧，我还能怎么做呢？”

“Well, how else would I do it?”

“好吧，我今天在咨询时遇到了同样的问题。项目负责人想知道如何进行绩效评估，所以我建议他们改用乘法。”

“Well, I had the same question today where I was consulting. The project leaders wanted to know how to do performance appraisals, so I suggested they use multiplication, instead.”

“我不明白，”丹妮说。“他们已经在抱怨成绩与现实世界不一样了。”

“I don’t understand,” Dani said. “They already complain that grades aren’t like the real world.”

“这正是我的观点。在现实世界中，你不应该通过将所有部分相加来为你的工作评分，而是通过乘法。如果你在工作的四个部分中每个部分的表现都达到了 80%，那么你的整体表现就不是 80%。” 我拿出我的计算器计算出 0.8 x 0.8 x 0.8 x 0.8 – 0.4096。“你看，这相当于整体表现的百分之四十左右。”

“That’s exactly my point. In the real world, you shouldn’t get graded on your job by adding up all the parts, but by multiplying. If you’re an eighty percent performer on each of four parts of your job, you’re not an eighty percent performer overall.” I took out my calculator and computed 0.8 x 0.8 x 0.8 x 0.8 – 0.4096. “You see, that amounts to about forty percent as an overall performance.”

领导者的乘法评分

Multiplicative grading for leaders

这些年来，我了解到丹妮对理论从来都不太感兴趣，尤其是我的理论，所以我准备好回答她的下一个问题。“这就是它在现实世界中的运作方式吗？”

Over the years, I’ve learned that Dani is never too impressed with theories, particularly mine, so I was prepared for her next question. “Is that the way it works in the real world?”

“我真的不能告诉你这就是它在正式评估系统中的工作方式，但对于处于领导地位的人来说，它肯定是非正式的工作方式，”我回答道。“尤其是技术领导者。您还记得 Minimaxi Software 的 Waldo 吗？毫无疑问，他是我见过的最合格的技术人员之一。他对他工作的计算机以及它们使用的语言和操作系统了如指掌。”

“I can’t really tell you that’s the way it works in formal appraisal systems, but it’s certainly the way it works informally for people in positions of leadership,” I replied. “Especially technical leaders. Do you remember Waldo at Minimaxi Software? He was without a doubt one of the most highly qualified technicians I ever met. He knew everything about the computers he worked on and the languages and the operating systems they used.”

“但他脾气暴躁，”丹妮说。

“But he had an uncontrollable temper,”Dani said.

“恰恰！他可能在技术知识上得到了 100% 的分数，但每隔一段时间他就会发疯，完全否定他可能因技术技能而获得的任何荣誉。由于 Waldo 脾气暴​​躁，再多的技术技能也无法弥补团队中其他人对 Waldo 的不信任。要评估他，你必须将他的技术技能得分乘以他的情感得分大概 50 分，这样你得到的不是平均分 75%，而是 50 分，这绝对是不及格的。”

“Precisely! He might have scored one hundred percent on technical knowledge, but every once in a while he went berserk and completely negated any credit he might have gotten for his technical skill. No amount of technical skill could compensate for the lack of trust that the rest of the group had in Waldo, because of his nasty temper. To evaluate him, you would have had to multiply his technical skills score of one times his emotional score of perhaps fifty, which would give you not an average of seventy-five percent, but a grade of fifty, which was a definite flunk.”

“这很有趣，”丹妮说。“它也走另一条路吗？”

“That’s interesting,” Dani said. “Does it go the other way, too?”

“还有什么办法？”

“What other way?”

“我在想同一支球队的菲利斯。她真的是一个很酷的顾客，但我认为她在技术上了解不多——也许 90% 是情感方面的，50%，也许是 60% 是技术技能方面的。”

“I was thinking of Phyllis on the same team. She was really a cool customer, but I don’t think she knew much technically—perhaps a ninety percent on emotion and fifty, maybe sixty, on technical skill.”

“嗯，”我说，“给她 60 分，她得 54 分，这又是一次不及格，但原因与沃尔多不同。作为一名成员，菲利斯可以为团队做一些有用的事情，但作为领导者，她无法赢得他们的尊重，因为她不太了解他们在做什么。”

“Well,” I said, “grant her the sixty, that would give her a score of fifty-four, which is another flunk but for a different reason than Waldo. Phyllis could do certain useful things for the team as a member, but as a leader, she couldn’t command their respect because she didn’t know much about what they were doing.”

“这对他们来说似乎有点不公平，”丹妮说。

“It seemed a little unfair of them,” Dani said.

“这可能不公平，但这是人们对其他自称是潜在领导者的人的反应方式。没有人说生活必须是公平的，尤其是当你试图争取更多的钱、更高的声望或更大的影响力时。寻找领导者行为中的缺陷是美国人的一大消遣。而且，解决问题的领导者要批评的领域是内容和过程的两倍。”

“It may be unfair, but that’s the way people react to other people who put themselves forward as potential leaders. Nobody said that life had to be fair, particularly when you’re trying for more money or more prestige or more influence. It’s the great American pastime to look for flaws in the leader’s behavior. And, problem-solving leaders have twice as many areas to criticize—both content and process.”

改进策略

A STRATEGY FOR IMPROVEMENT

“你可能是对的，”丹妮说，“这可能是第一天上学的另一个问题。”

“You may be right,” Dani said, “and that could account for another problem with the first day at school.”

“那是什么？”

“What’s that?”

“老师也是解决问题的领导者。我要把课讲好，但我也要有话要说，要有合理的权威。如果我在任何一项上都不及格，学生们会通过将两者相乘来降低我的成绩。所以如果我对他们这样做也许没关系。”

“The teacher is a problem-solving leader, too. I have to conduct the class well, but I also have to have something to say, be reasonably authoritative. If I fall down on either count, the students bring my grade down by multiplying the two together. So maybe it’s all right if I do it to them.”

“这就说得通了。”

“That makes sense.”

“这也解释了我一直想弄清楚的事情。如果我将两个分数相乘，而不是相加，这为我提供了一种改进教学的不同策略。”

“That also explains something I’ve been trying to figure out. If I multiply two scores together, rather than add them, it gives me a different strategy for improving my teaching.”

“你是什么意思？”

“What do you mean?”

“好吧，假设我的材料得分是八十分，而我的课堂处理能力得分是四十分。在附加系统下，任何一个增加 10 个点都同样好。我可能会选择提高我的技能分数，因为那更容易，而且我知道如何比现在做得更好。但如果我将这两个分数相乘，那么低分增加 10 分对我的总分的影响要大于高分增加 10 分。”

“Well, suppose the score for my material is eighty and the score for my ability to handle the class is forty. Under an additive system, a ten-point increase in either one would be equally good. I would probably choose to increase my skill score because that’s easier and I know how to do it even better than I already do. But if I’m multiplying the two scores, a ten-point increase in the lower score has a bigger influence on my overall score than a ten-point increase in the higher one.”

“没错，”我说。“是四十而不是三十二。我希望我今天能想到这个例子，以帮助我说服那些新的项目负责人不要那么努力地提高他们的技术技能。他们中的大多数人在技术上都相当不错，但他们在提高人际交往能力方面还有很长的路要走。”

“That’s right,” I said. “It’s forty instead of thirty-two. I wish I’d thought of that example today, to help me convince those new project leaders not to work so hard on improving their technical skills. Most of them were pretty good technically but they have a long way to go in improving their people skills.”

教学和领导是可以学来的吗？

CAN TEACHING AND LEADING BE LEARNED?

丹妮若有所思。思考了一分钟后，她说：“我认为新老师也是这样。他们的课堂处理能力往往比他们的学科知识要低得多。然而，他们往往更专注于他们的主题而不是他们的教学。事实上，我认为他们认为提高你的教学能力是有问题的。”

Dani looked thoughtful. After a minute of reflection, she said, “I think that new teachers are the same way. They tend to be much lower on their classroom handling than they are on their subject matter knowledge. Yet they tend to work on their subject matter much more than on their teaching. In fact, I think they believe there’s something wrong with working on your teaching ability.”

“这是为什么？”

“Why is that?”

“他们认为教学是一种天生的能力，而不是一种后天习得的技能。以某种神秘的方式，每个婴儿从一出生就应该知道如何教书。不知何故，如果你必须被教才能教书，那你一定有问题。”

“They think that teaching is a natural ability rather than a learned skill. In some mystical way, every baby ought to know how to teach from birth. Somehow, if you have to be taught to teach, there must be something wrong with you.”

“是的，他们试图告诉我关于领导技能的相同神话，”我补充道。“他们似乎觉得每个人都应该自然而然地知道如何召开会议或委派工作或……”

“Yes, they were trying to tell me the same myths about leadership skills,” I added. “They seemed to feel that everyone should just naturally know how to run a meeting or delegate work or…”

“……给出成绩或绩效评估。”

”… give grades or performance appraisals.”

“是的。但这些都不是天生的技能。它们是您学习或不学习的东西。但如果你认为它们是天生的能力，那么如果你没有它们，你可能会觉得自己有问题。”

“Yep. But those aren’t inborn skills. They’re things you either learn or don’t learn. But if you think they’re natural abilities, then you might feel there’s something wrong with you if you don’t have them.”

丹妮再次若有所思。“缺乏自尊是学习的一大障碍。当你害怕自己会失败时，你很可能是对的。就像开学第一天讨论成绩一样。”

Dani looked thoughtful again. “And lack of self-esteem is a great barrier to learning. When you’re afraid you’ll fail, you’re likely to be right. Like discussing grades on the first day of classes.”

第一天评分

GRADING ON THE FIRST DAY

“我以为这次谈话会分散你的注意力，”我笑道，“但你还是没有忘记这是开学的第一天。”

“I thought this conversation would distract you,” I laughed, “but you still haven’t forgotten it’s the first day of classes.”

“也许我需要更多的酒。这将是一个艰难的学期。”

“Perhaps I need more wine. It’s going to be a tough semester.”

“你每个学期都这么说，”我边说边又倒了一杯，“但结果从来没有你想象的那么糟糕。第一天学生对老师的批评最多。”

“You say that every semester,” I observed, pouring another glass, “but it never turns out quite as bad as you thought. Students are most critical of the teacher on the first day.”

“但愿如此。”

“I hope so.”

“我的项目负责人也是如此。他们刚刚从队伍中脱颖而出，这些队伍中的许多人都在问，'这个笨蛋有什么我没有的？

“It was the same for my project leaders. They’ve just emerged from the ranks, and many people in those ranks are asking, ‘What’s this bozo got that I haven’t got?’

“这听起来确实像是第一天上课的学生。他们让你经受一次非常严峻的考验，就像我们过去对代课老师所做的那样。探寻最细微的弱点……”

“That sure sounds like students on the first day of classes. They put you through a really severe test, like we used to do with substitute teachers. Probing for the slightest weakness …”

“……他们可以利用它来对您施加影响。所以，真的，乘法作为一种评分方法可能太简单了。”

”… which they can use to gain leverage on you. So, really, the multiplication might be too easy a grading method.”

“简单？”

“Easy?”

“是的，取所有不同成绩中的最低分可能会更准确。”

“Yes, it would probably be more accurate to take the minimum of all the different grades.”

“你的意思是，学生们以她最薄弱的地方来评判老师？”

“You mean that the students judge the teacher by her weakest area?”

“一开始他们当然会这样做，尽管后来他们可能愿意称赞她的长处。这就是为什么我的项目负责人要承受来自他们以前的同事的压力。”

“Certainly they do at the beginning, though later they may be willing to give her credit for her strengths. That’s why my project leaders were under such pressure from their former peers.”

丹妮叹了口气。“所以在第一天，当我有很多新事情要做时，我试图通过强调我的强项来给人留下好印象。但是同学们都在找我最薄弱的地方。难怪我筋疲力尽。”

Dani sighed. “So on the first day, when I have so many new things to do, I’m trying to make a good impression by emphasizing my strongest points. But the students are looking for my weakest point. No wonder I’m exhausted.”

一个可能的解决方案

A POSSIBLE SOLUTION

丹妮的酒杯已经空了，她似乎若有所思。当我给她倒酒时，她问道：“所以你认为我使用了错误的评分系统？”

Dani’s glass of wine was now empty, and she seemed pensive. As I refilled her glass, she asked, “So you think I use the wrong system of grading?”

“不，我认为那根本不是我要说的。你的评分系统没有问题，但这是你最薄弱的地方。这就是为什么学生们如此努力地调查你的原因。”

“No, I don’t think that’s what I was saying at all. There’s nothing wrong with your system of grading, but it’s your weakest point. That’s why the students probe you so hard about it.”

“如果没有错，那为什么是我的弱点？”

“If it’s not wrong, then why is it my weakest point?”

“因为你觉得这是你最薄弱的地方。因为你在那个方面对自己感觉不好，所以当学生在第一天上课时提起它时，你会感到不安。当他们向您询问课程表或教科书时，您不会感到沮丧。弱点不是评分系统，而是你对系统的感觉。” 丹妮笑了。“这话说起来容易，但要在全班同学面前控制住自己的情绪就没那么容易了。”

“Because you feel it’s your weakest point. Because you feel bad about yourself in that area, you get upset about it when the students bring it up on the first day of class. You don’t get upset when they ask you about the class schedule, or the textbook. The weakness is not the system of grading, but your feelings about the system.” Dani laughed. “That’s easy for you to say, but it’s not so easy to control your feelings up there in front of the class.”

“我不知道吗！但因为那是*我的弱点，所以我学会了组织我的课程来避免我的弱点，而不是强调它们。”

“Don’t I know! But because that’s *my weak point, I’ve learned to structure my classes to avoid my weaknesses, rather than emphasize them.”

“嘿，我可以做到！我根本不需要讨论评分。”

“Hey, I could do that! I don’t have to discuss grading at all.”

“当然，你可以给他们一份描述系统的讲义。”

“Sure, you could give them a handout describing the system.”

“不，你不了解学生心态。但至少我不必在我疲倦和超负荷、学生焦虑和困惑、我们彼此都不了解的时候讨论它。”

“No, you don’t know the student mentality. But at least I don’t have to discuss it at a time when I’m tired and overloaded, when the students are anxious and confused, and when neither of us knows the other very well.”

“当然。自从你接受自信训练以来，你总是提醒我，我不必在有人向我抛出问题时就回答每一个问题。”

“Sure. Ever since you took assertiveness training, you’re always reminding me that I don’t have to answer every question the moment someone throws it at me.”

“可是如果我推迟讨论打分，学生们会不会更着急呢？”

“But if I postpone the discussion of grading, won’t the students get even more anxious?”

“不必要。假设你告诉他们评分将在第二周的课程中全面讨论。与此同时，你向他们保证，他们在第一周所做的任何事情都不会影响他们的成绩。”

“Not necessarily. Suppose you tell them that grading will be discussed fully during the second week of class. In the meantime, you promise them that nothing they do in the first week will affect their grade.”

“这样我就可以上一周的课，以减轻他们对我的评分系统的担忧。

“And that would give me a week of classes to lower their anxieties about my grading system.

“......并了解你不是那种会在评分上背叛他们的人。”

”… and to get to know that you’re not the type of person who’s going to betray them on their grading.”

丹妮开始微笑。“我喜欢！至少，第一天上课可以少做一件事情，这不可能有坏处。”

Dani began to smile. “I like it! At the very least, it would be one less thing to do on the first day of class, which couldn’t possibly hurt.”

“而且这不仅仅是任何旧事物；这是让你和他们最焦虑的事情。很少有人对评估的想法感到满意，无论是评估还是被评估。”

“And it’s not just any old thing; it’s the thing that gives both you and them the most anxiety. Very few people are comfortable with the idea of evaluation, either evaluating or being evaluated.”

“我以为只有我一个人。”

“I thought it was just me.”

“不，不仅仅是你。那些新来的项目负责人，除了考核，什么也谈不上。我希望他们听到这个讨论。我总是晚几个小时才想起这些事情。”

“No, not just you. Those new project leaders couldn’t talk about anything but appraisals. I wish they’d heard this discussion. I always think of these things a couple of hours too late.”

丹妮笑了。“其实，亲爱的，你根本就没有想到。我想到了它，但下次出现该主题时请随意使用它。”

Dani smiled. “Actually, dear, you didn’t think of it at all. I thought of it, but feel free to use it the next time the subject comes up.”

“如果我写一篇关于这个主题的文章怎么样？”

“How about if I write an essay on the subject?”

“当然。只要你让人们知道你所有的好主意都是从哪里来的。”

“Sure. Just so long as you let people know where you get all your great ideas.”

“为什么，你知道我永远不会偷你的主意……”

“Why, you know I’d never steal an idea from you …”

“……除非绝对必要。没关系。一定要告诉他们，对评估的焦虑只会导致更多的焦虑。”

”… unless it was absolutely necessary. That’s all right. Just be sure to tell them that anxiety over appraisals only leads to more anxiety.”

“正确的。所以他们的首要工作是创造一个舒适的环境，这意味着熟悉新工作，尤其是做一些能让双方有机会建立彼此信任的事情。”

“Right. So their first job is to establish a comfortable environment, which means getting acquainted with the new job, and particularly doing things that give both parties a chance to develop trust for one another.”

“如果信任从未发展，你会告诉他们做什么？”

“And what will you tell them to do if trust never develops?”

这让我难住了片刻。“我想在那种情况下，他们使用的任何评分方案都会被拒绝，所以我不妨先尝试建立信任。只要我相信自己，我就可以接受我可能无法赢得他们信任的想法。”

That stumped me for a moment. “I suppose in that case any grading scheme they use is going to be rejected anyway, so I might as well try to build trust first. As long as I trust myself, I can accept the idea that I might fail to win their trust.”

“换句话说，任何评分系统都不重要，只要你能通过自己的评分系统。”

“In other words, none of the grading systems matter that much, as long as you can pass your own system of grading yourself.”

“恰恰。当您开始新课程或新工作时，该系统不会改变。无论走到哪里都带着它，只有你才能改变它。”

“Precisely. And that system doesn’t change when you start a new class or a new job. You carry it with you wherever you go, and only you can change it.”

问题

QUESTIONS

  1. 回想上次你自己的反应，当某个新领导被指派给你时，比如新老板或委员会主席。您如何评价这位领导者？领导给你打几分？两种评分系统之间有什么关系吗？
  2. Recall your own reactions the last time some new leader was assigned to you, like a new boss or committee chair. How did you grade this leader? How did the leader grade you? Was there any relationship between the two systems of grading?
  3. 回想一下上次任命新领导来代替你的情况。你首先看中那个人的是什么？在您信任新领导之前花了多长时间（如果有的话）？哪些行为增加了信任？哪些行为导致信任度下降？你是主动融入新领导，还是主动抵制？为什么？
  4. Recall the last time a new leader was appointed over you. What were the first things you looked at in that person? How long did it take, if ever, before you trusted the new leader? What actions led to increased trust? What actions led to decreased trust? Did you work actively to integrate the new leader, or did you actively resist? Why?
  5. 您是否参加过以某种方式衡量您的领导潜力的真实测试？结果是如何呈现给你的？它们是用来扩大你的可能性，还是用来限制你的？你如何使用它们来扩大你的可能性？
  6. Have you ever taken a real test that measured your leadership potential in some way? How were the results presented to you? Were they used to enlarge your possibilities, or to constrain you? How could you use them to enlarge your possibilities?
  7. 如果你参加领导测验，你希望看到什么问题？你会怎么回答？（根据需要多次执行此操作。）
  8. If you took a leadership quiz, what question would you like to see on it? How would you answer it? (Do this as many times as you wish.)
  9. 作为潜在领导者，您最薄弱的特质是什么？你在做什么来加强它？你在做什么来避免被测试？
  10. What’s your weakest attribute as a potential leader? What are you doing to strengthen it? What are you doing to avoid being tested on it?
  11. 你如何看待绩效考核？你喜欢给他们还是接受他们？或者根本不参与其中？你打算怎么办呢？
  12. How do you feel about performance appraisals? Do you prefer giving them or taking them? or not being involved in them at all? What are you going to do about it?
  13. 您进入新团队的策略是什么？您欢迎新成员加入您已经属于的团队的策略是什么？
  14. What’s your strategy for entering a new group? What’s your strategy for welcoming a new member into a group you already belong to?

第 21 章通过你自己的领导力测试

Chapter 21. Passing Your Own Leadership Tests

*如果你能在一切围绕着你的时候保持冷静

*If you can keep your head when all about you

正在失去他们的，并将其归咎于你，

Are losing theirs and blaming it on you,
