如果你能在所有人都怀疑你的时候相信自己，

If you can trust yourself when all men doubt you,

但也请原谅他们的怀疑；

But make allowance for their doubting too;

如果你能等，不因等而疲倦，

If you can wait and not be tired by waiting,

或被骗，不要用谎言交易，

Or being lied about, don’t deal in lies,

或被恨不屈服于恨，

Or being hated don’t give way to hating,

但是不要看起来太好，也不要说得太聪明：*

And yet don’t look too good, nor talk too wise:*

——拉德亚德·吉卜林，《如果》

—Rudyard Kipling, “If”

吉卜林设定了一个艰难的考验，但如果你想成为一名成功的解决问题的领导者，你必须通过的考验并不比你通过的更艰难。我想写一首诗来告诉你你需要什么特质，但我不是吉卜林。除此之外，我想提供科学数据，但我一般不能使用真实的组织作为实验的测试环境。

Kipling set a tough test, but no tougher than you’ll have to pass if you’re to have a successful career as a problem-solving leader. I’d like to write a poem that would tell you what traits you’ll need, but I’m no Kipling. Barring that, I’d like to supply scientific data, but I can’t generally use a real organization as a test environment for experiments.

由于对真实组织中的真实领导者进行直接实验是不切实际的，我必须找到其他方法来发现一个人的领导能力。在车间里，我可以模拟真实的环境，但大多数时候我不在车间里。为了有效地利用剩下的时间，我必须学会做生物学家和人类学家所做的事情：解释人们对自然产生的较小测试的反应。我特别喜欢解读人们解决问题的方式。

Since direct experiments on real leaders in real organizations are not practical, I have to find other ways to discover leadership abilities in a person. In a workshop, I can simulate real environments, but most of the time I’m not in a workshop. To use the rest of the time effectively, I must learn to do what the biologists and anthropologists do: to interpret people’s reactions to smaller tests that arise naturally. I particularly like to interpret the way people solve problems.

顶级执行测试

A TOP EXECUTIVE TEST

几年前，在日本“敬老日”这一天，我在东京参加了国际软件工程大会的宴会。我坐在日本电气公司 (NEC) 董事长 Koji Kobayashi 旁边。小林先生是几本关于计算机和控制的畅销书的作者，他聪明、机敏，并且充满了令人愉快的故事。75岁的他，真是一个值得尊敬的老人。

A few years ago, on the Japanese holiday “Honor Old People Day” I was in Tokyo attending the banquet of the International Conference on Software Engineering. I was seated next to Koji Kobayashi, chairman of the board of Nippon Electric Company (NEC). Author of several popular books on computers and control, Mr. Kobayashi is bright, alert, and full of delightful stories. At age 75, he is truly an old person to be honored.

他的一个故事可以追溯到 50 年前：Western Electric Company 寄给 NEC 的一些设备装在一个密封的盒子里，盒子上标有警告：请勿拆封！小林先生当时是一名非常好奇的年轻工程师，他觉得自己必须打开盒子，从而挑战要求顺从和服从权威的文化。他找到了一个简单的电路，NEC 可以在日本轻松制造。并且做到了。

One of his stories went back fifty years: The Western Electric Company had sent NEC some equipment in a sealed box marked WARNING: DO NOT BREAK SEAL! Mr. Kobayashi, then a young and very curious engineer, felt he simply had to open the box and thus defy a culture that demanded conformity and obedience to authority. He found a simple circuit, which NEC could easily manufacture in Japan. And did.

小林先生在多年后讲述这个故事时，清楚地认识到那个盒子是对品质的关键考验，正是这些品质最终使他成为了世界上最大的高科技公司之一的董事会主席——一个真正的解决问题的领导者. 最高层的人制定规则，换句话说，打破旧规则。懦弱的循规蹈矩者无法登上顶峰，但盲目叛逆者也无法登顶。成为董事长，必须要有打开密封盒的勇气，但也要坚守公司五十年！

Mr. Kobayashi, telling the story so many years later, clearly recognized that box as a crucial test of the qualities that eventually made him chairman of the board of one of the largest high-tech companies in the world—a true problem-solving leader. The person at the top makes the rules, which is another way of saying, breaks the old rules. Cowardly conformists don’t make it to the top, but then neither do blind rebels. To become chairman, you must have the courage to open the sealed box, but you must also stick with the company for fifty years!

经得起考验的能力

THE ABILITY TO WITHSTAND TESTS

我必须承认，正如我对许多高管所做的那样，我对小林先生进行了测试，利用他来更多地了解成为 NEC 董事长所需的特质。我相信他不会因为我的测试而受到冒犯，因为这样的领导特质之一就是经得起测试的能力。正如我们所见，一旦你到达显眼的位置，你就会一直受到考验。如果你没有被 Western Electric 或你的晚餐伙伴测试，你肯定会被你的追随者测试。和你自己，不停地。

I must confess to testing Mr. Kobayashi, as I do many executives, using him to learn more about the traits needed to become chairman of NEC. I’m confident that he wasn’t offended by my testing, because one such leadership trait is the ability to withstand tests. As we’ve seen, once you reach a conspicuous position, you’re always being tested. If you’re not tested by Western Electric or by your dinner companions, you’re certainly being tested by your followers. And by yourself, incessantly.

作为车间主任，我经常经历过同样的考验。第一天，群里肯定有人站出来挑战我的权威。如果我通过了那个测试，我会得到另一个。如果我不及格，我会再得到十个，并有可能失去对整个行动的控制。如果我做得好，一段时间后，他们会明确地停止测试。但他们仍然对偶然出现的有趣测试保持警惕。

As a workshop leader, I’ve often experienced the same kind of testing. On the first day, somebody in the group is sure to come forward with some challenge to my authority. If I pass that test, I get another. If I flunk, I get ten more, and risk losing control of the entire operation. If I do well, after a while, they stop testing—explicitly. But they still have one eye open for interesting tests that arise by chance.

人们测试自称是他们领导者的其他人是完全合理的。例如，孩子们不断测试父母的行为准则。如果那些未来的领导者能够考验他们，那就更加合理了。不过，就我个人而言，我讨厌接受测试，所以只要有可能，我都会安排研讨会让自己下台。我通过组织环境来领导他们的学习。

It’s perfectly reasonable for people to test others who purport to be their leaders. Children, for example, constantly test their parents’ rules for behavior. It’s doubly reasonable if those would-be leaders are in a position to test them. Personally, though, I despise being tested, so whenever possible, I arrange the workshop to get myself off stage. I lead by organizing the environment so as to empower their learning.

我的技术很简单。参与者想学习领导，所以我让他们领导研讨会。推销这个想法可能需要几天时间——虽然他们会测试我看我是否真心实意，但这是值得的。过了几天，当我筋疲力尽时，参与者接手了；我所要做的就是坐下来，让他们失去理智，把这一切都归咎于我。

My technique is simple. The participants want to learn to lead, so I let them lead the workshop. It may take a few days to sell this idea— while they test me to see if I really mean it, but it’s worth the effort. After a few days, when I’m exhausted, the participants have taken over; all I have to do is sit back and let them lose their heads and blame it all on me.

如何对付入侵者

HOW TO HANDLE AN INTRUDER

至少，这是理论。在实践中，我无法创造一个完美的环境。有时，会出现学生无法应对的情况，或者我认为他们无法应对的情况。如果我让他们，他们可能会处理得很好，但我变得懦弱，走出幕后干预。当我挺身而出时，我又一次没通过领导力测试，因为如果我不干预，他们的学习无疑会更好。

At least, that’s the theory. In practice, I can’t create a perfect environment. Sometimes, situations arise that the students can’t handle, or that I don’t think they can handle. If I let them, they probably could handle them perfectly well, but I turn cowardly and step out of the background to intervene. When I do come forward, I fail another leadership test, because it would be undoubtedly better for their learning if I didn’t interfere.

在最近的一次研讨会上，它又发生了。没有丝毫警告，门被猛地推开，一个身穿三件套西装、嘴唇紧闭的小个子男人啪地一声站在我们房间中间。他双手叉腰，怒视着我们，就好像他走进了一个没有老师的幼儿园。

It happened again in a recent workshop. Without the slightest warning, the door was flung open, and a tight-lipped little man in a three-piece suit planted himself smack in the middle of our room. With hands on hips, he glowered at us as if he had walked into a teacherless kindergarten.

“这里发生了什么？” 他要求。“谁负责？” 听到他咬着牙说话的声音有多大，真是令人着迷，所以我忘了回答他。他重复了这个问题。“谁负责？我不能让你打扰我们的会议。” 他指了指隔壁房间。“我要你在这里保持安静。”

“What’s going on in here?” he demanded. “Who’s in charge?” It was fascinating to hear how loud he could talk with his teeth clenched, so I forgot to answer him. He repeated the question. “Who’s in charge? I cannot allow you to disturb our meeting.” He gestured toward the next room. “I want you to keep it quiet in here.”

似乎有许多关于阻止人们制造噪音的最佳方法的理论。三件套的理论，显然是为了炒作。其他人认为您可以通过忽略噪音来阻止噪音。班级一定相信这一点，因为每个人都在不停地构建他们的 Tinkertoy 设计，而入侵者一直在向我们尖叫。然而，在我看来，这两种方法都不如将它们移到门外的方法有效：看不见，听不见。

There seem to be a number of theories about the best way to stop people from making noise. Three-Piece’s theory evidently was to make even more noise. Other people think that you stop noise by ignoring it. The class must have believed that, because everyone just kept building their Tinkertoy designs while the intruder kept screaming at us. In my opinion, however, neither of these methods is as effective as the move- ‘em-right-out-the-door method: out of sight, out of sound.

不幸的是，我不确定如何将我的理论付诸实践。首先，我透过双光眼镜凝视着他的眼睛，指着门，但我认为他甚至没有看到我，更不用说我的手指了。

Unfortunately, I wasn’t sure just how to put my theory into action. First I peered into his eyes over my bifocals and pointed to the door, but I don’t think he even saw me, let alone my finger.

接下来我试着平静地说话，虽然我觉得我的声音有点颤抖，尽可能礼貌地说，“我们正在这里演习。如果您允许我们继续，我们将不胜感激。”

Next I tried speaking calmly, though I think my voice wavered a bit when I said, as politely as I could, “We’re in the middle of an exercise here. We’d appreciate it if you’d allow us to continue.”

但他没有离开，只是瞪了我一眼，问道：“你是负责人吗？”

But instead of leaving, he simply glared at me and demanded, “Are you in charge?”

鉴于我领导研讨会的性质，我总是很难回答这个问题。我知道如果我告诉三件套没有人管，他是绝对不会相信的，所以我也就没管。我反而升级了。“这是我们的房间，你不属于这里。如果你能离开，我将不胜感激！现在！”

Given the nature of my leadership of the workshop, I always have a hard time answering that question. I knew that Three-Piece would never believe me if I told him that nobody was in charge, so I didn’t bother. I escalated instead. “This is our room, and you don’t belong here. I’d appreciate it if you’d leave! Now!”

这也没有用。在我的领导班级面前，我开始感到自己的领导能力受到考验，但我做得不太好。我决定尝试一些戏剧性的肢体语言。我走近了一些，希望通过侵犯他的个人空间来把他赶出门。它走了几步，直到他意识到自己离出口越来越近了。

This didn’t work, either. I was beginning to feel tested for my leadership ability in front of my leadership class, and I wasn’t doing too well. I decided to try some dramatic body language. I stepped closer, hoping to back him out the door by invading his personal space. It worked for a few steps, until he realized he was getting close to the exit.

“别逼我了！” 他喊道。我没有逼他，但显然他认为我是。接下来我知道的是，他把手放在我的胸口。或者也许我把手放在他的胸口。到这个时候，我根本没有想得太清楚。我可能没有通过领导力测试，但我会在健身方面获得 A+。（当然，我身高六英尺二英寸，而他不超过五英尺七英寸。）他马上就出了门，很快我们只能听到远处隐隐约约的威胁和咒骂声.

“Stop pushing me!” he shouted. I wasn’t pushing him, but evidently he thought I was. The next thing I knew, he had his hands on my chest. Or maybe I put my hands on his chest. By this time I wasn’t thinking too clearly at all. I may have failed my leadership test, but I would have earned an A+ in body-building. (Of course, I’m six feet two inches tall, and he wasn’t more than five feet seven inches.) He was out the door in a moment, and soon all we could hear were muffled threats and curses fading in the distance.

阿诺德的方法

Arnold’s approach

在车间里，发生的一切都是讨论的磨坊，尤其是无所畏惧的领导者和神秘入侵者之间的力量考验。似乎只有两个人觉得我做错了什么：一个是阿诺德，身高 6 英尺 6 英寸，体重大约 300 磅，另一个是拉蒙，他可能有 5 英尺 4 英寸和 120 磅，如果绑上铅块的话在他的脚下。

In the workshop, everything that happens is grist for the discussion mill, especially a test of strength between the fearless leader and the mysterious intruder. Only two people seemed to feel I had done something wrong: One was Arnold, who was six feet six and weighed around three hundred pounds, and the other was Ramon, who might have reached five feet four and one hundred twenty pounds with lead weights strapped under his feet.

在讨论过程中，阿诺德提醒我们注意从他们这边墙里传来的声音。“他们并没有真正打扰我，”他说，“但他们让我意识到另一种看待问题的方式。我们不受欢迎的访客认为问题出在我们身上，但任何团体都会发出一定程度的噪音。在我们看来，问题在于他的好战，但只有当他认为我们的噪音干扰了他的会议时，这才会引起问题。”

During the discussion, Arnold called our attention to the noises coming from their side of the wall. “They don’t really bother me,” he said, “but they make me aware of another way of looking at the problem. Our unwanted visitor thought that the problem was us, but any group makes a certain amount of noise. In our view, the problem was his belligerence, but that caused a problem only when he thought our noise disturbed his meeting.”

“这有什么意义，阿诺德？” 我仍然对我处理这件事的方式持防御态度。“我试图理性地与他交谈，但他不同意。”

“What’s the point, Arnold?” I was still defensive about the way I’d handled the incident. “I tried to talk to him rationally, but he wouldn’t have it.”

“看看这堵墙。如果它更厚，那一切都不重要了。他甚至不知道我们在这里，你也不必把他赶出去。”

“Look at this wall. If it had been thicker, none of that would have mattered. He wouldn’t have even known we were here, and you wouldn’t have had to throw him out.”

这堵墙是为酒店提供灵活会议室的可移动隔板之一。像大多数这样的墙一样，它以隔音为代价换取了便携性。酒店为自己解决了一个问题，却给我们制造了麻烦。有了更坚固的墙，我们就不需要这么强大的领导者了。“我同意，阿诺德，但这有什么用呢？我们很难要求酒店为我们吵闹的班级更换墙壁。”

The wall was one of those movable partitions that give hotels flexible meeting rooms. Like most such walls, it traded soundproofing for portability. In solving one problem for themselves, the hotel made problems for us. With a stronger wall, we wouldn’t have needed such a strong leader. “I agree, Arnold, but what good does it do? We could hardly ask the hotel to change walls just for our rowdy class.”

“当然不是，但一旦我们预料到墙的问题，我们可能还有其他事情可以做，比如选择不同的酒店。或者隔壁没有人的其他聚会时间。或者甚至提前警告他们，有几次我们可能会发出一些噪音。当噪音不是完全出乎意料时，它更容易被接受。”

“Of course not, but once we anticipated the problem with the wall, there might have been other things we could do, such as selecting a different hotel. Or a different meeting time, when there wouldn’t be anyone next door. Or even by warning them in advance that there would be a few times when we might make a bit of noise. Noise is a lot easier to accept when it’s not a complete surprise.”

“我不明白的是，阿诺德，你为什么这么通情达理。在这个房间里的所有人中，我原以为你会是赞成我强行把他赶出去的人。哎呀，你可以用一只手把他推出去。”

“What I don’t understand, Arnold, is why you’re being so reasonable. Of all the people in this room, I would have thought you would be the one to approve of my muscling him out. Heck, you could have pushed him out with one hand.”

“就是这样。有时小家伙会找我。他们测试我是因为我的身材对他们的男子气概构成了威胁。最初几次，我伤害了他们，真的很糟糕。”

“That’s just it. Sometimes little guys pick on me. They test me because my size is a threat to their machismo. The first few times that happened, I hurt them, real bad.”

“所以？你在乎什么？他们可能活该。”

“So? What do you care? They probably deserved it.”

“也许他们做到了，但伤害他们并没有让我对自己感觉良好。当然，我为自己的力量感到自豪，但这让我更加惭愧。我为什么要为自己比大多数人都大而自豪呢？”

“Maybe they did, but it didn’t make me feel good about myself to hurt them. Sure, I take pride in my strength, but that made me even more ashamed. Why should I be proud of the fact that I’m bigger than most other people?”

我自己也觉得有点不好意思，所以我改变了话题。“好吧，但假设你事先没有做任何这些事情，而你处于我的位置，这个大嘴巴把你的课堂搞得一团糟，你会怎么做？”

I was feeling a little ashamed myself, so I changed the subject. “Okay, but suppose you didn’t do any of those things in advance, and there you were in my position with this loudmouth messing up your class, what would you have done?”

“哦，我会直接把他推出房间，比你更快更猛烈得多！然后我会为自己一开始让这种情况发展而感到羞耻。”

“Oh, I would have pushed him right out of the room, a lot sooner and a lot more violently than you did! And then I would have been ashamed of myself for allowing that situation to develop in the first place.”

“这就是你赞成预防策略的原因？”

“And that’s why you favor a preventive strategy?”

“对，因为我不能很好地处理面对面的情况。”

“Right, because I can’t handle the face-to-face situation very well.”

拉蒙的做法

Ramon’s approach

后来，我们正处于一场安静而紧张的解决问题的过程中，这时隔壁的那群人开始播放一部响亮的电影。我愤怒地站起来想做点什么，但拉蒙示意我留在原地。他离开了我们的房间，片刻之后电影的声音太小以至于听不见。在讨论期间，我问拉蒙他做了什么。

Later, we were in the midst of a quietly intense problem-solving session, when the group next door started showing a loud movie. I rose angrily to do something about it, but Ramon gestured for me to remain where I was. He left our room and a moment later the sound from the movie was too soft to be heard. During the discussion period, I asked Ramon what he had done.

“真的没什么。我只是问他们是否介意将声音调小一点。”

“Nothing, really. I just asked them if they would mind turning down the sound a bit.”

“没有人被冒犯吗？”

“And nobody was offended?”

“不，他们没有意识到他们打扰了我们。他们一明白，就很乐意把声音调小。”

“No, they hadn’t realized they were disturbing us. As soon as they understood, they were happy to turn down the sound.”

阿诺瞪大眼睛看着拉蒙，道： “你太厉害了！如果我过去了，可能会发生一场战斗。你不可能付钱让我进去。”

Arnold looked at Ramon with wide eyes and said, “You’re incredible! If I had gone over, there probably would have been a fight. You couldn’t have paid me to go in there.”

“这就是你我之间的区别。我在一个简陋的街区长大。如您所见，我太小了，无法赢得战斗。而且，我不是一个快跑者。所以多年来，我学会了如何让每个人都冷静下来。”

“That’s the difference between you and me. I grew up in a rough neighborhood. As you can see, I’m too small to win fights. And also, I’m not a fast runner. So over the years, I learned how to keep everyone cool.”

领导者的两种主要应对方式取决于他们对动机和组织的重视程度。我将拉蒙基于动机的风格称为个性方法。Arnold 的，基于组织，是规划方法。当然，有很多变化，但最好知道你的主要优势在哪里，这样你就不会试图用别人的风格来处理情况。没有教科书能告诉你通过入侵者测试的正确方法，只能告诉你“做对你最有效的事情，不要让自己为此感到难过。”

Two major coping styles for leaders depend on their emphasis on motivation versus organization. I call Ramon’s style, based on motivation, the personality approach. Arnold’s, based on organization, is the planning approach. Of course, there are many variations, but it’s good to know where your major strength lies so you don’t try to handle situations with somebody else’s style. No textbook can tell you the right way to pass the intruder test, except to say, “Do what would work best for you, without making yourself feel bad about it.”

什么是正确的方法？

WHAT’S THE RIGHT WAY?

然而，避免你最弱的风格是不够的，因为一个悖论：为了避免你最弱的风格，你首先需要加强它。假设 Arnold 和 Ramon 都在开设课程。为避免面对面的情况，阿诺德建议与酒店另行安排。但要说服其他人采纳他的计划，他必须能够面对面地处理他们。如果他不能胜任面对面推销他的计划，阿诺德最终会在课堂上遇到更糟糕的面对面情况。

Avoiding your weakest style is never sufficient, however, because of a paradox: In order to avoid your weakest style, you first need to strengthen it. Let’s suppose both Arnold and Ramon are setting up classes. To avoid a face-to-face situation, Arnold proposes to plan some other arrangement with the hotel. But to convince other people to adopt his plan, he’ll have to be able to handle them face-to-face. If he’s not competent selling his plans face-to-face, Arnold will wind up in worse face-to-face situations in his classes.

另一方面，Ramon 会在自发组织的课程中表现出色。由于大多数课程都不是为了自发而组织的，Ramon 必须仔细计划，否则他会在其他人高度结构化的情况下结束——在这种情况下，他的个性方法不会那么有效。为了获得自发的机会，拉蒙需要计划。

Ramon, on the other hand, will do well in classes that are organized for spontaneity. Since most classes are not organized for spontaneity, Ramon must plan carefully or else he’ll wind up in situations highly structured by other people—situations in which his personality approach won’t be so effective. To get a chance to be spontaneous, Ramon needs to plan.

总的来说，技术人员在计划方面往往比个性方面更强。他们经常抱怨说，如果他们能让人倾听，他们就知道事情应该如何进行。这就是计算机程序员如此乐于使用他们的机器工作的原因：

By and large, technical workers tend to be stronger on the planning side than the personality side. They often complain that they know how things should be run, if they could only get someone to listen. That’s why computer programmers are so happy working with their machines:

个性不会影响计算机。

Personality doesn’t influence computers.

在更高级别的管理中，计划优势也很重要，因此计算机程序员可能会成为出色的高层管理人员，只要他们具备通过中级职位晋升的个性优势。不过，大多数情况下，他们只是被赶出了门。

At the higher levels of management, planning strengths also count heavily, so computer programmers might make terrific top executives, if only they had the personality strengths to get them through the intermediate ranks. Mostly, though, they just get shoved out the door.

使用和滥用测试

USING AND ABUSING TESTS

真正的测试并不有趣，甚至可能很危险。人事部门可以使用测试来标记人们是否对他们进行归类。管理者可以利用它们将自己作为领导者的失败归咎于员工，也可以被想成为领导者但又不敢表达恐惧的员工用作烟幕。如果我不再接受测试，我个人会很高兴，但我不愿意放弃我的工作坊。

Real tests are no fun and can even be dangerous. Tests can be used by personnel departments to label people for pigeonholing them. They can be used by managers to blame workers for their own failures as leaders, and they can be used as a smokescreen by workers who want to become leaders but are afraid to express their fears. I would personally be very happy if I were never tested again, but I’m not willing to give up my workshops.

如果实在经不起考验，就不要担任领导职务。但是如果你想成为领导者，你可以利用这些测试来发挥你的优势。测试可以为您提供开发基准，让您知道需要做什么。你的个人风格可以改变，但只能通过长期而艰苦的练习。获得这种练习的唯一方法是让自己置身于可以解决当前弱点的环境中，就像我在研讨会上所做的那样。当你成为一名经理时，你可以指望你的员工给你一个彻底的测试，也就是说，除非你惩罚他们测试你。

If you really can’t stand being tested, you should stay out of leadership roles. But if you want to become a leader, you can use the tests to your advantage. Tests can give you a baseline for development, letting you know what needs work. Your personal style can be changed, but only by long and difficult practice. The only way to get that practice is to put yourself in situations where you can work on your present weaknesses, as I do in workshops. When you become a manager, you can count on your staff to give you a thorough testing, that is, unless you punish them for testing you.

您如何测试自己尚未达到的新水平？如果你不喜欢测试，你可能会避免让你的员工或同事接受困难的测试。出于同样的原因，您自己的管理层可能不会对您施加足够的压力。他们希望你成功，这样他们才能成功。他们会将您置于可能会做得很好的情况下，而不是您可能会做得非常出色的情况。通过这种良性管理，你将很少或根本没有机会加强你的弱点。

How do you test yourself for a new level you haven’t yet reached? If you dislike testing, you probably avoid putting your staff or colleagues through difficult tests. For the same reason, your own management may not push you hard enough. They want you to succeed, so they can succeed. They will put you in situations where you are likely to do well, not situations where you might do spectacularly. With this sort of benign management, you will get little or no chance to strengthen your weaknesses.

你可以自由地为自己选择这种良性或“稳定期”策略，避免真正考验你最弱能力的情况。它会让你变得更好，但它永远不会让你下一个悬崖。

You are free to choose this benign, or “plateau,” strategy for yourself, avoiding situations that will really test your weakest abilities. It will make you better, but it will never get you up the next cliff.

你怎么知道你是否为下一次攀登做好了适当的准备？问问自己最近有多少重要的测试失败了。如果我没有在每个研讨会上失败至少五次，我就会开始担心。如果我没有经常失败，我可能没有非常彻底地测试自己。假设我永远不会遇到粗鲁的陌生人或其他我无法完美解决的问题。这是否意味着我是一个完全发展的、全面的人格？它可能。不过，更有可能的是，这意味着有人在保护我免于成长。也许是我。

How can you know if you’re being properly prepared for the next climb? Ask yourself how many important tests you’ve failed recently. I start worrying if I don’t fail at least five times in each workshop. If I haven’t failed that often, I’m probably not testing myself very thoroughly. Suppose I never run into rude strangers or other problems I can’t solve perfectly. Does it mean I’m a fully developed, well-rounded personality? It might. More likely, though, it means that someone is protecting me from growth. Perhaps it’s me.

我会通过领导力测试吗？我当然希望不会。

Will I pass my leadership tests? I certainly hope not.

问题

QUESTIONS

  1. 您对计划的情况还是自发的情况更自在？你是如何努力构建一个最近的情况来发挥你的优势的？
  2. Are you more comfortable with planned or spontaneous situations? How did you endeavor to structure a recent situation to play to your strength?
  3. 回想一下您最近尝试处理时感到不舒服的情况。你从尝试中学到了什么？下次你会做什么？
  4. Recall a recent situation you felt uncomfortable trying to handle. What did you learn from trying? What will you do the next time?
  5. 在工作中，您是否曾经偷看过关闭的文件或摆弄过您不应该接触的机器？这告诉你关于你自己的什么？
  6. At work, have you ever peeked into closed files or fiddled with machinery you weren’t supposed to touch? What does that tell you about yourself?
  7. 当你很早就知道你将要接受测试时，你的情绪反应是什么？例如，当你必须参加驾驶考试或接受新职位的面试时？这与你突然发现自己正在接受测试时的反应有何不同？您可以从每种情况中借鉴什么来帮助您应对另一种情况？
  8. What’s your emotional reaction when you know long in advance you’re going to be tested, for instance, when you must take a driver’s test or submit to an interview for a new position? How does this differ from your reaction when you suddenly find out that you’re being tested? What can you borrow from each situation to help you in the other?
  9. 想想工作中一些持续的冲突。你能想出三个可能使冲突消失的组织变革吗？三个人际关系的变化如何？哪个清单对您来说更容易制定？你觉得哪个更舒服？
  10. Think of some ongoing conflict at work. Can you think of three organizational changes that might make the conflict disappear? How about three interpersonal changes? Which list was easier for you to develop? Which do you feel more comfortable with?
  11. 你如何看待测试其他人？他们对您对他们进行的测试感觉如何？如果你不知道他们的感受，你为什么不知道？
  12. How do you feel about testing other people? How do they feel about your testing them? If you don’t know how they feel, why don’t you know?

第 22 章个人变革计划

Chapter 22. A Personal Plan for Change

我总是很小心，不让我的学校教育干扰我的教育。

I was always careful never to let my schooling interfere with my education.

——马克·吐温。从给朋友的信

—Mark Twain. from a letter to a friend

人们从哪里学习成为创新者所需的东西？一旦他们忙于成为领导者，他们如何保持这种学习？他们可以在学校学习，还是只能在艰苦的学校学习？

Where to people learn the things they need to become innovators? How do they maintain that learning once they are busy being leaders? Can they learn in school, or only in the school of hard knocks?

即使有优秀课程的帮助，也很难掌握自己的工作内容，更不用说跟上新的发展了。除此之外，如果你想成为解决问题的领导者，你还必须掌握过程技能。那么，如何才能同时成为问题解决者和领导者呢？这似乎是一个人类无法实现的理想，但如果你将大学习分解成一系列小学习，注意你的教育策略的效率，并意识到你的情绪反应，这是可能的学习。

Even with the help of outstanding courses, it’s hard to retain a grip on the content of your work, not to mention keeping up with new developments. Added to that, if you want to be a problem-solving leader, you must also master process skills. So how is it possible to be both a problem solver and leader at the same time? It seems to be an ideal that can’t be accomplished by real human beings, but it’s possible if you break down big learnings into a sequence of little ones, pay attention to the efficiency of your educational strategies, and become aware of your emotional reaction to learning.

一个实验

AN EXPERIMENT

你有兴趣取得一些成就吗？担心你不会成功？不知道该怎么做？这是您现在可以做的一个实验，它将让您从右脚开始：

Are you interested in achieving something? Worried that you won’t succeed? Don’t know how to go about it? Here’s an experiment you can do right now that will get you started on the right foot:

步骤 1. 把这本书支起来，这样你就可以不用拿着它来阅读它。

Step 1. Prop up this book so you can read it without holding it in your hands.

Step 2. 双手合十，十指交叉。

Step 2. Clasp your hands, fingers interlaced.

第 3 步。注意哪个拇指在您紧握的双手上方。

Step 3. Take note of which thumb is on top of your clasped hands.

第 4 步。将您的手重新扣住，另一只拇指放在上面。注意这样做的感觉。

Step 4. Reclasp your hands with the other thumb on top. Note how it feels to do this.

第 5 步。 阅读完本章后，以这种新方式握紧双手。

Step 5. Hold your hands clasped in this new way as you finish reading this chapter.

变革的精神氛围

THE MENTAL CLIMATE FOR CHANGE

感觉如何？如果你和大多数人一样，改变你握手的习惯方式会感觉很奇怪。一方面，您更加了解自己的双手。做一些新的事情可以提高意识，这很刺激，但也有点不舒服。

How did it feel? If you’re like most people, it feels strange to change the habitual way you clasp hands. For one thing, you are more aware of your hands. Doing something new heightens awareness, which is stimulating but also a little uncomfortable.

大多数人无法将他们的新握手维持足够长的时间来阅读本章。片刻后，随着双手的意识逐渐消退，他们不自觉地又按旧方式重新握手。我曾经做过一次演讲，我向任何能在整个小时内双手紧握的人提供一美元。我以前没有提出过这个提议，我真的很担心冒 120 美元的风险。我不必担心——120 人中只有一个人认领了美元。当他领奖时，他告诉我这是他赚到的最艰难的一美元。

Most people cannot sustain their new handclasp long enough to read through this chapter. After a while, as consciousness of their hands fades, they unconsciously reclasp their hands in the old way. I once gave a lecture in which I offered a dollar to anyone who could keep both hands clasped through the entire hour. I hadn’t made this offer before, and I was really nervous about risking $120. I needn’t have worried—only one of the 120 claimed the dollar. When he claimed his prize, he told me it was the most difficult dollar he had ever earned.

为什么做新事物如此困难？有时会有财务风险，比如我的 120 美元。有时会有人身风险，例如您的降落伞无法打开。有时会有看起来很傻的风险，比如在 PacMan 游戏中输给了您 8 岁的女儿。即使您消除了所有财务和物理风险，困难的核心仍然存在。毕竟，以不同的方式握手会有什么风险？

Why is doing something new so difficult? Sometimes there is financial risk, like my $120. Sometimes there is physical risk, like your parachute failing to open. And sometimes there’s the risk of looking stupid, like losing to your eight-year-old daughter at PacMan. Even when you remove all financial and physical risks, a core of difficulty remains. After all, what’s the risk in clasping your hands a different way?

我的理论是，困难完全是精神上的，与你试图做的特定事情无关。我相信它是我们大脑中抵制新行为模式的硬件的一部分。在这个世界上，新事物通常都是危险的，所以每当我们尝试新事物时，大脑都会试图通过以下方式保护我们

My theory is that the difficulty is entirely mental, independent of the particular thing you’re trying to do. I believe it is part of the hardware in our brain to resist new patterns of behavior. In the world at large, new things are generally dangerous, so whenever we try something new, the brain tries to protect us by

  1. 让我们进入一种特殊的警觉状态，让我们更加关注周围的一切，而不仅仅是新的活动，并且
  2. putting us into a special state of alertness, so that we pay more attention to everything around us, not just the new activity, and
  3. 每当我们不再注意时，试图让我们回到旧的、安全的模式。
  4. trying to ease us back into the old, safe pattern whenever we stop paying attention.

这种特殊的警觉状态可以令人振奋，也可以使人虚弱。我去新的地方度假，这样我就可以用我所有的能力去感受这个世界。我喜欢这种感觉，但有时我对周围的新事物感到不知所措，以至于我只是退回到酒店房间的安全熟悉中。每当我试图在工作中取得新成就时，同样的事情就会发生。

The special state of alertness can be either exhilarating or debilitating. I take vacations to new places so I can sense the world with all my faculties. I love the feeling, but sometimes I’m so overwhelmed by the newness of my surroundings that I just retreat to the safe familiarity of my room at the hotel. The same thing happens whenever I try to achieve something new in my work.

不过，我旅行的次数越多，就越容易应对陌生环境而不会感到虚弱。仿佛对陌生熟悉了起来。个人成就也是如此。通过实践许多小成就，我学会了如何处理在我尝试取得一些大成就时可能会阻碍我的不熟悉的感觉。有时练习只需要在我的脑海中。

The more I travel, though, the easier it gets to deal with the unfamiliarity without being debilitated. It’s as if I have gotten familiar with unfamiliarity. The same is true with personal achievements. By practicing many small achievements, I learn how to deal with the unfamiliar feelings that might hamper me when I try for some big achievement. Sometimes the practice only has to be in my mind.

个人成就计划

A PERSONAL ACHIEVEMENT PLAN

Bill Holcombe 和我设计了以下练习，让人们了解自己对个人成就的反应：

Bill Holcombe and I designed the following exercise, which exposes people to their own reactions to personal achievement:

Step 1. 设定个人成就目标，安全、新颖、可以自己完成的目标。成就应该是即时的，这样你就会马上知道你达到了什么水平。

Step 1. Set a personal achievement goal, something that is safe, novel, and can be done by yourself. The achievement should have an immediacy, so that you will know right away what level of performance you have reached.

第 2 步。在第一天建立一个基线表现，然后在你的日记中记录你每天的进步，因为你每天至少练习一次。

Step 2. Establish a baseline performance on the first day, then record your progress each day in your journal as you practice at least once a day.

第 3 步。在最后一天，准备好展示绩效并讨论您在取得成就时发生的事情。

Step 3. On the last day, be prepared to demonstrate performance and discuss what happened as you worked on your achievement.

以下报告是典型的，可以让您了解这个简单练习的威力。

The following reports are typical, and will give you an idea of the power of this simple exercise.

拉塞尔： “我的任务是平衡网球上的这些小鸡尾酒棒。我了解到网球的表面每天都在变化，可能是因为人们在房间里扔它。否则一百万年后我永远不会注意到这一点。结果，我开始注意到人们每天都在变化，这需要每天都有一种新的平衡。”

Russell: “My task was balancing these little cocktail sticks on a tennis ball. I learned that the surface of the tennis ball changed each day, probably because people were tossing it around the room. I never would have noticed that in a million years otherwise. As a result, I began to notice that people change every day, too, which requires a new kind of balancing each day.”

维尔纳： “我用同一个网球来平衡米尺。也许太容易了，也许是因为拉塞尔也在平衡，但我在第二天改变了任务。所以我学到的第一件事是，如果它不符合我的目的，我可以改变我为自己设定的目标。我的新任务是每天早上写三件我想在与他人互动时练习的东西。我做到了！我很惊讶它是多么容易，同时又很难。”

Werner: “I was using the same tennis ball to balance a meter stick. Maybe it was too easy, or maybe it was because Russell was also balancing, but I changed my task on the second day. So the first thing I learned was that I could change a goal I set for myself, if it wasn’t serving my purposes. My new task was writing three things each morning I wanted to practice while interacting with other people. And I did it! I was amazed at how easy it was, at the same time it was hard.”

蕾妮： “我玩过电子游戏 Gorf。我以前从未玩过这样的电子游戏，所以我从来没有真正理解自己在做什么。我什至不明白在这台机器上做得好意味着什么。结果，我没有设定明确的目标，所以我没有进行实验，也没有学到太多关于 Gorf 的知识。我确实了解了当我不设定目标时会发生什么。”

Renee: “I played Gorf, the video game. I’d never played a video game like that before, so I never really understood what I was doing. I didn’t even understand what doing well meant on this machine. As a result, I set no explicit goals, so I didn’t experiment, and I didn’t learn much about Gorf. I did learn about what happens when I don’t set goals.”

厄尔： “我的任务是处理三个截然不同的物体：一卷美纹纸胶带、一支记号笔和一个骰子。我以前玩过杂耍，而且很擅长，但是不同的大小和形状让这三个物体的杂耍就像管理三个不同的人一样，这就是我在工作中所做的，但我看不出有什么可以从一种情况转移到另一个。

Earl: “My task was juggling three very different objects: a roll of masking tape, a marker pen, and a die. I had juggled before and was pretty good at it, but the different sizes and shapes made juggling the three objects like managing three different people, which is what I do at work, but I couldn’t see anything I could transfer from one situation to the other.

“到第三天，我已经在 17 秒的峰值时间趋于平稳。杰里看着我问：“你总是从同一个位置开始吗？” 我从来没有想过，实际上一直在做两个不同的杂耍序列。在标准化开始顺序的五分钟内，我的最佳时间跃升至二十八秒！对我来说，这意味着在与三个截然不同的人打交道时，如果我知道我从哪里开始，就会容易得多。我将尝试将我的问题与他们的问题分开。”

“By the third day, I had leveled off at a peak time of seventeen seconds. Jerry was watching me and asked, ‘Do you always start from the same position?’ I had never thought about it, and had actually been doing two different juggling sequences. Within five minutes of standardizing the starting order, my best time jumped to twenty-eight seconds! To me, this means that in dealing with three very different people, it will be a lot easier if I know where I’ve started. I’m going to try separating my issues from theirs.”

Tanya： “我玩过吃豆人。我是一个普通的瘾君子，我希望在这个新环境中玩游戏可以帮助我的游戏。我确实玩了新的意识。我发现我太紧张了，所以我试着保持冷静。我可以有一段时间，然后我又回到了同样的过度强度模式。我需要一些东西来提醒我——定期将我从自己的参与中拉出来。”

Tanya: “I played PacMan. I’m a regular addict, and I hoped that playing in this new environment might help my game. I did play with new awareness. I found I was being too intense, so I tried to stay calm. I could for a while, then I fell back into the same pattern of excess intensity. I need something to remind me—to pull me outside of my own involvement at regular intervals.”

Shih： “我的工作是以某种方式从画架上弹起一个球，但我不太喜欢这样做。我发现自己没有这样做，所以我想，了解我，第二天我会回去补上我错过的练习。这就是我通常做的，通过坚持一些我知道是无用的东西来不断惩罚自己。但我很惊讶自己并没有回头。我了解到我不必完成愚蠢的事情，而且不会发生任何可怕的事情！”

Shih: “My job was bouncing a ball off the easel in a certain pattern, but I didn’t like doing it very much. I found myself not doing it, so I thought, knowing me, that I would go back the next day and make up the practice I’d missed. That’s what I usually do, keep punishing myself by sticking with something that I know is useless. But I surprised myself and didn’t go back to it. I learned that I didn’t have to finish stupid things, and that nothing horrible will happen!”

佩吉： “我决定学会在没有手表的情况下尽可能准确地计时五分钟。我很容易分心。我了解到，我习惯性地用一半的心思在一件事上，另一半在另一件事上。”

Peggy: “I decided to learn to keep time for five minutes without a watch, as accurately as I could. I was easily distracted. I learned that I habitually use half of my mind on one thing, half on another.”

德里克： “基于杰里说他曾尝试过的一个想法，我决定在讨论中将发言保留到最后，看看它是否会以任何方式伤害我。我学会了这样做，而且并不难。我还了解到，当我最后发言时，人们似乎听得更清楚了。”

Derek: “Keying on an idea that Jerry said he’d tried, I decided to hold my speaking to the last in a discussion, to see if it would hurt me in any way. I learned to do it, and it wasn’t that hard. I also learned that people seemed to hear me better when I spoke last.”

它能有所作为吗？

CAN IT MAKE A DIFFERENCE?

成就计划不仅仅是一种在网球上练习平衡鸡尾酒棒的方法，它还是一种学习如何取得成就的方法。要了解您自己的改变风格，您可以从制定自己的成就计划开始。

An achievement plan is not just a way to practice balancing cocktail sticks on tennis balls, it’s a way of learning how you achieve things. To find out about your own style of changing, you can start by setting up your own achievement plan.

你会学到的第一件事是你拒绝尝试学习某些东西的建议的风格。如果您怀疑在网球上平衡鸡尾酒棒的有用性，也许您太过小心翼翼地避免改变。没有人说成就必须是无用的。沃纳练习了与人的新型互动。

The first thing you’ll learn is your style of resisting suggestions that you try to learn something. If you have doubts about the usefulness of balancing cocktail sticks on a tennis ball, perhaps you’re too well protected against change. Nobody said that the achievement had to be useless. Werner practiced new kinds of interactions with people.

“但是，”你说，“这些只是很小的成就。要成为领导者，从我现在的位置开始，我必须做一些大事。” 这个论点听起来像是飞机乘客的推理，如果一个引擎的故障导致一小时的延误，那么所有四个引擎的故障只会导致四个小时的延误。

“But,” you say, “these are only small achievements. To become a leader, starting where I am now, I’d have to do something big.” This argument sounds like the airplane passenger who reasons that if the failure of one engine produces a one-hour delay, the failure of all four engines will simply produce a four-hour delay.

了解飞机结构的人可以预测，失去四个发动机会导致坠机。那些了解变化本质的人可以预测，沿着这条线的某个地方，一系列普通的、小的变化会突然把你带到巨大而非凡的事物的边缘。那些了解自己以及他们对变化的反应的人有勇气走到那个边缘并冒险一试。

Those who understand the structure of an airplane can predict that the loss of four engines will cause a crash. And those who understand the nature of change can predict that somewhere along the line, a series of ordinary, small changes will suddenly put you on the brink of something large and extraordinary. Those who understand themselves, and their reactions to change, have the courage to go to that brink and to take the plunge.

计划要素

ELEMENTS OF A PLAN

例如，假设您决定开始正规教育课程。尽管马克吐温和我对他们说过坏话，但学校为提高学习效率提供了真正的可能性。虽然大学课程可以很好地利用你的时间，但你必须知道，作为一个成人学习者，你是否仍然可以忍受学生的角色。这种对自己的了解也可以帮助你选择课程，因为一些大学课程是面向在职成年人的。

Suppose, for instance, that you decide to embark on a course of formal education. In spite of the bad things that Mark Twain and I have said about them, schools offer real possibilities for efficiency in learning. Although college courses can be a good use of your time, you must know whether you, as an adult learner, can still tolerate the student role. This knowledge of yourself can also help you select courses, because some college courses are oriented to working adults.

尽管大学通常会建议标准课程，但您最终必须根据自己的工作经验和学习方式选择自己的课程顺序。许多管理课程充满了理论，如果您在任命的领导角色方面经验不足，那么这些理论将毫无意义。经验使理论有意义，尽管有些人发现理论使经验有意义。确定您属于哪种类型，然后相应地选择您自己的课程顺序。

Although colleges often suggest standard programs, you must ultimately choose your own sequence of courses according to your work experience and style of learning. Many management courses are full of theory that won’t make any sense if you’ve had little experience in appointed leader roles. The experience makes the theory meaningful, though some people find that theory makes experience meaningful. Decide which type you are and choose your own sequence of courses accordingly.

不是每个人都需要正式课程，但有些人需要结构来激励他们。有时，是他们的老板需要结构化课程的激励。不允许阅读有关公司时间的书的人可能会发现同一家公司会支付他们请假上大学课程的费用。你必须了解你自己，你也必须了解你的环境。

Not everybody needs formal courses, but some need structure to motivate them. Sometimes, it’s their boss who needs the motivation of a structured course. People who are not allowed to read a book on company time may discover that the same company will pay them for taking time off for a college course. You must know yourself, and you must also know your environment.

如果您了解自己的学习方式和环境，那么您所学的课程不一定非要在大学里。许多公司提供内部课程；有些甚至有公司课程，由内部讲师和外部顾问教授。然后，还有各种营利和非营利组织举办的公开研讨会。诀窍是找出您的组织认为合法的东西，然后充分利用它。

If you know your learning style and environment, the courses you take don’t have to be in college. Many companies offer internal courses; some even have a company curriculum, taught by in-house instructors and outside consultants. Then, there are public seminars conducted by a variety of profit-making and non-profit organizations. The trick is to find out what your organization considers legitimate, then take full advantage of it.

你身边有成千上万的学习机会，但如果没有计划，你就会错过其中的大部分。通过集思广益一些想法来开始您的计划以添加到上述内容中，例如，

You are surrounded by thousands of opportunities for learning, but without a plan, you will miss most of them. Start your plan by brainstorming some ideas to add to those above, such as,

参加更多的会议。

Attend more conferences.

使用会议录音带。

Use audio tapes of conferences.

在培训活动中使用录像带。

Use video tapes at training events.

分组观看录像带。

Watch video tapes in groups.

花更多的时间讨论而不是看录像带。

Spend more time discussing than watching the tape.

在没有磁带的情况下组织一个讨论组。

Organize a discussion group without the tapes.

每周一次在午餐时间领导一个定期讨论小组。

Lead a regular discussion group at lunch once a week.

阅读一本书并根据实际工作进行讨论。

Read a book and discuss it in terms of real work.

教一个班。

Teach a class.

（并且，在 2010 年添加，使用互联网！）

(And, added in 2010, Use the internet!)

当你的头脑风暴枯竭时，寻求他人的帮助。例如，您可以阅读 Ronald Gross 的The Lifelong Learner（纽约：Simon 和 Schuster，1979 年）以获得更多想法。（或者，在 2010 年再次添加，使用互联网！）

When your brainstorm dries up, seek the help of others. For instance, you might read Ronald Gross’s The Lifelong Learner (New York: Simon and Schuster, 1979) for more ideas. (Or, again added in 2010, Use the internet!)

无论您集思广益列出什么清单，无论您从他人那里得到什么建议，您的个人改变计划都归结为了解自己。没有其他人可以为您做这件事，那么现在就制定您的第一个个人计划怎么样？你计划的第一步应该是：对你自己的教育负责。

Whatever list you brainstorm, whatever advice you get from others, your personal plan for change all comes down to understanding yourself. Nobody else can do it for you, so how about setting up your first personal plan, right now? The first step in your plan should be this: Take responsibility for your own education.

顺便问一下，你的手是怎么握紧的？

By the way, how are your hands clasped?

问题

QUESTIONS

  1. 选择一项小技能，今天用三个十五分钟的时间练习它。在日记中写下你的反应和观察。
  2. Pick a small skill and work on it for three fifteen-minute periods today. Write your reactions and observations in your journal.
  3. 选择一项稍大的技能，并在接下来的一周内练习五次，每节三十分钟，在日记中记录你的反应和观察结果。
  4. Pick a slightly larger skill and work on it for five thirty-minute periods in the coming week, recording your reactions and observations in your journal.
  5. 现在用一项技能做同样的事情，你将每周工作三个小时，持续一个月。当你完成这个月时，回顾你的日记并决定你下一步想做什么。
  6. Now do the same thing with a skill that you will work on three hours per week for a month. When you finish the month, review your journal and decide what you want to do next.
  7. 您以何种方式为试图改变自己的同事制造障碍？例如，您会嘲笑他们的失败，还是嘲笑他们选择改变的事情？这告诉你关于你自己的什么？
  8. In what ways do you create obstacles for co-workers who are trying to change something about themselves? For example, do you laugh at their failures, or at the things they have chosen to change? What does this tell you about yourself?
  9. 您一生中接受过多少小时的正规教育？其中有多少是针对您的技术能力的？其中有多少是针对你与他人合作的能力？
  10. How many hours of formal education have you had in your life? How much of that was directed to your technical abilities? How much of it was directed to your ability to work with other people?
  11. 您接受过多少小时的非正式教育？如何区分技术培训和人员培训？这些划分是否合适？他们是否在为您的职业目标服务？
  12. How many hours of informal education have you had? How was that divided between technical and people training? Are these divisions appropriate? Are they serving your career goals?
  13. 您能列出三位与您一起工作并且可以教您一些东西来提高您的技术能力的人吗？如果不是，你为什么在那里工作？如果是这样，您正在做什么以最大程度地利用此资源？
  14. Can you list three people who work with you and who can teach you things to increase your technical abilities? If not, why are you working there? If so, what are you doing to take maximum advantage of this resource?
  15. 过去一年你修了哪些课程？他们对您在技术领导方面取得的进步有何贡献？你可以做些什么来从每门课程中获得更多？
  16. What courses have you taken in the past year? What have they contributed to your progress toward technical leadership? What could you have done to get more out of each course?
  17. 你计划在来年学习哪些课程？您准备如何充分利用它们？
  18. What courses do you plan to take in the coming year? How are you preparing yourself to take maximum advantage of them?
  19. 过去三个月你读了哪些书？每个人对你的进步有何贡献？您正在做什么以确保您在接下来的三个月中阅读的书籍做出更大的贡献？
  20. What books have you read in the past three months? How has each contributed to your progress? What are you doing to make sure that the books you read in the next three months make a greater contribution?
  21. 列出过去一年中有助于维持您的技术实力或提高您的领导能力的活动。你能在列表中添加三个项目吗？你为什么犹豫？
  22. Make a list of activities that contributed to the maintenance of your technical prowess, or the growth of your leadership abilities, in the past year. Can you add three items to the list? Why do you hesitate?

第 23 章找时间改变

Chapter 23. Finding Time to Change

当你和一个好女孩坐在一起两个小时时，你会认为这只是一分钟。但是，当您坐在热炉子上一分钟时，您会认为是两个小时。这就是相对论。-艾尔伯特爱因斯坦

When you sit with a nice girl for two hours, you think it’s only a minute. But when you sit on a hot stove for a minute, you think it’s two hours. That’s relativity.—Albert Einstein

当我的客户挑战我用实际例子来支持我的理论时，时间比坐在热炉上慢。我最近和一些客户共进午餐，在理论化个人改变计划时，克莱顿说，“我忙于跟上我的工作，我怎么会有时间做出改变？”

When my clients are challenging me to back up my theories with practical examples, time goes slower than when sitting on a hot stove. I was having lunch with some clients recently, theorizing about planning for personal change, when Clayton said, “I’m so busy just keeping up with my job, how will I ever have time to make changes?”

克莱顿的评论使我们危险地接近了时间管理的讨论，我是一个普通的爱因斯坦：在这个主题上理论多，实践少。我想享受一顿悠闲的午餐，所以我试图让他闭嘴，说：“你没有时间，克莱顿，你有时间。” 你可以腾出时间做任何你真正想做的事情，所以如果你没时间做，也许你不想做。也许你应该想办法不这样做。”

Clayton’s remark brought us dangerously close to a discussion of time management, a subject on which I’m a regular Einstein: long on theory and short on practice. I wanted to enjoy a leisurely lunch, so I tried to shut him up by remarking, “You don’t find time, Clayton, you make time. You can make time for whatever you really want to do, so if you don’t find time for it, perhaps you don’t want to do it. Perhaps you should find a way not to do it.”

我认为这会吓到他们并结束这个话题，但梅勒妮转向我说：“这是个好主意，但有点含糊。你对如何打发时间有什么建议吗？”

I thought that would intimidate them and close the subject, but Melanie turned to me and said, “That’s a great idea, but a little vague. Do you have any suggestions on how to make time?”

“一些，”我撒谎，嚼着一根胡萝卜棒。“但现在没有时间进入这个主题。”

“A few,” I lied, chomping on a carrot stick. “But there’s no time to go into the subject right now.”

“当然有——如果你真的想的话，”梅兰妮说。“此外，这是我们在一起的最后几个小时。我们必须充分利用我们的时间。”

“Sure there is—if you really want to,” Melanie said. “Besides, these are the last few hours we have together. We have to make the most of our time.”

我试图转移话题，说我也很赶时间，因为那天下午我得赶飞机去瑞士。美国人通常可以通过讲瑞士的故事来分散注意力：威廉·泰尔的故事、海蒂的故事、爱因斯坦的故事。我描绘了一幅理想国家的图画：美丽、高效、干净、友好、治理良好。我解释了瑞士人如何定期和普遍地表现出那种让你认为每个瑞士人都必须同时做两件事的效率，但每个人都有时间向陌生人提供礼貌的帮助。我确信这会转移 Clayton 和 Melanie 的注意力，但没有奏效。

I tried to change the subject by remarking that I was in a hurry, too, because I had to catch a plane that afternoon for Switzerland. Americans can usually be distracted from anything by telling stories about Switzerland: William Tell stories, Heidi stories, Einstein stories. I painted a picture of the ideal country: beautiful, efficient, clean, friendly, and well-governed. I explained how the Swiss regularly and universally display the kind of efficiency that makes you think every Swiss must be doing two things at once, yet everyone has time to offer polite assistance to a stranger. I was sure this would divert Clayton and Melanie, but it didn’t work.

“他们是怎么做到的呢？” 克莱顿问道。“听起来我可以从瑞士的方法中学到一些关于时间管理的东西。”

“How do they do it?” Clayton asked. “It sounds like I could learn a few things about time management from the Swiss approach.”

保持目标

STAYING ON TARGET

看着克莱顿拔他的洋蓟，我开始体会到弗兰肯斯坦博士当时的感受。克莱顿曾经是一个反应迅速的客户，但现在他是我的怪物，我知道我必须照顾他的需要。“大多数人在讲述自己的故事时都会透露很多东西，”我回答说，希望用一个冗长的故事来分散他的注意力。

Looking at Clayton plucking his artichoke, I started to appreciate how Dr. Frankenstein must have felt. Clayton had been a responsive client, but now he was my monster, and I knew I would have to take care of his needs. “Most people reveal a great deal in the stories they tell about themselves,” I replied, hoping to distract him with a long-winded tale.

“给我们讲一个瑞士的故事，”克莱顿吞下诱饵说道，然后我继续讲我最喜欢的故事之一：

“Tell us a Swiss story,” Clayton said, swallowing the bait, and I proceeded with one of my favorites:

*在对年轻的瑞士联邦进行国事访问时，奥地利大公正在检阅军队。为了检验一支军队的真面目，他习惯性地挑出一名普通士兵进行盘问。“你们的军队有多少？” 他问他现在的选择，一个胡子拉碴的青年。

*On a state visit to the tiny young Swiss Confederation, the Archduke of Austria was reviewing the troops. To test the true mettle of an army, he habitually singled out one ordinary soldier for questioning. “How large is your army?” he asked his current choice, an unshaven youth.

“五千人，长官，”骄傲而准确的回答传来。

“Five thousand men, Sir,” came the proud, precise reply.

“嗯嗯。相当令人印象深刻。但是，如果我和一万人一起穿过你的边界，你会怎么办？”

“Hmmnh. Quite impressive. But what would you do if I should march across your border with ten thousand men?”

士兵毫不犹豫地说道：“那么，先生，我们应该每人发射两发子弹。”*

Without hesitation, the soldier said, “Then, Sir, we should each have to fire two bullets.”*

“我明白了，”克莱顿在我说完后说。“关于时间管理的寓言真是太棒了。瑞士的秘诀是让每颗子弹都击中目标，或者正如吉卜林所说，用 60 秒的长距离跑来填补“每一分钟”。如果你像瑞士人一样高效，你就有时间跟上一切。但怎样才能有效率呢？”

“I get it,” Clayton said when I had finished. “What a brilliant parable about time management. The Swiss secret is making each bullet hit the target or, as Kipling said, filling ‘each unforgiving minute with sixty seconds’ worth of distance run.’ If you’re efficient, like the Swiss, you have time to keep up with everything. But how to be efficient?”

我没有给克莱顿讲授一个不是我最擅长的主题，而是让桌上的其他人贡献他们最喜欢的逃避时间陷阱的方法：

Rather than give Clayton a lecture on a subject that isn’t my best, I let the other people at the table contribute their favorite ways to escape time traps:

不要重做您分配给其他人的工作。当你这样做时，你为同样的工作付出了数倍的代价：首先是花时间向他们解释，然后是在不伤害他们感情的情况下收回它（这真的行不通），然后是修理任何东西的时间他们造成的损害，终于是时候自己动手了。“每当有人表现出犯错的迹象时——甚至是不确定的迹象——我都会以教他们一些东西为借口把作品拿回来，”玛戈承认道。“我终于明白，你必须让他们犯错。这是你付出的代价的一部分，从长远来看，它更有效率。”

Don’t redo work you’ve assigned to others. When you do, you pay several times for the same work: first with the time to explain it to them, then with the time to take it back without hurting their feelings (which really won’t work), then the time to repair any damage they’d done, and finally the time to do it yourself. “Whenever someone showed signs of making a mistake—even of uncertainty—I grabbed the work back under the pretense of teaching them something,” Margo admitted. “I’ve finally learned that you must let them make mistakes. It’s part of the price you pay, and it’s more efficient in the long run.”

避免琐碎的技术争论来证明你的技术优势。“随着你事业的发展，”德克说，“你必须放弃某些事情。争论次要的技术问题表明你还在坚持。当我在技术上真的很优秀时，”他解释说，“没有任何冗长的争论，因为我可以迅速而轻松地说服人们。”

Avoid trivial technical arguments to prove your technical superiority. “As your career advances,” Dirk said, “you have to let go of certain things. Arguing over minor technical points indicates you’re still holding on. When I really am technically superior,” he explained, “there isn’t any long argument because I can convince people swiftly and easily.”

选择你自己的优先事项，不要等到危机来组织你的活动。琳达坦言：“突然间，当我被任命为组长时，我不仅要为其他人安排时间，更糟糕的是，我还要为自己安排时间。我对此没有任何经验。当出现危机时，我感觉非常好，因为危机为我计划了我的活动。现在一想到我什至可能引发了其中一些危机，我就感到困扰，因为它们让我看起来非常有条理。现在我觉得，真正考验一个领导者的，是她在无事可做时的所作所为。”

Choose your own priorities and don’t wait for a crisis to organize your activities. Linda admitted, “Suddenly, when I was appointed group leader, not only did I have to organize time for other people, but even worse, I had to organize it for myself. I didn’t have any experience at that. When there was a crisis, I felt real good because the crisis planned my activities for me. It bothers me now to think that I may have even precipitated some of those crises because they made me look really well- organized. Now I feel that the true test of a leader is what she does when there’s nothing to do.”

同时做两件事

DOING TWO THINGS AT ONCE

桌上的每个人都说了他们的作品，这让我有时间享用我自己的朝鲜蓟。但是克莱顿不会让我一个人呆着。“再来一个瑞士故事怎么样？” 他要求。克莱顿是我创造的，他知道我的弱点。

Everyone at the table had said their piece, which gave me time to enjoy my own artichoke. But Clayton wouldn’t let me alone. “How about another Swiss story?” he demanded. Clayton was my creation, and he knew my weaknesses.

“好吧，”我承认道。“但这是最后一个了。这是关于瑞士如何创建的故事。”

“All right,” I conceded. “But this is the last one. It’s the story of how Switzerland was created.”

*上帝在创造了地球上所有的民族之后，依次为每个人创造了一个国家。当第一个瑞士人被问及他对一个国家的期望时，他谦虚地回答说任何事情都可以。

*After creating all the peoples of the Earth, God created a country for each one in turn. When the first Swiss was asked what he wanted for a country, he replied humbly that anything would do.

当上帝坚持要他表达他的偏好时，瑞士人说：“好吧，如果你坚持的话，我想要几座山，顶上有雪，山坡上有绿草，山谷里有清澈的湖泊，头顶有蓝天几朵蓬松的白云。”

When God insisted he express his preference, the Swiss said, “Well, if Thou insisteth, I would like a few mountains, with snow on top, green grass on the slopes, clear lakes in the valleys, and a blue sky above with a few fluffy white clouds.”

上帝立即实现了这个愿望，然后询问瑞士人是否还有其他愿望。在多次抗议之后，瑞士人终于说：“如果有一座石屋顶的木屋，也许还有几头在草地上吃草的棕色奶牛，我将非常感激。”

God granted this wish instantly, then asked if the Swiss had any other desires. After much protesting, the Swiss finally said, “I would be most grateful for a wooden house with a stone roof, and perhaps a few brown cows grazing on the grass.”

上帝有义务，然后问瑞士人是否还有其他事情。“不行，你已经太大方了。我实在不能再求别的了，但你呢？这么多奇妙的礼物，一定有什么东西可以回报我吧？”

God obliged, then asked the Swiss if there was anything else. “No, Thou hast already been too generous. I really cannot ask for anything else, but what about Thee? There must be something I can provide in return for all these wondrous gifts?”

“嗯，实际上，”上帝说，“我对所有这些创造物感到有点口渴。如果你能给我一杯你的奶牛的新鲜牛奶，我将不胜感激。”

“Well, actually,” God said, “I am a bit thirsty from all this Creation. I’d be most obliged if you could give me a glass of fresh milk from one of your cows.”

“我会很高兴，”瑞士人说，然后跑去拿一杯美妙、浓郁的瑞士牛奶，上帝渴了就喝了。

“I’d be delighted,” said the Swiss, and ran off to get a glass of that wonderful, rich Swiss milk, which God drank thirstily.

现在上帝又转向了瑞士人。“肯定还有你想要的东西。世间万民，求财无穷。你想要什么？”

Now God turned again to the Swiss. “Surely there must be something else you desire. All the other people of the world have asked for riches without end. What do you wish?”

瑞士人犹豫了一下，然后说：“是的，还有一件小事。”

The Swiss hesitated, then said, “Yes, there is one more little thing.”

“那你告诉我这是什么，它就是你的了。”

“Then tell me what it is, and it shall be yours.”

“好吧，如果不是要价太高的话，请给我 1 法郎的牛奶费。”*

“Well, if it’s not asking too much, that will be one franc for the milk, please.”*

“太棒了，”克莱顿说。“瑞士人不仅效率高，而且效率高。瑞士人不祈求财富，而是祈求能产生收入的东西，能在未来继续支付的东西，即使在上帝不再创造或购买牛奶之后。”

“That’s terrific,” said Clayton. “The Swiss are not just efficient, they’re doubly efficient. Rather than ask for riches, the Swiss asks for something that will produce income, something that will continue paying in the future, even after God is no longer around to create or buy milk.”

“我想你是对的，克莱顿，”我回答道。“和瑞士人一样，成功的解决问题的领导者似乎能够创造出比付出多一点的情况。然而与此同时，这些情况似乎对所有相关人员都有好处；没有人被骗。”

“I suppose you’re right, Clayton,” I replied. “And like the Swiss, successful problem-solving leaders seem able to create situations where they get a little more out than they put in. Yet at the same time, these situations seem to benefit everyone involved; nobody gets cheated.”

“能举几个例子吗？”

“Can you give me some examples?”

“我宁愿再围着桌子转一圈，这样我就可以吃完午饭了。” 所以他们每个人都举了一个例子：

“I’d rather go around the table again, so I can finish my lunch.” And so they each gave an example:

梅兰妮： “我通过领导其他团队的技术审查来了解技术上的进展。它让我有机会练习我的人际交往能力。同时，我也受益于听到我们最好的技术人员就我们现在正在进行的项目交换意见。”

Melanie: “I keep up with what’s going on technically by leading technical reviews for other teams. It gives me a chance to practice my people skills. At the same time, I get the benefit of hearing our best technical people trade views about the very projects we’re working on now.”

琳达： “我也复习，但方式不同。我阅读期刊的技术论文并担任审稿人或编辑。它迫使我深入研究一些好的技术内容，并且我在管理方面得到了很多加分。这也提高了我自己的写作技巧。”

Linda: “I also review, but in a different way. I read technical papers for journals and act as reviewer or editor. It forces me to dig into some good technical content, and I get lots of brownie points with management for doing it. It also improves my own writing skills.”

德克： “我们有所有这些录像带上的技术课程，但每个人似乎都讨厌坐在一个小房间里看它们。所以我自愿辅导参加视频课程的人。它提高了我的沟通技巧，一对一的人际交往能力，但更重要的是，它让我有机会真正深入地学习技术材料。从技术上讲，我从中学到的东西比任何学生都多，但我每周花在这上面的时间从未超过四个小时。”

Dirk: “We have all these technical courses on video tape, but everybody seems to hate just sitting in a little room and watching them. So I volunteered to tutor people who were taking the video courses. It sharpens my communication skills, my one-to-one handling of people, but even more, it gives me a chance to really learn the technical material in depth. I get far more out of it technically than any of the students do, yet I never spend more than four hours a week doing it.”

金斯利： “我被任命为我们的访问演讲者计划的协调员。这意味着如果我感兴趣的话，我可以参加外部专家举办的每场演讲、研讨会或课程。但比这更好的是，我可以花时间和这些来访的专家一起讨论任何我想讨论的话题。这就像金钱可以买到的最聪明的人的私人辅导。而大部分实际的协调工作都是由我的秘书完成的。”

Kingsley: “I got myself appointed as coordinator of our visiting speaker program. That means I get to attend every speech, seminar, or class that’s done by an outside expert, if I’m interested. But better than that, I get to spend time with these visiting experts, time when I can discuss any subject I want. It’s like having private tutoring from the smartest people that money can buy. And most of the actual coordination work is done by my secretary.”

梅林： “我们的停车场是我在技术上跟上的地方。我每天花两个小时与我们的顶级设计师和两个最好的分析师在一起。我所要做的就是让谈话远离其他话题。”

Merlin: “Our car pool is where I keep up technically. I spend two hours every day with our top designer and two of our best analysts. All I have to do is keep the conversation steered away from other subjects.”

凯瑟琳： “直到我们三个人意识到我们遇到了同样的问题并决定分担负担之前，我永远无法跟上我所有的技术阅读。我们每个人都读了一些东西并向其他人报告。有时，第一位读者可以告诉他们跳过一半的材料。有时他们可以跳过这一切。我现在用同样的努力获得了三倍的覆盖率，而且我还与我小组中的其他两个人建立了特殊的关系。”

Kathryn: “I could never keep up with all my technical reading until three of us realized we had the same problem and decided to share the load. We each read something and report on it to the others. Sometimes, the first reader can tell them to skip half the material. Sometimes they can skip it all. I get three times the coverage now for the same effort, and I also have developed a special relationship with the other two in my group.”

最便宜的学费

THE CHEAPEST TUITION

“你知道，杰里，”我们吃完午餐回来时克莱顿说，“我不想让你误会，但我想我在午餐时学到的东西比我从其他咨询中学到的要多。”

“You know, Jerry,” Clayton said as we were returning from lunch, “I don’t want you to take this the wrong way, but I think I learned more at lunch than I did from the rest of your consulting.”

“我没有生气，”我说，尽量不表现出我的沮丧。“这只是双重工作时间的另一个例子。”

“I’m not offended,” I said, trying not to show my devastation. “It’s just another example of double-duty time.”

“你知道，”克莱顿笑着说，“我曾想去瑞士呆几年，进一步研究瑞士人的做事方式。”

“You know,” Clayton smiled, “I was thinking of going to Switzerland for a few years to study some more about the way the Swiss do it.”

“是的，这是一个不错的方法，但它可能相当昂贵。”

“Yes, that’s a nice way, but it can be rather expensive.”

“你能想出更便宜又不太糟糕的东西吗？”

“Can you think of something cheaper that’s not too awful?”

“我想你已经知道这种方法了。”

“I think you already know such a way.”

“我愿意？”

“I do?”

“当然。与其去瑞士旅游，何不去研究身边最优秀的人的学习方法呢？就像你今天午餐时做的那样。他们已经为你付了学费。你所要做的就是倾听，这甚至不需要你花一分钱。”

“Sure. Instead of traveling to Switzerland, why not study the learning methods of the best people around you? Like you did at lunch today. They’ve already paid the tuition for you. All you have to do is listen, and that doesn’t even cost you a franc.”

克莱顿把午餐时做的笔记递给我。“我知道你正在写一本关于成为领导者的书，”克莱顿说，“如果他们来自像我们这样的普通人，你的读者可能会更加重视这些。” 有那种鼓励，我怎么可能不给呢？所以他们在这里：

Clayton handed me the notes he had taken during lunch. “I know you’re writing a book on becoming a leader,” Clayton said, “and your readers might put more value on these if they came from ordinary people like us.” With that encouragement, how could I fail to give them? So here they are:

在一天中腾出更多时间

Making More Time in a Day

• 不要做你已经分配给别人的事情，即使你必须让他们犯错。

• Don’t do things you’ve already assigned to others, even if you must let them make mistakes.

• 避免像瘟疫一样的管理。

• Avoid administration like the plague.

• 不要浪费时间试图证明你的能力。

• Don’t waste time trying to prove your competence.

• 不要浪费时间争论浪费时间。

• Don’t waste time arguing about wasting time.

• 无事可做时注意自己的所作所为。

• Pay attention to what you do when there’s nothing to do.

• 以一个的价格获得至少两个。

• Get at least two for the price of one.

• 担任审核负责人。

• Act as review leader.

• 担任编辑。

• Act as editor.

• 担任导师。

• Be a tutor.

• 协调演讲或培训计划。

• Coordinate a speaking or training program.

• 使用您的拼车。

• Use your car pool.

• 分担阅读负担。

• Share the reading load.

• 吃一顿丰盛的午餐，但要有创意。

• Have a good lunch, but a creative one.

最重要的是

and most important

• 听别人已经学过的东西。

• Listen to what other people have already learned.

我忍不住再添加一个：

To which I can’t resist adding one more:

• 让其他人向您展示他们有多聪明。

• Let other people show you how smart they are.

问题

QUESTIONS

  1. 你如何受到时间压力的影响？你用什么策略来缓解时间压力？
  2. How are you affected by time pressures? What tactics do you use to relieve time pressures?
  3. 在你的什么活动中，时间过得最快？最慢？这些活动告诉你什么？关于你自己？
  4. During what activity of yours does time go by the fastest? the slowest? What does that tell you about these activities? about yourself?
  5. 举例说明你如何同时做两件事。请三位同事给你举类似的例子。你可以使用它们中的任何一个吗？
  6. Give an example of how you do two things at once. Ask three co-workers to give you similar examples. Can you use any of them?
  7. 当你无事可做时，比如约会在最后一刻被取消时，你会怎么做？
  8. What do you do when you don’t have anything to do, as when an appointment is cancelled at the last moment?
  9. 你有时能“什么都不做”吗？您是否有时间环顾四周并审视自己，看看为什么您没有更多时间？如果没有，请立即停止阅读并立即阅读。
  10. Are you able to “do nothing” some of the time? Do you have time to look around you and at yourself to discover why you don’t have more time? If not, stop reading immediately and do it now.

第 24 章寻找变革的支持

Chapter 24. Finding Support for Change

在动词“爱”之后，“帮助”是世界上最美丽的动词！

After the verb, “To Love,” “To Help” is the most beautiful verb in the world!

——伯莎·冯·苏特纳。1905 年诺贝尔和平奖获得者，地面武器

—Bertha von Suttner. Winner of the 1905 Nobel Peace Prize, Ground Arms

人们成为领导者时认为他们会帮助其他人。不久，他们意识到需要帮助的是他们自己。他们需要帮助才能像其他人一样看待自己，帮助他们克服错误，了解他人，并处理试图提供帮助的挫败感。学会乐于助人的唯一方法就是学会被帮助。

People become leaders thinking they will help other people. Before long they realize that it’s they who need help. They need help to see themselves as others see them, to carry them through their mistakes, to learn about other people, and to deal with the frustrations of trying to be helpful. The only way to learn to be helpful is by learning to be helped.

经受住从个人创新者转变为有效解决问题领导者的人通常得到大量其他人网络的支持，尽管他们并不总是认识到这种支持系统的存在。要么他们很幸运并且拥有一个，要么他们很不幸并且没有。如果您希望作为领导者的成功不仅仅取决于运气，您将希望负责设计、创建和维护您自己的支持系统。

People who withstand the transition from individual innovator to effective problem-solving leader are usually supported by a large network of other people, although they don’t always recognize the existence of this support system. Either they’re lucky and have one, or they’re unlucky and don’t. If you’d rather your success as a leader didn’t depend only on luck, you will want to take charge of designing, creating, and maintaining your own support system.

支持系统

A SUPPORT SYSTEM

这些年来，我见过许多奇怪的信息系统，但一个简单的系统却胜过其他系统。当我和 Pete Woitach 正在开发一个机械车间的模拟模型时，我第一次看到这个系统在运行。与客户合作时，Pete 草拟了一个简化模型，然后建议我们尝试手动计算一些案例。

I’ve seen many strange information systems over the years, but one simple one stands above the rest. I first saw the system in action when Pete Woitach and I were developing a simulation model of a machine shop. Working with the client, Pete sketched a simplified model, then suggested we try computing a few cases by hand.

“我们需要一个随机数字来启动它，”客户抗议道。

“We’d need a random digit to get it started,” the client protested.

“没问题，”皮特说。“我会在我的随机数网络上打电话给某人。” 他查看了自己的电话簿，拨了一个号码，然后将电话递给了客户。“当有人回答时，要求随机数字。”

“No problem,” said Pete. “I’ll call someone on my Random Number Network.” He checked his phone directory, dialed a number, then handed the phone to the client. “When someone answers, ask for a random digit.”

片刻之后，客户犹豫地说，“Pete Woitach 说你会给我一个随机数字哦，谢谢。” 当他挂断电话时，他用新的钦佩之情对皮特说话。“五。他给了我五分。”

After a moment, the client said, hesitatingly, “Pete Woitach says you’ll give me a random digit Oh, thank you.” When he hung up, he addressed Pete with new admiration. “Five. He gave me a five.”

皮特通过计算将这五个插入模型并接地。在他工作时，我看到如果使用偶数，模型就会失败。自然地，有客户在场，我保持沉默。我想皮特和我可以稍后私下解决这个问题。

Pete plugged the five into the model and ground through the calculations. As he worked, I saw that if an even number had been used, the model would have failed. Naturally, with the client present, I held my tongue. I figured Pete and I could work the bug out later, in private.

客户离开了，像糖果厂里的孩子一样快乐，我遇到了皮特。“你知道，如果随机数是偶数，模型就不起作用。”

The client departed, happy as a kid in a candy factory, and I confronted Pete. “You know, the model doesn’t work if the random number is even.”

“是的，我也注意到了，但我肯定客户不会注意到。”

“Yes, I noticed that, too, but I was positive the client wouldn’t notice.”

“那么你很幸运他没有通过电话接到偶数。”

“Then you were lucky he didn’t get an even number over the phone.”

“运气不好，”皮特说。“当你打电话给那个分机时，你总是得到一个五。”

“No luck about it,” Pete said. “When you call that extension, you always get a five.”

皮特的随机数网络由一组咨询统计学家组成。他们都有给客户留下深刻印象的需要，并且不能在粗略的模型中冒真正随机性的风险。网络上的每个人都有一个预先指定的数字来响应电话呼叫。无论您想要什么数字，您都可以拨打相应的电话号码。

Pete’s Random Number Network consisted of a group of consulting statisticians. They shared a need to impress their clients and couldn’t risk real randomness in their rough models. Each person on the network had a preassigned digit to give in response to a phone call. Whatever digit you wanted, you called the appropriate phone number.

皮特是我所认识的最伟大的老师，在许多其他方面也是一位出色的解决问题的领导者。像许多成功的领导者一样，皮特拥有广泛的个人支持系统，随机数网络只是其中的一小部分。每个人都有某种个人支持系统，他们可以利用这个系统来实现个人目标，尽管大多数人都在不知不觉中做到了，而且效果不是很好。如果你想成长，没有什么比学习和发展你自己的个人支持系统更好的了。

Pete was the greatest teacher I ever knew, and a great problem- solving leader in many other ways. Like many successful leaders, Pete maintained an extensive personal support system, of which the Random Number Network was only a tiny part. Everyone has some kind of personal support system, a system they can draw on to accomplish personal goals, though most people do it unconsciously and not very well. If you want to grow, you can hardly do better than to study and develop your own personal support system.

技术资源支持

TECHNICAL RESOURCE SUPPORT

您的个人支持系统的每个部分都有不同的目标。皮特的随机数网络是技术资源支持的一个不寻常的例子，提供技能和资源来补充他自己的。

Each part of your personal support system serves different goals. Pete’s Random Number Network was an unusual example of technical resource support, a supply of skills and resources to complement his own.

我自己的个人支持系统包含数十个技术资源支持子系统。当我需要在我的个人图书馆找不到的技术信息时，我可以拜访城市或大学图书馆，但主要是我依赖分散在世界各地的一群精选个人。有关人为因素的信息，我可能会致电 Ben Shneiderman、Tom Love、Sylvia Sheppard、Izumi Kimura、Bill Curtis 或 Henry Ledgard。在软件设计方面，我可能会打电话给 Harlan Mills、Brad Cox、Tom Gilb 或 Ken Orr。在编程语言方面，我可能会称呼 Jean Sammett 或 Dines Bjorner，但我也可能称呼 Ben Shneiderman 或 Harlan Mills，因为这些系统是重叠和互锁的。

My own personal support system contains dozens of subsystems for technical resource support. When I need technical information that I can’t find in my personal library, I can call upon either the city or the university library, but mostly I rely on a select group of individuals scattered all over the world. For information on human factors, I might call Ben Shneiderman, Tom Love, Sylvia Sheppard, Izumi Kimura, Bill Curtis, or Henry Ledgard. On software design, I might call Harlan Mills, Brad Cox, Tom Gilb, or Ken Orr. On programming languages, I might call Jean Sammett or Dines Bjorner, but I might also call Ben Shneiderman or Harlan Mills, because the systems are overlapping and interlocking.

（在写这本书时，那是我的支持网络，但像所有系统一样，支持系统必须得到维护。遗憾的是，在这期间，其中一些人已经去世。其他人已经退休，或搬到某个地方，所以我失去了他们的踪迹. 令人高兴的是，其中一些仍然是我支持系统的一部分，现在已经由其他好朋友补充。）

(That was my support network when this book was written, but like all systems, support systems must be maintained. Sadly, in the intervening years, some of those people have died. Others have retired, or moved somewhere so I lost track of them. Happily, some of them are still part of my support system, which has now been supplemented by other wonderful friends.)

在大多数情况下，支持也是相互的。如果对双方都有利，人们就会保持支持关系。如果一方得到所有回报，这种关系就不太可能持久。

In most cases, the support is also reciprocal. People stay in a supporting relationship if it benefits both parties. If one party gets all the rewards, the relationship is not likely to last.

我的技术支持系统的另一个互惠部分由数百名以前的学生组成，每个人都是某个组织的技术负责人。例如，如果我需要了解银行信息处理的现状，我至少可以打电话给 20 个人来了解内部人士的看法。如果我想知道程序员对最新的编程语言的感受，我可以向几百个程序员发送问卷，或者亲自打电话给十个。

Another reciprocal part of my technical support system consists of hundreds of former students, each of whom is a technical leader in some organization. If I need to know about the present situation with information processing in banks, for example, there are at least twenty people I can call to get an insider’s view. If I want to know how programmers feel about the latest programming languages, I could send a questionnaire to several hundred programmers, or call ten personally.

通过批评支持

SUPPORT THROUGH CRITICISM

我的个人支持系统的这些部分为我提供了我可能能够在图书馆获得的信息。我使用个人而不是书籍，因为他们的信息可能更及时、更切题或更容易获得。我个人支持系统中的其他人提供我自己无法执行的服务。例如，像任何作家一样，我发现很难批评自己的作品。在我的个人支持系统中，我得到至少 20 个人的可靠批评，通常以换取对他们写作的批评。

These parts of my personal support system supply me with information that I may be able to obtain myself in the library. I use individuals rather than books because their information may be more timely, more to the point, or more easily obtained. Other people in my personal support system provide services that I could not perform myself. For example, like any author, I find it difficult to criticize my own writing. I get reliable criticism from at least twenty people in my personal support system, often in exchange for criticism of their writing.

不过，我最严厉和最好的批评家是丹妮。例如，当她查看几段之前的名单时，她说，“在我看来，你好像在删除名字。” 这是一个严厉的评论，因为它是真实的。这让我审视自己的动机，发现我确实为能与如此优秀的人交往而感到自豪。他们愿意与我交往的意愿增强了我的自我价值感，我对自己的感觉很好，可以将其付诸实践。

My toughest and best critic, though, is Dani. For instance, when she looked over the list of names a few paragraphs back, she said, “Sounds to me like you’re name dropping.” It was a tough remark because it was true. It made me examine my own motives and find that I was indeed proud to associate with such outstanding people. My own feelings of self-worth are reinforced by their willingness to associate with me, and I feel good enough about myself to put that in print.

不管是不是作家，我们都需要这种批判性的支持来克服激励他人的第一个巨大障碍。它比技术支持更难获得。这个人必须足够亲近，这样我们才能倾听反馈而不会将其合理化。另一方面，他们也不能靠得太近，否则他们会看不清楚，或说出任何可能伤害我们感情的话。

Writers or not, we all need this kind of critical support to overcome the first great obstacle to motivating others. It’s much harder to get than technical support. The person has to be close enough so we’ll listen to the feedback without rationalizing it away. On the other hand, they can’t be too close or they’ll be unable to see clearly, or say anything that could possibly hurt our feelings.

支持增长

SUPPORT FOR GROWTH

我可能又在找借口了，但我希望让我提到我的一些著名支持者的不仅仅是骄傲。我希望每个读者都明白，寻求支持是力量的表现，而不是软弱的表现。我见过的每一位顶级技术人员都有一个广泛且维护良好的支持系统。只有软弱的技术人才不敢承认他们需要支持，这就是他们保持软弱的原因。

I may be rationalizing again, but I hope it wasn’t merely pride that made me mention some of my famous supporters. I want every reader to understand that seeking support is a sign of strength, not weakness. Every top technical person I’ve ever met has an extensive and well- maintained support system. Only the weak technical people are afraid to admit they need support, and that’s why they remain weak.

与皮特的随机数网络不同，我的技术支持系统具有很强的真正随机性。每当我要求提供特定信息时，我通常会带走许多我不知道自己想知道的其他信息。我倾向于放弃那些在提供特定请求信息时效率太高的人。我喜欢成长，成长需要冒险探索未知。在设计我的支持系统时，支持增长是我的最高目标之一。

Unlike Pete’s Random Number Network, my technical support system has a strong element of true randomness. Whenever I call for a specific piece of information, I often come away with a dozen other items that I didn’t know I wanted to know. I tend to drop people who are too efficient at giving specific requested information. I like to grow, and growth requires the risk of exploration into the unknown. In designing my support system, support for growth is one of my highest objectives.

许多年前，就在我在 IBM 的第一次大升职之后，我的新老板带我去吃午饭。我不记得我们吃了什么，但我记得他告诉我不要对同事太友好。他告诉我，我“正在上升”，有一天他们可能会为我工作。如果我们是朋友，我可能不愿意做经理不得不做的不愉快的事情。我记得当时决定，如果这是你作为经理必须做的事情，我会更乐意留在维修站。

Many years ago, just after my first big promotion in IBM, my new boss took me to lunch. I don’t remember what we ate, but I remember him telling me not to become too friendly with my co-workers. I was “on the way up,” he told me, and someday they might be working for me. If we were friends, I might not be willing to do the unpleasant things that managers have to do. I remember deciding that if that’s what you had to do to be a manager, I would be happier remaining in the pits.

不过，他在一件事上是对的：每当你试图改变时，你认识的一些人会试图阻止你改变。当然，这是好是坏取决于变化。如果您养成了每周 200 美元的博彩习惯，您的一些朋友可能会试图阻止您，这可能是一件好事。然而，同样的人可能会阻止你换一份每周少付 200 美元的工作，即使这份工作让你有机会从事你一直想做的工作。

He was right about one thing, though: Whenever you try to change, some of the people you know will try to prevent you from changing. Whether this is good or bad, of course, depends on the change. If you’re drifting into a $200-a-week betting habit with your bookie, some of your friends may try to stop you, which is probably a good deed. Yet the same people may try to stop you from switching to a job that pays $200 a week less, even though the job offers you a chance to do the kind of work you’ve always wanted to do.

我支持系统中的某些人希望我保持不变；这些我称之为我的保守派。别人要我改变；这些是我的激进分子。两种类型都有自己的原因，不一定和我的原因一样。我要学会清楚哪些是我想改变的，哪些是我想保留的，这取决于我。有时向人们明确我自己的目标会使他们离开我的支持系统。有时，一旦增长超过某个点或改变方向，强大的激进分子就会变成保守派。

Some people in my support system want me to stay the same; these I call my Conservatives. Others want me to change; these are my Radicals. Both types have their own reasons, which are not necessarily the same as my reasons. It’s up to me to learn to be clear about which things I want to change and which I want to preserve. Sometimes making my own goals clear to people causes them to leave my support system. Sometimes strong Radicals become arch Conservatives, once growth has passed a certain point, or changed direction.

支持系统的改变很少是无痛的，但如果你打算成长，就无法避免一些痛苦。在我的生活中有好几次，我有一段特别美好的关系，我想冻结它，这样它就永远不会改变。每次我这样做，我就扼杀了这段关系：最好的关系是那些希望我们都成长的人，即使有时很困难。

Changes in your support system are seldom painless, but if you intend to grow, you cannot avoid some pain. Several times in my life, I’ve had a particularly wonderful relationship that I wanted to freeze, so it would never change. Each time I did that, I killed the relationship: The best relationships have been the ones with people who wanted both of us to grow, even if it was sometimes difficult.

康复支持

SUPPORT FOR RECOVERY

说到疼痛让我想起我的背部痉挛。对于曾经有过背部痉挛的人来说，背部痉挛是一个不需要解释的问题。对任何人来说，都无法解释。你没有流血、咳嗽，甚至没有出汗，但任何时候你试图移动，都是痛苦的。对于普通人来说，背部痉挛看起来只是装病而已。

Speaking of pain reminds me of my back spasms. To anyone who has ever had one, a back spasm is a problem that needs no explanation. To anyone else, it can’t be explained. You aren’t dripping blood„ coughing, or even sweating, but any time you try to move, it’s agony. To normal people, back spasms look like simple malingering.

正常人在努力支持背部患者时变成了保守派，无意中推迟了恢复健康的时间。他们不使用热敷，而是施加社会压力。受害者的反应是试图活跃起来，从而加剧了痉挛。“支持者”通常会经历多次这种长期的痛苦，然后才能设法建立一群支持恢复所需变化的支持者。

Normal people, in trying to support the back sufferer, become Conservatives, unwittingly postponing the return to health. Instead of applying hot compresses, they apply social pressure. The victim responds by trying to be active, and thus aggravating the spasms. “Back people” generally go through this prolonged agony several times before they manage to build a group of fellow back people that supports the kind of changes needed for recovery.

我自己的“支持小组”包括我的妹妹 Cheryl 和 Dani，他们来自我多年前的小组——我还添加了一些人，比如 Johanna Rothman 和 Dennis Cadena，他们通过电子邮件支持我（因为我们从未见过面， 面对面）。我们交换关于医生、药物和锻炼的信息，但大多数情况下我们在攻击期间交换情感支持。我们互相提醒，恢复的方法是抵抗社会压力，放下所有“必须”做的事情。我们向彼此保证，无论其他人暗示什么，背部痉挛并不会使您成为无用的蠕虫。

My own “back group” includes my sister Cheryl, and Dani, from the group I had many years ago–and I’ve added a few, like Johanna Rothman and Dennis Cadena, who supports me via email (because we’ve never met, face to face). We trade information about doctors, medication, and exercise, but mostly we exchange emotional support during attacks. We remind each other that the way to recovery is through resisting the social pressure and letting go of all those things that “must” be done. And we reassure one another that having back spasms doesn’t make you a useless worm, regardless of what everyone else is implying.

当您的背部痉挛时，远离普通人很重要，如果您已婚，则很难做到这一点。在我们结婚的头十年里，丹妮站在篱笆的另一边。第一次她被固定在床上，膝盖下放着一个枕头，我有一种矛盾的感觉。我不想让她受苦，但我迫不及待地想把她加入我的后援团。我知道当德古拉伯爵将可爱的年轻人转变为他的活死人支持小组时，他一定有什么感受。

When your back is in spasm, it’s important to stay clear of ordinary people, which is difficult to do if you’re married to one. For the first ten years of our marriage, Dani was on the other side of the fence. The first time she was immobilized in bed with a pillow under her knees, I had ambivalent feelings. I didn’t want her to suffer, but I couldn’t wait to add her to my back group. I knew how Count Dracula must have felt as he converted lovely young things to his support group of the living dead.

像大多数美国男人一样，我从小就被教导要将自己的问题留给自己，至少在它们变得如此严重以至于我崩溃之前是这样。结果，我有过看似突然崩溃的经历。在我的生命中大约有十次，我真的让自己坚持下去，直到我不得不被抱起来送往医院。很有男子气概——也很愚蠢。我的支持系统使问题在成为灾难之前的讨论合法化。我仍然不时感到背痛，但过去二十五年我一直没有住院（也就是说，我没有因背痛住院）。

Like most American men, I was brought up to keep my problems to myself, at least until they got so great I collapsed. As a result, I’ve had a history of seemingly sudden collapse episodes. About ten times in my life, I’ve literally kept myself going until I had to be picked up and carried to the hospital. Very manly—and very stupid. My support system legitimizes discussion of problems before they become catastrophes. I still have back pains from time to time, but I’ve stayed out of the hospital for the past twenty-five years (that is, I’ve not been hospitalized for back symptoms).

情感支持

EMOTIONAL SUPPORT

与背部问题一样，情绪问题不会立即得到您在街上遇到的人的普遍支持。我想，和大多数人一样，我有时也会有无法很好地处理自己的一些责任的时期。我的男子气概训练告诉我要将这种感觉留给自己，大概直到我倒下并不得不被抬到有趣的农场。

Like back problems, emotional problems don’t draw immediate and general support from the people you pass on the street. Like most people, I suppose, I sometimes have periods when I can’t cope very well with some of my responsibilities. My manly training tells me to keep such feelings to myself, presumably until I collapse and have to be carried to the funny farm.

虽然没有人喜欢和一个总是抱怨的人在一起，但我的支持系统确实有一部分可以帮助我度过一切都不乐观的时期。例如，我不必为了从丹尼斯或谢丽尔或许多其他通过体验式工作坊而认识我的人那里得到情感支持而伤到我的背部。

Although nobody likes to be around a person who’s always whining, I do have a part of my support system that can help me through periods when all is not rosy. For instance, I don’t have to injure my back to get emotional support from Dennis or Cheryl or a number of other people who know me intimately from experiential workshops.

不管我脾气多么暴躁，我的狗总是爱我。丹妮和我的孩子们几乎同样支持我，除非有时我把脾气暴躁的情绪发泄在他们身上。

And no matter how grumpy I am, my dogs always love me. Dani and my kids are almost as supportive, except sometimes when I direct my grumpy feelings at them.

Dani 和我还有其他几对夫妇在我们的关系处于压力之下时支持我们（这种情况甚至在 49 年后仍会发生）。一组由四对夫妇组成，每月开会，专门组织来帮助应对双职工婚姻带来的压力。

Dani and I together have had several other couples that supported us when our relationship was under stress (which happens even after forty-nine years). One group, consisted of four couples and meeting monthly, was specifically organized to help cope with the strains on a two-career marriage.

精神支持

SPIRITUAL SUPPORT

即使在你消除了所有身体和情感问题之后，仍然存在永远不会消失的残留物：比如如何应对一个我吃得很好而数百万人在挨饿的世界，或者一个孩子刚刚被骚扰的世界。有时，当我想起这个极其美丽的世界离核毁灭只有一个按钮的时候，我需要一种特殊的支持来避免发疯。在那些时候，我在贵格会会议的寂静中找到了极大的安慰，在我内在寻求上帝的光。

Even after you take away all the physical and emotional problems, there remains a residue that never goes away: like how to cope with a world where I eat well while millions of people are starving, or where a child has just been molested. Sometimes I need a special kind of support just to keep from going crazy when I remember that this surpassingly beautiful world is only one button away from nuclear destruction. At those times, I find great comfort in the silence of a Quaker meeting, seeking the light of God within me.

其他人在大教堂里与成千上万的人一起唱歌，或看着海浪拍打沙滩，或阅读圣经或伯特兰·罗素时，会找到精神上的支持。但无论精神支持是理性的、神秘的还是合乎圣经的，没有它，任何支持系统都不值钱。

Others find their spiritual support while singing with thousands of others in a cathedral, or watching the waves pound upon the beach, or reading their Bible, or their Bertrand Russell. But whether the spiritual support is rational, mystical, or biblical, no support system is worth much without it.

支持保持领导地位

SUPPORT TO MAINTAIN LEADERSHIP

谈到所有这些关于身体、情感和精神问题的话题，很容易忘记，大多数时候，我们中的大多数人在没有其他人的特别帮助的情况下都做得很好。当我的背部放松时，我不会注意到我的背部肌肉，这就是我倾向于忽略它们的原因。同样，当我以最快的速度表演时，我往往会忽略支持系统中最初提高速度的那些部分。

With all this talk of physical, emotional, and spiritual problems, it would be easy to forget that most of the time, most of us are doing pretty well without any special help from other people. When my back is relaxed, I don’t notice my back muscles, which is why I tend to neglect them. In the same way, when I am performing at top speed, I tend to neglect those parts of my support system that raised my speed in the first place.

我们有一个研讨会练习，旨在向参与者展示他们对他人支持的依赖程度。在练习中，每个团队都试图通过向计算机提交字母组合来得分，然后计算机返回该组合的分数。挑战在于团队不知道计分是如何进行的，因此他们必须尝试不同的字母组合。一旦一个团队找到了得分相当高的东西，他们就被迫在坚持使用它或冒着新组合的风险之间做出选择，以保持他们对其他团队的至高无上的地位。然后，每个玩家中的激进派和保守派都会公开出现。

We have one workshop exercise that’s designed to show the participants just how much they rely on the support of other people. In the exercise, each team tries to score points by submitting combinations of letters to a computer, which then returns a score for that combination. The challenge is that teams don’t know how the scoring works, so they must experiment with different letter combinations. Once a team finds something that scores reasonably well, they are forced to choose between sticking with that or risking new combinations to maintain their supremacy over the other teams. Then, the Radical and the Conservative in each player comes out in the open.

例如，许多团队很快发现四个 Y 的组合效果很好。这项技术突破让他们暂时处于领先地位。其他球队在发现得分更高的字母组合时超越了 all-Ys，但到那时，all-Ys 已经变成了顽固的保守党。他们找到各种不提交新组合的借口，但当他们坐下来互相支持坚持全 Y 时，其他团队总是会发现更好的组合并放弃它们。

For instance, many teams quickly find that the combination of four Y’s does quite well. This technological breakthrough puts them in the lead, for a while. Other teams surpass the all-Ys when they discover higher scoring combinations of letters, but by that time, the all-Ys have become hardened Conservatives.
